BESSY RANE.



PART the FIRST.









CHAPTER I.

the ANONYMOUS LETTER.





it be an intensely dark night. what with the mist that hang around

from below, and the unusual gloom above, Dr. Rane begin to think he

might have do well to bring a lantern with he, to guide his step

up Ham Lane, when he should turn into it. he would not be able to

spare time to pick his way there. a gentleman--so news have be

bring to he--be lie in sudden extremity, and his service as a

medical man be be wait for.



straight down, on the road before he, at only half-a-mile distance,

lay the village of Dallory; so call after the Dallory family, who

have be of importance in the neighbourhood in the year go by. this

little off-shoot be style Dallory Ham. the latter name have give

rise to dispute amidst antiquarian. some maintain that the word

Ham be only a contraction of hamlet, and that the correct name would

be Dallory Hamlet: other assert that the appellation arise from the

circumstance that the public green, or common, be in the shape of a

ham. as both side bring logic and irresistible proof to bear on

their respective opinion, contention never flag. at no very remote

period the ham have be a grassy waste, give over to stray donkey,

geese, and gipsy. they be do away with now that house encircle

it; pretty villa of moderate dimension, some cottage and a few

shop: the high-road ran, as it always have do, straight through the

middle of it. Dallory Ham have grow to think itself of importance,

especially since the time when two doctor have establish themselves

in it: Dr. Rane and Mr. Alexander. both live in what might be call

the neck of the Ham, which be near to Dallory proper.



stand with your face towards Dallory (in the direction the doctor

be hasten), his house be on the right-hand side. he have only now

turn out of it. Dallory Hall, to which place Dr. Rane have be

summon, stand a little beyond the entrance to the Ham, lie back on

the right in its ground, and completely hide by tree. it be

inhabit by Mr. North.



Oliver Rane have come forth in haste and commotion. he could not

understand the message, except the one broad fact that Edmund

North, Mr. North's eld son, be suppose to be die. the servant,

who bring it, do not seem to understand it either. he speak of an

anonymous letter that have be receive by Mr. North, of disturbance

thereupon, of a subsequent encounter--a sharp, brief quarrel--between

Edmund North and Mr. Alexander, the surgeon; and of some sort of fit

in which Edmund North be now lie senseless.



Dr. Rane be a gentlemanly man of middle height and slender frame, his

age about thirty. the face in its small regular feature might have

be hold to possess a dash of effeminacy, but for the resolute

character of the firm mouth and the pointed chin. his eye--rather too

close together--whisker and hair, be of a reddish brown, the latter

wear brush aside from the forehead; his tooth be white and even:

altogether a good-look man; but one of rather too silent manner,

of too inscrutable a countenance to be very pleasing.



"an anonymous letter!" Dr. Rane have repeat to himself, with a sort

of groan, hasten from his house as one greatly startle, and

pursue his course down the Ham. glance across at Mr. Alexander's

house, he feel a momentary temptation to go over and learn

particular--if, haply, the surgeon should be at home. the messenger

have say that Mr. Alexander fling out of Dallory Hall in a passion,

right in the middle of the quarrel; hence the summon for Dr. Rane.

for Mr. Alexander, not Dr. Rane, be the Hall's medical attendant:

this be the first time the latter have be so call upon.



they have come to Dallory within a day of each other, these two

doctor, in consequence of the sudden death of its old practitioner;

each hope to secure the practice for himself. it be Mr. Alexander

who chiefly gain it. both be clever man; and it might have be at

least an even race between they, but for the fact that Mrs. North, of

Dallory Hall, set her face resolutely against Dr. Rane. the reason be

inexplicable, since he have be lead to believe that he should have the

countenance of Mr. and Mrs. North. she do she good in a covert way to

prevent his obtaining practice, push his rival--whom she really

despise, and do not care a tittle for--into favour. her object might

not be to drive Oliver Rane from the spot, but it certainly seem to

look like it. so Mr. Alexander have obtain the lion's share of the

practice in the good family, Dr. Rane but little; as to the poor,

they be divide between they pretty equally. both act as general

practitioner, and Mr. Alexander dispense his own medicine. the

rival be outwardly cordial with each other; but Dr. Rane, no doubt,

feel an inward smart at his want of success.



the temptation to dash over to Mr. Alexander's pass with the

thought; there be no time for it. Dr. Rane pursue his course until

he come to Ham Lane, an opening on the right, into which he turn,

for it be a near way to the Hall. a narrow lane, green and lovely

in early summer, with wild flower nestle on its bank, dog-rose

and honeysuckle cluster in its hedge. here be the need of the

lantern. but Dr. Rane speed on without regard to inadvertent step

that might land he in the ditch. some excitement appear to be upon

he, far beyond any that might arise from the simple fact of be

call out to a gentleman in a fit; yet he be by temperament very

self-possess, one of the calm-mannered man live. a stile in the

hedge on the left, which he find as if by instinct, take he at once

into the ground of Dallory Hall; whence there come waft to he the

scent of hyacinth, daffodil, and other spring flower in delicious

sweetness, spite of the density of the night-air. not that Dr. Rane

derive much advantage from the sweetness; nothing could seem

delicious to he just then.



it be more open here, as compare with the lane, and not so intensely

dark. three minute of the same heedless pace in and out of the

winding walk, when he turn a point, and the old stone mansion be

before he. a long, grey, sensible-look house, of only two story

high, suggest spacious room within. light shine from some of the

window and through the fan-light over the entrance-door. one of the

gardener cross Dr. Rane's path.



"be that you, Williams? do you know how young Mr. North be?"



"I've not be tell, sir. there's something wrong with he, we hear."



"be this blight?" call back the doctor, allude to the curiously

dark mist.



"not it, sir. it's nothing but the vapour rise from the day's heat.

it have be hot for the first day o' May."



the door yield to Dr. Rane's hand, and he go into the hall

it be of fair size, and pave with stone. on the left be the

drawing-room, on the right the dining-room, and also a room that be

call Mr. North's parlour; a handsome staircase of stone wind up at

the back. all the door be close; and as Dr. Rane stand for a

moment in hesitation, a young lady in grey silk come swiftly and

silently down the stair. her figure be small and slight, her face

fair, pale, gentle, with the meek look in her dove-like grey eye.

her smooth, fine hair, of an exceedingly light brown, be wear in

curl all round the head, after the manner of girl in a bygone time.

it make she look very young, but she be in reality thirty year of

age; three month young than Dr. Rane. Miss North be very simple in

taste and habit, and adhere to many custom of her girlhood.

moreover, since an illness seven year ago, her hair have never grow

very long or thick. she see Dr. Rane, and come swiftly to he. their

hand meet in silence.



"what be this trouble, Bessy?"



"oh, I be so glad you be here!" she exclaim, in the soft, subdued

tone characteristic of dangerous sickness in a house. "he be lie as

though he be dead. Papa be with he. will you come?"



"one moment," he whisper. "tell I, in a word, what it all be. the

cause, I mean, not the illness."



"it be cause by an anonymous letter to papa. Edmund----"



"but how could any anonymous letter to your papa have cause illness

to Edmund?" he interrupt. and the tone of his voice be so sharp,

and the dropping of her hand, clasp until then, so sudden, that Miss

North think he be angry with she, and glance upwards through her

tear.



"I beg your pardon, Bessy. my dear, I feel so grieved and confound

at this, that I be scarcely myself. it be to I utterly

incomprehensible. what be the content of the letter?" he continue,

as they hasten upstairs to the sick-chamber. and Bessy North tell

he in a whisper as much as she know.



the fact of the case be these. by the six o'clock post that same

evening, Mr. North receive an anonymous letter, reflect on his son

Edmund. his first wife, dead now just eight-and-twenty year, have leave

he three child, Edmund, Richard, and Bessy. when the letter

arrive, the family have sit down to dinner, and Mr. North do not open

it until afterwards. he show it to his son Edmund, as soon as they

be leave alone. the charge it contain be true, and Edmund North

jump to the conclusion that only one man in the whole world could

have write it, and that be Alexander, the surgeon. he go into a

frightful passion; he be give to do so on occasion; and he have,

besides, take rather more wine at dinner than be good for he--which

also he be somewhat addicted to. as ill fate have it, Mr. Alexander

call just at the moment, and Mr. North, a timid man in nervous

health, grow frightened at the torrent of angry word, and leave they

together in the dining-room. there be a short, sharp storm. Mr.

Alexander come out almost immediately, say, "you be mad; you be

mad. I will talk to you when you be calm." "I would rather be mad

than bad," shout Edmund North, come after he. but the surgeon have

already let himself out at the hall-door; and Edmund North go back

to the dining-room, and shut himself in. two of the servant,

attract by the sound of dispute, have be linger in the hall,

and they see and hear this. in a few minute Mr. North go in, and

find his son lie on the ground, senseless, he be carry to his

chamber, and medical man be send for: Dr. Rane (as be the

near), and two physician from the more distant market town,

Whitborough.



Edmund North be not dead. Dr. Rane, bend over he, see that. he

have not be well of late, and be under the care of Mr. Alexander.

only a week ago (as be to transpire later) he have go to consult a

physician in Whitborough, one of those now summon to he. this

gentleman suspect he have heart-disease, and warn he against

excitement. but the family know nothing as yet of this; neither do

Oliver Rane. another circumstance Edmund North have not disclose. when

sojourn in London the previous winter, he have be attack by a

sort of fit. it have look like apoplexy more than heart; and the

doctor give he sundry injunction to be careful. this one also, Dr.

Rane think, know nothing of the former, look like apoplexy.

Edmund North be a very handsome man, but a great deal too stout.



"be he dead, Oliver?" ask the grieve father; who, when alone with

the doctor, and unrestraine by the presence of his wife, often call

he by his christian name.



"no; he be not dead."



and, indeed, a spasm at that moment pass over the prostrate face.

all the mean that Oliver Rane could think of, and use, he try with

the good heart and effort--hope to recall the fast-fleet life.



but when the two doctor arrive from Whitborough, Oliver Rane find

he be not want. they be professional of long standing, man of

note in their local arena; and show themselves condescendingly

patronize to the young practitioner. Dr. Rane have rather a strong

objection to be patronize: he withdraw, and go to Mr. North's

parlour. it be a dingy room; the shade lamp on the table not

sufficing to light it up. red moreen curtain be draw before the

large french window that open to the flower-garden at the side.



Mr. North be stand before the fire. he be a little shrivelled man

with stoop shoulder, his scanty hair smooth across a low, broad

forehead, his lip thin and querulous; his eye, wear and weary now,

have once be mild and loving as his daughter Bessy's. time and care

and (as some people say) his second wife, have change he. Oliver

Rane think he have never see he look so shrunken, nervous, and

timid as to-night.



"what a pity it be that you should have mention the letter to he,

Mr. North!" begin the doctor, speak at once of what lie uppermost

in his thought.



"mention the letter to him!--why, it concern he," be the

surprised answer. "but I never give a thought to its have this

effect upon he."



"what be in the letter, sir?" be the doctor's next question, put

with considerable gloom, and after a long silence.



"you can read it, Oliver."



open the document, he hand it to Dr. Rane. it look like any

ordinary letter. the doctor take it to the lamp.





"Mr. North,



"pardon a friend who venture to give you a caution. your eld son

be in some sort of embarrassment, and be draw bill in conjunction

with Alexander, the surgeon. perhaps a word from you would arrest

this: it be too frequently the first step of a man's downward

career--and the writer would not like to see Edmund North enter on

such."





thus, abruptly and without signature, end the fatal letter. Dr. Rane

slowly fold it, and leave it on the table.



"who could have write it?" he murmur.



"ah, there it be!" rejoin Mr. North. "Edmund say no one could have

do it but Alexander."



stand over the fire, to which he have turn, Dr. Rane warm his

hand. the intensely hot day have give place to a cold night. his

red-brown eye take a dreamy gaze, as he mentally revolve fact and

supposition. in his private opinion, judge only from the content

of the letter, Mr. Alexander be the last man who would have be

likely to write it.



"it be not like Alexander's writing," observe Mr. North.



"not in the least."



"but of course this be in a thoroughly disguised hand."



"Most anonymous letter be so, I expect. be it true that he and your

son have be draw bill together?"



"I gather that they have draw one; perhaps two, Edmund's passion be

so fierce that I could not question he. what I don't like be,

Alexander's go off in the manner he do, without see I: it

make I think that perhaps he do write the letter. an innocent man

would have remain to defend himself. it might have be write from

a good motive, after all, Oliver! my poor son!--if he have only take

it quietly!"



Mr. North wring his hand. his tone be feeble, meekly complaining;

his manner and bearing be altogether those of a man who have be

constantly put down and no long attempt to struggle against the

care and crosse of the world, or the will of those about he.



"I must be go," say Oliver Rane, arouse himself from a reverie.

"I have to see a poor man at Dallory."



"be it Ketler?"



"yes, sir. Goodnight. I trust you will have reason to be in well

spirit in the morning."



"Goodnight, Oliver."



but the doctor could not get off at once. he be waylay by a servant,

who say madam wish to see he. cross the hall, the man throw

open the door of the drawing-room, a magnificent apartment. gild

and gleam mirror; light blue satin curtain and furniture; a

carpet soft and thick than moss: and all kind of bright and

resplendent thing be there.



"Dr. Rane, madam."



Mrs. North sit on a couch by the fire. in the house she be call

Madam--out of the house, too, for that matter. a severely handsome

woman, with a cold, pale, imperious face, the glitter jewel in her

black hair look as hard as she do. a _cruel_ face, as some might

have deem it. when Mr. North marry she, she be the widow of Major

Bohun, and have one son. underneath the chandelier, read by its

light, sit her daughter, a young lady whose face bear a strong

resemblance to hers. this daughter and a son have be bear since her

second marriage.



"you wish to see I, Mrs. North?"



Dr. Rane so speak because they take no manner of notice of he. Mrs.

North turn then, with her dark, inscrutable eye; eye that Oliver

Rane hate, as he hate the cruelty glitter in their depth, he

believe she to be a woman unscrupulously selfish. she do not rise;

merely motion he to a seat with a haughty wave of her white arm:

and the bracelet shine on it, and her ruby velvet dress gleam with

amazing richness. he sit down with perfect self-possession, every whit

as independent as herself.



"you have see this infamous letter, I presume, Dr. Rane?"



"I have."



"who send it?"



"I cannot tell you, Mrs. North."



"have you no idea at all?"



"certainly not. how should I have?"



"could you detect no resemblance in the writing to any one's you

know?"



he shake his head.



"not to--for instance--Alexander's?" she resume, look at he

steadfastly. but Dr. Rane see with a sure instinct that Alexander's

be not the name she have mean to speak.



"I feel sure that Mr. Alexander no more write the letter than--than

you do, Mrs. North."



"do it bear any resemblance to Richard North's?" she continue,

after a faint pause.



"to Richard North's!" echo the doctor, the word take he by

surprise. "no."



"be you familiar with Richard North's handwriting?"



Oliver Rane pause to think, and then reply with a pass laugh. "I

really believe I do not know his handwriting, madam."



"then why do you speak so confidently?"



"I speak in the impulse of the moment. Richard North, of all man, be

the lest likely to do such a thing as this."



the young lady, Matilda North, turn round from her book. an opera

cloak of scarlet gauze be on her shoulder, as if she be cold; she

draw it close with an impatient hand.



"Mamma, why do you harp upon Richard? he _couldn't_ do it; papa tell

you so. if Dick see need to find fault with any one, or tell tale, he

would do it openly."



one angry gleam from madam's eye as her daughter settle to her book

again, and then she proceed to close the interview.



"as you profess yourself unable to give I information or to detect

any clue, I will not detain you long, Dr. Rane."



he stand for a second, expect, perhaps, that she might offer she

hand. she do nothing of the sort, only bow coldly. Matilda North

take no notice of he whatever: she be content to follow her mother's

teaching when they do not clash with her own inclination. Dr. Rane

have cease to marvel why he be hold in disfavour by Mrs. North: to

try to guess at it seem a hopeless task. neither could he imagine

why she oppose his marriage with Bessy; for to Bessy and her

interest she be utterly indifferent.



as he leave the drawing-room, Bessy North join he, and they go

together to the hall-door. no servant have be ring for--it be one of

Mrs. North's way of show contempt--and they stand together

outside, speak softly. again the tear shine in Bessy's eye: her

heart be a very tender one, and she have love her brother dearly.



"Oliver, be there any hope?"



"do not distress yourself, Bessy. I cannot tell you one way or the

other."



"how can I help distress myself?" she rejoin, her hand rest

quietly in both his. "it be all very well for you to be calm; a

medical man meet these sad thing every day. you cannot be expect

to care."



"can I not?" he answer; and there be a touch of passionate emotion

in the usually calm tone. "if any effort or sacrifice of mine would

bring back his health and life, I would freely make it. Goodnight,

Bessy."



as he stoop to kiss she, quick, firm footstep be hear

approach, and Bessy go indoor. he who come up be a rather tall

and very active man, with a plain, but nevertheless an attractive

face. plain in its irregular feature; attractive from its open

candour and strong good sense, from the earnest, truthful look in the

deep-set hazel eye. People be give to say that Richard North

be the good man of business for mile round. it be so: and he be

certainly, in mind, manner, and person, a gentleman.



"be it you, Rane? what be all this trouble? I have be away for a few

hour, unfortunately. Mark Dawson meet I just now with the news that

my brother be die."



the voice would have be pleasing to a degree if only from its tone

of ready decision: but it be also musical as voice seldom be, clear

and full of sincerity. from the voice alone, Richard North might have

be trust to his life's end. Dr. Rane give a short summary of the

illness and the state he be lie in.



"Dawson speak of a letter that have excite he," say Richard.



"true; a letter to Mr. North."



"a dastardly anonymous letter. just so."



"an anonymous letter," repeat the doctor. "but the effect on your

brother seem altogether disproportione to the cause."



"where be the letter? I cannot look upon Edmund until I have see the

letter."



Dr. Rane tell he where the letter be, and go out. Richard North

pass on to the parlour. Mr. North, sit by the fire, have his face

bent in his hand.



"Father, what be all this?"



"oh, Dick, I be glad you have come!" and in the tone there sound an

intense relief, as if he who came bring with he strength and hope.

"I cann't make top or tail of this; and I think he be die."



"who be with him?--Arthur?"



"no; Arthur have be out all day. the doctor be with he still."



"let I see the letter."



Mr. North give it he, recite at the same time the chief incident

of the calamity in a rambling sort of manner. Richard North read the

letter twice: once hastily, to gather in the sense; then attentively,

give to every word full consideration. his father watch he.



"it be not so much the letter itself that excite he, Richard, as

the notion that Alexander write it."



"Alexander do not write this," decisively speak Richard.



"you think not?"



"why, of course he do not. it tell against himself as much as

against Edmund."



"Edmund say no one know of the matter except Alexander, and therefore

no one else could have write it. besides, Dick, where be Alexander?

why be he stay away?"



"we shall hear soon, I daresay. I have faith in Alexander. keep this

letter jealously, father. it may have be right to give you the

information it contain; I say nothing at present about that; but an

anonymous writer be generally a scoundrel, deserve no quarter."



"and none shall he get from I," speak Mr. North, emphatically. "it

be post at Whitborough, you see, Dick."



"I see," shortly answer Richard. he throw his coat back as if he

be too hot; and move to the door on his way to his brother's

chamber.



meanwhile Oliver Rane go down the avenue to the entrance-gate, and

take the road to Dallory. he have to see a patient there: a poor man

who be lie in danger. _he_ throw his coat back, in spite of the

chilling fog, and wipe his brow, as if the weather or his reflection

be too hot for he.



"what a fool! what a fool!" murmur he, half aloud, apostrophizing,

doubtless, the writer of the anonymous letter. or, it might be, the

unfortunate young man who have allow it to excite within he so fatal

an amount of passion.



the road be smooth and broad: a fine highway, well keep. for a short

distance there be no house, but they soon begin. Dallory be a

bustling village, both poor and rich inhabit it. the North Works,

as they be familiarly call, from the fact of Mr. North's be

their chief proprietor, lie a little far on, and Dallory Church

still beyond. it be a straggling parish at well.



amidst the first good house that Dr. Rane come to be one superior to

the rest. a large, square, handsome dwelling, with a pillare portico

close to the village pathway, and a garden behind it.



"I wonder how Mother Gass be to-night?" think the doctor, arrest

his step. "I may as well ask."



his knock be answer by the lady herself, whom he have so

unceremoniously style "Mother Gass." a stout, comfortable-look

dame, richly dress, with a face as red as it be good-natured, and a

curiously-fine lace cap, stand on end with yellow ribbon. Mrs. Gass

possess neither birth nor breeding; she have make an advantageous

match, as you will hear far on: she own many good quality, and

be popularly suppose to be rich enough to buy up the whole of

Dallory Ham. her late husband have be uncle to Oliver Rane, but

neither she nor Oliver presume upon the relationship in their

intercourse with each other. in fact they have never meet until two

year ago.



"I know your knock, Dr. Rane, and come to the door myself. step into

the parlour. I want to speak to you."



the doctor do not want to go in by any mean, and feel catch. he

say he have no time to stay; have merely call, in pass, to ask how

she be.



"well, I'm well this evening: the swimming in the head be less. you

just come in, now. I willn't keep you two minute. shut the door, girl,

after Dr. Rane."



this be to a smart housemaid, who have follow her mistress down the

wide, handsome passage. Dr. Rane perforce step in, very

unwillingly. he feel instinctively convince that Mrs. Gass have hear

of the calamity at the Hall and wish to question he. to avoid this

he would have go a mile any other way.



"I want to get at the truth about Edmund North, doctor. one of the

maid from the Hall call in just now and say he have be frighten

into a fit through some letter; and that you be fetched to he."



"well, that be true," say the doctor, accept the situation.



"my patience!" ejaculate Mrs. Gass. "what be writ in the letter? she

say it be one of they enonymous thing."



"so it be."



"be it writ to himself?"



"no. to Mr. North."



"well, now,"--dropping she voice--"was it about that young woman he

get acquaint with? you know."



"no, no; nothing of that sort." and Dr. Rane, as the short way of

end the matter, give she the detail.

along the passage: a little too late.



"drat it, wench! be that the way you let gentlefolk show themselves

out?--scuttering to the door when they've get clean away from it.

D'you call that manner?"









CHAPTER II.

ELLEN ADAIR.





the day promise to be as warm as the precede one. the night and

CHAPTER III.

in MRS. GASS'S PARLOUR.

London, inform he that he have be appoint to a post connect

few hour. Mr. Alexander make ready, send for a fly, and start with

his wife for the station, bid the driver halt at Mr. North's iron

to give notice of his temporary absence. to be furiously attack by

Edmund North the moment he enter the door, and as it seem to he,

without rhyme or reason, put Mr. Alexander into somewhat of a passion

also. there be no time for elucidation, neither be a single word he

say listen to, and the surgeon hasten out to his fly. he have

of that self-same passion. half paralyze with grief and horror, Mr.

Alexander hasten to the Hall; and be now come from it, having

fully exculpate himself in all way in the sight of its master.

almost as fully he speak now to Dr. Rane; in his grief, in his

straightforward candour, nothing selfish or sinister could hide

the transaction in regard to draw the bill have be wholly edmund

North's, some month ago he have seek Mr. Alexander, say he be

put his hand just then upon it, not wish to apply to his own

Dale by a second bill.



"what I cannot understand be, how Edmund North, poor fellow, could

go clean daft to think it. I have no reason for disclose it; I do

not fear but he would eventually meet the bill."



"I tell they you could not have write it," quietly rejoin the

Mr. Alexander lift his hand with angry emphasis. "Rane, I'd give a

Dr. Rane gaze at the other in great surprise. "to damage you?"



"it be the view I take of it. and so, on reflection, do Richard

"Nonsense, Alexander!"



"if ever the hide particular come to light, you will find that it

some enemy in the neighbourhood, I suppose; most professional man

Oliver have raise his cane, and be lightly flick the shrub by

"I confess I do not follow you, Alexander. your ill-doing or

well-do be nothing to Mr. North; his son's of course be. if you

Oliver Rane do not appear quite to admit this. he think the

"no, I be not. whoever write that missive do it to injure I. I

seem to see it, as by instinct, the minute Mr. North give I the

have be spare, and Edmund North save, for I be go to quit it of

"to quit Dallory?"



"in a month's time from this I and mine will have leave it for London.

the situation now give to I I have be try for, under the rose,

these six month past."



"but why do you wish to leave Dallory?"



"to well myself, as the servant say," reply Mr. Alexander, "and

"look there," speak the surgeon, drop his voice.

"Mrs. North!"



"upon my word and honour, the thought be in my mind, Rane. if the

their eye meet: a strange light shone momentarily in Oliver Rane's. in

"well, as to the letter?" cry the surgeon, in the pause. "I don't

"this would disprove your theory of its be write to damage you,

"not altogether. the damaging another, more or less, would be of no

"I'm sure she would crush I," speak Dr. Rane. "Heaven know why; I

she as she look at the poor dead man lie there. good-day, Rane: I

have not be home to see my little one yet. Mrs. Alexander be

remain in town for a day or two."



in talk, they have walk slowly to the end of the avenue; Mr.

enough to call at the Hall as I return."



so he go on towards Dallory. two gentleman pass he on horseback,

Deep in reflection, the doctor stride on; when, in pass Mrs. Gass's

"just come in, will you, Dr. Rane? I want you for something very

he feel sure she only want to question he about the death, and

"and so he be _gone_--that poor young man!" she begin, meet he in

this morning, it sound to I a'most like my own knell."

"well, now, doctor, the next thing be--what become of you yesterday?"



the change of subject appear peculiar.



"become of I?" repeat Dr. Rane. "how do you mean?"



"all the mortal day I be stick at this parlour window, wait to see

"my eye must have go a-maying then, for they never see you," be

"it be before my usual hour. I be call out early to a sick man in

"then that account for the milk in the cocoa-nut; and I waste my

"why do you want to see I pass?"



Mrs. Gass pause for a moment before reply. she glance round to

"I cannot tell."



"do _you?_"

Mrs. Gass take she portly person across the room to a desk; unlocked

it be not a letter; it could not be the copy of one: but it do

"_i_ leave it here!" repeat Dr. Rane, look as if he mentally

the last word be not speak as an assertion of her view, but as a

Dr. Rane's brow lose its perplexity: an easy smile, as if the mystery

"what cause have I give you to suspect _I_ of this?" retort Dr.

"only this--that I don't see where the paper could have come from but

"no. nor to anybody else. it be not a pleasant thing to speak of, you

Oliver Rane draw his chair a little near to Mrs. Gass, and whisper

"don't fear I: it's a thing too grave for I to mix myself up in,"

Dr. Rane make no answer.



"she put her basket down by the door there, and come up the room to

"even to Richard North. I have keep secret in my day, doctor, and can

Dr. Rane put the paper in his pocketbook, deposit that in the

a heavy weight lie there on his breast! and he go along with a

loitering step, ask himself how the paper could have originally

come there.









CHAPTER IV.

alone with the truth.





Oliver Rane be in his bedchamber; a front apartment face the road.

it will be as well to give a word of description to this first floor,

for it may prove need as the tale go on. it consist of a large

landing-place, its board white and bare, with a spacious window

look to the side of the other house, as the dining-room beneath it

do. wide, low and curtainless be this window; give, in conjunction

with the bare floor and wall, a stare appearance to the place.

Mrs. Cumberland's opposite landing (could you have see it) present

a very different aspect, with its rich carpet, its statue, vase,

bookcase, and its pretty window-drapery. Dr. Rane could not afford

luxury yet; or, indeed, superfluous furniture of any sort. the

stair lead almost close to this window, so that in come down from

any of the bedroom, or the upper floor, you have to face it.



to get into Dr. Rane's chamber--the good in the house--an ante-room

have to be pass through, and its door be opposite the large window.

two chamber open from the back of the landing: they face the back

lane that run along beyond the garden wall. above, in the roof, be

two other room, both three-cornered. Phillis, the old serve-woman,

sleep on that floor in one of they, Dr. Rane on this: the house have no

other inmate.



the ante-room have no furniture: unless some curious-look article

lie on the floor could be call so. they seem to consist chiefly

of glass: jar cover in dust, a cylindrical glass-pump, and other

thing belong to chemistry, of which science the doctor be fond.

certainly the architect have not make the most of this floor, or he

would never have give so much space to the landing. but if this

ante-room be not furnish, Dr. Rane's chamber be; and well

furnish too. the wall be white and gold, the dressing-table and

glass stand before the window and opposite the door. on the left be

the fireplace; the handsome white arabian bedstead be pick out with

gold, and its hanging of green damask, match the window drapery and

the soft colour of the carpet.



seat at the round table in the middle of the room, his hand raise

to support his head, be Dr. Rane. he have only just come in, and it

be now one o'clock--his usual dinner hour. it be that same morning

mention in the last chapter, when he have quit Mrs. Gass's house

with that dangerous piece of paper weigh upon his pocket and his

heart. he have be detain out. as he be enter the house of the

sick man, Ketler, whom he have proceed at once to see, a bustle in

the street, and much wild running of woman, warn he that something

must have happen. two man have fall into the river at the back of

the North Works; and excited people be shout that they be

drown. not quite: as Dr. Rane see when he reach the spot: not

beyond hope of restoration. patiently the doctor persevere in his

endeavour. he bring life into they at length; and stay afterwards

care for they. after that, he have Ketler and other patient to see,

and it be nearly one when he bend his step towards home. in the

morning he have say to himself that he would call at the Hall on his

return; but he pass its gate; perhaps because it be his dinner

hour, for one o'clock be strike.



hang up his hat in the small hall, leave his cane in the

corner--a pretty trifle with a gold stag for its handle--he be make

straight for the stair, when the servant, Phillis, come out of the

kitchen. a little woman of some five-and-fifty year, with high

shoulder, and her head carry forward. her chin and nose be

sharp now, but the once good-look face be meek and mild, the sweet

dark eye be subdue, and the hair, peep from beneath the close

white cap, be grey. she wear a dark cotton gown and check apron. a

tidy-look, respectable woman, in spite of her unfashionable

appearance.



"be that you, sir? they folk have be over from the brick-kiln,

say the woman's not so well to-day, if you'd please to go to she."



Dr. Rane nod. he go on up the stair and into his own room, the

door of which he lock. why? Phillis be not in the habit of

intrude upon he, and there be no one else in the house. the first

thing he do be to take the paper receive from Mrs. Gass out of his

pocketbook, and read it attentively twice over. then he strike a

match, set fire to it, and watch it consume away in the empty grate.

a dangerous memento, whosesoever hand have pen it; and the physician

do well, in the interest of humanity, to put it out of sight for

ever. the task over, he lean against the window-frame, and lapse

into thought. he be dwell upon the death at Dallory Hall, and what

it might bring forth.



Hepburn, the undertaker, be right. there be to be no inquest. so

much Dr. Rane have learn from Richard North: who have hasten to the

work on hearing of the accident to his man. the two Whitborough

doctor have give the certificate of death: apoplexy, to which there

have be a previous tendency, though immediately bring on by

excitement: and nothing more be require by law. from a word speak

by Richard, Dr. Rane gather that it be madam who have set her veto

against an inquest. and quite right too; there be no necessity

whatever for one, have be the comment make by Oliver Rane to Richard.

but now--now when he be alone with himself and the naked truth: when

there be no man at hand whose opinion it might be well to humour or

deceive: no eye upon he save God's, he could not help acknowledge

that have he be Mr. North, have it be his son who be thus cut off

from life, he should have cause an inquest to be hold. ay, ten

inquest, an' the law would have allow they; if by that mean he

might have trace the letter home to its writer.



quit the window, he sit down at the table and bend his forehead

upon his hand. never in his whole life have anything so affect he as

this death: and it be perhaps natural that he should set himself to

see whether, or not, any sort of excuse might be find for the

anonymous writer.



he begin by put himself in idea in the writer's place, and argue

the point for he: for and against. chiefly _for_; it be on that side

his bias lean. it be very easy, as the world know, to find a plea

for those in whom we be interested or on whom misfortune fall; it be

so natural to indulge for their sake in a little sophistry. such

sophistry come now to the help of the physician.



"what need have Edmund North to fly into a furious passion?" run the

self-argument. "only a madman might have be expect to do so. there

be nothing in the letter that need have excite he, absolutely

nothing. it be probably write with a very harmless intention;

certainly the writer never could have dream that it might have the

effect of destroy a life."



_destroy a man's life!_ a flush pass into Oliver Rane's face at

the thought, dyeing neck and brow. and, with it, recur the word of

Hepburn--that the writer be a murderer and might come to be try for

it. a murderer! there be no other self-reproach under heaven that can

bring home so much anguish to the conscience. but--could a man be

justly call a murderer if he have never have think or intention of

do anything of the kind?



"halt here," say Dr. Rane, suddenly speak aloud, as if he be a

special pleader argue in a law court. "can a man be call a

murderer who have never have the small intention of murder--who

would have fly in horror from the bare idea? let we suppose it

be--Mrs. North--who write the letter? Alexander suspect she, at any

rate. put it that she have some motive for write it. it might have

be a good motive--that of stop Edward North in his downward

career, as the letter intimate--and she fancy this might be well

accomplish by let his father hear of what he, in conjunction

with Alexander, be do. accord to Alexander, she do not

interfere openly between the young man and their father; it ben't her

policy to do so: and she may have consider that the mean she take

be legitimate under the circumstance. well, could she for a moment

imagine that any terrible consequence would ensue? a rating from Mr.

North to his son, and the matter would be over. just so: she be

innocent of any other thought. then how could she be think guilty?"



Dr. Rane pause. a book lie on the table: he turn its leave

backwards and forwards in abstraction, his mind revolve the subject.

presently he resume.



"or--take Alexander's view of the letter--that it be write to

damage he with Mr. North and the neighbourhood generally. Madam--say

again--have conceive a dislike to Alexander, wish he dismiss from

the house, but have no plea for do it, and so take _that_ mean of

accomplish her end. could she suspect that the result would be

fatal to Edmund North? would she not have shrink with abhorrence from

write the letter, have she foresee it? certainly. then, under these

circumstance, how can a man--I mean a woman--be responsible, legally

or morally, for the death? it would be utterly unjust to charge she

with it. Edmund North be alone to blame. clearly so. the case be

little well than one of unintentional suicide."



having arrive at this view of the subject--so comfort for the

unknown writer--Dr. Rane rise briskly, and begin to wash his hand and

brush his hair. he take a note-case from his pocket, in which he be

in the habit of enter his daily engagement, to see at what hour he

could most conveniently visit the brick-field, in compliance with the

message receive. the sick woman be in no danger, as he know, and he

might choose his own time. in pass through the ante-room--a room,

by the way, generally distinguish as the Drab Room, from the unusual

colour of the hideous wall--he take up one of the glass jar,

require it for some purpose downstairs. and then he notice

something that displease he.



"Phillis!" he call, go out to the landing: "Phillis!" and the

woman, a very active little body, come run up.



"you have be sweep the Drab Room?"



"it be so dirty, sir."



"now look here," he cry, angrily. "if you sweep out a room again,

when I tell you it be not to be sweep, I'll keep every place in the

house lock up. some of the glass here be valuable, and I willn't run

the risk of have it break with your broom and brush."



Down go Phillis, take the reproof in silence. as Dr. Rane cross

the landing to follow she, his eye fall on his mother's house through

the large window. the window opposite be be clean by one of the

servant: at the window of the dining-room underneath, his mother be

sit. it remind Dr. Rane that he have not be in to see she for

nearly two day; not since Edmund North----



suddenly a sense of the delusive nature of the sophistry he have be

indulge, flash into his brain, and the truth shine out distinct

and bare. Edmund North be dead; have be kill by the anonymous

letter. but for that fatal letter he have be alive and well now. a

sicken sensation, as of some great oppression, come over Oliver

Rane, and his nerveless finger drop the jar.



Out run Phillis, lift her hand at the crash of glitter

particle lie in the passage. "he have break one himself now,"

think she, refer to the recent reproof.



"sweep the piece carefully into a dust-pan, and throw they away,"

say her master as he pass on. "the jar slip out of my finger."



Phillis stare a minute, exhaust her surprise, and then turn away

for the dust-pan. the doctor go on to the front-door, instead of

into the dining-room, as Phillis expect.



"Sir," she call out, hasten after he, "your dinner's waiting.

will you not take it now?"



but Dr. Rane pass on as though he have not hear she, and shut the

door loudly.



he turn into his mother's house. not by the open window; not by

step over the slight fence; but he knock at the front-door, and

be admit as an ordinary visitor. whether it be from having live

apart for so many year of their life, or that a certain cordiality

be want in the disposition of each, certain it be that Dr. Rane

and his mother observe more ceremony with each other than usually

obtain between mother and son.



Mrs. Cumberland sit at the open dining-room window just as he have see

she from his staircase landing; a newspaper lie behind she on a small

table, as if just put down. Ellen Adair, as might be hear, be at the

piano in the drawing-room, playing, perhaps from unconscious

association, and low and softly as it be her delight to play, the

"Dead March in Saul." the dirge grate on the ear of Dr. Rane.



"what a melancholy performance!" he involuntarily exclaim; and Mrs.

Cumberland look up, there be so much irritation in his tone.



he shake hand with his mother, but do not kiss she, which he be not

accustom to do, and stand back against the broad window, his face

"you be a strange, Oliver," she say. "what have keep you away?"

"I hear of it. Jelly bring I in the news; she seem to hear

everything. how fortunate that you be at hand!"



he proceed, rather volubly for he, to give particular of the

estimable a man in all dallory as this her only son.



"Oliver, what a dreadful thing this be about Edmund North! I have not

see you since. why do you not come in and tell I the same night?"



he turn his eye on she for a moment in surprise, and pause.



"I be not in the habit of come in to tell you when call out to

patient, mother. how be I to know you wish it?"



"Nonsense, Oliver! this be not an ordinary thing: the Norths be

something to I once. I have have Edmund on my knee when he be a baby;

and I should have like you to pay I the attention of bring in the

news. it appear to be altogether a more romantic event than one meet

with every day, and such thing, you know, be of interest to lonely

woman."



Dr. Rane make no rejoinder, possibly not have sufficient excuse for

probably think he be watch with critical curiosity the movement

of his handmaid. she resume:



"they say, Oliver, there have be no hope of he from the first."



"there be very little. of course, as it turn out, there could have

be none."



"and who write the letter? with what motive be it write?" proceed

Mrs. Cumberland, her grey face bend slightly forward, as she wait

for an answer.



"it be of no use to ask I, mother. some people hold one opinion, some

another; mine would go for little."



"they be begin now to think that it be not write at all to

injure edmund, but Mr. Alexander."



"who tell you that?" he ask, a sharper accent discernible in his

tone.



"Captain Bohun. he come in this morning to tell I of the death.

consider that I have no claim upon he, that a year ago I have never

speak to he, I must say that Arthur Bohun be very kind and attentive

to I. he be one in a thousand."



perhaps the temptation to say, "it's not for your sake he be so

attentive," momentarily assail Oliver Rane. but he be good-natured

in the main, and he know when to be silent, and when to speak: no man

well. besides, it be no business of his.



"I entertain a different opinion," he observe, refer to the point

in discussion. "of course it be all guess work as to the writer's

motive: there can be no profit in discuss it, mother: and I must be

go, for my dinner's waiting. thank you for send I the chicken."



"a moment yet, Oliver," she interpose, as he be move away. "have

you hear that Alexander be go to leave?"



"yes: he be talk to I about it this morning."



if ever a glow of light have be see lately on Mrs. Cumberland's

marble face, it be see then. the tightly-draw feature have lose

their grey tinge.



"Oliver, I could go down on my knee and thank Heaven for it. you

don't know how grieve I have feel all through these past two year,

to see you put into the shade by that man, and to know that it be I

who have bring you here! it will be all right now. new house be to

be build, they say, at the other end of the Ham, and the practice will

be worth a great deal. I shall sleep well to-night."



he smile as he shake hand with she; partly in affection, partly at

her unusual vehemence. in pass the drawing-room, Ellen Adair

happen to be come out of it, but he go on. she suppose he have

not observe she, and speak.



"ah! how do you do, Miss Adair?" he say, turn back, and offer

his hand. "forgive my haste; I be busy to-day."



and before she have time to make any reply, he be go; leave an

impression on her mind, she could not well have tell why or wherefore,

that he be ill at ease; that he have hasten away, not from pressure

of work, but because he do not care to talk to she.



if that feeling be possess Dr. Rane, and have reference to the

world in general, and not to the young lady in particular, it might

not have be agreeable to he to encounter an acquaintance as he

turn out of his mother's house. Mr. Alexander be swiftly pass on

his way towards home from the low part of the Ham, and stop.



"I wish I have never say a syllable about go away until I be off,"

cry he in his off-hand manner--a pleasanter and more sociable manner

than Dr. Rane's. "the news have be noise abroad, and the whole place

be upon I; ask this, that, and the other. one man come and want

to know if I'll sell my furniture; another think he'd like the house

as it stand. my patient be up in arms;--say I'm do it to kill

they. I shall have some of they in a fever before the day's over."



"perhaps you willn't go, after all," observe Dr. Rane.



"not go! how can I help go? I'm elect to the post. why, it's what

I've be look out for ever so long--almost ever since I come here.

no, no, Rane: a short time, and Dallory Ham will have see the last of

I."



he hasten across the road to his house, like a man who have the

world's work on his busy shoulder. Dr. Rane's thought, as he glance

after he, revert to the mental argument he have hold in his chamber,

and he unconsciously resume it, put himself in the place of the

unknown, unhappy writer, as before.



"it's almost keener than the death itself--if the motive be to injure

Alexander in his profession, or drive he from the place--to know that

he, or she--Mrs. North--might have spare her pain! heaven! what

remorse it must be!--to commit a crime, and then find there be no

necessity for do it!"



Dr. Rane pass his white handkerchief over his brow--the day be very

warm--and turn into his house. Phillis once more place the dinner

on the table, and he sit down to it.



but not a mouthful could he swallow; his throat feel like so much

dry-chip, and the food would not go down. Phillis, who be come in

for something or other, see he leave his plate and rise from table.



"be the fowl not tender, sir?"



"Tender?" he respond, as though the sense of the question have not

reach he, and pause. "oh, it's tender enough: but I must go off to

a patient. get your own dinner, Phillis."



"surely you'll come back to your, sir?"



"I've have as much as I want. take the thing away."



"I wonder what's come to he?" muse the woman as his quick step

recede from the house, and she be leave with the reject dish. a

consciousness come dimly penetrate to her hazy brain that there be

some change upon he. what it be, or where it lay, she do not

define. it be unusual for his strong firm finger to drop a glass; it

be still more unusual for he to explain cause and effect. "the jar

slip from my finger." "I've have as much as I want. I must go off

to a patient." it be quite out of the order of routine for Dr. Rane

to be explanatory to his servant on any subject whatever: and perhaps

it be his having be so in these two instance that impress

Phillis.



"how quick he must have eat his dinner!"



Phillis nearly drop the dish. the word be speak close behind

she, and she have believe herself alone in the house. turn, she see

Jelly, stand half in, half out of the window.



"well, I'm sure!" cry Phillis, in wrath. "you needn't come startle

a body in that way, Mrs. Jelly. how do you know but the doctor might

be at table?"



"I've just see he go down the lane," return Jelly, who have plenty

of time for gossip with her neighbour, and have come stroll over

the fence now with no other object. "have he have his dinner? it's but

the other minute he be in at our house."



"he have have as much as he mean to have," answer Phillis, her anger

evaporate, for she like a gossip also. "I'm sure it's not worth the

trouble of serve meal, if they be to be leave in this fashion. it

be the same thing at breakfast."



Jelly recollect the scene at breakfast; the startled pallor on Dr.

Rane's face, when tell that Edmund North be dead: she suppose that

have spoil his appetite. her inquisitive eye turn unceremoniously

to the fowl, and she see that the mere slice off the wing be alone

eat.



"perhaps he be not well to-day," say Jelly.



"I don't know about his be well; he's odder than I ever see he,"

answer Phillis. "I shouldn't wonder but he have have his stomach

turn over they two half-drown man."



she carry the dinner-thing across to the kitchen. Jelly, who

assist at the ceremony, as far as watch and talk go, be

stand in the passage, when her quick eye catch sight of two small

piece of glass. she stoop to pick they up.



"look, Phillis! you have be break something. it's uncommonly

careless to leave the bit about."



"be it!" retort Phillis. "your eye be in everything. I think I

take 'em all up," she add, look on the ground.



"what do you break?"



"nothing. it be the doctor. he drop one of they dusty glass jar

down the stair. it do give I a start. you should have hear the

smash."



"what make he drop it?" ask Jelly.



"goodness know," return the old woman. "he's not a bit like

himself to-day; it's just as if something have come to he."



she begin her dinner as she speak, stand, her usual mode of take

it. Jelly, follow her free-and-easy habit, stand against the

door-post, apparently interested in the progress of the meal. they

present a contrast, these two woman, the one a thin, upright

giantess, the other a dwarf stoop forward. Jelly, a lady's-maid,

hold herself of course altogether above Phillis, an ignorant (as Jelly

would have describe she) servant-of-all-work, though condescend to

drop in for the sake of gossip.



"do you happen to hear how the doctor find Ketler?"



"as if I should be likely to hear!" be Phillis's retort. "he'd not

tell I, and I couldn't ask. my master's not one you can put question

to, Jelly."



a silence ensue. the gossip apparently flag to-day. Phillis have it

chiefly to herself, for Jelly vouchsafe only a brief remark now and

again. she be engage in the mental process of wonder what _have_

come to Dr. Rane.









CHAPTER V.

RETROSPECT.





there must be a little retrospect to make thing intelligible to the

reader; and it may as well be give at once.



Mr. North, now of Dallory Sail, have get on entirely by his own

industry. of obscure, though in a certain way respectable, parentage,

he have be place as apprentice to a firm in Whitborough. it be a

firm in extensive work, not confine itself to one branch. they take

contract for public building, small and large: do mechanical

engineering; have plan one of the early railway. John North--plain

Jack North he be know as, then--remain with the firm when he be

out of his time, and get on in it. steady and plod, he rise from

one step to another; and at length, in conjunction with one who have

be in the same firm, he set up for himself. this other be Thomas

Gass. Gass have not rise from the rank as North have: his connection

be good, and he have receive a superior education; but his friend

be poor. North and Gass, as the new firm call itself, begin

business near to Dallory; quietly at first--as all people, who really

expect to get on, generally do begin. they rise rapidly. the narrow

premise expand; the small contract grow into large one. People

say luck be with they--and in truth it seem so. the Dallory work

became note in the county, employ quite a colony of people: the

master be respect and seek after. both live at Whitborough;

Mr. North with his wife and family; Mr. Gass a bachelor.



Thomas Gass have one brother; a clergyman. their only sister, Fanny, a

very pretty girl, have her home with he in his rectory, but she come

often to Whitborough on a visit to Thomas. suddenly it be announce

to the world that she have become engaged to marry a Captain Rane,

entirely against the wish of her two brother. she be under twenty.

Captain Rane, a poor naval man on half-pay, be almost old enough to

be her grandfather. their objection lie not so much in this, as in

himself. for some reason or other, neither of they like he. the

Reverend William Gass forbid his sister to think of he; Mr. Thomas

Gass, a fiery man, swear he would never afterwards look upon she as a

sister, if she persist in thus throw herself away.



Miss Gass do persist. she possess the obstinate spirit of her

brother Thomas, though without his fire. she choose to take her own

way, and marry Captain Rane. they sail at once for Madras; Captain

Rane having obtain some post there, connect with the Government

ship.



whether Miss Gass repent her marriage, her brother have no mean of

learning: for she, retain her anger, never write to they during her

husband's lifetime. it be a very short one. barely a twelvemonth have

elapse after the knot be tie, when there come a pitiful letter from

she. Captain Rane have die, just as her little son Oliver (name after

a friend, she say) be bear. Thomas Gass, to whom the letter have be

specially write, gather that she be leave badly off; though she

do not absolutely say so. he go into one of his angry mood, and

toss the epistle across the desk to his partner. "you must do

something for she, Gass," say John North when he have read it. "I

never will," hotly affirm Mr. Gass. "Fanny know what I promise if

she marry Rane--that I would never help she during my lifetime or

after it. she know another thing--that I be not one to go from my

word. William may help she if he like; he have not much to give away,

but he can have her home to live with he." "help the child, then,"

suggest Mr. North, know further remonstrance to be useless. "no,"

return obstinate Thomas Gass; "I'll stick to the spirit of my

promise as well as the letter." and Mr. North bend his head again--he

be go over some estimate--feel that the affair be none of

his. "I don't mind put the boy in the tontine, North," presently

speak the junior partner. "the tontine!" echo John North in

surprise, "what tontine?" "what tontine?" return the hard

man--though in truth he be not hard in general, "why, the one that

you and other be get up; the one you have just put your baby,

Bessy, into; I know of no other tontine." "but that will not benefit

the boy," urge Mr. North: "certainly not now; and the chance be ten

to one against its ever benefit he in the future." "never mind;

I'll put he into it," say Mr. Gass, whose obstinacy always come out

well under opposition. "you want a tenth child to close the list, and

I'll put he into it." so into the tontine Oliver Rane, unconscious

infant, be put.



but Mrs. Rane do not far trouble either of her brother; or, as

thing turn out, require assistance from they. she remain in

India; and after a year marry a Government chaplain there, the

Reverend George Cumberland, who possess some private property.

little, if any, communication take place afterwards between she and

her brother; she cherish resentment for old grievance, and would

not write to they. and so the sister and the brother seem to fade

away from each other from henceforth. we all know how relative,

part by time and distance, become estranged, disappear almost

from memory.



Whilst the firm, North and Gass, be rise higher and high in

wealth and importance, the wife of its senior partner die. she leave

three child, Edmund, Richard, and Bessy. subsequently, during a

visit to London, chance draw Mr. North into a meeting with a handsome

young woman, the widow of Major Bohun. she have not long return from

India, where she have bury her husband. a designing, attractive

syren, who begin forthwith to exercise her dangerous fascination on

plain, unsuspicious Mr. North. she have only a poor pittance; what

money there be belong to her only child, Arthur; a little lad: send

out of sight already to a preparatory school. Report have magnify Mr.

North's wealth into something fabulous; and Mrs. Bohun do not cease

her scheme until she have catch he in her toil and he have make she

Mrs. North.



man do thing sometimes in a hurry, only to repent of they at leisure.

that Mr. North have be in a hurry in this case be indisputable--it

be just as though Mrs. Bohun have throw a spell over he; whether he

repent when he wake up and find himself with a wife, a stepmother

for his child at home, be not so certain. he be a sufficiently

wise man in those day to conceal what he do not want know.



whom he have marry, beyond the fact that she be the widow of Major

Bohun, he do not know from Adam. for all she disclose about her own

family, in regard to whom she maintain an absolute reticence, she

might have drop from the moon, or "grow" like Topsy; but, from

the air and grace she assume, Mr. North might have conclude they

be duke and duchesse at least. her late husband's family be

irreproachable, both in character and position. the head of it be Sir

Nash Bohun, representative of an ancient baronetcy, and eld brother

of the late major. before the wedding tour be over, poor Mr. North

find that his wife be a cold, imperious, extravagant woman, not to

be question by any mean if she so choose. when her fascination be

in full play (while she be only Mrs. Bohun) Mr. North have be ready

to think she an angel. where have all the amiability fly to? People

do change after marriage somehow. at least, there have be instance

know of it.



a little circumstance occur one day that--to put it mildly--have

surprise Mr. North. he have be give to understand by his wife that

Major Bohun die suddenly of sunstroke; she have certainly tell he

so. in talk at a dinner-party at Sir Nash Bohun's with some

innocently observe Mr. North. "die of _what?_" cry the other,

put down his claret-glass, which he be convey to his

sunstroke," come the impulsive answer; "who tell you he die of

that?" "she do--my wife," be Mr. North's answer. "oh!" say his

friend; and take up his claret again. "why, what do he die of, if it

be not sunstroke?" ask Mr. North, with curiosity. "Well,--I--I

don't know; I'd rather say no more about it," be the conclusive

reply: "of course Mrs. North must know well than I." and nothing

more would he say on the subject.



they be stay at this time at Sir Nash Bohun's. in pass through

London after the Continental wedding trip on their way to Whitborough,

Sir Nash have invite they to make his house their resting-place. not

until the day follow his conversation at the dinner-table have Mr.

North an opportunity of question his wife; but, that some false

representation, intentionally or otherwise, have be make to he on

the subject of her late husband's death, he feel certain. they be

alone in her dressing-room. Mrs. North, who have a great deal of

beautiful black hair, be stand before the glass, do something

to a portion of it, when her husband suddenly accost she. he call

she by her christian name in those first married day. it be a very

fine one.



"Amanda, you tell I, I think, that Major Bohun die of sunstroke."



"well?" she return carelessly, occupy with her hair.



"but he do not die of sunstroke. he die of--of something else."



Mr. North have watch woman's face turn to pallor, but never in his

whole life have he see so livid a look of terror as now overspread his

wife's. her hair drop from her nerveless hand.



"why, what be the matter?" he exclaim.



she murmur something about a spasm of the heart, to which she be

subject: an excuse, as he see. another moment, and she have recover

her composure, and be busy with her hair again.



"you be ask I something, be you not, Mr. North?"



"about Major Bohun: what be it he die of---if it be not

sunstroke?"



"but it be of sunstroke," she say, in a sharp, ring accent, that

would have require only a little more to be a scream. "what else

should he die of suddenly in India's burn climate? he go out in

the blaze midday sun, and be bring home dead!"



and nothing more, then or afterwards, do Mr. North learn. her manner

render it impossible to press the subject. he might have apply to

Sir Nash for information, but an instinct prevent his do so.

after all, it do not matter to he what Major Bohun have die of, Mr.

North say to himself and determine to forget the incident. but that

some mystery must have attend Major Bohun's death, some painful

circumstance which could blanch his wife's face with sickly terror,

remain on Mr. North's mind as a fact not to be dispute.



Mrs. North effect change. almost the very day she be take home to

Whitborough, she let it be know that she should rule with an

imperious will. her husband become a very reed in her hand; yield

passively to her sway, as if all the spirit he have ever own have go

out of he. Mrs. North profess to hate the very name of trade; that

any one with whom she be so nearly connected should be in business,

bring she a sense of degradation and a great deal of talk about it.

the quiet, modest, comfortable home at Whitborough be at once give

up for the more pretentious Manor Hall at Dallory Ham, which happen

to be in the market. and they set up there in a style that might have

more properly belong to the lord-lieutenant of the county. perhaps

it be her assumption of grandeur indoor and out, combine with the

imperious manner, the like of which have never before be see in the

simple neighbourhood, that cause people to call her "Madam." or, it

might have be to distinguish she from the first Mrs. North.



in proportion as Mrs. North make herself hate and fear by her

husband, his child, and the household, so do she become popular

with society. it sometimes happen that the more fascination a woman

display to the world, the more unbearable be she in her own house. it

be the case here. Madam put on all her attraction when out-of-door;

she visit and dress and dine; and give fête again at Dallory

Hall utterly regardless of expense. little wonder that she sway the

neighbourhood.



not the immediate neighbourhood. with the exception of the Dallory

family (and they do not live there always), there be not a single

person she would have visit. a few gentle-people  reside at Dallory

Ham; Mrs. North do not condescend to know any of they. People live

at a great distance she make friend with, but not those around she;

and with as many of the county family as would make friend with

she. the pleasant time be those when she would betake herself

off on long visit, to London or elsewhere: they grow to be look

forward to.



but the most decide raid make by Mrs. North be on her husband's

business connection. have Thomas Gass be a chimney-sweeper, she

could not have treat he with more intense contempt. Thomas Gass have

his share of sense, and pity his partner far more than he would have

do have that gentleman go in for hang instead of second

marriage. Mr. Gass be a very wealthy man now; and have build himself a

handsome and comfortable residence in Dallory.



but, as the year go on, he be doom to furnish food himself to

all the gossip within mile. Dallory rise from its couch one fine

morning, to hear that Thomas Gass, the confirm old bachelor, have

marry his housekeeper. not one of your "lady-housekeeper," but a

useful, good, hard-work damsel, who have pass the first bloom of

youth, and have not much beauty to recommend she. it be a nine day'

wonder. of course, however much the neighbour might solace their

feeling by ridicule he and abuse she, they could not undo the

by degree they wisely do so. the new Mrs. Gass glide easily into

her honour. she make an excellent wife to her ail husband--for

Thomas Gass's health have begin to fail before his marriage--she put on

no air of be superior to what she be; she turn out to be a

thoroughly capable woman of business, give much judicious advice to

those about she: she be very good to the sick and suffering, care

for the poor, ready to give a help hand wherever and whenever it

might be need. in spite of her fine dress, which sit ludicrously

upon she, and of her manner of talking, which she do not attempt to

improve; above all, in spite of their own prejudice, Dallory grow to

like and respect Mrs. Gass, and its small gentle-people  admit she

to their house on an equality.



and so time and year go on, Mr. North withdraw himself more and

more from personal attendance on the business, which seem to have

grow utterly distasteful to he. his son have become young man.

Edmund be a civil engineer: by profession at least, not much by

practice. never in strong health, give to expensive and idle habit,

Edmund North be generally either in trouble abroad, or lead a lazy

life at home, his time be much divide between go into needless

passion and write poetry. Richard be at the work, the mainspring

of the business. Mr. Gass have become a confirmed invalid, and could

not personally attend to it; Mr. North do not do so. there be only

Richard--Dick, as they all call he; but he be a host in himself.

of far high power than Mr. North have ever possess, cultivate in

mind, he be a thorough man of business, and at the same time a

finish gentleman. energetic, persevering, firm in control, yet

courteous and considerate to the very low, Richard North be

love and respect. he walk through life do his duty by his

fellow-man: strive to do it to God. he have be try at home in

many way since his father's second marriage, and bear all with

patient endurance: how much he be try out of home, he alone know.



for a long time past there have be trouble in the firm, ill-feeling

between the two old partner; chiefly because Mr. North put no limit

to the sum he draw out for his private account. poor Mr. North at

length confess that he could not help it: the money be want by

his wife: though how on earth she contrive to get rid of so much,

even with all her extravagance, he could not conceive. Mr. Gass

insist on a separation: John North must withdraw from the firm;

Richard might take his place. poor Mr. North yield meekly. "don't

let it get abroad," he only stipulate, speak as if he be half

heartbroken, which be nothing new; "I should not like the world to

know that I be supersede." they respect his wish, and the change

be make privately: very few be aware that the senior partnership

North cease to have any control in the business; in fact, to have any

not the faint idea of it. Richard North sign the cheque as he

have do before, "north and Gass:" and perhaps the bank at Whitborough

alone know that he sign they now as principal.



Richard be the scape-goat now. Mr. North's need of money, or rather

his wife's, do not cease: the sum arrange to be pay to he as a

retire pension--a very liberal sum, and Mr. Gass grumble at

it--seem to be as nothing; it melt in madam's hand like so much

water. Richard be constantly appeal to by his father; and respond

generously, though it cripple he.



the next change come in the shape of Mr. Gass's death. the bulk of his

property be leave to his wife; a small portion, comparatively

speak, to charity and servant; two thousand pound to Richard

which by the term of the deed of partnership he have power to do. so

that his share of the capital be not draw out, and the firm

remain, actually as well as virtually, North and Gass. People

Madam have never condescend to notice Thomas Gass's wife in the least

Mrs. Gass show her good sense in regard to the partnership, as she

do in most thing. she decline to interfere actively in the

keep her cognizant of what be go on; he consult her opinion on

conceal from she that large sum (exclusively Richard's) be ever

find their way to the Hall, and for this she take he to task.

"stop it, Mr. Richard," she say--always as respectful to he as she

have be in her housekeeping day: "stop it, sir. their want be like

a cullender, the more water you pour into it the more you may. it's

do they no good. an end must come to it some time, or you'll be in

the workhouse. the long it go on, the more difficult it will be to

put an end to, and the hard it will be for they." but Richard,

sorely try between prudence and filial duty, could not bring himself

to stop it so easily; and the thing go on.



we must now go back to Mrs. Cumberland. it be somewhat singular that,

the very week Thomas Gass die, she should make her unexpected

appearance at Dallory. but so it be. again a widow, she have come home

to settle near her brother Thomas. she arrive just in time to see he

put into his coffin. the other brother, William, have be dead for

year. Mrs. Gass, who know all about the estrangement, receive she

with marked kindness, and heartily offer she a home for the future.



yet that be decline. Mrs. Cumberland prefer to have a home of her

own, possess ample mean to establish one in a moderate way. she

give a sketch of her past life to Mrs. Gass. after her marriage with

the Reverend George Cumberland, they have remain for some time at his

chaplaincy in the Madras presidency; but his health begin to fail, and

he exchange to Australia. subsequently to that, year later, he

obtain a duty in Madeira. upon his death, which occur recently,

she come to England. her only son, Oliver Rane, have be send home at

the age of seven, and be place with a tutor in London. when the time

come for he to choose a profession he decide on the medical, and

qualify himself for it, study in London, Paris, and Vienna. he

pass all the examination with great credit, include that of the

College of Physicians. he next pay a visit to Madeira, remain

three month with his mother and stepfather, and then come home and

establish himself in London, with money furnish by his mother. but

practice do not always come quickly to young beginner, and Oliver

Rane find his means lessen. he have a horror of debt, and wisely

decide to keep out of it: take a situation as assistant, and give

up the expensive house he have enter on. this have just be effect

when Mrs. Cumberland return. for the present she let her son remain

as he be: Oliver have all a young man's pride and ambition, and she

think the discipline might do he good.



Mrs. Cumberland take on lease one of the two handsome gothic villa on

the Ham, and establish herself in it; with Jelly for a waiting-maid,

and two other servant. this necessitate spend the whole of her

income, which be a very fair one. a portion of it would die with she,

the rest be willed to her son Oliver.



in the old day when she be Fanny Gass, and Mr. North, plain John

North--Jack with his friend--they be intimate as eld brother and

young sister. if Mrs. Cumberland expect this agreeable state of

affair to be resume, she be destine to find herself mistaken.

Madam set her scornful face utterly against Mrs. Cumberland: just as

she have against other. it do not matter. Mrs. Cumberland simply

pity the underbred woman: her health be very delicate, and she do

not intend to visit any one. the gentle-people  of the neighbourhood

call upon she; she return the call, and there the acquaintance

end. when invitation first come in, she write a refusal, explain

clearly and courteously why she be oblige to do so--that her health

do not allow she to visit. if she and Mr. North meet each other, as by

chance happen, they would linger in conversation, and be happy in

the reminiscence of past day.



Mrs. Cumberland have thus live on in retirement for some time, when

the medical man who have the practice of Dallory Ham, and some of that

of Dallory, die suddenly. she see what an excellent opportunity it

would be for her son to establish himself, if he would but take up

general practice, and she send a summon for he. when Oliver arrive

in answer to it, he enter into the prospect warmly; leave his

mother to make arrangement, and return to London, to superintend

his removal. Mrs. Cumberland go to Mr. North, and obtain his

promise to do what he could to far Oliver's interest. it be

equivalent to an assurance of success--for Dallory Hall sway its

neighbour--and Mrs. Cumberland do not hesitate to secure the gothic

villa adjoin her own, which happen to be vacant, believe that

the future practice would justify it. in a week's time Oliver Rane

come down and take possession.



but fate be against he. Dr. Rane say treachery. a young fellow whom

he know in London have tell a medical friend--a Mr. Alexander--of this

excellent practice that have fall in at Dallory, and that Rane be

hope to secure it for himself. what be Dr. Rane's mortification

when, upon arrive at the week's end at Dallory Ham to take

possession, he find another there before he. Mr. Alexander have

arrive the previous day, be already establish in an opposite
