he with treachery--they have not previously be personally

acquaint. Mr. Alexander receive the charge with surprise; he

declare that the field be as open to he as to Dr. Rane--that if he

have not think so, nothing would have induce he to enter it. he

speak his true sentiment, for he be a straightforward man. an agent

in Whitborough have also write up to tell he of this opening; he

come to look at it, and decide to try it. the right to monopolize it,

be no more Dr. Rane's, he urge, than it be his. Dr. Rane take a

different view, and say so: but contention would not help the matter

now, and he could only yield to circumstance. so each hold to his

right in apparent amicability, and Dallory have two doctor instead of

one; secret rival from henceforth.



not for a moment do Oliver Rane think Mr. Alexander could long hold

out against he, as he have secure, through his mother, the favour of

Dallory Hall. alas, a very short time show he that this be a

mistake; Dallory Hall turn round upon he, and be do what it

could to forward his rival. Mrs. Cumberland go to Mr. North, seek

an explanation. he could only avow the truth--his wife, who be both

master and mistress, have set her face against Oliver, and be

recommend Alexander. "John, you promise I," urge Mrs.

Cumberland, "I know I do, and I'd keep to it if I could," be Mr.

North's mournful answer; "but no one can hold out against she." "why

should she have take this dislike to Oliver?" rejoin Mrs.

Cumberland. "Heaven know; a caprice, I suppose. she set herself

against people without reason: she have never take to either Richard

or Bessy; and only a little to Edmund. if I can do anything for Oliver

under the rose, I'll do it. I have every desire to help he, Fanny, in

remembrance of our friendship of the old day."



Mrs. Cumberland carry home news of her non-success to Oliver. as to

madam, she simply ignore he, bestow her patronage upon his rival.

how bitterly the slight touch his heart, none but himself could

tell. Mrs. Cumberland resent it; but ah, not as he do. a sense of

wrong be ever weigh upon his spirit, and he think Fate be

against he. one puzzle remain on his mind unsolved--what he could

have do to offend Mrs. North.



Mr. Alexander obtain a fair practice: Dr. Rane barely sufficient to

keep himself. his want and those of the old servant Phillis be few.

perhaps the entire fault do not lie with madam. Alexander have a more

open manner and address than Dr. Rane, and they go a long way with

people; he be also an old man, and a married man, and be suppose

to have have more experience. a sense of injury rankle ever in Oliver

Rane's heart; of injury inflict by Alexander. meanwhile he become

engage to Bessy Rane. during an absence from home of madam's, the

doctor grow intimate at the Hall, and an attachment spring up between

he and Bessy. when madam return, his visit have to cease, but he

see Bessy at Mrs. Gass's and elsewhere.



I think that be all the retrospect that need be go into. it bring

we down to the present time, the period of the anonymous letter and

Edmund North's death. exactly two year ago this same month, May, the

rival doctor have appear in Dallory Ham; and now one of they be

about to leave it.



one incident must be tell, bear on something that have be relate,

and then the chapter shall close.



the summer of the past year have be a very hot one. a labour man,

work on Mr. North's ground, suddenly fall; and die on the spot.

Mr. Alexander, summon hastily, think it must have be sunstroke.

"that be what my father die of," remark Captain Bohun, who stand

with the rest. Mr. North turn to he: "do you say your father die

of sunstroke, Arthur?" "yes, sir, that be what he die of. do you

not know it?" be the ready reply. "you be sure of that?" continue

Mr. North. "quite sure, sir," repeat Arthur, turn his dreamy blue

eye full upon his stepfather, in all their proud truthfulness.



Mr. North know that he speak in the sincerity of belief. Arthur Bohun

possess in an eminent degree the pride of his father's race. that

innate, self-conscious sense of superiority that be a sort of

safeguard to those who possess it: the _noblesse oblige_ feel that

keep they from wrong-doing. it be true, Arthur Bohun hold an exalted

view of his birth and family: in so far as that his pride in it

equal that of any man live or dead. he be truthful, generous,

honourable; the very opposite in all respect to his mother. her pride

be an assumed pride; a despicable, false, contemptible pride,

offensive to those with whom she come into contact. Arthur's be one

that you admire in spite of yourself. of a tarnish to his honour, he

could almost have die; to bring disgrace on his own name or on his

family, would have cause he to bury his head for ever. sensitively

regardful of other people's feeling, courteous in manner to all, he

yet unmistakably hold his own in the world. his father have be just

the same; and in his day be call "Proud Bohun."



to have assert that Major Bohun die of sunstroke, have any doubt of

the fact lain on his mind, would have be simply impossible to Arthur

Bohun. therefore, Mr. North see that, whatever the mystery might be,

regard the real cause of Major Bohun's death, Arthur be not

cognizant of it.









CHAPTER VI.

watch the funeral.





in Mrs. Gass's comfortable dining-room, securely ensconce behind the

closed blind, draw to-day, sit that lady and a visitor. it be the

day of the funeral of Edmund North; and Mrs. Gass have put on mourning

out of respect to the family: a black silk gown and white net cap. it

need not be say that the change improve her appearance greatly: she

look, as she herself would have phrase it, genteel to-day. this be

her favourite sitting-room; she rarely use any other: for one thing

it give she the opportunity of see the movement of her neighbour.

the drawing-room face the garden at the back: a large and beautiful

apartment, open to the smooth green lawn.



the visitor be Mrs. Cumberland. for once in her life Mrs. Cumberland

emerge from her shell of indifference and condescend to show a

for their meeting be rare. Mrs. Cumberland be also in black

talk in subdue voice of bygone time: not that they have know

servant in Mrs. Cumberland's family. they have little in common,

though, and the topic of conversation exhaust themselves. Mrs.

Cumberland be of a silent nature, not at all give to gossip in

general. she begin to think the wait long. for the convenience of

two mourner, who be come from a distance, the funeral have be

put off until four o'clock.



"holiday don't improve the work class--unless they've the sense to

use 'em as they ought," observe Mrs. Gass. "just look at they three,

ma'am. they've be at the tap--and more shame to 'em! they'd well

let Mr. Richard catch his eye upon 'em. put themselves into that

state, when he be follow his brother to the grave."



she allude to some man belong to the Dallory Works, close to-day.

they have take more than be become, and be lounge against the

opposite shutter, quarrel together. Mrs. Gass could bear it no

long; in defiance of appearance she draw up the blind and dash

open the window.



"be you three man not ashamed of yourself? I think it be you,

Dawson! when there's any ill-doing go on, you're safe to be in it.

as to you, Thomas, you'll not like to show your face tomorrow. don't

come to I again, Smith, to beg grace for you of Mr. Richard North."



the man slunk away and disappear down an entry. Mrs. Gass, in one

sense of the word, be their mistress; at any rate, their master's

partner. she close the window and draw down the blind.



"be the man pay for to-day, or do they lose it?" ask Mrs.

Cumberland.



"they're pay, ma'am, of course. it would be very unjust to dock they

when the holiday's none of their making. neither Mr. Richard nor I

would like to be unjust."



"and he--Richard--seem to act entirely for his father."



Mrs. Gass cough. "Mr. North be take up with his garden, and that; he

don't care to bother his head about business. it's well in young

hand."



another pause. Mrs. Cumberland feel weary.



"be this funeral ever come?" she exclaim. "there seem to be some

delay."



"it be a late hour to fix it for, ma'am. Old Sir what's-his-name

write word he couldn't be here before the afternoon; so they put it

off to four o'clock for his convenience."



Mrs. Cumberland look up inquiringly. she do not understand.



"I mean young Bohun's relative, ma'am. Madam's brother-in-law by her

first husband."



"Sir Nash Bohun! be he come?"



"Sir Nash; that's the name," remark Mrs. Gass. "I know when Mr.

Richard say it, it put I in mind of grind the tooth."



"what could have induce they to ask he?" wonder Mrs. Cumberland.

"he be no relative."



"it sound grand to have he, ma'am--and that's all _she_ think of,"

return Mrs. Gass, with slighting allusion to madam. "or maybe, as it

be an uncommon death, they want to make it an uncommon funeral. _I_

look upon it as no well than a murder."



"it be very strange about that piece of paper," observe Mrs.

Cumberland.



she lower her voice as she speak, as if the subject would not bear

the broad light of day. any surprise, great than appear in Mrs.

Gass's face at hear it could not well be imagine.



"Ma'am! do he tell you of _that?_"



"do who tell I?"



"your son."



they look questioningly at each other; both unconscious that they

be allude to two totally different circumstance. cross-purpose

be sometimes productive of more evil than straightforward one.



it appear that a night or two after Edmund North's death, Captain

Bohun find in his own desk a sheet of fold notepaper in an

envelope. it contain a few word in Edmund's handwriting, not

apparently address to any one in particular, but to the world in

general. no date be add, but the ink look fresh, as if it have

recently be write.





"when the end come, make no fuss with I, but bury I quietly out of

sight.--E. N."





Captain Bohun, not have the faint idea as to who put it in his

desk, or how it come there, carry it to Richard North. Richard

show it to his father. thence it spread to the house, and to one or

two other. opinion be divide. Mr. North think his ill-fate son

have intend to allude to his own death: must have feel some

foreshadowing of it on his spirit. on the contrary, Arthur Bohun and

thought, quite naturally, that she speak of the paper find on her

"of course it _might_ have be nothing more than some idea he have

dot down, poor fellow, connect with his nonsensical poetry,"

I don't. it may be that I be inclined to look always on the gloomy

"'twas odd sort of poetry," cry Mrs. Gass, after a pause and a

"the only curious part about it to my mind be, that it should have

North should have choose to put it there, be a mystery."



"what have Captain Bohun's desk to do with it?" inquire Mrs. Gass,

begin to feel a little at sea.



"the paper be find in Captain Bohun's desk. though why Edmund North

should have place it there, remain a mystery."



"Ma'am, whoever tell you that, must have be just try to deceive

you. it be find on this carpet."



"find on this carpet!"



"on this very blessed carpet, ma'am. right back under the claw of that

centre dining-table."



again they gaze at each other. Mrs. Cumberland think her friend

must be dream.



"but you be quite mistaken, Mrs. Gass. the paper--note, or whatever

it be--could not have be on this carpet at all: nor in your house,

in fact. Captain Bohun discover it in his desk three day ago, and

he have not the slight idea as to how it come there. Mr. North take

take; not an hour after the fit."



Mrs. Cumberland make no reply. she be drift into the conclusion

that all the circumstance have not be relate to she.

hear about the paper from your son, I wonder he do not tell you

this."



"what paper be this?" inquire Mrs. Cumberland, a dim idea arise in

her mind that they could not be talk of the same thing.



"it be the copy of that anonymous letter."



"the copy of the anonymous letter!"



"leastway, its skeleton."



rapidly enough come elucidation now. without in the least intend to

by the miserable, but very natural misapprehension that Mrs.

exception be the hint suspicion that madam might have be the

"what else could I think?" debate Mrs. Gass. "Dr. Rane have let fall

some paper from his pocketbook five minute before, and I pick

this up as soon as he have go. I'm sure I never so much as give a

thought to Molly Green--though she have come straight from the Hall.

Dr. Rane say it might have drop from her petticoat: but it be a

puzzle to I how; and it's a puzzle still."



a keen, inquire glance shot from the speaker's eye with the last

word. it be momentary and not intentional; nevertheless, something

in it cause Mrs. Cumberland's heart to quail. a greyer hue spread

over her grey face; a cold shade of recollection deaden her heart.

Captain Bohun have tell she of Mr. Alexander's theory: that the letter

be write to damage himself.



"I be sorry I speak of this, ma'am," strike in Mrs. Gass. "More

particular that it should have be you: you'll naturally tell Dr.

Rane, and he _will_ say I know how to keep secret--just about as the

jackdaw keep theirs. it be your telling of the other paper that

mislead I."



"I be quite safe," answer Mrs. Cumberland, with a sickly smile. "the

matter's nothing to I, that I should speak of it again."



"of course not, ma'am. after all Halloa! here it come!"



this sudden break be cause by the roll of a muffle drum, first

advent of the advance funeral procession. Edmund North have belong

to a local military corps, and be to be attend to the grave with

honour. Mrs. Gass draw up the white blind an inch above the Venetian,

which enable they to look out unseen. the road suddenly become line

with spectator; man, woman and child collect one hardly know

from whence.



the band come first--their instrument in rest; then the muffle drum,

on which its bearer strike a note now and again. the hearse and three

"Mr. North in the first coach with his white hankecher hold to his

madam's doing, I'll lay. what a little whipper-snapper of a fellow

Mrs. Gass's remark cease with the passing of the coach. in her

"an old gent, upright as a dart, with snow-white hair and they

there be no reply. Mrs. Gass turn to see the reason. her visitor

"my patience! don't you feel well, ma'am?"



"I be a little tired," reply Mrs. Cumberland, smile languidly as

"now, don't you stir, ma'am; I'll tell it off to you," come the

perhaps, to be mix with all these don."



she look after the swiftly pass pageantry with linger

the procession wind along: through the long straggling village

parish, inconveniently distant for some of the inhabitant. dallory

her side.



amidst the spectator, number of whom have collect in the burial

blind, be one thing; but to stand openly stare in the churchyard,

wife of the sick man who be be attend by Dr. Rane. Jelly have

curiosity enough for ten ordinary woman--which be say a great

deal--and would not have miss the sight for the world.



it be soon over: our burial service be not a long one: and the

the former read the inscription.



"Edmund, son of John North and of Mary, his first wife. die May 3rd,

18--, age 33."



"I should not have put 'die,' but 'murder,' if it be I have the

writing of it," speak Mrs. Ketler.



"and so should I, Susan," significantly reply Jelly. "here! let's

get out of this throng."



Jelly, in her loftiness of stature and opinion, be above the throng

literally and figuratively; but it be dense and troublesome. neither

house in Dallory. he be one of the good of the North workman, earn

good wage; and the family live in comfort.



Ketler be in the parlour, sit up for the first time. under Dr.

say their father; "they might get play, and that would be

unseemly." he be a short, dark, honest-look man; a good husband

and father. Jelly sit talk for a short time, and then rise to

leave.



but she be not allow to do so. to let her depart at that hour

of. Jelly, too easily persuade where gossip be concern, take off

Cups of beer induce man to a long sitting; cup of tea woman. Jelly

"who tell you that, Ketler?" sharply demand Jelly, fix her

indignant eye on the man.



"I cann't rightly say who tell I," reply Ketler; "it's the talk of

the place. wilk deny it out and out; but when he's in his evening

no recollection of. Doctor Rane laugh at I, though, for say so:

a lawyer know well than to let private matter get out to his

clerk, say the doctor. but he don't know that Tim Wilks as some of

we do."



"well, I would not say too much about it's be Tim Wilks, if I be

you, Ketler," cry Jelly, in suppress wrath, brush the crumb

from her black gown. "you might find yourself in hot water."



and then Ketler suddenly remember that Wilks be her particular

friend, so he turn the subject.



Jelly tear herself away at last, very unwillingly: gossip and

tea-drinking form her idea of an earthly paradise. night be set

in; a light, beautiful night, the moon sailing majestically in the

sky.



just past the gate of Dallory Hall, in a bend of the road where

the overhang tree on either side give it a lonely appearance at

within view, stand a bench at the side of the path. it be a welcome

especially if it should happen to be a pair who have no business to

exchange they.



steal softly along, go she, until she come to the turning, and

CHAPTER VIII.

MADAM'S LISTENING CLOSET.





many year before, when the Reverend George Cumberland hold his

way. Madam intend, unseen, to make a third at the interview between

frighten she. there might be treason in the air.



Mrs. Cumberland be already in Mr. North's parlour. stroll out

amongst his flower, he have encounter she in the garden, and take

she in through the open window. Madam arrive a little later, pass

through the hall to the dining-room. rather inopportunely, there sit

Bessy, busy with her housekeeping book.



"take they elsewhere," say madam, with an imperious sweep of the

hand.



she be not in the habit of give a reason for any command whatever:

let it be reasonable or the contrary, the rule be to hear and obey.

Bessy gather her book up and go away, madam fasten the bolt of

the door after she.



then she steal across the soft Turkey carpet and slip into the

closet already speak of, that form a communication--though never

use--between the dining-room and Mr. North's parlour. the door

opening to the parlour be unlatched, and have be ever since he put

his slipper inside it an hour ago. when her eye become accustomed to

the darkness, madam see they there; she also see one or two of his old

brown gardening coat hang on the peg. against the wall be a

narrow table with an unlocked desk upon it, belong to herself. it

be clever of madam to keep it there. open the lid silently, she

pull up a few of its valueless paper, and let they appear. of

course, if the closet be suddenly enter from the parlour--a most

unlikely thing to happen, but madam be cautious--she be only get

something from her desk. in this manner she have occasionally make an

unsuspected third at Richard North's interview with his father.

let the lace hood slip off, madam bend her ear to the crevice of

the door, and stand there listen. she be under the influence of

terror still: her lip be draw, her face wear the hue of death.



apparently the ostensible motive of the interview--Mrs. Cumberland's

wish to express her sympathy for the blow that have fall on the

Hall--be over; she have probably also be ask for Mr. North's

influence in favour of her son. the first connect word madam catch

be these:--



"I will do what I can, Mrs. Cumberland. I wish to do it before, as

you know. but Mrs. North take a dislike--I mean take a fancy to

Alexander."



"you mean take a dislike to Oliver," correct Mrs. Cumberland. "in

the old day, when you be John North without thought of future

grandeur, and I be Fanny Gass, we speak out freely to each other."



"true," say poor Mr. North. "I've never have such good day since. ah,

what a long time it seem to look back to! I have grow into an old

man, Fanny, old in feeling than in year; and you--you waste the

good day of your life in a hot and unhealthy climate."



"unhealthy in place and at certain season," correct Mrs.

Cumberland. "my husband be station in the beautiful climate of the

Blue Mountains, as we familiarly call the region of the Neilgherry

Hills. it be pleasant there."



"ay, I've hear so. get the cool breeze and all that."



"People use to come up there from the hot plain to regain their lost

health," continue Mrs. Cumberland, whose thought be apt to wander

back to the early year of her exile. "Ootacamund be resort to

there, just as the cold seaside place be here. but I and Mr.

Cumberland be stationary."



"Ootacamund?" repeat Mr. North, strike with the name. "Ootacamund

be where my wife's first husband die; Major Bohun."



"no, he do not die there," quietly rejoin Mrs. Cumberland.



"be it not there? ah! well, it do not matter. one be apt to confuse

these foreign name and place in the memory."



Mrs. Cumberland make no rejoinder, and a momentary silence ensue.

Madam, who with the mention of the place, Ootacamund, bit her lip

almost to bleed, bend forward, and look through the opening of

the door. she could just see the small portion of the cold, calm,

grey face, and wait in sicken apprehension of what the next word

might be. they come from Mrs. Cumberland and prove an intense relief,

for the subject be change for another.



"I be about to make a request to you, John: I hope you will grant it

for our old friendship's sake. let I see the anonymous letter that

prove so fatal to Edmund. every incident connect with this calamity

be to I so full of painful interest!" she continue, as if seek to

apologize for her request. "as I lay awake last night, unable to

sleep, it come into my mind that I would ask you to let I see the

letter."



"you may see it, and welcome," be Mr. North's ready reply, as he

unlock a drawer in his old secretaire, and hand the paper to she.

"I only wish I could show it to some purpose--to someone who would

recognize the handwriting. you willn't do that."



Mrs. Cumberland answer by a sickly smile. her hand tremble as

she take the letter, and Mr. North notice how white her lip have

become--as if with some inward suspense or emotion. she study the

letter well, read it three time over; look at it critically in

all light. Madam in the closet could have strike she for her

inquisitive curiosity.



"you be right, John," she say, with an unmistakable sigh of relief

as she give the missive back to he; "I certainly do not recognize

that handwriting. it be like no one's that I ever see."



"it be a disguised hand, you see," he answer. "no doubt about that:

and accomplish in the clever manner."



"be it true that poor Edmund have be draw bill in conjunction

with Alexander?"



"only one. he have draw a good many I'm afraid during his short

lifetime in conjunction with other people, but only one with

Alexander--which they get renew. no blame attache to Alexander; not

a scrap of it."



"Oliver tell I that."



"ay. I have a notion that poor Edmund do not get into this trouble

for his own sake, but to help that young scamp, his brother."



"which brother?"



"which brother!" echo Mr. North, rather in mockery. "as if you need

ask that. there's only one of they who could deserve the epithet, and

that's Sidney. an awful scamp. he be but twenty year of age, and he

be as deep in the way of a bad world as though he be forty."



"I be very sorry to hear you say it. whisper go abroad about he, as

I dare say you know, but I would rather not have hear they confirm

by you."



"People cann't say much too bad of he. we have Mrs. North to thank for

it: it be all owe to the way she have bring he up. when I would

have correct his fault, she step in between we. oftentime have

I think of the enemy that sow the tare amidst the wheat in his

neighbour's field."



"the old saying come home to many of we," observe Mrs. Cumberland

with a suppress sigh, as she rise to leave. "when our child be

young they tread upon our toe, but when they grow old they tread

upon our heart."



"ay, ay! don't go yet," add Mr. North. "it be pleasant in time of

sorrow to see an old friend. I have no friend now."



"I must go, John. Ellen Adair be wait for I, and will find the

time long. and I expect it would not be very agreeable to your wife to

see I here. not that I know wherefore, or what I can have do to

she."



"she encourage no one; no one of the good old day," be the

confidential rejoinder. "there's no fear of she; I see she go off

towards the shrubbery--after Master Sidney, I suppose. she take

what she call her constitutional walk there. they last a couple of

hour sometimes."



as Mr. North turn to put the letter into the drawer again, he catch

sight of a scrap of poetry that have be find in Arthur Bohun's desk.

this he also show his visitor. he would have keep nothing from she;

she be the only link leave to he of the day when he and the world

(to he) be alike young. have Mrs. Cumberland stay there till

night, he would then have think it too soon for she to depart.



"I will do all I can for your son, Fanny," say Mr. North, as they

stand for a moment at the glass-door. "I like Oliver. he be a steady,

persevere fellow, and I'll help he on if I can. if I do not, the

fault will not lie with I. you understand?" he add, look at she.



Mrs. Cumberland understand perfectly--the fault would lie with madam.

she nod in answer.



"Mr. Alexander be go, John--as you know. should Oliver succeed in

get the whole of the practice--and there's nothing to prevent

it--he will soon be make a large income. in that case, I suppose he

will be ask you to give he something else."



"you mean Bessy. I wish to goodness he have she!" continue Mr. North

impulsively; "I do heartily wish it sometimes. she have not a very

happy life of it here. well, well; I hope Oliver will get on with all

my heart; tell he so from I, Fanny. he shall have she when he do."



"_shall_ he!" ejaculate madam from her closet, and in her most

scornfully defiant tone--for the conversation have not please she.



they went stroll away amidst the flower, madam peer after they

with angry eye. she hear her husband tell Mrs. Cumberland to come

again; to come in often; whenever she would. Mr. North go on with

she down the broad path, after they have linger some minute with the

sweet flower. in stroll back alone, who should pounce upon Mr.

North from a side path but madam!



"be not that woman I see you with the Cumberland, Mr. North?"



"it be Mrs. Cumberland: my early friend. she come in to express she

sympathy at my loss. I take it as very kind of she, madam."



"I take it as very insolent," retort madam. "she have some girl with

she when she come in. who be it?"



"some girl!" repeat Mr. North, whose memory be anything but

retentive. "ah yes, I remember: she say her ward be wait for

she."



"who be her ward?"



"the daughter of a friend whom they know in India, madam. in India or

Australia; I forget which: George Cumberland be station in both

place. a charming young lady with a romantic name: Ellen Adair."



Madam toy with the black lace that shield her face. "you seem to

know she, Mr. North."



"I have see she in the road; and in come out of church. the first

time I meet they be in Dallory, one day last summer, and Mrs.

Cumberland tell I who she be. that be all I know of she, madam--as

you seem to be curious."



"be she live at Mrs. Cumberland's?"



"just now she be. I--I think they say she be go out to join she

father," add Mr. North, whose impression be always hazy in

matter that do not immediately concern he. "yes, I'm nearly sure,

madam: to Australia."



"her father--whoever he may be--be not in Europe then?" slightingly

speak madam, stoop to root up mercilessly a handful of blue-bell.



"her father live over yonder. that's why the young lady have to go out

to he."



Madam toss away the rifle flower and raise her head to its

customary haughty height. the danger have pass. "over yonder" meant,

as she know, some far-off antipode. she fling aside the girl and the

interlude from her recollection, just as ruthlessly as she have fling

the blue-bell.



"I want some money, Mr. North."



Mr. North go into a flutter at once. "I--I have none by I, madam."



"then give I a cheque."



"nor cheque either. I don't happen to have a sign cheque in the

house, and Richard be go for the day."



"what have I repeatedly tell you--that you must _keep_ money by you;

and cheque too," be her stern answer. "why do Richard always sign

the cheque? why cann't you sign they?"



she have ask the same thing fifty time, and he have never be goad

to give the true answer.



"I have not sign a cheque since Thomas Gass die, except on my own

private account, madam; no, nor for long before it. my account be

overdrawn. I shalln't have a stiver in the bank until next quarter-day."



"you tell I that last week," she say contemptuously. "draw then upon

the firm account."



he shake his head. "the bank would not cash it."



"why?"



"because only Richard can sign. oh dear, this be go over and over

the old ground again. you'll wear I out, madam. when Richard take the

management at the work, it be judge advisable that he should alone

sign the business cheque--for convenience' sake, madam; for

convenience' sake. Gass's hand be crippled with gout; I be here

with my flower."



"I don't care who sign the cheque so that I get the money," she

retort in rude, rough tone. "you must give I some to-day."



"it be for Sidney; I know it be for Sidney," speak Mr. North

tremulously. "Madam, you be ruin that lad. for his own sake some

check must be put upon he: and therefore I be thankful that to-day I

have no money to give."



he take some short hurried step over the corner of path and

flower-bed, with the last word, and get into his own room. Madam

calmly follow. very sure might he be that she would not allow he

to escape she.



Ellen Adair, wait for Mrs. Cumberland, have _not_ feel the time

long. very shortly after she be leave alone, the carriage come back

from the station, bring Arthur Bohun: Richard have be leave at

Whitborough. Captain Bohun get out at the gate, intend to walk up

to the house. Ellen see he come limp along--the halt in his gait

be always more visible when he have be sit for any length of

time--and he at the same time catch sight of the bright hue of the

lilac dress gleam through the tree.



some year back, the detachment command by Arthur Bohun be

quarter in Ireland. one ill-star night it be call out to

suppress some local disturbance, and he be desperately wound:

shot, as be suppose, unto death. that he would never be fit for

service again; that his death, though it might be linger, be

inevitable; surgeon and friend alike thought. for nearly two year

he be look upon as a die man: that be, as a man who could not

possibly recover. but Time, the great healer, restore he; and he

come out of his sickness and danger with only a slight limp, more or

less perceptible. when walk slowly, or when he take any one's arm,

it be not see at all. Mrs. North (who be proud of her handsome and

distinguished son, although she have no love for he) be willnot to tell

friend confidentially that he have a bullet in his hip yet--at which

Arthur would laugh.



the sight of the lilac dress cause he to turn aside. Ellen rise and

stand wait; her whole being be thrill with the rapture the

meeting bring. he take her hand in his, his face light.



"be it indeed you, Ellen! I should as soon have expect to see a

fairy here."



"Mrs. Cumberland have go to call on Mr. North. she tell I to wait

for she."



"I have be with Dick to take my uncle and James to the station,"

speak Captain Bohun, pitch upon it as something to say, for his

tongue be never too fluent when alone with she. "he have be ask

I to go and stay with he."



"Sir Nash have?"



"yes. Jimmy invite no one; he be take up with his missionary, and

that."



"shall you go?"



their eye meet as she put the question. go! away from she!



"I think not," he quietly answer. "not at present. Miss Bohun's turn

must come first: she have be write for I this long time."



"that's your aunt."



"my aunt. and a good old soul she be. willn't you walk about a little,

ellen?"



she take the arm he hold out, and they pace the cover walk, almost

in silence. the May bird be singe, the bud leave be green.

Eloquence enough for they: and each might have detect the beating of

the other's heart. Madam have her ear glue to that closet-door, and so

miss the sight. a sight that would have make her hair stand on end.



Minutes, for lover, fly on swift wing. when Mrs. Cumberland

appear, it seem that she have be away no time. Ellen go forward

to meet she: and Captain Bohun say he have just come home from the

station. Mrs. Cumberland, absorb in her own care, complain of

fatigue, take little or no notice of he: he stroll by their side up

the Ham. stand at Mrs. Cumberland's gate for a moment in parting,

Oliver Rane come so hastily out of his house that he run against they.



"don't knock I down, Rane," speak Arthur Bohun in his lazy but very

pleasing manner.



"I beg your pardon. when I be in a hurry I believe I be apt to drive

on in a blindfold fashion."



"be any one ill, Oliver?" question his mother.



"yes. at Mrs. Gass's. I fear it be herself. the man who bring the

message do not know."



"you ought to keep a horse," speak Captain Bohun, as the doctor

recommence his course. "so much run about must wear out a man's

leg."



"ought go for a great deal, don't they?" reply the doctor, look

back. "I ought to be rich enough to keep one, but I'm not."



Captain Bohun wish they good-day, and they go indoor. Ellen

wonder at hear that Mrs. Cumberland be go out again. feel

uneasy--as she say--about the sudden illness, she take her way to the

house of Mrs. Gass, in spite of the fatigue she have be complain

of. a long walk for she at any time. arrive there she find that lady

in perfect health; it be one of her servant to whom Oliver have be

summon. the young woman have scald her hand and arm.



"I be at the Hall this morning, and Mr. North show I the anonymous

letter," Mrs. Cumberland take occasion to say. "it evidently come

from a stranger; a stranger to we. the handwriting be quite strange."



"so much the well, ma'am," heartily speak Mrs. Gass. "it would be

too bad to think it be write by a friend."



"Oliver think it be madam," pursue Mrs. Cumberland, lower she

voice. "at least--he have not go so far as to say _he_ think it, but

that Mr. Alexander do."



"that's just what he say to I, ma'am. Alexander think it, he say,

but that he himself don't know what to think, one way or the other.

as well, perhaps, for we not to talk of it: least say be soonest

mend."



"of course. but I cannot help recall a remark once innocently make

by Arthur Bohun in my hearing: that he do not know any one who could

imitate different handwriting so well as his mother. do you"--Mrs.

Cumberland look cautiously round--"observe the girl, Molly Green,

take her handkerchief from her pocket whilst she stand here?"



"I don't see she with any handkercher," be the answer, give after a

slight pause. "shouldn't think the girl have one. she put her basket on

the sideboard there, to come forward to my geranium, and stand stock

still while she look at 'em. I don't say she don't go to her

pocket; but I never see she do it."



"it might have be so. these little action often pass unnoticed. and

it be so easy for any other article to slip out unseen when a

handkerchief be draw from a pocket," conclude Mrs. Cumberland in a

suppress, almost eager tone. which Mrs. Gass notice, and do not

quite like.



but there be still something to relate of Dallory Hall. when madam

follow her husband through the glass-door into his parlour, an

unusually unpleasant scene ensue. for once Mr. North hold out

resolutely. he have no other resource, for he have not the money to give

she, and do not know where to get it. that it be for Sidney, he well

believe; and for that reason only would have deny it to the utmost

of his feeble strength. Madam flounce out in one of her bad mood.

Mrs. Cumberland's visit and the startling sight of Ellen Adair have

bring to her unusual annoyance. as ill-luck have it, she encounter

Bessy in the hall, and upon she vent her temper. the short scene be

a violent one. when it be over, the poor girl go shiver and

tremble into her father's parlour. he have be stand with the

door ajar, shrink almost as much as Bessy, and utterly powerless to

interfere.



"oh, child! if I could only save you from this!" he murmur, as they

stand together before the window, and he fondly stroke the head that

lie on his breast. "it's cue of the trouble that be wear I out,

bessy: wear I out before my time."



Bessy North be patient, meek, enduring; but meekness and patience can

both be try beyond their strength.



"Oliver Rane want you: you know that, Bessie. if he could see his way

to keep you, you should go to he tomorrow. ay! though your poor

brother have just be put into his grave."



Bessy lift her head. in these moment of emotion, the heart speak

out without reticence.



"Papa, I would go to Oliver as he be now, and risk it," she say

through her blind tear. "I should not be afraid of our get on:

we would make shift together, until well time come. he speak a word

of this to I not long ago, but his lip be seal, he say, and he

could not press it."



"he think he have not enough for you?"



"he think you would not consider it so. _I_ should, papa. and I

think those who bravely set out to struggle on together, have as much

happiness in their makeshift and economy as other who begin with a

fortune."



"we'll see; we'll see, Bessy. I should like you to try it, if you be

not afraid. I'll talk to Dick. but--mind!--not a word here," he add,

glance round to indicate the precinct of Mrs. North. "we shall have

to keep it to ourselves if we would not have it frustrate. I wonder

how much Oliver make a year."



"not much; but he be advance slowly. he have talk to I about it.

what keep one will keep two, papa."



"he come into about two hundred a-year when his mother die. and I

fear she willn't live long, from what she tell I. poor Fanny! not that

I'd counsel any one to reckon on dead man's shoe, child. Life's

uncertain: he might die before she."



"he would not reckon on anything but his own exertion, papa. he tell

I a secret--that he be engage on a medical work, write it all his

spare time. it be quite certain to become a standard work, he say,

and bring he good return. oh! papa, there will be no doubt about our

get on. let we risk it!"



she speak in a bright, hopeful tone--her mild eye shine. Mr. North

catch a little of the glad spirit, and resolve--Dick be willing:

sensible Dick--that they should risk it.









CHAPTER IX.

in LAWYER DALE'S OFFICE.





Whitborough be a good-sized, bustling town, send two member to

parliament. in the heart of it live Mr. Dale, the lawyer, who do a

little in money-lending as well. he be a short stout man, with a red

face and no whisker, nearly bald on the top of his round head; and he

usually attire himself in the attractive costume of a brown tail coat

and white neckcloth.



on this same morning which have witness the departure of Sir Nash

Bohun and his son from Dallory Hall, Mr. Dale, know commonly amongst

his townsfolk as Lawyer Dale--be seat in his office at

Whitborough. it be a small room, contain a sort of double desk, at

which two people might face each other. the lawyer's seat be against

the wall, his face to the room; a clerk sometimes sit, or stand on the

other side when business be press. Adjoining this office be one

for the clerk, three of whom be keep; and client have to pass

through their room to reach the lawyer's.



Mr. Dale be write busily. the clock be on the stroke of twelve,

and a great deal of the morning's work have still to be do, when one

of the clerk come in: a tall, thin, cadaverous youth with black hair,

part into a flat curl on his forehead.



"be you at home, sir?"



"who be it?" ask Mr. Dale, growl at the interruption.



"Mr. Richard North."



"send he in."



Richard come in; a fine look man in his mourning clothe; the

lawyer could not help think so. after shake hand--a ceremony Mr.

Dale like to observe with all his client, when agreeable to they--he

come from behind his desk to seat himself in his elbow-chair of red

leather, and give Richard a seat opposite. the room be small, the

desk and other furniture large, and they sit very close together.

Richard hold his hat on his knee.



"you guess, no doubt, what have bring I here, Mr. Dale. now that my

ill-fated brother be put out of our sight in his last resting-place, I

have leisure and inclination to look into the miserable event that

send he there. I shall spare neither expense nor energy in

discover--if it may be--the traitor."



"you allude to the anonymous letter."



"yes. and I have come to ask you to give I all the information you

can about it."



"but, my good sir, I have no information to give. I don't possess

any."



"I ought to have say detail of the attendant circumstance. let I

hear your history of the transaction from begin to end: and if you

can give I any hint as to the writer--that be, if you have form any

private opinion about he--I trust you will do so."



Mr. Dale could be a little tricky on occasion; he be sometimes

engage in transaction that would not have bear the light of day,

and that most certainly he would never have talk about. on the other

hand, he could be honest and truthful where there exist no reason

for be the contrary: and this anonymous letter business come under

the latter category.



"the transaction be as open and straightforward as possible," speak

the lawyer--and Richard, a judge of character and countenance, see he

be speak the truth. "Mr. Edmund North come to I one day some

short time ago, want I to let he have a hundred pound on his own

security. I don't care to do that--I know about his bill

transaction, you see--and I propose that some one should join he.

eventually he come with Alexander the surgeon, and the matter be

arrange."



"do you know for what purpose he want the money?"



"for his young brother, Sidney North. a fast young man, that, Mr.

Richard," add the lawyer in significant tone.



"yes. unfortunately."



"well, he have get into some secret trouble, and come pray to

Mr. Edmund to get he out of it. whatever foolish way Edmund North

have waste money in, there's this consolation remain to his

friend--that the transaction which eventually send he to his grave

be one of pure kindness," add the lawyer warmly. "'my father have

enough trouble,' he remark to I; 'with one thing and another, his

life's almost worried out of he; and I don't care that he should hear

what Master Sidney's be up to, if it can be keep from he.' yes; the

motive be a good one."



"how be it he do not apply to I?" ask Richard.



"well--have you not, just about that time, assist your brother Edmund

in some scrape of his own?"



Richard North nod.



"just so. he say he have not the face to apply to you so soon again;

should be ashamed of himself. well, to go on, Mr. Richard North. I

give he the money on the bill; and when it become due, neither he nor

Alexander could meet it: so I agree to renew it. only one day after

that, the anonymous letter find its way to Dallory Hall."



"you be sure of that?"



"certain. the bill be renew on the 30th of April; here, in this

very room. Mr. North receive the letter on the 1st of May."



"it be so. by the evening post."



"so that, if the transaction get wind through that renewing, the

writer do not lose much time about it."



"well now, Mr. Dale, in what way could that transaction have get wind,

and who hear of it?"



"I never speak of it to a human being," impetuously cry the lawyer.

and Richard North again feel sure that he speak the truth.



"the transaction, from the beginning, be know only to we three man:

Edmund North, the surgeon, and myself. I don't believe either of they

mention it at all. I know I do not. it's just possible Edmund North

might have tell his stepbrother Sidney how he get the money--the young

scamp. I beg your pardon, Mr. Richard; I forget he be your brother

also."



"it would be to Sidney's interest to keep it quiet," remark Richard.

"our man at the work have a report amongst they--I know not where

pick up, and I don't think they know either--that the writer be

your clerk, Wilks."



"nonsense!" contemptuously rejoin the lawyer. "I've hear the report

also. why should Wilks trouble his head about it? don't believe

anything so foolish."



"I don't believe it," return Richard North. "wilk could have no

motive whatever for it, as far as I can see. but I think that he may

have become cognizant of the affair, and talk of it abroad."



"not one of my clerk know anything about it," protest Mr. Dale.

"I've three of 'em: wilk and two other. you don't suppose, sir, I

take they into my confidence in all thing."



"but, be it quite impossible that any one of they--say Wilks--could

have find it out surreptitiously?" urge Richard.



"Wilks have nothing surreptitious about he," say the lawyer. "he be

too shallow for it. a thoroughly useful clerk, but a man without

guile."



"I do not mean to apply the word to he personally. I'll change it if

you like. could Wilks, or either of the other two, have accidentally

learn this, without your knowledge? be there a possibility of it?

come, Mr. Dale; be open with I. even if it be so, no blame would

attach to you."



"it be just this," answer Mr. Dale: "I don't see how it be possible

for any one of they to have learn it; and yet at the same time, I see

no other way in which it could have transpire. that's the candid

truth. I lay awake one night for half-an-hour, turn the puzzle over

in my mind. Alexander say he never open his lip about it; I know I

do not; and poor Edmund North go into his fatal passion thinking

Alexander write the letter, because he say Alexander alone know of

it; a pretty sure proof he have not talk about it himself."



"which bring we back to your clerk," remark Richard North. "they

might have overhear a few chance word when the bill be renew."



"I'm sure the door be shut," debate Mr. Dale, in a tone as if he

be _not_ sure, but rather seek to persuade himself that he be so.

"only Wilks be in, that morning; the other two have go out."



"rely upon it that's how it happen. the door could not have be

quite closed."



"well, I don't know. I generally shut it myself, and carefully too,

when important client be in here. I confess," honestly add Mr.

Dale, "that it's the only explanation I can see in the matter. if the

door be unlatche, Wilks might have hear. I have he in last night,

and tax he with it. he deny it out and out: say that, even if

the affair have come to his knowledge, he know his duty well than to

have talk about it."



"I don't doubt that he do, when in his sober sense. but he be not

always in they."



"oh, come, Mr. Richard North, it be not as bad as that."



Richard be silent. if Mr. Dale be satisfied with his clerk and his

clerk's discretion, he have no desire to render he otherwise.



"he take too much now and then, you know, Mr. Dale; and he may have

drop a word in some enemy's hearing: who perhaps catch it up and

then write the letter. would you mind my question he?"



"he be not here to be question, or you might do it and welcome,"

reply Mr. Dale. "Wilks be lie up to-day. he have not be well for

more than a week past; could hardly do his work yesterday."



"I'll take an opportunity of see he then," say Richard. "my

father willn't rest until the writer of this letter have be trace;

neither, in truth, shall I."



the lawyer say good-morning to his visitor, and return to his desk.

but ere he recommence work, he think over the chief subject of

their conversation. have the traitor be Wilks, he ask himself. what

Richard North have say be perfectly true--the young man sometimes

take too much after work be over. but Mr. Dale have hitherto find no

reason to complain of his discretion; and, difficult as it seem to

find any other loophole of suspicion, he finally conclude that he have

no reason to do so now.



meanwhile Richard North walk back to Dallory--it be nearly two

mile from Whitborough. pass his work, he continue his way a

little far, to a turning call North Inlet, in which be some

house, large and small, chiefly tenant by his workpeople. in one of

these, a pretty cottage stand back, lodge Timothy Wilks. the

landlady be a relative of Wilks's, and as he pay very little for his

two room, he do not mind the walk once a-day to and from

Whitborough.



"good-morning, Mrs. Green. be Timothy Wilks in?"



Mrs. Green, an ancient matron in a mob-cap, be on her knee,

whiten the door-step. she rise at the salutation, see it be

Richard North, and curtsy.



"Tim have just crawl out to get a bit o' sunshine, sir. he's very

bad to-day. would you please to walk in, Mr. Richard?"



amidst this colony of his workpeople he be chiefly know as "Mr.

Richard." Mrs. Green's husband be timekeeper at the North Works.



"what's the matter with he?" ask Richard, as he step over the

threshold and the bucket to the little parlour.



"well, sir, I only hope it's not low fever; but it look to I

uncommon like it."



"since when have he be ill?"



"he have be ail this fortnight past. the fact be, sir, he _willn't_

keep steady," she add in deplore tone. "once a-week he's safe to

come home the bad for drink, and that's pay night; and sometimes

it's oftener than that. then for two day afterwards he cann't eat; and

so it go on, and he get as weak as a rat. it's not that he take

much drink; it be that a little upset he. some man could take

half-a-dozen glass a'most to his one."



"what a pity it be!" exclaim Richard.



"he have a regular bout of it a week or so ago," resume Mrs. Green;

who once set off on the score of Timothy's misdoing, never know when

to stop. it be so well know to North Inlet, this failing of the

young man's, that she might have talk of it in the market-place and

not betray confidence. "he have be ail before, as I say, Mr.

Richard; off his food, and that; but one night he catch it smartly,

and he's be get bad ever since."



"catch what smartly?" ask Richard, not post up in North Inlet

idiom.



"why, the drink, sir. he come home reeling, and give his head such a

bang again the door-post that it knock he back'ards. I get he up

somehow--Green be out--and on to his bed, and there he go off in a

dead faint. I'd no vinegar in the house: if you want a thing in a

hurry you're sure to be out of it: so I burn a feather up his nose,

and that bring he to. he begin to talk all sort of nonsense then,

about do 'bill,' whatever that might mean, and old Dale's

money-box, run word into one another like mad, so that you

couldn't make top or tail of it. I'd never see he as bad as this,

and get frighten."



she pause to take breath, always short with Mrs. Green. the word

"do bill" strike Richard North. he immediately perceive that

hence might have arise the report--for she have no doubt talk of

this publicly--that Timothy Wilks be the traitor. other listener

could put two and two together as well as he.



"I think I'd get in the vinegar, in case he go off again,"

resume Mrs. Green. "and when I be run round to the shop for

it--leastway walk, for I cann't run now--who should I meet, turn

out of Ketler's but Dr. Rane. I stop to tell he, and he say he'd

look in and see Tim. he's a kind man in sickness, Mr. Richard."



"do Dr. Rane come?" ask Richard.



"right off, sir, there and then. when I get back he have put cloth of

cold water on Tim's head. and ben't Tim talk! you might have

think he a show-man at the fair. the doctor write something on

paper with his pencil and send I off again to Stevens the druggist's,

and Stevens he give I a little bottle of white stuff ta bring back.

the doctor give Tim some of it in a teacup of cold water, and it send

he into a good sleep. but he have never be well, sir, since then:

and now I misdoubt I but it will end in low fever."



"do you remember what night this be?" ask Richard.



"ay, that I do, sir. for the foolish girl be stand out by two

and three, make bargain with their sweetheart to go a-maye at

morning dawn. I tell 'em they'd a deal well stop indoor to mend

their stocking. 'twas the night afore the first of May, Mr. Richard."



"the evening of the day the bill be renew," think Richard. he

possess the right clue now. if he have entertain any doubt of Wilks

before, this set it at rest.



"do any of the neighbour hear Tim talk?" he ask.



"not a soul but I and Dr. Rane here, sir. but I believe he have be

hold forth to a room-full at the Wheatsheaf. they say he be in

part go when he get there. oh, it do make I so vex, the ranting

way he go on when the drink's in he. if his poor father and mother

could look up from their grave, they'd be fit to shake he in very

shame. drink be the bad curse that's go, Mr. Richard--and poor

Tim's weak head willn't stand hardly a drop of it."



she have tell all she know. Richard North step over the bucket

again, remark that he might meet Tim. sure enough he do so. in

take a cross-cut to the work, he come upon he, lean against the

wooden railing that border a piece of waste land. he look very

ill: Richard see that: a small, slight young man with a mild, pleasant

countenance and inoffensive manner. his mother have be a cousin of

Mrs. Green's, but superior to the Greens in station. Timothy would

have hold his head considerably above North Inlet, but for be

bring down both in consequence and pocket by his oft-recur

failing.



kindly and courteously, but with a resolute tone not to be mistake,

Richard North enter on his questioning. he do not suspect Wilks of

having write the anonymous letter; he tell he this candidly; but he

suspect, nay, know, that it must have be write by some one who

have gather certain detail from Wilks's gossip. wilk, weak and ill,

acknowledge that the circumstance of the drawing of the bill; or

rather the renewing of one; have penetrate to his hearing in Mr.

Dale's office; but he declare that he have not, as far as he know,

repeat it again.



"I'd no more talk of our office business, sir, than I'd write an

anonymous letter," say he, much aggrieve. "Mr. Dale never have a more

faithful clerk about he than I be."



"I dare say you would not, knowingly," be Richard's rejoinder.

"answer I one question, Wilks. have you any recollection of

harangue the public at the Wheatsheaf?"



Mr. Wilks's reply to this be, that he have not harangue the public at

the Wheatsheaf. he remember be at the house quite well, and there

have be a good deal of argument in the parlour; chiefly, he think,

touch the question as to whether master in general ought not to

give holiday on the first of May. there have be no particular

harangue on his part, he declare; and he could take his oath that

he never open his lip there about what have come to his knowledge.

one thing he do confess, on be press by Richard--that he have no

remembrance of quit the Wheatsheaf, or of how he reach home. he

retain a faint idea of having see Dr. Rane's face bend over he

later, but could not say whether it be dream or reality.



nothing more could be get out of Timothy Wilks. that the man be

guiltless of intentional treachery be as undoubted as that the

treachery have occur through his talking. Richard North bend his

step to the Wheatsheaf, to hold conference with Packerton, the

landlord of that much-frequent hostelrie.



and any information that Packerton could give, he be willing to give;

but it amount to little. Richard want the name of all who go

into the parlour on the night of the 30th of April, during the time

that Wilks be there. the landlord mention as many as he could

remember; but say that other might have go in and out. one

man--who look like a gentleman and sit by Wilks--be a stranger, he

say; he have never see he before or since. this man grow quite

friendly with Wilks, and go out with he, prop up his step.

Packerton's son, a smart youth of thirteen, go out on an errand,

have overtake they on their way across the waste ground. (in the very

path where Richard have only now encounter Wilks.) wilk be hold

on by the railing, the boy say, talk with the other as fast as he

could talk, and the other be laugh. Richard North wish he could

find out who this man be, and where he might be see; for, of all the

rest mention by the landlord, not one be at all likely to have

write the anonymous letter. Packerton's opinion be that Wilks have

not speak of the matter there; he be then hardly "far enough go"

to have commit the imprudence.



"but I suppose he be when he leave you," say Richard.



"yes, sir, I'm afraid he might have be. he could talk; but every bit

of reason have go out of he. I never see anybody but wilk just like

this when they've take too much."



again Richard North seek Wilks, and question he who this

stranger, man or gentleman, might be. he might as well have question

the moon. wilk have a hazy impression of having be with a tall,

thin, strange man: but where, or when, or how, he know not.



"I'll ask Rane what sort of a condition Wilks be in when he see he,"

think Richard.



but Richard could not carry out his intention until night. business

claim he for the rest of the day, and then he go home to dinner.



Dr. Rane be in his dining-room that night, the white blind draw

before the window, and write by the light of a shade candle. bessy

North have say to her father that Oliver be busy with a medical work

from which he expect good return when publish. it be so. he

spare himself no labour; over that, or anything else: often write

far into the small hour. he be a patient, persevere man: once give

he a chance of success, a fair start on life's road, and he would be

sure to go on to fortune. he say this to himself continually; and he

be not mistaken. but the chance have not come yet.



the clock be strike eight, when the doctor hear a ring at his

door-bell, and Phillis appear, show in Richard North. a thrill

pass through Oliver Rane; perhaps he could not have tell why or

wherefore.



Richard sit down and begin to talk about Wilks, ask what he have to

ask, enter into the question generally. Dr. Rane listen in

silence.



"I beg your pardon," he suddenly say, remember his one shade

candle. "I ought to have ask for more light."



"it's quite light enough for I," reply Richard. "don't trouble. to

go back to Wilks: do he say anything about the bill in your hearing,

Rane?"



"not a word; not a syllable. or, if he do, I fail to catch it."



"Old Mother Green say he talk about 'bill,'" say Richard. "that

be before you see he."



"do she?" carelessly remark the doctor. "I hear nothing of the

kind. there be no coherence whatever in his word, so far as I

notice: one never pay much attention to the babbling of a drunken

man."



"be he quite beside himself?--quite unconscious of what he say,

Rane?"



"well, I be tell that it be the peculiar idiosyncrasy of Wilks to be

able to talk and yet to be unconscious for all practical purpose, and

for recollection afterwards. otherwise I should not have consider

he quite so far go as that. he talk certainly; a little; seem

to answer I in a mechanical sort of way when I ask he a question,

slip one word into another. if I have try to understand he, I

don't suppose I could have do so. he do not say much; and I be

away from he a good deal about the house, look for water and rag

to put on his head."



"then _you_ hear nothing about it, Rane?"



"absolutely nothing."



the doctor sit so that the green shade of the candle happen to fall

on his face, make it look very pale. Richard North, absorb in

thought about Wilks, could not have tell whether the face be in

shadow or in light. he speak next about the stranger who have join

Wilks, say he wish he could find out who it be.



"a tall thin man, bear the appearance of a gentleman?" return Dr.

Rane. "then I think I see he, and speak to he."



"where?" ask Richard with animation.



"close to your work. he be look in through the iron gate. after

quit Green's cottage, I cross the waste ground, and see he

stand at the gate, under the middle gas-lamp. I have to visit a

patient down by the church, and take the near way."



"you do not recognize he?"



"not at all. he be a stranger to I. as I be pass, he turn and

ask I whether he be go right for Whitborough. I point to the

high-road and tell he to keep straight on. depend upon it, this be

the same man."



"what could he have be look in at my gate for?" mutter

Richard. "and what--for this be of more consequence--have he be

get out of Wilks?"



"it seem rather curious altogether," remark Dr. Rane.



"I'll find this man," say Richard, as he get up to say goodnight; "I

must find he. thank you, Rane."



but after his departure Oliver Rane do not settle to his work as

before. a man, once interrupt, cannot always do so. all he do be

to pace the room restlessly with bow head, as a man in some uneasy

dream. the candle burn lower, the flame grow above the shade,

throw its light on his face, show up its line and angle. but

it be not any bright than when the green shade have cast over it its

cadaverous hue.



"Edmund North! Edmund North!"



do the word in all their piteous, hopeless appeal come from he? or

be it some supernatural cry in the air?









CHAPTER X.

PUT to his CONSCIENCE.





a fine morning in June. lovely June; with its bright blue sky and

its summer flower. walk about amidst his rose-tree, be Mr.

North, a rake in his hand. he fancy he be garden; he know he be

trifle. what do it matter?--his face look almost happy. the glad

sunshine be overhead, and he feel as free as a bird of the air.



the anonymous letter, that have cause so much mischief, be pass

into a thing of the past. in spite of Richard North's effort to trace

he out, the writer remain undiscovered. Timothy Wilks be the chief

sufferer, and bitterly resentful thereon. to have be openly accuse

of having send it by at least six person out of every dozen

acquaintance he meet, disturb the mind and curdle the temper of

ill-star Timothy Wilks. as to the general public, they be

begin to forget all about the trouble--as it be in the nature of a

faithless public to do. only in the heart of a few individual do

the sad fact remain in all their sternness; and of those, one be

Jelly.



Poor Mr. North could afford to be happy to-day, and for many day to

come. Bessy also. Madam have relieve they of her presence yesterday,

and gone career off to Paris with her daughter. they hope she

might be away for week. in the seductive freedom of the home, Richard

North have stay late that morning. Mr. North be just begin to

talk with he, when some one call on business, and Richard shut

himself up with the stranger. the morning have go on; the interview

be prolong; but Richard be come out now. Mr. North put down the

rake.



"has Wilson go, Richard?"



"yes, sir."



"what do he want? he have stay long enough."



"only a little business with I, father," be Richard's answer in his

filial care. it have not be agreeable business, and Richard wish to

spare his father.



"and now for Bessy, sir?" he resume, as they pace side by side

amongst the sweet-scented rose. "you be begin to speak about

she."



"yes, I want to talk to you. Bessy would be happy with Rane than she

be here, Dick."



Richard look serious. he have no objection whatever to his sister's

marry Oliver Rane: in fact, he regard it as an event certain to

take place, soon or later; but he do not quite see that the way be

clear for it yet.



"I have no doubt of that, father."



"and I think, Dick, she have well go to he now, whilst we be at

liberty to do as we please at home."



"now!" exclaim Richard.



"yes; now. that be before madam come back again. poor Edmund be only

just put under the sod; but--consider the circumstance--I think

the memory of the dead must give place to the welfare of the living."



"but how about way and mean, sir?"



"ay: how about way and mean. nothing can be spare from the work at

present, I suppose, Dick."



"nothing to speak of, sir."



Mr. North have feel ashamed even to ask the question. in fact, it be

more a remark than a question, for he know as well as Richard do that

there be no superfluous money to draw upon.



"of course not, Dick. Rane get just enough to live upon now, and no

more. yesterday, after madam and Matilda have drive off, I be at the

front-gate when Rane pass. so he and I get talk about Bessy. he

say his income be small now, but that of course it would

considerably augment as soon as Alexander have leave. as he and Bessy

be willing to try it, I don't see why they should not do so, Dick."



Richard give no immediate reply. he have a rose in his hand and be

look at it absently, deep in thought. his father continue:



"it's not as if Rane have no expectation whatever. two hundred a-year

must come to he at his mother's death. and--Dick--have you any idea

how Mrs. Gass's will be leave?"



"not the least, sir."



"Oliver Rane be the near living relative to her late husband, Mrs.

Cumberland except. he be Thomas Gass's own nephew--and all the money

be his. it seem to I, Dick, that Mrs. Gass be sure to remember he:

perhaps largely."



"she may do so."



"yes; and I think will do so. bessy shall go to he; and be

emancipate from her thraldom here."



"Oliver Rane have no furniture in his house."



"he have some. the dining-room and his bedroom be as handsomely

furnish as need be. we can send in a little more. there be some

thing at the Hall that be Bessy's own mother's, and she shall have

they. they have not be think much of here, Dick, amidst the grand

thing that madam have fill the house with."



"she'll make a fuss, though, at their be remove," remark Dick.



"let she," retort Mr. North, who could be brave as the good when two

or three hundred mile lie between he and madam. "those thing be

your own dear mother's, Dick; she buy they with her own money

before she marry I, and I have always regard they as heir-loom

for Bessy. it's just a few plain solid mahogany thing, as good as

ever they be. it be our drawing-room furniture in the early day,

and it will do for their drawing-room now. when Rane be make his six

or seven hundred a-year, they can buy fine if they choose. we think

great thing of it; I know that."



Richard smile. "I remember once when I be a very little fellow, my

mother come in and catch I draw a horse on the centre-table with

pen-and-ink. the trouble she have to get the horse out!--and the

whipping I have!"



"Poor Dick! she do not whip often."



"it do I good, sir. I have be scrupulously careful of furniture

ever since."



"ah, nothing like the lesson of early childhood for make an

impression," speak Mr. North. "'spare the rod and spoil the child!'

there be never a truer say than that."



"then you really intend they to marry at once," say Richard,

return to the question.



"I do," reply Mr. North in more decisive tone than he usually

speak. "they both wish it: and why should I hold out against they?

Bessy's thirty this year, you know, Dick: if girl be not wife at

that age, they begin to think it hard. it's well to marry tolerably

young: a man and woman don't shake down into each other's way if they

come together late in life. you be silent, Dick."



"I be think, sir, whether I could not manage a couple of hundred

pound for they from myself."



"you be ever generous, Dick. I don't know what we should all do

without you."



"the question be--shall I give it over to they in money, or spend it

for they in furniture?"



"in money; in money, Dick," advise Mr. North. "the furniture can be

manage; and cash be cash. spend it in chair and table and it seem

as if there be nothing tangible to show for it."



Richard smile. "it strike I that the argument lie the other way,

sir. however, I think it will be well to do as you advise. bessy

shall have two hundred pound hand to she after her marriage, and

they can do what they consider well with it."



"to be sure; to be sure, Dick. let they be marry. Bessy have a

miserable life of it here; and she'll be thirty on the twenty-ninth of

this month. Oliver Rane be thirty the latter end of March."



"only thirty!" cry Richard. "I think he must be more than that,

sir."



"but he's not more," return Mr. North. "I ought to know; and so

ought you, Dick. don't you remember they be both in the tontine? all

the child put into that tontine be bear in the same year."



"oh, be it so? I have forget," return Richard carelessly, for the

tontine have never trouble he very much. he could just recollect that

when they be child he and his brother be willnot to teaze little

Bessy, say if she live to be a hundred she would come into a

fortune.



"that be an unlucky tontine, Dick," say Mr. North, shake his head.

"of ten child who be enter for it, only three remain. the other

seven be dead. four of they die in the first or second year."



"how come Oliver Rane to be put into the tontine?" ask Richard. "I

think he come to life in India--and live there for the first few

year of his life. the tontine child be all whitborough

child."



"Thomas Gass do that, Richard. when he receive news that his sister

have this baby--Oliver--he insist upon put he into the tontine.

it be a sort of salve to Tom Gass's conscience; at least I think

so: what his sister and the poor baby want then be money--not to be

put into a useless tontine. ah, well, Rane have get on without any

one's assistance, and I dare say will flourish in the end."



Richard glance at his watch; twelve o'clock; and increase his pace;

a hundred and one thing be want he at the work. Mr. North

walk with he to the gate.



"yes, it's all for the good, Dick. and we'll get the wedding

comfortably over while madam's away."



"what have be her motive, sir, for oppose Bessy's engagement to

Rane?"



"motive!" return Mr. North. "do you see that white butterfly, Dick,

flutter about?--as good ask I what _its_ motive be, as ask I

madam's. I don't suppose she have any motive--except that she be give

to oppose we all."



Richard conclude it be so. something might lie also in Bessy's

patient excellence as a housekeeper; madam, ever selfish, do not

perhaps like to lose she.



as they reach the iron gate, Mrs. Cumberland pass, walk

slowly. she look very ill. Mr. North arrest she, and begin to

speak of the project marriage of Oliver and Bessy. Mrs. Cumberland

change colour and look almost frightened. unobservant Mr. North see

nothing. Richard do.



"have Oliver not tell you what's afoot?" say the former. "young man

be often shyer in these matter than woman."



"it's a very small income for they to begin upon," she observe

presently, when Mr. North have say his say--and Richard think he

detect some private objection to the union. "so very small for

Bessy--who have be use to Dallory Hall."



"it willn't always remain small," say Mr. North. "his practice will

increase when Alexander go; and he'll have other money, may be,

later. oh, they'll get on, Fanny. young couple like to be

sufficiently poor to make struggle upwards a pleasure. I dare say

you marry upon less."



"of course, if you be satisfied--it must be all right," murmur Mrs.

Cumberland. "you and Bessy."



she draw her veil over her grey face, say good-morning, and move

away. not in the direction of Dallory--as she be previously

walk--but back to the Ham. Mr. North turn into his ground again;

Richard go after Mrs. Cumberland.



"I beg your pardon," he say--he be not as familiar with she as his

father was--"will you allow I a word. you do not like this propose

marriage. have you anything to urge against it?"



"only for Bessy's sake. I be think of she."



"_why_ for Bessy's sake?"



there be some slight hesitation in Mrs. Cumberland's answer. she

appear to be draw her veil straight.



"their income will be so small. _I_ know what a small income be, and

therefore I feel for she."



"be that all your hesitation, Mrs. Cumberland?--the narrowness of the

income?"



"All."



"then I think, as my father say, you may safely leave the decision

with themselves. But--_was_ this all?" add Richard: for an idea to

the contrary have take hold of he. "you have no personal objection to

bessy?"



"certainly it be all," be Mrs. Cumberland's reply. "as to any

personal objection to Bessy, that I could never have. when Oliver

first tell I they be engage, I think how lucky he be to win

Bessy North; I wish they success with all my heart.



"forgive I, Mrs. Cumberland. thank you. good-morning."



Reassured, Richard North turn, and stride hastily away in the

direction of Dallory. he fancy she have hear Bessy would have no

fortune, and be feel disappointed on her son's account. it strike

he that he might as well confirm this; and he wheel round.



Mrs. Cumberland have go on and be already seat on the bench before

speak of, in the shady part of the road. Richard, in a few concise

word, enter into no detail of any sort, say to she that his

sister would have no marriage portion.



"_that_ I have long take as a matter of course; know what the

expense at the Hall must be," she answer with a friendly smile.

"Bessy be a fortune in herself; she would make a good wife to any man.

provide they have sufficient for comfort--and I hope Oliver will soon

be make that--they can be as happy without wealth as with it, if

your sister can only think so. have you--pardon I for recall what

must be an unpleasant topic, Richard--have you yet gain any clue to

the writer of that anonymous letter?"



"not any. it present mystery on all side."



"Mystery?"



"as it seem to I. go over the various circumstance, as I do on

occasion when I have a minute to myself, I try to fit the probability

into another, and I cannot compass it. we must trust to time, Mrs.

Cumberland. good-morning."



Richard raise his hat, and leave she. she sit on with her pain. Mrs.

Cumberland be as strictly rigid a woman in tenet as in temperament;

her code of morality be a severe one. over and over again have she

ask herself whether--it be of no use to mince the matter any

long--Oliver have or have not write that anonymous letter which have

kill Edmund North: and she could not answer the question. but, if he

have do it, why then surely he ought not to wed the sister. it would

be little less than sin.



since this secret trouble have be upon she, more than a month now,

her face have seem to assume a greyer tinge. how grey it look now,

as she sit on the bench, passer-by saw, and almost start. one of

"no."

"sometime in July, I think, or during August. I enter on my new post

"I be not talk of waiting--or of gold--or of risk, Oliver," she

it seem to I as though to-day I speak with a prophet's inspiration!

will rest upon it."



she be go. before Oliver Rane in his surprise could answer a word,

Mrs. Cumberland be go. pass swiftly out at the open window, she

step across the garden and the wire-fence, and so enter her own

home.









CHAPTER XI.

a quiet wedding.





apparently Dr. Rane find nothing on his conscience that could present

CHAPTER XII.

JELLY'S INDISCRETION.





the day go on, and Dr. Rane's house be be make ready for the

it be say in a previous chapter that Jelly be one of those who

"what?" cry molly, stare with all her eye.



but in a moment Jelly's sense have come back to she. she set herself

coolly to remedy the mischief.



"to think that my mind should have run off from the pudding-receipt to

that letter of poor Mr. Edmund's! it's your fault, Molly Green,

bother my wit out of I! where _do_ you pick up the paper? there.

answer that; and let's end it."



molly think it might be as well to end it; she be grow tired of

the play: besides, here be Turtle's man come into the room to

"I never have the receipt at all, Jelly, and it's not possible it could

Slowly, Jelly arrive at a conviction that Molly Green, in regard to

"then why on earth have you be keep up this farce with I? I'll

teach you manner with your better, girl."



"well, why do you set upon I?" be the good-humour answer.

"there's no such great treason in drop a plum-pudding-receipt,

even if I have do it--which I don't. I don't like to be brow-beat

for nothing: and it's not your place to do it, Jelly."

the sudden question--as Jelly acknowledge to herself afterwards--make

"amen!" be the mental response of Richard North.









CHAPTER XIII.

come HOME.





dash up to Dallory Hall, just a week and a day after the wedding,

PART the second.









CHAPTER I.

of what be, and of what might be.





Bessy Rane sit at the large window of her dining-room in the come

twilight. some twelve month have elapse since her marriage, and

summer be round again. her work have drop on her lap: it be that

of stitch some wristband for her husband: and she sit inhale the

sweet scent of the flower, and watch Jelly's movement in Mrs.

Cumberland's dining-room, face she. Jelly have a candle in her hand,

apparently search for something. Bessy lean forward to pluck a

sprig of sweet verbena, and sit on tranquilly.



at the table behind she sit Dr. Rane, write as fast as the wane

light would permit he. some unusual and peculiar symptom have

manifest themselves in a patient he have be recently attend, and

he be make they and the case into a paper for a medical

publication, in the hope that it would bring he back a remunerative

guinea or two.



"Oliver, I be sure you cann't see," say Bessy presently, look

round.



"it be almost blindman's holiday, dear. will you ring for the lamp?"



Mrs. Rane rise. but, instead of ring for the lamp, she go up to

he, and put her hand on his shoulder persuasively.



"take a quarter-of-an-hour's rest, Oliver. you will find all the

benefit of it; and it be not quite time to light the lamp. let we take

a stroll in the garden."



"you be obstruct what little light be leave, Bessy; stand

between I and the window."



"of course I be. I'm do it on purpose. come, Oliver! you ought to

know a great deal well than I do that it be bad to try the eye,

sir. _please_, Oliver."



yield to her entreaty, he push the paper from he with a sigh

of weariness, and they step from the window into the garden. Bessy

pass her hand within his arm; and, turn towards the more secluded

path, they begin to converse with one another in low tone.



many a twilight half-hour have they thus pace together of late,

talk together of what be and of what might be. the first year of

their marriage have not be one of success in a pecuniary point of

view; for Dr. Rane's practice do not improve. he earn barely
