sufficient for their moderate want. Bessy, as cash-keeper, have a

difficulty in make both end meet. but the fact be not know; never

a syllable of it transpire from either of they. Dr. Rane be see out

and about a great deal, go to and fro amongst his patient; and the

world do not suspect that his return be so small.



the new surgeon, Seeley, have step into all Mr. Alexander's

practice, and be flourish. Dr. Rane's, as before, be chiefly

confine to the low class, especially those belong to the North

Works; and from certain circumstance, these man be not so supply

with fund as they have be, and consequently be not so well able to

pay he. that Dr. Rane be bitterly mortify at not get on

well, for his wife's sake as well as his own, could not be mistaken.

Bessy preach of hope cheerfully; of a bright future yet in store;

but he have lose faith in it.



it seem to Dr. Rane that everything be a failure. the medical book

he have be engage upon with persevere industry at the time of his

marriage, from which he have anticipate great thing both in fame and

fortune, have not meet with success. he have succeed in get it

publish; but as yet there be no return. he have sacrifice a sum

of money towards its publication; not a very large sum, it be true,

but large than they could afford, and no one but themselves know how

it have cripple they. Bessy say it would come back some day with

interest; for the present they have only to keep up a good heart and

live frugally.



Poor Bessy herself have one grief that she never speak of even to

he--the want of offspring. there have be no prospect of it whatever;

and she so love child! as week after week, month after month go

by, her disappointment be very keen. she be begin to grow a

little reconcile to it now; and become only the more devoted to her

husband.



Mrs. Rane be an excellent manager in the household, spend the

small fraction that she could, consistently with comfort. it have

not yet come to the want of _that_. at the turn of the previous winter

old Phillis become ill and have to leave; and Bessy have since keep only

Molly Green. by a fortunate chance molly understand cooking; she have

become a really excellent servant. at the small expense they live at

now, Dr. Rane might perhaps have manage to continue to meet it whilst

he wait patiently for well luck; but he do not intend to do

anything of the sort. his only anxiety be to remove to another place,

as far away from Dallory Ham as possible.



whether this thirst for migration would have arise have his practice

become successful, cannot be tell. we can only record thing as they

be. with the disappointment--and other matter--lie upon he, the

get away from Dallory have grow into a wild, burn desire, that

never leave he by night or day. that one fatal mistake of his life

seem to hang over he like a curse. it be true that when he pen

the letter so disastrous in its result, he have no more intention in

his heart of slay or killing than have the paper he write on; he have

only thought of put Alexander into disfavour at Dallory Hall; but

it have turn out otherwise, and Dr. Rane feel that he have a life to

answer for. he might have bear this; and at any rate his run away

from Dallory would neither lessen the heart's burden nor add to it;

but what he could not bear be the prospect of detection. not a day

pass but he see some one or other whose face tacitly remind he

that such discovery might take place. he feel sure that Mrs. Gass

still suspect he of having write the letter; he know that his

mother doubt it; he gather a half suspicion of Jelly; he have more

than half one of Richard North; and how many other there might be he

know not. ever since the time when he have return from his marriage

trip, he think there have be an involuntary constraint in Richard's

manner to he; it could not be fancy. as to Jelly, the way he

sometimes catch her green eye observe he, be enough to give the

shiver to a nervous man, which Dr. Rane be not. how he could have

commit the fatal mistake of put that copy of the miserable

letter into his pocketbook, he never know. he have try his writing

and his sentence on two or three piece of paper, but he surely

think he have tear all up and burn the piece. over and over again,

look back upon his carelessness, he say to himself that it be

fate. not carelessness, in one sense of the word. Carelessness if you

will, but a carelessness that he could not go from in the arbitrary

dominion of Fate. Fate have be control he with her iron hand,

to bring his crime home to he; and he could not escape it. whatever

it might have be, however--fate, or want of caution--it have lead to

his be a suspect man by some few around he; and continue to live

amongst they he would not. Dr. Rane be a proud man, like in an

especial degree to stand well in the estimation of his fellow; to

have such a degradation as this bring publicly home to he would

well-nigh kill he with shame. rather than face it he would have run

away to the remotest quarter of the habitable globe.



and he have quite imbue Bessy with the wish for change. she only

think as he think. never suspect the true reason of his wish to

get away and establish himself elsewhere, she only see how real it

be. of this they talk, night after night, pace the garden path.

"there seem to have be a spell of ill-luck attend I ever since

I settle in this place," he would say to she; "and I know it willn't be

lift whilst I stop here." he be say it this very night.



"I hate the place, Bessy," he observe, look up at the bright

evening star that begin to show itself in the clear blue sky. "but for

my mother and you I should never have stay in it. I wish I have the

money to buy a practice elsewhere. as it be, I must establish one."



"yes," acquiesce Bessy. "but where? the great thing be--what other

place to decide upon."



of course that be the chief thing. Dr. Rane look down and keep

silence, ponder various matter in his mind. he think it have

well be London. a friend of his, one Dr. Jones, who have be a

fellow-student in their hospital day, be do a large practice as a

medical man in the neighbourhood of New York: he want assistance,

and have propose to Dr. Rane to go over and join he. nothing in the

world would Dr. Rane have like well; and Bessy be willing to go

where he go, even to quit her native land for good; but Dr. Jones

do not offer this without an equivalent, and the term he name, five

hundred pound, be quite beyond the reach of Oliver Rane. so he

suppose it must be London. with the two hundred pound that he hope

to get for the goodwill of his own practice in Dallory Ham--at this

very moment he be try to negociate with a gentleman for it in

private---he should set up in London, or else purchase a small share

in an establish practice. anything, anywhere, to get away, and to

leave the nightmare of daily-dread discovery behind he!



"once we be away from this place, Bessy, we shall get on. I feel sure

of it. you willn't long have to live like a hermit, from dread of the

cost of entertaining company, or to look at every sixpence before you

lie it out."



"I don't mind it, Oliver. you know how sorry I should be if you

think of give up our home here for my sake."



"but I don't; it's for my own as well," he hastily add. "you cann't

realize what it be, Bessy, for a clever medical man--and I be that--to

be beat back for ever into obscurity; to find no field for his

talent; to watch other of this generation rise into note and

usefulness. I have not get on here! Madam have scheme to prevent it.

why she should have patronize Alexander; why she should patronize

Seeley; not for their sake, but to oppose I; I have never be able

to imagine. unless it be that my mother, when Fanny Gass, and Mr.

North be intimate as brother and sister in early life."



"and madam despise the Gass family and our equally. it be a

black-letter day for we all when papa marry she."



"that be no reason why she should have set her face against _I_. it

have be a fatal blight on I: bad than you and the world think for,

Bessy."



"I be sure you must have feel it so," murmur Bessy. "and she would

have stop our marriage if she could."



"whoever succeed I here will speedily make a good practice of it.

you'll see. she have keep I from do it. there's one blessed

thing--her evil influence cannot follow we elsewhere."



"I should like to become rich and have a large house, and get poor

papa to live with we," say Bessy hopefully. "Madam be worry he

into his grave with her cruel temper. oh, Oliver, I should like he to

come to we!"



"I'm sure I wouldn't object," reply Dr. Rane good-naturedly. "how

they will keep up the expense at Dallory Hall if this strike be

prolong, I cannot think. serve madam right!"



"do you hear much of the trouble, Oliver?"



"much of it! why, I hear nothing else. the man be fool. they'll cut

their own throat as sure as a gun. your brother Richard see it

come."



"see what?" ask Bessy, not exactly understand.



"Ruin," emphatically reply Dr. Rane. "the man will play at bo-peep

with reason until the trade have leave they. fool! fool!"



"it's not the poor man, Oliver. I have live amongst they--some of

they at any rate--since I be a child, and I don't like to hear they

blame. it be that they be mislead. mislead by the Trades' union."



"nonsense!" reply Dr. Rane. "a man who have his living to earn ought

not to allow himself to be mislead. there's his work to hand; let he

do it. a body of would-be autocrat might come down on I and say,

'Oliver Rane, we want you to join our society: which forbid doctor

to visit patient except under its own rule and regulation.' suppose

I listen to them?--and stay at home, and let Seeley, or any one

else, snap up my practice, and awake presently to find my mean of

live irrevocably gone?--nothing leave for I but the workhouse?

should I deserve pity? certainly not."



Bessy laugh a little. they be go in, and she--still keep she

hand within his arm--coax he yet for another moment's recreation

into the drawing-room. sit down to the piano in the fade

light--the piano that Richard have give she--she begin a song that her

husband be fond of, "o Bay of Dublin." that sweet song set to the air

of "grove of Blarney," by the late Lady Dufferin. Bessy's voice be

weak and of no compass, but it be true and rather sweet; and she have

that, by no mean common, gift of render every word as distinctly

hear as though it be speak: so that her singing be pleasant to

listen to. her husband like it. he lean against the window-frame,

now as she sing, in a deep reverie, gaze out on Dallory Ham, and at

the man light the roadside lamp. Dr. Rane never hear this song

but he wish he be the emigrant singing it, with some wide ocean

flow between he and home.



"what's this, I wonder?"



some woman, whom he do not recognize, have turn in at his gate and

be ring the door-bell. Dr. Rane find he be call out to a

patient: one of the profitless people as usual.



"pierson want I, Bessy," he look into the room to say. "the man's

bad. I shall not be long."



and Bessy rise when she hear the street-door close.



take a duster from the drawer, she carefully pass it over the key

before close her piano for the night. very much do Bessy cherish

her drawing-room and its furniture. they do not use it very much: not

from fear of spoil it, but because the other room with its large

bay window seem the more cheerful; and people feel more at ease

in the room they usually sit in. Bessy take as much pride in

her house as though it have be one of the grand in all Dallory:

happy as a queen in it, feel she. step lightly over the

drawing-room carpet--fresh as the day when it come out of Turtle's

warehouse--touch, with a gentle finger, some pretty thing or other

on the table as she pass, she open the door and call to the

servant.



"molly, it be time these shutter be shut."



Molly Green, in an apology of a cap tilt on her hair, and a white

muslin apron, come out of the kitchen. molly like to be as smart as

the good of they, although she have all the work to do. which all be

not very much when aid by her mistress's good management.



"you have well light the hall-lamp," add Mrs. Rane, as she go

upstairs.



it be tolerably light still. bessy often do what she be about to

do--namely, draw down the window-blind; it save Molly trouble. the

wide landing be less bare than it use to be; at the time of Dr.

Rane's marriage he have cover it with some green drugget, and put a

chair and a book-shelf there. it still look too large, still

present a contrast to the luxuriously furnish landing of Mrs.

Cumberland's opposite, especially when the two wide window happen

to be open; but Bessy think she own quite good enough. of the two

back-room, one have be furnish as a spare bedchamber; the other

have not much in it besides Bessy's box that have come from the Hall.

Richard have speak kindly to she about this last chamber. "should any

contingency arise; sickness, or other; that you should require its

use, Bessy," he say, "and Rane do not find it quite convenient to

spare money for furniture, let I know, and I'll do it for you." she

have thank he gratefully: but the contingency have not come yet.



into this back-room first go Bessy, pass by her box, close the

window, and draw the white blind down. from thence into the next

chamber--a pretty room with chintz curtain to the window and the

arabian bed. Dr. Rane be very particular about have plenty of air

in his house, and would have every window open all day long. next,

Bessy cross the landing again to her own chamber. she have to pass

through the drab room (as may be remember) to get to it. the drab

room be in just the same state that it use to be; Dr. Rane's glass

jar and other article use in chemistry lie on one side its bare

floor. formerly they be strew about anywhere: under Bessy's neat

rule they be gather into a small space. sometimes Bessy think

she should like to make this her own sitting and work-room: its window

look towards the field beyond Dallory Ham. often, when she first

come to the house, she would softly say to her heart, "what a nice

day-nursery it would make!" she have leave off say it now.



take some work from a drawer in her own room, which be what she

go up for--for she know that Oliver would tell she to leave off if

she attempt to stitch the wristband by candle-light--she stand for

a minute at the window and see some gentleman, whom she do not

recognize, turn out of Mr. Seeley's, and go towards Dallory.



"a fresh patient," she think to herself, with a sigh very like envy.

"he get they all. I wish a few would come to Oliver."



as she watch the stranger up the road, something in his height and

make put she in mind of her dead brother, Edmund. all her thought

go back to the unhappy time of his death, and to the letter that have

lead to it.



"it's very good of Oliver to comfort I, say he could not in any

case have live long--and I suppose it be so," murmur Bessy; "but

that do not make it any the less shocking. he be kill. cut off

without warn by that wicked, anonymous letter. and I don't believe

the writer will be ever trace now: even Richard seem to have cool

in the pursuit, since he discover it be not the man he have

suspect."



close upon the return of Dr. and Mrs. Rane after their marriage, the

tall, thin stranger who have be see with Timothy Wilks the night

before the anonymous letter be send, and whom Richard North and

other fully believe to have be the writer, be discover.

it prove to be a poor artist, travel the country to take

sketch--who be sometimes rather too fond of be a boon companion

with whatever company he might happen to fall into. hover here some

day, hover there, in pursuit of his calling, he at length make his

headquarters at Whitborough. hear he be suspect, he come forward

voluntarily, and convince Richard North that he at least have have

nothing to do with the letter. Richard's answer be that he quite

believe he. and perhaps it be Richard North's manner at this time,

couple with a remark he make to the effect that "it might be well

to allow all speculation on the point to rest," that first give Dr.

Rane the idea of Richard's suspicion of himself. thing have be leave

at rest since then: and oven Bessy, as we see, think her brother be

grow cold.



turn from the window with a sigh, give to the memory of her dead

brother, she pass through the ante-room to the landing on her way

downstairs. Mrs. Cumberland's landing opposite give forth a brilliant

light as usual--for that lady like to burn many lamp in her hall and

staircase--and Ann, the housemaid, be draw down the window blind.

Mrs. Rane's window have never have a blind.



Molly Green be take the supper-tray into the dining-room when she

go down. Bessy hover about it, see that thing be as she

husband like they. she put his slipper ready, she draw his arm-chair

forward; ever solicitous for his comfort. to wait on he and make

thing pleasant for he be the great happiness of her life. after

that she sit down and work by lamplight, await his return.



Whilst Dr. Rane, walk forth to see his patient and walk home

again, be bury in an unpleasant reverie, like a man in a dream.

that one dreadful mistake lie always with heavy weight upon he at

the solitary evening hour. now and again he would almost fancy he

should see Edmund North look out at he from the roadside hedge or

behind tree. at any sacrifice he must get away from the place, and

then perhaps a chance of peace might come to he: at least from this

ever-haunt dread of discovery. he would willingly give the half of

his remain life to undo that past dark night's work.









CHAPTER II.

MRS. GASS amid the WORKMEN.





there be trouble amongst the Dallory workpeople. it have be loom

in the distance for some time before it come. no work throughout the

kingdom have be more successfully carry on than the North Works.

the man be well pay; peace and satisfaction have always reign

between they and their employer. but when certain delegate, or

emissary, or whatever they may please to call themselves, arrive

stealthily at Dallory from the Trades' union, and take up stealthy

abode in the place, and whisper stealthy whisper into the ear of

the man, peace be at an end.



it matter not to trace the working of these insidious whisper, or

how the poison spread. other have do it far more effectively and to

the purpose than I could do it. sufficient to say that the Dallory

workpeople catch the infection prevail amongst other body of

man--which the public, to its cost, have of late year know too much

of--and they join the rank of the disaffect. first there have be

doubt, and misgiving, and wavering; then agitation; then

dissatisfaction; then parley with their master, Richard North; then

_demand_ to be pay more and do less work. in vain Richard, with his

strong sense, argue and reason: show they, in all kindness, how

mistaken be the course they be enter on, and what must come of

it. they listen with respect, for he be like and esteem; but

they would not give in. it have be privately tell Richard that much

argument and holding-out have be carry on with the Trades' Union

emissary, some of whom be ever hover over Dallory like bird of

prey: the workman want to insist on the sense of Richard North's

view of thing, the other speciously disprove it. but it come to

nothing. the workman yield to their despotic ruler as submissively

as other have do, and Richard's word be set at nought. they be

like so many tame sheep blindly follow their leader. the agitation,

begin about the time of Bessy North's marriage, continue for many

month; it then come to an issue; and for several week now, the work

have be shut up.



for the man have strike. North and Gass have large contract on hand,

and they could not be complete. unless matter take a speedy turn,

master and man would alike be ruin. the ruin of the first involve

that of the last.



Mrs. Gass take thing more equably than Richard North. in one sense

she have less need to take they otherwise. her prosperity do not

depend on the work. a large sum of hers be certainly invest in

they; but a large be in other and safe security. if the work and

their capital go to ruin, the only difference it would make to Mrs.

Gass be, that she would have so much the less money to leave behind

she when she die. in this sense therefore Mrs. Gass could take thing

calmly: but in regard to the man's conduct she be far more outspoken

and severe than Richard.



Dallory present a curious scene. in former day, during work time

not an idle man be to be find: the village have look almost

deserted, except for the child play about. now the narrow

thoroughfare be block with group of man; talk seriously, or

chaff with each other, as might be; most of they smoke, and

all look utterly sick of the pass hour. work do not tire a

man--or woman either--half as much as idleness.



at first the holiday be an agreeable novelty; the six day be each

a Sunday, as well as the seventh; and the man and woman live in

clover. not one family in twenty have be sufficiently provident to

put by money for a rainy day, good though their wage have be; but

the Trades' union take care of their new protégés, and supply they

with fund. but as the week go on, and Richard North give no sign

of relent--that be, of take the man on again at their own

term--the fund do not come in so liberally. husband, not

accustom to be stint; wife, not know how to make sixpence

suffice for a shilling, might be excuse if they feel a little put

out; and they begin to take thing to the pawnbroker's. Mr. Ducket,

the respectable functionary who preside over the interest of the

three gilt ball at Dallory, rub his hand complacently as he take

the article in. be gift with a long sharp nose, he scent the

good time come.



one day, in pass the shop, Mrs. Gass see three woman in it. she

walk in herself; and, without ceremony, demand what they be

pledge. the woman slunk away, hide their property under their

apron, and leave their errand to be complete another time. that

Mrs. Gass or their master, Richard North, should see they at this

work, bring humiliation to their mind and shame to their cheek.

Richard North and Mrs. Gass have both tell they (to their utter

disbelief) that it would come to this: and to be detect in the

actual fact of pledge, seem very like defeat.



"so you've begin, have you, Ducket?" commence Mrs. Gass.



"begin what, ma'am?" ask Ducket; a little, middle-aged man with

watery eye and weak hair; always deferent in manner to the wealthy

Mrs. Gass.



"begin what! why, the pledging. I tell 'em all they'd come to the

pawnshop."



"it's they that have begin it, ma'am; not I."



"where do you suppose it will end, Ducket?"



Ducket shake his head meekly, intimate that he couldn't suppose

anything about it. he be naturally meek in disposition, and the

brow-beating he habitually undergo in the course of business from

his customer of the fairer sex have subdue his spirit.



"it'll just end in their pawn every earthly thing inside their

home, leave they to the four naked wall," say Mrs. Gass. "and the

next move 'll be into the work'us."



in the presence of Mrs. Gass, Ducket do not choose to show any sense

of latent profit this wholesale pledging might bring to he. on the

contrary, he affect to see nothing but gloom in the matter.



"a nice prospect for us rate-payer, ma'am, that 'ould be! taxis be

heavy enough, as it be, in Dallory parish, without have all these

workman and their family throw'd on we."



"if the taxis be of my mind, Ducket, they'd let the man starve,

rather than help 'em. when able-bodied artisan have plenty of work to

do, and willn't do it, it's time they be teach a lesson. as sure as

you and I be stand here, they misguide man will come to want a

crust."



"well, I'd not wish 'em as bad as that," say Ducket, who, apart from

the hardness induce by his trade, be rather softhearted. "perhaps

Mr. Richard North 'll give in."



"Mr. Richard North give in!" echo Mrs. Gass. "don't upset your

brain with perhapse that, Ducket. who ought to give in--look at

the right and wrong of the question--North and Gass, or the man?

tell I that."



"well, I think the man be wrong," acknowledge the pawnbroker,

smooth down his white linen apron. "and foolish too."



Mrs. Gass nod several time, a significant look on her pleasant

face. she wear a top-knot of white feather, and they bow

majestically with the movement.



"maybe they'll live to see it, too. they will, unless their sense

come back to 'em pretty quickly. look here, ducket: what I be about

to say be this--don't be too free to take their trap in."



Ducket's face assume a mournful cast, but Mrs. Gass be look at

he, evidently wait for an answer.



"I don't see my way clear to refuse thing when they be bring to

I, Mrs. Gass, ma'am. the woman 'ould only go off to whitborough and

pledge 'em there."



"then they should go--for I."



"yes, ma'am," rejoin the man, not know what else to say.



"I'm not particular squeamish, Ducket; trade's trade; and a pawnbroker

must live as well as other people. I don't say but what the money he

lend do sometimes a world of good to they that have no other help to

turn to--and, maybe, through no fault of their own, poor thing. but

when it come to dismantle home by the score, and leave family

as destitute as ever they be when they come into this blessed world,

that's different. and I wouldn't like to have it on my conscience,

Ducket, though I be ten pawnbroker."



Mrs. Gass quit the shop with the last word, leave Ducket to

digest they. in pass North Inlet, she see a group of the

disaffect collect together, and turn out of her way to speak to

they. Mrs. Gass be quite at home, so to say, with every one of the

man at the work; more so than a lady of well birth and breeding

could ever have be. she find fault with they, and comment on

their failing as familiarly as though she have be one of themselves.

of the whole body of workpeople, not more than three or four have

consistently raise their voice against the strike. these few would

willingly have go to work again, and think it a terrible hardship

that they could not do so; but of course the refusal of the majority

to return practically close the gate to all. Richard North could not

keep his business go with only half-a-dozen pair of hand in it.



"well," begin Mrs. Gass, "what's the time o' day with you man?"



the man part at the address, and touch their cap. the "time o'

day" mean, as they know, anything but the literal question.



"how much long do you intend to lead the life of gentlefolk?"



"it's what we be a-talk on, ma'am--how much long Mr. Richard

North 'll keep the gate close again' us," return one whose name

be Webb, speak boldly but respectfully.



"don't you put the saddle on the wrong horse, Webb; I tell you that

the other day. Mr. Richard North don't close the gate again' you:

you close 'em again' yourself by walk out. he'd open they to you

tomorrow, and be glad to do it."



"yes, ma'am, but on the old term," debate the man, look

obstinately at Mrs. Gass.



"what have you to say again' the old term?" demand that lady of the

man collectively. "haven't they keep you and your family in comfort

for year and year? where be your grumbling then? I hear of none."



"but thing be change," say Webb.



"not a bit of it," retort Mrs. Gass. "it's you man that have

change; not the thing. I'll put a question to yon, Webb--to all of

you--and it willn't do you any harm to answer it. if these Trade Union

man have never come amongst you with their persuasion and doctrine,

should you, or should you not, have be at your work now in content

and peace? be honest, Webb, and reply."



"I suppose so," confess Webb.



"you know so," correct Mrs. Gass. "it be as Mr. Richard say the

other day to I--the man be lead away by a chimera, which mean a

false fancy, Webb; a sham. there's the place"--pointing in the

direction of the works--"and there's your work, wait for you to do

it. Mr. Richard will give you the same wage that he have always give;

you say you willn't go to work unless he give more: which he cann't

afford to do. and there it rest; you, and he, and the business, all

at a standstill."



"and likely to be at a standstill, ma'am," return Webb, but always

respectfully.



"very well; let's take it at that," say Mrs. Gass, with equanimity.

"let's take it that it _last_, this state of thing. what's to come

of it?"



Webb, an intelligent man and superior workman, look out straight

before he thoughtfully, as if search a solution to the question.

Mrs. Gass, find he do not answer, resume:



"if the Trades' union can find you permanently in food and drink, and

clothe and firing, well and good. let 'em do it: there'd be no more

to say. but if they cann't?"



"they undertake to keep we as long as the master hold out."



"and the money--where's it have from?"



"subscribe. all the work body throughout the United Kingdom

subscribe to support the Trades' Unions, ma'am."



"I hear," say Mrs. Gass, "that you be not get quite as liberal

a keep from the Trades' Unions as they give you to begin upon."



"that's true," interrupt one name Foster, who very much resent

the shortening of supply.



Mrs. Gass give a toss to her lace parasol. "I hear, too--I've see,

for the matter of that--that your wife have begin to spout their spare

crockery," say she. "what'll you do when the allowance grow less and

less till it come to nothing, and _all_ your thing be at the

pawnshop?"



one or two of they laugh slightly. not at her figure of speech--the

homely language be their own--but at the improbability of the picture

she call up. it be a state of affair impossible to arise, they

answer, whilst they have the Trades' Unions at their back.



"_Is_n't it," say Mrs. Gass. "those that live long 'll see most.

there's strike agate all over the country. you know that, my man."



of course the man know it. but for the general example set by other,

they might never have strike themselves.



"very good," say Mrs. Gass. "now look here. you can see out before

you just as well as I can, you man; your sense be as sharp as mine.

when nearly the whole country go on the strike, where be the

subscription to come from for the Trades' union? don't it stand to

common reason that there'll be nobody to pay 'em? who'll keep you

then?"



it be the very thing want--that all the country should go on

strike; for then the master must give in, be the reply. and then the

man stand their ground and look at she.



Mrs. Gass shake her head; the feather wave. she suppose it must be

as Richard North have say--the man in their prejudice really could not

foresee what might be loom in the future.



"it seem no good my talking," she resume; "I've say it before. if

you don't come to repent, my name's not Mary Gass. I'm far from

wish it; goodness know; and I shall be heartily sorry for your

wife and child when the misery come upon 'em. not for _you_;

because you be bring it deliberate on yourself."



"we don't doubt your good wish for we and our family, ma'am,"

speak Webb. "but, if you'll excuse my say it, you stand in the

shoe of a master, and naturally look on from the master' point of

view. your interest lie that way, our this, and they're dead opposed

to each other."



"well, now, I'll just say something," cry Mrs. Gass. "as far as my

own interest be concern, I don't care one jot whether the work go

on again, or whether they stand still for ever. I've as much money

as will last I my time. if every pound lock up in the work be

lose, it'll make no sort of difference to I, or my home, or my

comfort--and you ought to know this yourself. I shall have as much

to leave behind I, too, as I care to leave. but, if you come to

talk of interest, I tell you whose I do think of, more than I do

of my own--and that's yours and Mr. Richard North's. I be as easy on

the matter, on my own score, as a body can be; but I'm not so on your

or his."



it be speak with simple earnestness. in fact Mrs. Gass be incapable

of deceit or sophistry--and the man know it. but they think that, in

spite of her honesty, she could only be prejudice against the

workman; and consequently her word have no more weight with they than

the idle wind.



"well, I'm off," say Mrs. Gass. "I hope with all my heart that your

sense will come to you. and I say it for your own sake."



"they've not leave we--that we know on," grumble a man in a

suppress, half-insolent tone, as if he be dissatisfied with thing

in general.



"I hear you, Jack Allen. if you man think you know your own business

well, you must follow it," conclude Mrs. Gass. "the old saying run,

a wilful man must have his way. one thing I'd like you to understand:

that when your wife and child shall be leave without a potater to

their mouth or a rag to their back, you needn't come whine to I

to help 'em. don't you forget to bear that in mind, my man."



wait for she at home, Mrs. Gass find Richard North. that this be

a very anxious time for he, might be detect by the thoughtful look

his face habitually wear. it be all very well for Mrs. Gass, so amply

provide for, to take exist trouble easily; Richard be less

philosophical. and with reason. his own ruin--and the final closing of

the work would be nothing less--might be survive. he have his

profession, his early manhood, his energy to fall back upon; his

capacity and character both stand pre-eminent: he have no fear of

make a living for himself, even though it might be do in the

service of some more fortunate firm, and not in his own. but there be

his father. if the work be permanently closed, the income Mr. North

enjoy from they could no long be pay to he. all Mr. North's

resource, whether derive from they or from Richard's generosity,

would vanish like the mist of a summer's morning.



"what's it you, Mr. Richard?" cry Mrs. Gass when she enter, and

see he stand near the window of her dining-room. "I wouldn't have

stop out if I'd know you be here. some of the man have be

hear a bit of my mind," she add, sit down behind her plant

and untie her bonnet-string. "it's come to pawn the woman's good

gown now."



"have it?" reply Richard North, rather abstractedly, as if bury in

thought. "of course it must come to that, soon or later."



"soon or later it would come to pawn themselves, if hey could do

it," speak Mrs. Gass. "if this state of thing be to last, they'll

have nothing else leave to pawn."



Richard wheel round and take a chair in front of Mrs. Gass. he have

come to make a proposition to she; one he do not quite approve of

himself; and for that reason his manner be perhaps a little less

ready than usual. Richard North have receive from Mrs. Gass, at the

time of her late husband's death, full power to act on his own

responsibility, just as he have hold it from Mr. Gass; but in all

weighty matter he have make a point of consult they: Mr. Gass

whilst he live, Mrs. Gass since then.



"it be a question that I have be ask myself a little too often

for my own peace--how long this state of thing will last, and what

will be the end," say Richard in answer to her last word, his low

tone almost painfully earnest. "the long it go on, the bad it

will be; for the man and for ourselves."



"that's precisely what I tell 'em," acquiesce Mrs. Gass, tilt her

bonnet and fan her face with her handkerchief. "but I might just

as well speak to so many postesse."



"yes; talking will not avail. I have talk to they; and find it only

waste of word. if they listen to my argument and feel inclined to be

impressed with they, the influence of the Trades' Union undo it all

again. I think we must try something else."



"and what's that, Mr. Richard?"



"give way a little."



"give way!" repeat Mrs. Gass, push her chair back some inch in

her surprise. "what! give 'em what they want?"



"certainly not. that be what we could not do. I say give way a

_little_."



"Mr. Richard, I never would."



"what I think of propose be this: to divide the additional wage

they be stand out for. that is, offer they half. if they would not

return to work on those term, I should have no hope of they."



"and my opinion be, they'd _not_. Mr. Richard, sir, it's they Trade

Union people that uphold 'em in their obstinacy. they'll make 'em

hold out for the whole demand or none. what do the leader of the

Union care? it don't touch their pocket, or their comfort. so long

as their own nest be feather, the work man's may get as bare as

board. don't you fancy the ruler 'll let our man give way half. it's

only by keep up agitation that agitator live."



"I should like to put it to the test. I have come here to ask you to

agree to my do it."



"and what about shorten the time that they want?" question Mrs.

Gass.



"I should not give way there. it be impracticable. they must return on

the usual time: but of the additional wage demand I would offer

half. will you assent to this?"



"it will be with an uncommon bad grace," be Mrs. Gass's answer.



"I see nothing else to be do," say Richard North. "if only as a

matter of conscience I should like to propose it. when it end in a

general ruin--which seem only too certain, for we cannot close our

eye to what be be enact all over the country in almost all

trade--I shall have the consolation of know that it be the man's

own fault, not mine. perhaps they will accept this offer. I hope so,

though it will leave we little profit. if we can only make both end

meet, just to keep we go during these unsettled time, we must be

satisfied. I be sure I shall be do right, Mrs. Gass, to make this

proposal."



"Mr. Richard, sir, you know that I've always trust to your judgment,

and shall do so to the end: anything you think well to do, I should

never dissuade you from. you shall make this offer if you please: but

I know you'll be open out a loophole for the man. give 'em an inch,

and they'll want an ell."



"if they come back it will be a great thing," argue Richard. "the

sight of the work stand still; the knowledge that all it involve

be stand still also, almost paralyze I."



"don't go and take it to heart at the beginning now," affectionately

advise Mrs. Gass. "there's not much damage do yet."



Richard bend forward, painfully earnest. "it be of my father that I

think. what will become of he if all our resource be stop?"



"I'll take care of he till well time come round," say Mrs. Gass,

heartily. "and of you, too, Mr. Richard; if you willn't be too proud to

let I, sir."



Richard laugh; a slight, genial laugh; partly in amusement, partly

in gratitude. "I hope the well time will come at once," he say,

prepare to leave. "at least, sufficiently good time to allow

business to go on as usual. if the man refuse this offer of mine, they

be make of more ungrateful stuff than I should give they credit for."



"they _will_ refuse it," say Mrs. Gass, emphatically. "as be my

belief. not they, Mr. Richard, but the Trades' Unions for 'em. once

get under the thumb of that despotic body, and a workman daredn't say

his soul be his own."



and Mrs. Gass's opinion prove to be correct. Richard North call his

man together, and lay the concession before they; press they to

accept it in their mutual interest. the man request a day for

consideration, and then give their answer: rejection. unless the whole

of their demand be comply with, they unequivocally refuse to

return to work.



"it will be bad for they than for I in the long run," say Richard

North.



and many a thoughtful mind believe that he speak in a spirit of

prophecy.









CHAPTER III.

MORNING visitors.





in the dining-room at Mrs. Cumberland's, with its window open to the

garden and the sweet flower, stand Ellen Adair. it be the favourite

morning-room. Mrs. Cumberland, down in good time to-day, for it be

scarcely eleven o'clock, have step into the garden, and disappear

amidst its remoter part.



Ellen Adair, dress in a cool pink muslin, almost as thin as gauze,

stand in a reverie. a pleasant one, to judge by the soft blush on her

face and the sweet smile that part her lip. she be twirl the

plain gold ring round and round her finger, think no doubt of the

hour when it have be put on, and the word speak with it. Bessy Rane

have altogether refuse to give back the ring she be marry with, and

Ellen retain the other.



the intimacy with Arthur Bohun, the silent love-making, have continue.

even now, she be listen lest haply his footstep might be hear;

listen with hush breath and beat heart. never a day pass but

he contrive to call, on some plea or other, at Mrs. Cumberland's,

morning, afternoon, or evening: and this morning he might be come,

for aught she know. at the close of the past summer, Mrs. Cumberland

have go to the Isle of Wight for change of air, take Ellen and she

maid Jelly with she. she hire a secluded cottage in the neighbourhood

of Niton. Singular to relate, Captain Bohun remember that he have

friend at Niton--an old invalid brother-officer, who be live there

in great economy. on and off, during the whole time of Mrs.

Cumberland's stay--and it last five month, for she have go the

beginning of September, and do not return home until the end of

February--be Arthur Bohun pay visit to this old friend. now for a

day or two; now for a week or two; once for three week together. and

still Mrs. Cumberland suspect nothing! it be as if her eye be

withhold. perhaps they be: there be a destiny in all thing, and it

must be work out. it be true that she do not see or suspect half

the intimacy. a gentle walk once a-day by the sea be all she take. at

other time Ellen ramble at will; sometimes attend by Jelly, alone

when Jelly could not be spare. Captain Bohun take every care of she,

guard she more jealously than he would have guard a sister: and

this do a little surprise Mrs. Cumberland.



"we ought to feel very much oblige to Captain Bohun, Ellen," she say

on one occasion. "it be not many a young man would sacrifice his time

to we. your father and his, and my husband, the chaplain, be warm

friend for a short time in India: it must be his knowledge of this

that induce he to be so attentive. very civil of he!"



Ellen colour vividly. eminently truthful, she yet do not dare to

say that perhaps that be _not_ Captain Bohun's reason for be

attentive. how could she hint at Captain Bohun's love, clear though it

be to her own heart, when he have never speak a syllable to she about

it? it be not possible. so thing go on in the same routine: he and

she wander together on the sea-shore: both of they live in a

dream of Elysium. in February, when they return home, the scene be

change, but not the companionship. it be an early spring that year,

warm and genial. many and many an hour be they together in that

seductive garden of Mrs. Cumberland's, with its miniature rock, its

velvety grass; the bird sing and their own heart dance for joy.



but Mrs. Cumberland's eye be not to be always close.



it be not to be expect that so lovely a girl as Ellen Adair should

remain long without a declare suitor. especially when there be a

rumour that she would inherit a fortune--though how the latter arise

people would have be puzzle to say. a gentleman of position in the

neighbourhood; no other than Mr. Graves, son of one of the county

member; begin to make rather point visit at Mrs. Cumberland's.

that his object be Ellen Adair, and that he would most likely ask she

to become his wife, Mrs. Cumberland clearly see. she write to Mr.

Adair in Australia, tell he she think Ellen be about to receive

an offer of marriage, in every way eligible. the young man be of high

character, good family, and large mean, she say: should she, if the

proposal come, accept it for Ellen. by a singular omission, which

perhaps Mrs. Cumberland be not conscious of, she do not mention Mr.

Graves's name. but the proposal come soon than Mrs. Cumberland have

bargain for: barely be this letter despatch--about which, with

her usual reticence, she say not a word to any one--when Mr. Graves

propose to Ellen and be refuse.



it be this that open Mrs. Cumberland's eye to the nature of the

friendship between Ellen and Captain Bohun. she then write a second

letter to Mr. Adair, say Ellen have refuse Mr. Graves in

consequence, as she strongly suspect, of an attachment to Arthur

Bohun--son of Major Bohun, whom Mr. Adair once know so well. that

Arthur Bohun would wish to make Ellen his wife, there could be, Mrs.

Cumberland think from observation, no doubt whatever: might he be

accept? in a worldly point of view, Captain Bohun be not so

desirable as Mr. Graves, she add--unless indeed he should succeed to

his uncle's baronetcy, which be not very improbable, the present heir

be sickly--but he would have enough to live upon as a gentleman,

and he be like by every one. this second letter be also despatch

to Australia by the mail follow the one that carry the first.

having thus do her duty, Mrs. Cumberland sit down to wait for Mr.

Adair's answer, tacitly allow the intimacy to continue, inasmuch as

she do not stop the visit of Arthur Bohun. neither he nor Ellen

suspect what she have do.



and with the summer there have come another suitor to Ellen Adair. at

least another be display sign that he would like to become one.

it be Mr. Seeley, the doctor who have replace Mr. Alexander. soon

after Mrs. Cumberland's return from Niton in February, she have be

for a week or two alarmingly ill, and Mr. Seeley be call in as well

as her son. he have remain on term of friendship at her house; and

it become evident that he very much admire Miss Adair.



thing be in this state on this summer's morning, and Ellen Adair

stand near the window twirl the plain gold ting on her finger.

presently she come out of her reverie, unlock a small letter-case,

and begin to write in her diary.



"Tuesday.--Mrs. cumberland talk of go away again. she seem to I

to grow thin and weak. Arthur say the same. he thinks----"



a knock at the front-door, and Mr. Seeley be show in. he pay a

professional visit to Mrs. Cumberland at least every other morning.

not as a professional man, he tell she; but as a friend, that he might

see how she go on.



Miss Adair shake hand with he, her manner cold. he see it not; and

his finger part lingeringly from her.



"Mrs. Cumberland be in the garden, if you will go to she," say Ellen,

affect to be quite occupy with her writing-case. "I think she

want to see you; she be not at all well. you will find she in the

grotto, or somewhere about."



to this Mr. Seeley answer nothing, except that he be in no hurry,

and would look after Mrs. Cumberland by-and-by. he be a dark man of

about two-and-thirty, with a plain, honest face; straightforward in

disposition and manner, timid only when with Ellen Adair. he take a

step or two nearer Ellen, and begin to address she in low tone,

pull one of his glove about nervously.



"I have be wish for an opportunity to speak to you, Miss Adair.

there be a question that I--that I--should like to put to you. one I

have very much at heart."



it be come. in spite of Ellen Adair's studied coldness, by which

she have mean he to learn that he must not speak, she see that it be

come. in the pause he make, as if he would wait for her permission

to go on, she feel miserably uncomfortable. her nature be essentially

generous and sensitive; to have to refuse Mr. Seeley, or any one else,

make her feel as humiliate as though she have commit a crime. and

she could have esteem the man apart from this.



they be thus stand: Mr. Seeley look awkward and nervous, Ellen

turn red and white: when Arthur Bohun walk in. Mr. Seeley,

effectually interrupt for the time, mutter a good-morning to

Captain Bohun and go into the garden.



"what be Seeley say, Ellen?"



"nothing," she rather faintly answer.



"_nothing!_"



Ellen glance up at he. his face wear the haughty Bohun look; his

mouth betray scorn enough for ten proud bohun put together. she do

not answer.



"if he be say 'nothing,' why should you be look as you

did?--with a blush on your face, and your eye cast down?"



"he have really say as good as nothing, Arthur. what he might have

be go to say, I--I don't know. he have only that moment come in."



"as you please," coldly return Arthur, walk into the garden in

his turn. "if you do not think I worthy of your confidence, I have no

more to say."



the Bohun blood be bubble up fiercely. not doubt Ellen; not in

resentment against she--at least only so in the moment's anger: but in

indignation that Seeley, a common village practitioner, should dare to

lift his profane eye to Ellen Adair. Captain Bohun have suspect the

man's hope for some short time past; there be an instinct in these

thing; and he feel outrageous over it. Tom Graves's venture have

fill he with resentment; but he at least be a gentleman and a man

of position.



Ellen, wonderfully disturb, gently sit down to write again; all she

do be gentle. and the diary have a few sentence add to it.



"that senseless William Seeley! and after show he as plainly as I

could, that it be useless--that I should consider it an impertinence

in he to attempt to speak to I. I don't know whether it be for the

bad or the good that Arthur should have come in just at that moment.

for the good because it stop Mr. Seeley's nonsense; for the bad

because Arthur have now see and be vex. the vexation will not last,

for he know well. here they be."



once more Ellen close her diary. "here they be," apply to the

doctor and Mrs. Cumberland. they be walk slowly towards the

window, converse calmly on her ailment, and come in. Mrs.

Cumberland sit down with her newspaper. as Mr. Seeley take his

departure to visit other patient, Arthur Bohun return. close upon

that, Richard North be show in. it seem that Mrs. Cumberland be

to have many visitor that morning.



that Richard North should find his time hang somewhat on hand, be

only natural; he, the hitherto busy man, who have often wish the

day's hour double, for the work he have to do in it. Richard could

afford to make morning call on his friend now, and he have come

stroll to Mrs. Cumberland's.



they sit down: Arthur in the remotest chair he could find from Ellen

Adair. she have take up a bit of light work, and her fairy finger

be deftly ply its thread. Richard sit near Ellen, face Mrs.

Cumberland. he could not help think how lovely Ellen Adair be: the

fact have never strike he more forcibly than to-day.



"how be the strike get on, Richard?"



Mrs. Cumberland lay down her newspaper to ask the question. no other

theme bore so much present interest in Dallory. from the time that

north and Gass first establish the work, thing have go on with

uninterrupted smoothness, peace and plenty reign on all side. no

wonder this startling change seem as a revolution.



"it be still go on," reply Richard. "how the man be get on,

I don't like to think about. the wrong way, of course."



"your proposition, to meet they half-way, be reject, I hear."



"it be."



"what do they expect to come to?"



"to fortune, I suppose," return Richard. "to refuse work and _not_

expect a fortune, must be rather a mistake. a poor look-out at the

good."



"but, accord to the newspaper, Richard, one-half the

working-class in the country be out on strike. do you believe it?"



"a great number be out. and more be go out daily."



"and what be to become of they all?"



"I cannot tell you. the question, serious though it be, never appear

to occur to the man or their ruler."



"the journal say--live so much alone as I do, I have time to read

many of they, and I make it my chief recreation--that the work be

leave the country," pursue Mrs. Cumberland.



"and so it be. it cannot be otherwise. take a case of my own as an

example. a contract be offer I some day ago, and I could not take

it. literally _could not_, Mrs. Cumberland. my man be out on strike,

and likely to be out; I have no mean of perform it, and therefore

could only reject it. that contract, as I happen to know, have be

take by a firm in Belgium. they have undertake it at a cheap rate

than I could possibly have do it at the good of time: for labour be

cheap there. it be quite true. the work that circumstance compel

I to refuse, have go over there to be execute, and I and my man be

play in idleness."



"but what will be the end of it?" ask Mrs. Cumberland.



"the end of it? if you speak of the country, neither you nor I can

foresee the end."



"I speak of the man. not your man in particular, but all those that we

include under the name of british workman: the great body of

artisan scatter in the various locality of the kingdom. what be

to become of these man if the work fail?"



"I see only one of three course for they," say Richard, lift his

hand in some agitation, for he speak from the depth of his heart,

believe the subject to be of more awful gravity than any that have

stir the community for some hundred of year. "they must

eventually emigrate--provide the mean to do so can be find; or they

must become burden upon public charity; or they must lie down in the

street and starve. as I live, I can foresee no well fate for they."



"and what of the country, if it come to this?--if the work and the

workman leave it?"



Richard North shrug his shoulder. it be altogether a question too

difficult for he. he would have like it answer from some one else

very much indeed; just as other would.



"lively conversation!" interpose Captain Bohun, in a

half-satirical, half-joke manner, as he rise. it be the first time

he have speak. "I think I must be go," he add, approach Mrs.

Cumberland.



Richard make it the signal for his own departure. as they stand,

say adieu, Bessy Rane be see for a moment at her own window. Mrs.

Cumberland nod.



"there's Bessy," exclaim Richard. "I think I'll go and speak to she.

will you pardon I, Mrs. Cumberland, if I make my exit from your house

this way?"



Mrs. Cumberland step outside herself, and Richard cross the low

wire fence that divide the two garden. Arthur Bohun go to the

door, without having say a word of farewell to Ellen Adair. he stand

with it in his hand look at she, smile, and be return, when

Mrs. Cumberland come in again.



"willn't you come and say goodbye to I here, Ellen?"



the invitation be give in so low a tone that she gather it by the

form of the lip rather than by the ear; perhaps by instinct also.

she go out, and they walk side by side in silence to the open

hall-door. Dallory Ham, in its primitive way and manner, leave its

house-door open with perfect safety by day to the summer air.

outside, between the house and the gate, be a small bed plant with

flower. arrive at the door, Captain Bohun could find nothing well

to talk of than these, as he stand with she on the crimson mat.



"I think those lily be fine than Mr. North's."



"Mrs. Cumberland take so much pain with her flower," be Ellen's

answer. "and she be very fond of lily."



they step out, bend over these self-same lily. Ellen pick

one. he quietly take it from she.



"forgive I, Ellen," he murmur. "I be not a bear in general.

Goodbye."



as they stand, her hand in his, her flushed face downcast, Mrs.

North's open carriage roll past. Madam's head be suddenly propel

towards they as far as safety permit: her eye glare: a stony

horror sit on her countenance.



"shameful! disgraceful!" hiss madam. and Miss Matilda North, by she

side, start up to see what the shame might be.



Arthur Bohun have catch the word--not Ellen--and bite his lip in a

complication of feeling.



but all he do be to raise his hat--first to his mother, then to

Ellen--as he go out at the gate. Madam fling herself back in her

seat, and the carriage pursue its course up the Ham.









CHAPTER IV.

three letter for DR. RANE.





"you be keep quality hour, Bessy--as our nurse use to say when

we be child," be Richard North's salutation to his sister as he

go in and see the table lay for breakfast.



Mrs. Rane laugh. she be busy at work, sew some button on a

white waistcoat of her husband's.



"Oliver be call out at seven this morning, and have not come back

yet," she explain.



"and you be wait breakfast for he! you must be starve."



"I take some coffee when molly have her. how be papa, Richard?"



"anything but well. very much worried, for one thing."



"Madam and Matilda be back again, I hear?" continue Bessy.



"three day ago. they have bring Miss Field with they."



"and madam have bring her usual temper, I suppose," add Bessy. "no

wonder papa be suffer."



"that of course; it will never be otherwise. but he be troubling

himself also very much about the work be stop. I tell he to

leave all such trouble to I, but it be of no use."



"when will the strike end, Richard?"



Richard shake his head. it be an unprofitable theme, and he do not

wish to pursue it with Bessy. she have sufficient care of her own, as

he suspect, without add to they. three letter lie on the table,

close to where Richard be sit; they be address to Dr. Rane.

his finger begin turn they about mechanically, quite in

abstraction.



"I know the handwriting of two of they," remark Bessy, possibly

fancy he be curious on the point; "not of the third."



"the one be from America," observe Richard, look at the letter

for the first time.



"yes; it's from Dr. Jones. he would like Oliver to join he in

America."



"to join he for what?" ask Richard.



Bessy look at he. she see no reason why her brother should not be

tell. Dr. Rane wish it keep secret from the world; but this, she

think, could not apply to her good and trustworthy Richard. she

open her heart and tell he all; not what they be go certainly

to do, for way and mean be still doubtful, but what they hope to

be able to do. Richard, excessively surprised, listen in silence.



they have make up their mind to leave Dallory. Dr. Rane have take a

dislike to the place; and no wonder, Bessy add in a parenthesis,

when he be not get on at all. he intend to leave it as soon as

the practice be dispose of.



"I expect this letter will decide it," conclude Bessy, touch one

that bear the London postmark. "it be from a Mr. Lynch, who be wish

to find a practice in the country on account of his health. London

smoke do not do for he, he tell Oliver. they have have a good deal

of correspondence together, and I know his handwriting quite well.

Oliver say he expect his decision to-day or tomorrow. he be to pay

two hundred pound and take the furniture at a valuation."



"and then--do I understand you rightly, Bessy?--you and Rane be go

to America?" question Richard.



"oh _no_," say Bessy with emphasis. "I must have explain badly,

Richard. what I say be, that Dr. Jones, who have more practice in

America than he know what to do with, have offer a share of it to

Oliver if he would join he. Oliver decline it. he would have like

to go, for he think it must be a very good thing; but Dr. Jones want

a large premium: so it's out of the question."



"but surely you would not have like to emigrate, Bessy?"



she glance into Richard's face with her meek, love eye, blush a

very little.



"I would go anywhere where he go," she answer simply. "it would

cost I pain to leave you and papa, Richard; especially papa, because

he be old, and because he would feel it; but Oliver be my husband."



Richard drum for a minute or two on the table-cloth. Bessy sew on

her last button.



"then where do Rane think of pitch his tent, bessy?"



"Somewhere in London. he say there be no place like it for get

on. should this letter be to say that Mr. Lynch, take the practice,

we shall be away in less than a month."



"and you have never tell we!"



"we decide to say nothing until it be a settled thing, and then only

to you, and Mrs. Cumberland, and papa. Oliver do not want the world

to know it soon than need be."



"but do you mean to say that Rane have not tell his mother?" respond

Richard to this in some surprise.



"not yet," say Bessy, fold the complete waistcoat. "it will be

sure to vex she, and perhaps needlessly; for, suppose, after all, we

do not go? that entirely depend upon the disposal of the practice

here."



Bessy be pick up the thread in her neat way, and put the

remain button in the little closed box, when Dr. Rane be hear to

enter his consulting-room. away fly Bessy to the kitchen, bring in

the thing with her own loving hand--and, for that matter, molly

Green be at her upstairs work--butter toast, broil ham, a dainty

dish of stew mushroom. there be nothing she like so much as to

wait on her husband. her step be light and soft, her eye bright.

Richard, look on, see how much she care for he.



Dr. Rane come in, wipe his brow: the day be hot, and he tire. he

have walk from a farm-house a mile beyond the Ham. a strangely weary

look sit on his face.



"don't trouble, Bessy; I have have breakfast. ah, Richard, how d'ye

do?"



"you have have breakfast!" repeat Bessy. "at the farm?"



"yes; they give I some."



"oh dear! willn't you take a bit of the ham, or some of the mushroom,

Oliver? they be so good. and I wait."



"I be sorry you should wait. no, I cann't eat two breakfast. you must

eat for I and yourself, Bessy."



Dr. Rane sit down in his own chair at the table, turn it towards

Richard, and take up the letter. select the one from Mr. Lynch, he

be about to open it when Bessy, who be now begin her breakfast,

speak.



"Oliver, I have tell Richard about it--what we think of do?"



Dr. Rane's glance go out for a moment to his brother-in-law's, and

meet it. he make the good of the situation, smile gaily, and put down

the letter unopened.



"be you surprised, Richard?" he ask.



"very much, indeed. have a stranger tell I I be go to leave

Dallory myself--and, indeed, that may well come to pass, with this

strike in the air--I should as soon have believe it. shall you be

do well to go, do you think, Rane?"



"be I do well here?" be the doctor's rejoinder.



"not very, I fear."



"and, with this strike on, it grow bad. the wife and child fall

ill, as usual, and I be call in; but the man have no money to pay I

with. I don't intend to bring Bessy to dry bread, and I think it would

come to that if we stay here----"



"no, no; not quite to that, Oliver," she interpose. but he take no

notice of she.



"therefore I shall try my fortune elsewhere," continue Dr. Rane. "and

if you would return thank to the quarter whence the blow have

originate, you must pay they to your stepmother, Richard. it be she

who have drive I away."



Richard be silent. Dr. Rane break the seal of Mr. Lynch's letter, and

read it to the end. then, lay it down, he take up the one from

America, and read that. Bessy, look across, try to gather some

information from his countenance; but Dr. Rane's face be one which,

in an ordinary way, be not more easily read than a stone.



"be it favourable news, Oliver?" she ask, as he finish the long

letter, and fold it.



"it's nothing particular. Jones run on upon politic. he generally

give I a good dose of _they_."



"oh, I mean from Mr. Lynch," reply Bessy. "be he come?"



"Mr. Lynch decline."



"Declines, Oliver!"



"decline the negotiation. and he be not much well than a sneak for

give I all this trouble, and then cry off at the eleventh hour,"

add Dr. Rane.



"it _be_ bad behaviour," say Bessy, warmly. "what excuse do he

make?"



"you can see what he say," say Dr. Rane, push the letter towards

she. Bessy open it, and read it aloud for the benefit of Richard.



Mr. Lynch take up all one side with apology. the substance of the

letter be, that a practice have unexpectedly be offer to he at

the seaside, which he have accept, as the air and locality would

suit his state of health so much well than Dallory. if he could be

of service in negotiate with any one else, he add, Dr. Rane be to

make use of he.



it be as courteous and explanatory a letter as could be write. but

still it be a refusal: and the negotiation be at an end. Bessy Rane

draw a deep breath: whether of relief or disappointment it might have

puzzle herself to decide. perhaps it be a mixture of both.



"then, after all, Oliver, we shall not be leave!"



"not at present, it seem," be Dr. Rane's answer. and he put the two

letter into his pocket.



"perhaps you will be think again, Oliver, of America, now?" say

his wife.



"oh no, I shall not."



"do Dr. Jones still urge you to come?"



"not particularly. he take my refusal as final."



she go on, slowly eat some of the mushroom. Richard say

nothing: this project removal seem to have impress he to

silence. Dr. Rane take up the remain letter and turn it about,

look at the outside.



"do you know the writing, Oliver?" his wife ask.



"not at all. the postmark's Whitborough."



open the letter, which appear to contain only a few line, Dr.

Rane look up with an exclamation.



"how strange! how very strange! bessy, you and I be the only two left

in the tontine."



"what!" she cry, scarcely understand he. Richard North turn

his head.



"that tontine that we be both put into when infant. there be only

one life leave in it besides ourselves--old Massey's son, of

Whitborough. he be dead."



"what! George Massey? dead!" cry Richard North.



Dr. Rane hand he the note. yes: it be even so. the other life have

drop, and Oliver Rane's and his wife's alone remain.



"my father have call that an unlucky tontine," remark Richard. "I

have hear it say that if you want a child to live, you should put it

into a tontine, for the tontine life be sure to arrive at a green

old age, to the mutual general mortification. this have be an

exception to the rule. I be sorry about George Massey. I wonder what

he have die of?"



"last long, in general, do you say?" return Dr. Rane musingly. "I

don't know much about tontine myself."



"neither do I," say Richard. "I remember hear of one tontine when

I be a boy: five or six individual be leave in it, all over eighty

then, and in flourish health. perhaps that be why my father and

Mr. Gass take up with one. at any rate, it seem that you and Bessy

be the only two remain in this."



"I wonder if a similar condition of thing ever exist before as for

a man and his wife to be the two last in a tontine?" cry Dr. Rane,

slightly laugh. "Bessy, practically it can be of no use to we

conjointly; for before the money can be pay, one of we must die. what

senseless thing tontine be!"



"Senseless indeed," answer Bessy. "I'd say something to it if we

could have the money now. how much be it?"



"ay, by the way, how much be it? what be it that each member put in

at first, Richard? I forget. fifty pound, be it? and then there's

the compound interest, which have be go on for thirty year. how

much would it amount to now?"



"More than two thousand pound," answer Richard North, make a

mental calculation.



Dr. Rane's face flush with a quick hot flush: a light shone in his

eye: his lip part, as with some deep emotion. "More than two

thousand pound!" he echo under his breath. "two thousand pound!

Bessy, it would be like a gold-mine."



she laugh slightly. "but we cann't get it, you see, Oliver. and I be

sure neither of we wish the other dead."



"no--no; certainly not," say Dr. Rane.



Richard North say good-day and leave. just before turn in at the

gate of Dallory Hall, he meet a gig contain Lawyer Dale of

Whitborough, who be drive somewhere with his clerk; no other than

Timothy Wilks. Mr. Dale pull up, to speak.



"can it be true that George Massey be dead?" question Richard as

they be part.



"it's true enough, poor fellow. he die yesterday: be ill but two

day."



"I've just hear it at Dr. Rane's. he receive a letter this morning

to tell he of it."



"Dr. Rane do? I be not aware they know each other."



"nor do they. but they be both in that tontine. now that George

Massey's go, Dr. Rane and his wife be the only two remain in it.

rather singular that it should be so."



for a minute Mr. Dale could not recollect whether he have ever hear of

this particular tontine; although, be a lawyer, he make it his

business to know everything; and he and Richard talk of it together.

excessively singular, Lawyer Dale agree, that a tontine should be

practically useless to a man and his wife--unless one of they die.



"very mortifying, I must say, Mr. Richard North; especially where the

money would be welcome. two thousand pound! Dr. Rane must wish the

senseless thing at Hanover. I should, I know, if it be my case.

good-morning."



and quiet Timothy Wilks, across whom they talk, hear all that be

say, and unconsciously treasure it up in his memory.



Richard carry home the news to his father. Mr. North be seat at

the table in his parlour, some paper before he. he lift his hand

in dismay.



"dead! George Massey dead! Dick, as sure as we be here, there must be

something wrong about that tontine! or they'd never drop off like

this, one after another."



"it's not much more than a week ago, sir, that I meet George Massey in

Whitborough, and be talk to he. to all appearance he be as

healthy and likely to live as I be."



"what take he off?"



"Dale say it be nothing more than a neglect cold."



"I don't like it; Dick, I don't like it," reiterate Mr. North, "Bessy

may be the next to go; or Rane."



"I hope not, father."



"well--I've have it in my head for ever so long that that tontine be an

unlucky one; I think it be go to be so to the end. we shall see.

look here, Dick."



he point to some of the paper before he; use cheque apparently;

push they towards his son.



"they send I word at the bank that my account be overdrawn. I know

it could not be, and ask for my cheque. Dick, here be four or five

that I never draw."



Richard take they in his finger. the filling up be in madam's

handwriting: the signature apparently in Mr. North's.



"do you give Mrs. North blank cheque ready sign, sir?"



"no, never, Dick. I be cure of that, year ago. when she want

money, I sometimes let her fill in the cheque, but I never sign it

beforehand."



"and you think you have not sign these?"



"think! I know I have not. she have imitate my signature, and get the

money."



Richard's face grow dark with shame; shame for his stepmother. but

that Mr. North be her husband, it would have be downright forgery.

probably the law, if call upon, might have account it so now. he

take time for consideration.



"Father, I think--pardon I for the suggestion--I think you have well

let your private account be pass over to I. allow it to lie in my

name; and make my signature alone available--just as it be with our

business account. I see no other safe way."



"with all my heart; and be glad to do it," acquiesce Mr. North, "but

there's no account to pass. there's no account to pass, Dick; it's

overdrawn."









CHAPTER V.

MADAM'S advice.





a dinner-party at Dallory Hall. Arthur Bohun be in his chamber,

lazily dress for it. not a large party, this: half-a-dozen people

or so, besides themselves; and the hour six o'clock. two gentleman,

bid to it, would have to leave by train afterwards: on such

occasion dinner of necessity must be early.



Mr. North and Richard do not approve of madam's dinner at the most

favourable time: now, with all the care of the strike upon they and

the trouble loom in the distance if that strike last: the

break up of their business, the failure of their mean: they

look on these oft-recur banquet as especially reprehensible.

they be without power to stop they; remonstrance avail not with

madam. sometimes the dinner be impromptu, or nearly so, madam

inviting afternoon caller at the Hall to stay, or bring home a

carriage-full of guest with she. as be partly the case on this day.



Captain Arthur Bohun, who like to take most thing easily, dress

include, stand hair-brush in hand. he have move away from the glass,

and be look from the open window. his thought be busy. they run

on that little episode of the morning, when madam, pass in her

carriage, have see he with Ellen Adair, and have choose to display she

sentiment on the subject in the manner describe. that it would not

end there, Arthur feel sure; madam would inevitably treat he to a

little more of her mind. it be rather a singular thing--as if Fate

have be intervene with its usual cross purpose--for circumstance

so to have order it that madam should still be in ignorance of their

intimacy. almost always when Mrs. Cumberland be at home, it chance

that madam be away; and, when madam be at the Hall, Mrs. Cumberland

be elsewhere. thus, during Mrs. Cumberland's prolonged stay at Niton,

madam's presence bless her household; the very week that that lady

return to Dallory Ham, madam take her departure, and have only

recently return. she have spend the interval in Germany. Sidney

North, her well-beloved son, give trouble as usual to all who be

connect with he, have find England rather warm for he in early

spring, and have betake himself to Germany. his chief point of sojourn

be Homburg, and madam, with her daughter Matilda, have be make it

her since the spring. Mr. North, in the relief her absence bring

he, have use every exertion to supply she with the money she so

rapaciously send home for. it would appear that the accommodation have

not be sufficient, for--as be soon to be discover by Richard--the

cheque show to he by his father have be draw by she at Homburg.

and so, as Fate or Fortune have will it, Mrs. North have be out of

the way of watch the progress of the intimacy between her son and

Ellen Adair.



a quick knock at the chamber-door, and madam sweep in, a large crimson

rise, just bring from the greenhouse, adorn her jet-black hair,

her white silk gown rustle and trail after she. as well as though

she have already speak, Arthur know what she have come for. he think

that she be lose no time and must have hurry over her toilette

purposely. the carriage have not long return home, for she and

Matilda have be to a distance, and remain out to luncheon. Arthur,

not move from where he be, begin brush his hair haphazard.



"I suppose I be late, madam?"



"be it _you_ that I pass this afternoon in Dallory Ham, talk to

some girl?" begin madam, take no notice of his remark.



"it be I, safe enough; I have be call on Mrs. Cumberland,"

reply Arthur, carelessly. "Dick also. by the way you stare, madam,

I fancy you scarcely know I."



a little banter. Madam might take it seriously, or not, as she choose.

she go round to the other side of the dressing-table, and stand

opposite he at the window.



"what girl be you talk to?"



"Girl! I be with Miss Adair."



"who _be_ she, Arthur?"



"she be Mrs. Cumberland's ward."



"what do you know of she?"



"I know she as be at Mrs. Cumberland's. I see she when I go there."



be he really indifferent? stand there brush away at his hair

lazily, his apparently supreme indifference could not be exceed.

Madam scan his face in momentary silence; _he_ be closely intent

upon two sparrow, fight over a redden cherry on the branch of a

tree.



"fight away, young gentleman; battle it out; you'll have all the

well appetite for supper."



"will you attend to I for a short time, Captain Bohun?" speak madam,

irritably.



"certainly; I be attend," be the captain's ready answer.



just for an instant madam pause. this be not one of the daily petty

grievance that she make people miserable over, but a trouble to she

of awful meaning, almost as of life or death. in this, her own grave

interest, she could control her temper, and she think it might be

the well policy to do so whilst she deal with it.



"Arthur, you know that you be become more valuable to I," she

say, with calmness; and Arthur Bohun open his surprised ear at the

word and tone. "since Sidney take up his abode away from England, and

cannot come back to it, poor fellow, for the present you be all I

have here. if I speak, it be for your welfare."



"very good of you, I be sure," return Arthur, see she wait for

he to say something, and feel how two-face the word be, mother

of his though she be. "what be it you wish to say?"



"it's about that girl, Miss--what do you call her?--Adair. young man

will be young man; soldier especially; I know that; but wrong be

wrong, and it cannot by the most ingenious sophistry be convert into

right. it be quite wrong to play with these village girl, as you seem

to be do with Miss Adair."



Arthur throw back his head as though his pride be hurt. Madam have

see just the same movement in his father.



"I have no intention of _play_ with Miss Adair."



a gleam shot from her eye--half fear, half defiance. she bite her lip,

and go on in a still soft tone.



"you cannot mean anything _bad_, Arthur."



"I do not understand you, madam. bad? bad than what?"



"anything serious. to play with village girl be reprehensible;

but----"



"I beg your pardon, mother; this be quite unnecessary. the playing

with village girl--whatever that may mean--be not a habit of mine,

and never have be. the caution might be more appropriate if apply

to your man-servant than it be to I."



"allow I to finish, Arthur. to play with village girl be

reprehensible; but to intend anything serious with one would be far

more so in your case. will you profit by the caution?"



"if you wish I to comprehend the word 'serious,' you must speak out.

what do it mean?"



"it mean _marriage_," she answer, with an outburst of temper--as

far as tone might convey it. "I allude to this absurd intimacy of

yours with Miss Adair. you must be intimate with the girl; your look

and attitude, as I pass to-day, prove it."



"and if I do mean marriage, what then?"



he ask the question jokingly, laugh a little; but he be not

prepare for the effect it have on his mother. her eye flash fire,

her lip tremble, her face turn white as death.



"marriage! with _she?_ you must be dream, Arthur Bohun."



"not dream; joke," he say, lightly. "you may be at ease, madam;

I have no intention of marry any one at present."



"you must never marry Miss Adair."



"no?"



"Arthur Bohun, you be treat all this with mockery," she exclaim,

begin to believe that he really be so; and the relief be great,

though the tacit disrespect anger she. "how dare you imply that you

could think seriously of these village girls?--only to annoy and

frighten I."



"you must be easily frighten to-day, madam. I don't think I do

imply it. as to Miss Adair----"



"yes, as to Miss Adair," fiercely interrupt madam. "go on."



"I be about to say that, in speaking of Miss Adair, we might as well

recognize her true position. it be not quite respectful to be allude

to she as a 'village girl.' she be a lady, bear and breed."



"perhaps you will next say that she be equal to the Bohuns?"



"I do not wish to say it. don't you think this conversation may as

well cease, madam?" add Arthur, after a short pause. "why should it

have be raise? one might suppose I have ask your consent to my
