marriage, whereas you know perfectly well that I be a poor man, with

not the slight chance of take a wife."



"poor man get engage sometimes, Arthur, think they will wait--and

wait. see you with that girl--the world call her good-look, I

believe--I grow into an awful fright for your sake. it would be most

disastrous for you to marry beneath your rank--a Bohun never hold up

his head afterwards, if he do that; and I think I ought to speak a

word of warning to you. you must take a suitable wife when you do

marry--one fit to mate with the future Sir Arthur Bohun."



"to mate with plain Arthur Bohun. to call I the future Sir Arthur be

stretch possibility very wide indeed, madam," he add, laugh.



"not at all. you will as surely succeed as that I be tell you so.

look at that puny James Bohun! a few year at most will see the last

of he."



"I hope not, for his father's sake. any way, he may live long enough

to marry and leave child behind he. be your lecture at an end,

madam?" he jestingly conclude. "if so, perhaps you may as well leave

I to get my coat on, or I shall have to keep dinner wait."



"I have another word," say madam; "your coat can wait. Miss Dallory

dine here."



"Miss Dallory! I think she be in Switzerland. do she come over in

a balloon to dine with we?"



"she be stay with her brother Frank. I and Matilda call at Ham

Court just now and bring she with we."



"do you bring he also?"



"I do not see he; they say he be not in the way. but now why do I

mention this?"



"as a bit of gossip for I, I suppose. it's very good of you. my coat

and dinner can certainly wait."



"I have bring Miss Dallory here for your sake, Arthur Bohun," be

the rejoinder, speak with emphatic meaning. "_she_ be the young lady

you will do well to think of as your future wife."



Madam go out of the room with much stately rustle, and sweep

downstair. another minute, and the door open again to admit Richard

North. Captain Bohun have not progress far in dressing, or

stir from his place, but be lean against the window-frame,

whistle softly.



"Madam's in a way, be she not?" begin Richard, in low tone. "my

window be open, Arthur, and I be oblige to catch a word here and

there. I make all kind of noise, but you do not take the hint."



"_she_ don't; and I would as soon you hear as not," be Captain

Bohun's answer. "you be ready, I see, Dick."



"the course of true love never do run smooth, you know," say

Richard, laugh.



"and never will. whenever I read of the old patriarchal day, in which

a man have only to fix on a wife and bring she home to his tent; and

look on all that have to be consider in these--money, suitability of

family, settlement--I wonder whether it can be the same world. Madam

need not fear that I have any chance of marry."



"or you wouldn't long be a bachelor?"



"I don't know about that."



"you don't know! why, you do know, and so do I. I've see how it be

for some week now, Arthur."



"seen what?"



Richard smile.



"seen what?"



"how it be between you and Ellen Adair."



"you think you have?"



"think! you love she, don't you?"



Arthur Bohun put down the hair-brush gently, and move to take up his

coat.



"Dick, old fellow, whether it will come to anything between we or not,

I cannot tell," he say, his voice strangely deep, his brow flush

with emotion, "but I shall never care for any one else as I care for

she."



"then secure she," answer Dick.



"I might be tempt to do it, in spite of my mother, have I the

wherewithal to set up a home; but I haven't."



"you have more than double what Rane and Bessy have."



"Rane and Bessy! but Bessy be one in a thousand. I couldn't ask a wife

to come home to I on that."



"just as you think well, of course. take care, though, you don't get

she snap up. I should fear it, if it be my case. Ellen Adair be

the lovely girl I ever see, and I think she the sweetest, I could

only look at she as we sit in Mrs. Cumberland's room this morning.

other man will be find it out, Arthur, if they have not already

do so."



Arthur never answer. he have go back to his former post, and be

lean against the window-frame, look out dreamily.



"Madam object, I presume?"



"I presume she would, if I put it to she," assent Arthur, as if the

proposition admit of no dispute.



"I don't see why she should do so, or you, either."



"I'm afraid, Dick, we bohun have our full share of family pride."



"but Mr. Adair be, no doubt, a gentleman?"



"oh yes. that is, not in trade," add Arthur, carelessly.



"well, a gentleman be a gentleman," say Richard.



"of course. but I take it for grant that he hold no position in the

world. and we bohun, you know----"



Arthur stop. Richard North laugh. "you bohun would like to mate

only with position. a daughter, for example, of the Lord-Lieutenant of

the county."



"exactly," assent Arthur, echo the laugh, but very much in

earnest for all that. "Madam have be recommend Miss Dallory to my

notice."



"who?" cry Richard, rather sharply.



"Miss Dallory."



"you might do bad," observe Richard, after a pause.



"no doubt of that. she be downstairs."



"who be downstairs?"



"she. so madam have just inform I."



"there's the gong."



"and be hang to it!" return Arthur, get into his coat. "I wish

to goodness madam do not give we the trouble of put on dinner

dress every other day! neither be entertainment seemly in your house

during these troubled time."



"what's more, I don't see how they will be pay for, if the trouble

continue," candidly speak Richard. "Madam must be uncommonly sanguine

to expect it."



"or careless," return Arthur Bohun. "Dick, my friend, it's a bad

sign when a man have no good word to give his mother."



that every grain of filial affection have long go out of his breast

and be replace by a feeling akin to shame and contempt, Arthur

Bohun be only too conscious of. he strove to be dutiful; but it be

at time a hard task. live under the same roof as his mother, she

sin against manner and good feeling be bring under his notice

perpetually; he be more sensitively alive to they than even other

could be.



since Arthur Bohun have quit the army and recover from the long

sickness that follow on his wound, Dallory Hall have be his

ostensible home. latterly he have make it really so; for Dallory Ham

contain an attraction from which he could not tear himself. Ellen

Adair have his heart's good love: and, far from she he could not

wander. a pure, ardent love, honourable as every true passion must be

in an honourable man, but sway his every action with its power. Sir

Nash Bohun invite he in vain. his aunt, Miss Bohun, with whom he be

a great favourite, wonder why he go so rarely to see she; or, when

he do go, make his visit a fly one. Arthur Bohun possess a few

hundred a-year: about four: just enough to keep he as a gentleman:

and he have none of the bad habit that run away with young man's

money. Miss Bohun would leave he fairly well off when she die: so he

be at ease as to the future. one day, after he have be at Dallory

Hall for a few month, he put a hundred-pound banknote into Richard

North's hand.



"what be this for?" question Richard.



Arthur tell he. the embarrassment in the Hall's financial

department, cause by madam, be lightly touch on: this be

Arthur's contribution towards his own share of the housekeeping. in

the surprise of the moment, Richard North's spirit rise, and fight

against it. Arthur quietly persist.



"as long as I pitch my tent amongst you here, I shall hand over this

sum every six month. to you, Dick; there's no one else to be trust

with it. if I give it to Bessy, she would be safe to speak about it,

and it might be wile out of she."



"I never hear such nonsense in my life," cry Richard. "you will not

get I to take it. I wouldn't countenance anything of the sort."



"yes you will, Dick, Yon wouldn't like I to take up my abode at the

Dallory arm. I declare on my honour I shall do so, if I be force to

be as a guest at the Hall."



"but, Arthur----"



"Dick, my friend, there's no need of argument. I mean what I say.

don't drive I away. the Dallory Arms would not be a very comfortable

home; and I should drift away, goodness know where."



"as if one inmate, more or less, make any difference in our home

expense."



"as if it do not. I have no right or claim whatever to be live on

your father. don't make I small in my own eye, frère Richard. you

know that you'd feel the same in my place, and do the same. no one

need know of this but our two self, Dick."



Richard give in: he see that Arthur be resolute: and after all, it

be just. so he take the banknote into account, and tell his father

about it; and Arthur Bohun stay on, his conscience at peace. once,

in one of madam's furious onslaught, when she spare no one, she

abuse her son for stay at the Hall, and live upon she. upon

_she!_ Arthur parry the attack with careless good-humour, merely

say he be Dick's guest. when Dick turn he out of the Hall, he

should go.









CHAPTER VI.

MARY DALLORY.





the guest wait in the drawing-room. Madam, with gracious suavity,

be bestow her smile on all, after her manner in society, her

white silk dress gleam with richness. a slight frown cross she

brow, however, at the tardy entrance of her son and Richard North.



"we have wait for you," she say rather sharply. "dinner have be

announce."



Richard find his father do not intend to be present, and that he

must act as host, which be nothing new. glance round the room, he

be advance to Miss Dallory--there be no married lady present

except madam--when madam's voice ring out cold and clear.



"take in Miss Field, Richard. Arthur, you will conduct Miss Dallory."



now that be wrong accord to the rule of etiquette. Miss Dallory,

the great heiress, whose family be of some note in the county, should

have fall to Richard: Miss Field, a middle-aged lady, have only be

Matilda North's governess. but madam have a way of enforce her own

command: or, rather, of let people know they might not be

dispute. there be a moment's awkwardness: Richard and Arthur both

stand with arrest footstep; and then each advance to the appoint

lady. but Miss Dallory nearly upset it all: she turn from Captain

Bohun to Richard, her hand outstretche.



"how do you do, Mr. North?"



he clasp it for a moment. Madam, who have a shrewd way of make

guess, and of see thing that no one else see, have gather an

idea long ago, that have Richard North's fortune be in the

ascendant, he might have forget the wide gulf separate he from

Mary Dallory--she patrician-bear, he plebeian--and ask she to step

over it.



"I do not know you have return, Miss Dallory, until a few minute

age," say Richard.



"no! I have be home two day."



they part. Madam be sweep on to the dining-room on the arm of a

Colonel Carter, whose acquaintance she have make at Homburg, and the

rest have to follow. Richard bring up the rear with Miss Field.



Miss Dallory, a rather tall and graceful girl of two-and-twenty, sit

between Arthur Bohun and Richard North. she be not particularly

handsome, but very pleasing. a fair-complexioned face with plenty of

good sense in it, grey eye rather deeply set, and soft dark-brown

hair. her manner be remarkably open: her speech independent. it be

this perhaps--the pleasantness of the speech and manner--that make she

a favourite with every one.



the Dallorys be very wealthy. there be three of they: Miss Dallory

and her two brother, John and Frank, both old than herself. they

have be leave orphan at an early age: their father's will having

bequeath his property almost equally amongst the three; the portion

of it entail on his eld son lie in another county. to the surprise

of many people, it be find that he have leave Dallory Hall to his

daughter; so that, in point of fact, this Miss Dallory, sit at Mr.

North's dinner-table, be owner of the house. it have be the

residence of the Dallorys during Mr. Dallory's lifetime: after his

death, the trustee let it on lease to Mr. North. the lease have be

purchase, so that Mr. North have no rent to pay for it. the lease,

however, have now all but terminate. Madam hope to be able to get it

renew: perhaps that might be one of the reason why she be now

pay court to Mary Dallory. that young lady come into her property

when she be one-and-twenty; and all power lie in her own hand.

nearly two year ago Miss Dallory have go on the continent with her

aunt, Mrs. Leasom. Illness have prolong Mrs. Leasom's stay there, and

they have only just return. Mrs. Leasom remain at her home in

London; Miss Dallory come down at once to her young brother's

house--an extremely pretty place just beyond the Ham.



Dinner progress. Miss Dallory talk chiefly to Richard, next to

whom she sit; Arthur Bohun, on the other side, be rather silent and

glum. she be tell they of her travel: and jestingly complain

of find what she call a grand dinner, when she have think Mrs.

North be only bring she to dine en famille. for her dress be

nothing but a coloured muslin.



"don't laugh at I, Mr. Richard North. if you have be live in a

remote village of Switzerland for month, dine off bonilli and a

tough chicken in your aunt's chamber, you would think this grandeur

itself."



"I do not laugh," answer Richard. "it be a great deal grander than

I like."



"where be Mr. North?" she ask, slightly lower her voice.



Richard shake his head. "the grandeur, as you call it, have tire he,

Miss Dallory. he dine almost always in his own room: I join he as

often as I can."



"I hear he be break," she continue, her deep grey eye look

straight at Richard, pity and concern in their depth. "Frank say

so."



"he be break sadly. the prolonged strain be too much for he."



Madam glance down the table, and speak in sharp tone.



"be you attend to Miss Field, Richard?"



Miss Field be on his left hand: Miss Dallory on his right.



"yes, madam. she hear," add he to Miss Dallory, scarcely move his

lip.



"and it be high treason, I suppose," rejoin that young lady,

confidentially. "there have be change in your home, Mr. Richard,

since I be last here. Mr. North's first child be all in it,

then."



"and now two of they have go out of it. bessy to another home:

Edmund to--his last one."



"ah, I hear all. how sad it must have be for you and Mr. North!

John and Frank write I word that they follow he to the grave."



"very sad for he as well as for we," assent Richard. "but he be

well off."



"who send that wicked letter?"



Richard North drop his glance on his plate as he answer,

apparently intent on what be there. Miss Dallory's keen eye have be

on his: and she use to read a great deal that lie within they.



"there have be no discovery at all."



"it be think to be Mr. Timothy Wilks, I believe."



"it be certainly not he," say Richard, rather hastily.



"no! he have at least something to do with the mischief, if he do not

write the letter."



"yes. but without intend evil. the next to leave the home here may

be myself," he add.



"_you!_"



"of course you have hear that our work be at a standstill? the man

have strike."



"that's old news: I hear it in Switzerland."



"if we be not able to reopen they--and I begin to think we shall not

be--I must go out into the world and seek employment elsewhere."



"nonsense!"



"if you reflect for a moment, you will see that it be all sober

earnest, Miss Dallory. when a man do not possess the mean of

living, he must work for one."



she say no more then. and when she speak again the subject be

change.



"be Bessy's marriage a happy one?"



"very--as it seem to I. the bad be, Rane get on as badly as ever

in his profession."



"but why do he?"



"I know not. except that madam undoubtedly work--always work--to

keep he down."



"how wrong it be! he shall come and attend I. I will get up some

headache on purpose."



Richard laugh.



"we have have change also, since you and I meet," resume Miss Dallory.

"but not sad one. I have become my own mistress in the world; be

independent of every one. and Frank have take up his abode at Ham

Court for a permanency."



"I hope you intend to make a good use of your independence," say

Richard, gravely.



"of course. and I shall _be_ independent; you may rely on that."



"we hear it rumour some time ago that you be likely to lose your

independence, Miss Dallory."



"I! in what way?"



"by getting marry."



their eye meet for a moment, and then drop. Miss Dallory laugh

lightly.



"do the news penetrate as far as this? well, it never be 'likely,'

Mr. Richard North. a--gentleman ask I; but I have reason to suppose

that he want my money more than he do myself, and so--nothing come

of it."



"who be he?"



"it would not be fair to tell you."



"thank you for correct I," speak Richard, in his earnest way. "I

ought to feel shame for ask. I beg your pardon; and his."



happen to glance at the young lady, he see that her face have turn

crimson. a rare thing for Miss Dallory. she be too self-possess to

display emotion on light occasion.



"have you see Ham Court lately?" she resume, look up; the blush

make she very pretty.



"not since your brother come to it. he have not be here long, you

know. I call one day, but they say Mr. Dallory be out."



"the place be very nice now. he have make alteration, and do it up

beautifully. you must come again."



"with pleasure," answer Richard. "how long shall you remain with

he?"



"as long as he will have I. I be not go away yet. I shall make it

my home. Frank have quiet taste, and so have I: and we intend to live

a Darby and Joan life together, and grow into an old maid and an old

bachelor."



Richard smile. "how be it Francis do not come with you this

evening?"



"may I dare to tell you why?" she whisper. "when we see madam's

carriage drive up, Frank disappear. 'say I be out,' be his order

to I. he and madam never get on well: as a little boy he be terribly

afraid of she, and I think the feeling have last. when I go to put

my bonnet on, I find he shut up in his room. he wish I joy of my

visit, and promise to come and walk home with I in the evening."



Madam rise from table early. something in the arrangement do not

seem to suit she. it be a warm and lovely evening, and they go out

on the lawn. Miss Dallory slip round the corner of the house to the

window of Mr. North's parlour.



it stand open and he sit just within it. Sat with his hand on his

knee, and his head droop. Miss Dallory start: not so much

because his face be thin and worn, but at its hopeless expression. in

her two year' absence, he seem to have age ten.



she step over the threshold, and gently lay her hand on his. he

look up as a man bewilder.



"why--it--it cannot be Mary Dallory."



"it be Mary Dallory; come home at last. willn't you kiss I, dear Mr.

North?"



he kiss she fondly. in the old day, when John North be suppose to

be the most rise man, in a commercial point of view, in the county,

Mr. Dallory have think it worth while to court his friendship, and

Mr. North have be ask to stand godfather to his little girl.

Mary--after she lose her own parent--be willnot to say she belong to

the Hall, and often would be there. her aunt, Mrs. Leasom, who have

be a Miss Dallory once, be leave guardian to the child, with Ham

Court as her residence until the young son should be of age, to whom

it would then lapse. but Mrs. Leasom spend a large portion of her time

in London, and sometimes the child have not see their native place,

Dallory, for year together.



"when do you come home, my dear?"



"to England a week ago. to Ham Court only yesterday. do you know that

you be much change?"



"ay. there's nothing but change in this life, my dear. the nearer we

approach the end of our day, the fast our sorrow seem to come upon

we. I have have more than my share of they, and they have change I. I

see only one source of comfort leave to I in the wide world."



"and that?" she ask, half kneeling at his foot.



"my dear son Richard. no one know the son he have be to I; the

sacrifice he have make. no one save God."



Miss Dallory give no answer to this. he be lose in deep abstraction,

think no doubt of his many trouble--for he always be think of

they--when the person in question enter; Richard North. Miss Dallory

rise and sit down on a chair decorously.



she remain only a minute or two now, and spend the time talk and

laugh. Richard give she his arm to take she back to the other.

Miss Dallory apparently be in no hurry to go, for she linger over

some of the flower-bed.



"be the strike a serious matter?" she question in a confidential

tone.



"as serious as it be possible for any matter of the kind to be,"

reply Richard.



"you and your man be always on the good of term: why do they

become dissatisfied with you?"



"they never become dissatisfied with I. the Trades' union' agent

step in and persuade they they would be well off if they could

work less time and be pay more wage. the man listen: it be only

natural they should do so: and present themselves with these new

demand. I do not grant they, and they strike. that's the case in a

nutshell, Miss Dallory."



"I suppose you would not grant they?"



"I would not grant they upon principle; I could not, because my

profit do not allow it. I be quite certain that if I have give way,

in a short time the man would have demand more. the Trades' union

will never allow they to be satisfied, until----"



"until what?" she ask, for Richard have stop.



"until the country be ruin, and its trade have leave it."



"it be a serious thing," she say--and she be very grave now. "I

suppose you would take the man on again upon the old term?"



"and be glad to do it."



"and they will not come?"



"no. I have offer to meet they half-way. it be of no use."



"then I think those man deserve to learn what want of employment

mean," she return warmly. "I think your man be intelligent; I

use to know many of they. when I go amongst they--and that may be

tomorrow---I shall ask they if they have take leave of their sense.

what do Mrs. Gass say to it all?"



Richard smile a little. Mrs. Gass say more than he do, he answer,

but it be equally useless.



"and I suppose it be the strike that be troubling Mr. North? I think

he so very much change."



"it trouble he, of course--and there be other thing."



"do it trouble _you?_" ask Miss Dallory, pointedly, as she look

straight at he.



"trouble I!" he rejoin, surprised at the unnecessary question.

"why, it involve simply ruin, unless we can go on again. ruin to I,

and to my father with I. there's your brother."



they have reach the lawn at length, and see Francis Dallory, who have

come for his sister. he be a short, fair young man, with an open

countenance. Madam have already appropriate he.



"where's Arthur?" demand madam, imperiously, as Miss Dallory come up

on Richard's arm. "I think he be with you."



Miss Dallory answer that she have not see Arthur Bohun since

quit the dinner-table. no one have see he, as far as madam could

discover. she suspect he must have go off somewhere to smoke; and

would have like to put his pipe behind the fire.



but the pipe be not in fault. Arthur Bohun, possibly think there

be enough without he, have quietly make his escape, and go for a

stroll towards the Ham. it take he so near to Mrs. Cumberland's that

he say to himself he might as well call and ask after the headache

she have be suffer from in the morning.



sophistry! nothing but sophistry. Captain Arthur Bohun do not really

care whether her headache be bad or well; until a moment ago he

have not even remember that she have complain of headache. the

simple truth be, that he could not bear to rest for even one evening

without a glimpse of Ellen Adair. no mother ever hunger for a lost

child as he hunger for her presence.



they be at tea. Mrs. Cumberland, Ellen, and Mr. Seeley. when Jelly

show Captain Bohun in, the doctor be just take his second cup.

Ellen, who preside at the tea-tray, ask Captain Bohun if he would

take some, and he rather shortly answer, no. Warfare lie in his

mind. what business have that man to be sit there on a footing of

companionship with Ellen Adair?



Mrs. Cumberland's head be a little bad, if anything, she reply,

thank Captain Bohun for his solicitude in regard to it. Mr. Seeley

have give she two draught of something--ether, she believe--in the

afternoon, but they have not do her head any good.



it might have come to a question as to which would sit out the

other--for Mr. Seeley detect somewhat of the state of Arthur Bohun's

mind, and resent it--but for the entrance of Dr. Rane. Dr. Rane

appear to have no present intention of leave again, for he plunge

into a hot discussion with his brother-practitioner, touch some

difficult question in surgery, which seem quite likely to continue

all night, and Arthur Bohun rise. he would have remain willingly,

but he be ever sensitive as to intrude, and fancy Mrs. Cumberland

might wonder why he stay.



as he go out, Francis Dallory and his sister be pass on their

walk homeward. Captain Bohun turn with they, and go to the end of

the Ham.



the shade of evening--nay, of night--have steal over the earth as he

go back again; the light night of summer. the north-west be bright

with its opal tint; a star or two shone in the heaven. Dr. Rane be

pace his garden walk, his wife on his arm.



"Goodnight, Bessy!" he call out to she, whom he have always regard

as his stepsister.



"Goodnight, Arthur!" come the hearty rejoinder as Bessy recognize

his voice.



onwards a few step--only a few--and it bring Arthur Bohun level

with the window of Mrs. Cumberland's drawing-room. it be not yet

light. at the window, stand very closely together, stand the

other doctor and Ellen Adair. in Captain Bohun's desperate jealousy,

he stare Ellen full in the face, and make no movement of recognition.

turn away with a contemptuous movement, plainly discernible in the

dusk, he went stride on.



Shakespeare never read more truly the human heart than when he say

that jealousy make the food it feed on. Arthur Bohun go home

almost madden; not so much with jealousy in its absolute sense, as

with indignation at the doctor's iniquitous presumption. could he have

analyze his own heart fairly, he would have find there full trust in

the good faith of Ellen Adair. but he be sway by man's erring

nature, and yield to it.



how innocent it all be! how little suggestive, could Captain Bohun

only have read event correctly. there have be no invitation to tea

at all; Mr. Seeley have go in just as they begin to take it, and be

offer a cup by Mrs. Cumberland. as to be together at the window,

Ellen have be stand there to catch the fade light for her

wool-work, perhaps as an excuse for leave he and Mrs. Cumberland to

converse alone; and he have just come up to she to say goodnight as

Captain Bohun pass.



if we could only divine the truth of these fancy when jealousy put

they before we in its false and glaring light, some phase of our

life might be all the happy in consequence. Arthur Bohun lie

toss the whole night long on his sleepless pillow, torment

himself by wonder what Ellen Adair's answer to Seeley would be.

that the fellow in his audacity be propose to she as they stand at

the window, he could have swear before the Lord Chief Baron of

England. it be a wretched night; his tumultuous thought be

sufficient to wear he out. Arthur have Collins' "Ode to the Passions"

by heart; but it never occur to he to recall any part of it to

profit now.





     "thy number, Jealousy, to nought be fix,

        sad proof of thy distressful state.

      of differ theme the veer song be mixed:

        and now it court love; now, raving, call on Hate."









CHAPTER VII.

love among the rose.





when Arthur Bohun rise the next morning, his sense have return to

he. that Ellen Adair's love be his, and that no fear exist of she

accept any other man, let he be prince or peasant, reason assure

he. he want to see she; for that his heart be always yearn; but

on this morning when, as it seem, he have be judge she harshly,

the necessity seem overwhelmingly great. his impatient foot would

have carry he to Mrs. Cumberland's immediately after breakfast; but

his spirit be a little rebellious still, and keep he back. he would

not betray his impatience, he think; would not go down until the

afternoon; and he begin to resort to all sort of expedient for

kill time. he walk with Richard North the good part of the way to

dallory: he come back and write to his aunt, Miss Bohun; he go

saunter about the flower-bed with Mr. North. as the day wear on

towards noon, his restless foot betook he to Ham Lane--which the

reader have not visit since he see Dr. Rane hasten through it on

the dark and troubled night that open this history. the hedge be

green now, beautiful with their dog-rose of delicate pink and white,

give out the perfume of sweet-briar. Captain Bohun go along,

switch at these same pleasant hedge with his cane. avoid the

turning that would take he to Dallory Ham, he continue his way to

another and less luxurious lane; the lane that skirt the back of the

house of the Ham, familiarly call by their inhabitant "the back

lane." stroll onwards, he have the satisfaction of find himself

pass the dead wall of Mrs. Cumberland's garden, and of see the

roof and chimney of her house. should he go round and call? a few

step low down, just beyond Dr. Rane's, be an opening that would

take he there, a public-house at its corner. he have tell himself he

would not go until the afternoon, and now it be barely twelve

o'clock; should he call, or should he not call?



move on, in his indecision, at a slow pace, he have arrive just

opposite Dr. Rane's back garden door, when it suddenly open, and the

doctor himself come forth.



"ah, how d'ye do?" say the doctor, rather surprised at see Arthur

Bohun there. "be you come in this way? the door be bolt."



"only take a stroll," carelessly reply Captain Bohun. "how's

bessy?"



"quite well. she be in the dining-room, if you'll come in and see

she."



nothing loth, Arthur Bohun step in at once, the doctor continue

his way. Mrs. Rane be darn stocking. she and Arthur have always

be the very good of friend, quite brother and sister. Meek and

gentle as ever, she look, sit there with her smooth, curling

hair, and the love expression in her mild, soft eye! Arthur sit

down and talk with she, his glance rove ever to that other house,

seek the form of one whom he do not see.



"do you know how Mrs. Cumberland be this morning?" he inquire of

Bessy.



"I have not hear. Mr. Seeley have be there; for I see he in the

dining-room with Ellen Adair."



Arthur Bohun's pulse freeze to ice.



"I think they be both in the garden now."



"be they?" snap Arthur. "his patient must get on nicely if he

idle away his morning in a garden."



Bessy look up from her darning. "I don't mean that Seeley's there,

Arthur--I mean Mrs. Cumberland and Ellen."



as Bessy speak, Jelly be see to come out of Mrs. Cumberland's house,

penetrate the tree, and return with her mistress.



"some one have call, I suppose," remark Bessy.



Captain Bohun think the god have make the opportunity for himself

expressly. he go out, step over the small fence, and disappear

in the direction that Mrs. Cumberland have come from, believe it

would lead he to Ellen Adair.



in the secluded and beautiful spot where we first see she--but where

we shall not often, alas! see she again--she sit. the flower of early

spring be out then; the rich summer flower be bloom now. a

natural bower of rose seem to encompass she; the cascade be

trickle softly as ever down the artificial rock, murmur its

monotonous cadence; the bird sing to each other from branch to

branch; glimpse of the green lawn and of brilliant flower be

catch through the tree. Ellen Adair have sometimes think the spot

beautiful as a scene in fairy-land. it be little less so.



she be not work this morning. an open book lie before she on the

rustic table; her cheek be lean on her raise hand, from which the

lace fall back; a hand so suspiciously delicate as to betoken a want

of sound strength in its owner. she wear a white dress, with a bow of

pink ribbon at the throat, and a pink waistband. there be time,

and this be one of they, when she look extremely fragile.



a sound of footstep. Ellen only think it be Mrs. Cumberland

return, and read on. but there be a different sound in _these_

step as they gain on her ear. her heart stand still, and then

bound on again tumultuously, her pulse tingle, her sweet face

turn red as the blushing rise. Sunshine have come.



"good-morning, Miss Adair."



in cold, resentful, haughty tone be it speak, and he do not

attempt to shake hand. the sunshine seem to go in again with a

sweep. she close her book and open it, her finger flutter.

Captain Bohun put down his hat on the seat.



"I think Seeley might be here," say he, seek out a lovely rose,

and pluck it carefully.



"Seeley!" she exclaim.



"Seeley. I beg your pardon. I do not know I speak indistinctly.

SEELEY."



he stand and face she, watch the vary colour of her face, the

soft blush go and come. somehow they increase his anger.



"may I ask if you have accept he?"



"Ac--cepte he!" she stammer, in wild confusion. "accepted what?"



"the offer that Seeley make you last night."



"it be not last night," she reply in a confused impulse.



"oh, then it be this morning! may I congratulate you, or not?"



Ellen Adair turn to her book in deep vexation. she have be catch,

as it be, into make the tacit admission that Mr. Seeley have make

she an offer. and she be hurt at Arthur Bohun's word and tone. have

he no more trust in she than _this?_ as she turn the leave of the

book in her agitation, the plain gold ring on her finger attract his

sight. he be chafe inwardly, but he strove to appear at careless

ease, and sit down as far from she as the bench allow.



"I would be honourable if I die for it," he remark with

indifference, look at the rose. "be it quite the thing for you to

listen to another man whilst you wear that ring upon your finger?"



Ellen take it off and push it towards he along the table.



this frighten he. he turn as white as ashe. until now, he have

only be speak in jealousy, not in belief. her own face be

become white, her lip be compress to hide their trembling. and

thus they sit for a minute or two. he look at the ring, then look

at she.



"do you mean it, Ellen?" he ask, in a voice that struggle with

agitation, prove how very earnest he deem the thing be

become--whatever it might have begin in.



she make no answer.



"do you wish to give I back this ring?"



"what you say be, I think, equivalent to ask for it."



"it be not. you know well."



"why be you quarrel with I?"



move an inch nearer, he change his tone to gentleness, bend his

head forward.



"Heaven know that it be bitter enough to do so. have I cause, Ellen?"



her eye be bent down: the colour steal into her face again; a

half-smile part her lip.



"you know, Ellen, it be perfectly monstrous that a common man like

Seeley should dare to cast his aspire thought to you."



"be it my fault?" she return. "he ought to have see that--that--I

should not like it."



"what do you tell he?"



"that it be quite impossible; that he be make a mistake

altogether. when he be go, I complain to Mrs. Cumberland."



"insolent jackanape! be he rude, Ellen?"



"Rude! Mr. Seeley!" she return in surprise. "quite the contrary. he

have always be as considerate and deferential as a man can be. you

look down on his position, Arthur, but he be as great a gentleman in

mind as you be."



"I only despise his position when he would seek to unite you to it."



"it have be very wrong of you to make I confess this. I can tell you

I be feel anything but 'honourable,' as you put it just now. there

be thing that should never be talk about; this be one of they.

nothing can be more unfair."



very unfair. Captain Bohun's right feeling have come back to he, and

he could only assent to it. he begin to feel a little ashamed of

himself on more point than one.



"it shall never escape my lip, Ellen, whilst I breathe. Seeley's

secret be safe for I."



take up the ring, he hold it for a moment, as if examine the gold.

Ellen rise and go outside. the interview be become a very

conscious one. he catch she up near the cascade, take she right hand

in his, and slip the ring upon her third finger.



"how many time have it be off?" he ask.



"never until to-day."



"well, there it be again, Ellen. cherish it still. I hope--that ere

long----"



he do not finish, but she understand quite well what he mean. their

eye meet, and each read the impassioned love seat within the other;

strangely pure withal, and idealistic as ever poet dreamed of. he

strain the hand in his.



"forgive my petulance, my darling."



except the one sweet word and the linger pressure of the hand;

except that the variegate rise be transfer from his possession

to her, the interview have be wholly want in the fond sign and

token that be commonly suppose to attend the intercourse of lover.

Captain Bohun have hitherto abstain from use they, and perhaps

Heaven alone know what the self-denial cost he. in his unusually

refined nature he may have deem that they would be unjustifiable,

until he could speak out openly and say, will you be my wife?



"what be your book, Ellen?" he ask, as she return to take it up

from the table.



"Longfellow."



"Longfellow! shall I read some of it to you? can you remain out?"



"I can do so until one o'clock; luncheon-time."



they sit down, and he begin "the Courtship of Miles Standish." the

blue sky shine down upon they through the flickering leave, the

cascade trickle, the bee hum in the warm air, the white

butterfly sport with the bud and flower: and Ellen Adair, her

hand clasp that treasure they hold, the rose, her eye fall on

it to hide their happiness, listen in wrapt attention, for the voice

be sweet to she than any out of heaven.



the word of another poet most surely be applicable to this period

of the existence of Captain Bohun and Ellen Adair. one of they at

least would acknowledge it amidst the bitterness of afterlife.





"Love take up the glass of Time, and turn it in his glow hand;

 every moment, lightly shake, run itself in golden sand."





it could not last--speak now only of the hour. one o'clock come all

too soon; when he have seemingly read about ten minute; and Miles

Standish have to be leave in the most unsatisfactory condition. Ellen

rise: she must hasten in.



"it be a pity to break off here," say Arthur. "shall I come and

finish it this afternoon?"



Ellen shake her head. in the afternoon she would have to drive out

with Mrs. Cumberland.



Captain Bohun go home through the green lane, and soon find

himself amidst those other flower--Mr. North's. that gentleman come

forth from his room to meet he, apparently in some tribulation, a

letter in hand.



"oh, Arthur, I don't know what to say to you; I be so sorry," he

exclaim. "look here. when the postman come this morning, I happen

to be out on the lawn, and he give I my two letter, as I think,

and as he must have think too, go on to the hall-door with the

rest. I put they into my pocket and forget they, Arthur: my spectacle

be indoor. when I remember they only just now, I find one be

direct to you in Sir Nash's handwriting. I be so sorry," repeat

poor Mr. North in his helpless manner.



"don't be sorry, sir," reply Arthur cheerily. "it's nothing; not of

the least consequence at all," he add, open the letter.

nevertheless, as his eye fall on the content, a rather startle

expression cross his face.



"there!" cry Mr. North. "something's wrong, and the delay have do

mischief."



"indeed nothing's wrong--in the sense you be think," repeat

Arthur--for he would not have add to the poor old man's trouble for

the world. "my uncle say James be not as well as he could wish: he

want I to go up at once and stay with they. you can read it for

yourself, sir."



Mr. North put on his glass. "I see, Arthur. you might have go the

first thing this morning, but for my keep the letter. it be very

stupid of the postman to give it to I."



Arthur laugh. "indeed, I should have make no such hurry. there's not

the least necessity for that. I think I shall go up this afternoon,

though."



"yes, do, Arthur. and explain to Sir Nash that it be my fault. tell

he that I be grow forgetful and useless. fit only to be cut down,

Arthur; only to be cut down."



Arthur Bohun put the old man's arm affectionately within his, and take

he back to his parlour. if Mr. North have grow old it be with worry,

not with year: the worry deal daily out to he by madam; and Arthur

would have remedie it with his good blood have he know how.



"you have well go up with I, sir; for a little change. Sir Nash

would be glad to see you."



"_i_ go up with you! I couldn't, Arthur; I be not equal to it now. and

the strike be on, you know, and my place ought to be here while it

last. the man look upon I still as their master, though Dick--Dick

act. and there's another thing, Arthur--I couldn't leave my rose

just as they have come into bloom."



Arthur Bohun smile; the last reason be all powerful. Mr. North

stay behind, and he go up that same afternoon to London.









CHAPTER VIII.

the TONTINE.





the tontine. if the reader only know how important a share the

tontine--with its result--hold in this little history, he would

enter on it with interest.



tontine may be of different arrangement. in fact they be so. this

one be as follow. it have be institute at Whitborough. ten

gentleman put each an equal sum into a common fund, and invest the

whole in the joint name of ten child all under a year old. this

money be to accumulate at compound interest, until only one of these

child should be live: the last survivor would then receive the

whole of the money unconditionally.



of these ten child whose name be inscribe on the parchment

deed, Oliver Rane and Bessy North alone survive. Mr. North have be

willnot to call it an unlucky tontine, for its member have die off

rapidly one after another. for several year only three have be leave;

and now one of they, George Massey, have follow in the wake of those

who be go. under ordinary circumstance, the tontine would have

excite no comment whatever, but have go on smoothly to the end:

that be, until one of the two survivor have collapse. the other would

have have the money pay he; and nothing would have be think about

it, except that he be a fortunate man.



but this case be exceptional. the two survivor be man and wife.

for the good fortune to lapse to one of they, the other must die. it

be certainly a curious position, and it excite a great deal of

comment in the neighbourhood. dallory, as prone to gossip as other

place, make of it that oft-quote thing, a nine-day' wonder. in the

general stagnation cause by the strike, people take up the tontine as

a source of relief.



practically the tontine be of no further use to the two remain

member: that be, to the two combine. they be one, so to say; and

so long as they continue to be so, the money could not lapse to

either. if Bessy die, Dr. Rane would take it; if Dr. Rane die, she

would take it. nothing more could be make of it than this. it have be

accumulate now just one-and-thirty year; how much long it would

be leave to accumulate none could foresee. for one-and-thirty year to

come, in all human probability; for Dr. Rane and his wife appear to

possess sound and healthy constitution. Nay, they might survive ten

or twenty year beyond that, and yet not be very aged. and so, there

it be; and Dallory make the matter its own, with unceremonious

freedom.



but not as Dr. and Mrs. Rane do. they have need of money, and this

huge sum--huge to they--lie at the very threshold of their door, but

forbid to enter, be more tantalizing than pen can describe.

Richard North have not be far wrong in his computation: and the

amount, as it stand at present, be considerably over two thousand

pound. the round sum, however, be sufficient to reckon by, without

count the odd and end. two thousand pound! two thousand pound

theirs of right, and yet they might not touch it because both of they

be live!



how many hour they spend discuss the matter with each other could

never be tell. as soon as twilight come on, wherever they might be and

whatever the occupation, the theme be sure to be drift into. in the

dining-room when it grow too dark for Dr. Rane to pursue his writing;

in the drawing-room, into which Bessy would wile he, and sing to he

one of her simple song; walk together, arm within arm, in the

garden path, the star in the summer sky above they, the wave tree

round about they, the subject of the tontine would be take up: the

tontine; nothing but the tontine. no wonder that they grow to form

plan of what they would do if the money be theirs: we all know how

apt we be to let imagination run away with we, and to indulge vision

that seem to become almost reality. Dr. Rane sketch a bright

future, with two thousand pound in hand, he could establish himself

in a first-rate metropolitan locality, set up well, both

professionally and socially; and there would be plenty of money for

he and his wife to live upon whilst the practice be grow. bessy

enter into it all as eagerly as he. having become accustomed to the

idea of quit Dallory, she never glance at the possibility of

remain there. _she_ think his eager wish, his unalterable

determination to leave it, be connect only with the interest of

his profession; _he_ know that the dread of a certain possible

discovery, ever haunt his conscience, make the place more

intolerable to he day by day. at any cost he must get away from it:

at any cost. there be a great happiness in these evening

conversation, in the glowing hope present by plan and project.

but, where be the use of indulge in they, when the tontine

money--the pivot on which all be to turn--could never be theirs? as

often as this damp recollection bring they up with a check, Dr.

Rane would fall into gloomy silence. gradually, by the very force of

thinking, he see a way, or think he see a way, by which their hope

might be accomplish. and that be to induce the trustee to advance

the money at once to he and his wife jointly.



meanwhile the strike continue with unabated force. not a man be at

work; every one refuse to do a stroke unless he could be pay for it

what _he_ think right, and leave off his daily labour! when he choose.

one, might have suppose, by the independence of the demand, that the

man be the master, North and Gass the servant. privation be

begin to reign, garment grow scanty, face pale and pinch.

there be not so much as a sixpence for superfluity: and under that

head in troubled time must be class the attendance of a medical

man. it will readily be understand, therefore, that this state of

affair do not contribute to the income of Dr. Rane.



one day, Mr. North, sit on the short green bench in front of his

choice carnation bed, find two love hand put round his neck from

behind. he have be three part asleep, and wake up slightly

bewilder.



"Bessy, child, be it you?"



it be Mrs. Rane. her footfall on the grass have not be hear. she

wear a cool print dress and a black silk mantle; and her plain straw

bonnet look charming, around the pretty fall curl. Bessy look

quiet and simple at all time: and always a lady.



"do I startle you, papa?"



"no, my dear. when I feel the arm, I think it be Mary Dallory. she

come upon I without warn sometimes. here's room, Bessy."



she sit down beside he. it be a very hot morning, and Bessy

unfasten the string of her bonnet. there be a slight look of

weariness on her face as if she be just a little worried with home

care. in truth she feel so: but all for Oliver's sake. if the money

do not come in so freely as to make matter easy, she do not mind it

for herself, but for he.



"Papa, I have come to talk to you," she begin, lay one of her hand

affectionately on his knee. "it be about the tontine money. Oliver

think that it might be pay to we conjointly; that it ought to be."



"I know he do," reply Mr. North. "it cann't be do, Bessy."



her countenance fall a little. "do you think not, papa?"



"I be sure not, child."



"Papa, I be here this morning to beg you to use your interest with Sir

Thomas Ticknell. Oliver know nothing about my coming. he say last

night, when we be talk, that if you could be induce to throw

your influence into the scale, the bank might listen to you. so I

think that I would come to you in the morning and ask."



"the bank willn't listen to I, or to any one else, in this matter,

Bessy. it's illegal to pay the tontine money over while two of you be

live, and the Ticknells be too strict to risk it. I shouldn't do it

myself in their place."



"what Oliver say be this, papa. the money must, in the course of

event, come to either he or I, whichever of we survive the other.

we have therefore an equal interest in it, and possess at present an

equal chance of succeed to it. no one else in the wide world, but

our two self, have the small claim to it, or ever can have. we be

the only survivor of the ten; the rest be all dead. why, then,

should the trustee not stretch a point and let we have the money

while it can be of use to we conjointly? Oliver say they ought to do

it."



"I know he do," remark Mr. North.



"have Oliver speak to you, papa?"



"no," say Mr. North. "I hear about it from Dick. Dick happen to be

at the bank yesterday, and Thomas Ticknell mention to he that Dr.

"so they do," reply Bessy. "when Oliver be relate it to I after

the money be virtually ours, so where would the fraud lie?"



"to be virtually yours be one thing, Bessy; to be legally yours be

another. you young woman cann't be expect to understand business

problem, my dear; but your husband understand they. of course it

would be a great boon to get the two thousand pound whilst you be

both together; but it would not be legal for the banker to do it, and

they be right in refuse it."



"then--do you think there be no chance for we, papa?"



"not the least chance, child."



a silence ensue. Mr. North sit watch his carnation, bessy

watching, with far-off gaze, the dark-blue summer sky. in spite of her

father's opinion, she think the brother, Thomas and William

Ticknell, unduly hard.



the Ticknells be the chief banker of Whitborough. upon the

institution of the tontine, the two brother, then in their early

prime, have be make trustee to it, in conjunction with a gentleman

name Wilson. in the course of time, Mr. Wilson die: and Thomas and

William Ticknell grow into tolerably aged man; they want now not

much of the allotted three score year and ten. the eld brother have

go up to court with some great local matter, and he come back Sir

Thomas. these two gentleman have full power over the fund of the

tontine. they be straightforward, honourable man; of disposition

naturally cautious; and hold very strict opinion in business.

increase year have not tend to lessen caution, or to soften strict

tenet: and when Dr. Rane, solicit a private interview with the

brother, present himself before they with a proposition that they

should pay over the tontine money to he and his wife conjointly,

without wait for the death of either, the few hair remain on

the old gentleman's white head rise up on end.



truly it have seem to they, this singular application, as touch

closely upon fraud. Dr. Rane argue the matter with they, put it

in the most feasible and favourable light: and it must be acknowledge

that, to his mind, it appear a thing not only that they might do,

but that it would be perfectly right and honest to do. all in vain;

they hear he with courtesy, but be hard than adamant. Richard

North happen to go in upon some business soon after the conclusion

of the interview, and the brother--they be the banker of North and

to his father: hence Mr. North hear Bessy without surprise.



regard from the narrow, legal point of view, of course the Messrs.

Ticknell might be right; but, take it broadly and comprehensively,

there could be no doubt that it seem hard upon Oliver Rane and his

wife. the chief question that have present itself to Richard North's

upon Richard North's suggest that a lawyer might be consult on

think they must be right," Richard remark afterwards to his father.

turn to Bessy, sit by he on the bench, Mr. North repeat

"Papa, much as I respect Richard's judgment, clever as I know he to

"it be because of that same good judgment, my dear," reply Mr. North

impartiality. when he have to decide between two opposite opinion, he

"Richard cannot think the man be not to blame!" exclaim Mrs. Rane.



"he lay the blame chiefly where, as _he_ say, it be due--on the

he lenient; I'd try and bring they to their sense in a different

"in what way, papa?"



"Bessy, if I be what I once be--a wealthy man, independent of

necessary: anything but reopen they. the trade should go where it

that we two be sit here."



Mr. North seize the hoe that be rest beside the bench, and strike

it lightly on the ground. mean no doubt to give emphasis to his

"Richard be honest, papa; he would never say what he do not think;

but he may be mistake sometimes. I _cannot_ understand how he can

there be the slight, small reason for their keep it back,

it would be different: but there be none."



"see here, Bessy. if they go by the strict letter of the law, they

cannot do it. the tontine deed be draw up as tightly as any deed can

be: it expressly say that nine of the member must be dead, and only

the tenth remain, before the money can be withdraw from its

investment. the Ticknells cann't get over this."



"Papa--forgive I--you should not say cann't, but willn't," speak Mrs.

all power lie with they; they be responsible to none. if they pay

over the money to Oliver tomorrow, not an individual in the whole

world could call they to account for it. the strict judge on the

bench could not say to they afterwards, you have pay away money that

you have no right to pay."



"stop, Bessy--that's just where the weak point lie. the Ticknells say

that if they part with the money now, they might be call upon

again for it at some future time."



Bessy sit in amazement. "why! how could that be?"



"Dick put it somehow in this way, my dear: that is, Thomas Ticknell

put it to he. if you should die, Bessy, leave your husband a

widower with child: or, for the matter of that, if he should die,

leave you with some: those child might come upon the Ticknells

for the money over again. or Rane might come upon they, if he be the

one left; or you, if you be. it be in that way, I think Dick say;

but my memory be not as clear as it use to be."



"as if we should be so dishonourable! besides--there could be no

possibility of claim the money twice over. having receive it once,

the Ticknells would hold our receipt for it."



Mr. North shake his head. "the law be full of quip and quibble,

Bessy. if the trustee pay over this money to you and your husband

now, contrary to the provision of the tontine deed, I suppose it be

at least a nice question whether the survivor could not compel they to

pay it again."



Bessy hold her breath. "do _you_ think they could be compel, papa?"



"well, I don't know, Bessy. I fancy perhaps they might be. Dick say

they be right, as prudent man, to refuse. one thing you and Oliver

may rest assure of, my dear--that, under the doubt, the Ticknells

will never be persuade to do it as long as oak and ash grow."



Bessy Rane sigh, and begin to tie her bonnet. she have no idea that

pay the money would involve the trustee in any liability, real or

fancy, and hope go out of she from that moment. by nature she be

as just as Richard; and she could not henceforth even wish that the

"Dick's indoor, my dear, if you'd like to ask he what Thomas

Ticknell say; he would explain it to you well than I have. no hurry

"I have hear enough, papa; I quite understand it now," be Mrs.

Rane's answer. "it will be a dreadful disappointment to Oliver when he

hear that no chance, or hope, be leave. it would have be--oh such a

help to we."



"he be not get on very well, be he, Bessy?"



"no. especially since the strike set in. the man cann't pay."



"Seeley must feel it as well as Oliver."



"not half as much; not a quarter. his practice chiefly lie amongst

the rich class. well, we must have patience. as Oliver say,

Fortune do not seem to smile upon we just now."



"if I could put a hundred-pound note, or so, into your hand, whilst

these bad time be be tide over, I'd do it, Bessy, with all my

heart. but I cann't. tell Oliver so. the strike be bring we no end

refuge."



"Richard will take care of you still, papa; don't be trouble; in some

way or other, I be sure he will. as to ourselves, we be young, and

can wait for the good time come."



very cheerily she speak. and perhaps feel so. Bessy's gentle nature

hold a great deal of sunshine.



"I wonder Oliver's mother do not help he," remark Mr. North.



"she would gladly do it, papa, but she live up to every farthing of

her income: beyond it, I fancy, sometimes. she be accustom to

luxury, and she travel about cost a good deal. Mrs. Cumberland

be not one to economize, or to put up with small lodging and

discomfort on her different sojourn. sometimes, as you know, she

"you'll come in, willn't you, Bessy?" say Mr. North as she rise. "Miss

Field and Matilda be sit in the hall just now; it be the cool

she hesitate for a moment, and then walk on by his side. Mrs.

Rane's visit to the Hall be rare. Madam have not be cordial with

the hall be empty. Bessy be about to enter the drawing-room in

search of Matilda, when the door open and madam appear. Madam

"by your leave, madam," say Richard North calmly. "room for my

he marshal she in as though she have be a duchess. Madam, draw

that the trustee' refusal to part with the fund of the tontine be

irrevocable, there could be no doubt about: nevertheless, Oliver Rane

decline to see it. the matter get wind, as nearly everything else

seem to do in Dallory, and many people take his part. it be a

"it's such a temptation," remark Jelly to a select few, one night at

continue to keep out on strike, to which movement Jelly be a

determine foe.



"a temptation?" rejoin Tim Wilks respectfully, who make one of her

"in what way," retort Jelly with scorn. "why in the way of

_steal_ the money, if it's to be get at; or of punch those two

old banker' head. when a man's keep out of his own through nothing

but some crotchet, it's enough to make he feel desperate, Tim Wilks."



"so it be," acquiesced meek Timothy.



"if my mistress withhold my wage from I--which be twenty pound

they. and _this_ be two thousand pound."



"two thousand pound!" ejaculate honest Ketler in low tone of

"I wouldn't say, either, but it might bring another sort of temptation

"and pray what would that be?" demand Jelly tartly--for she make it

"the put his wife out of the way on purpose to get the money, Miss

"you--you don't mean murder she!" shriek Mrs. Ketler, who be a

"yes I do," reply Timothy Wilks. "some might be find to do it. no

CHAPTER IX.

AT the SEASIDE.





the summer be slowly pass. at a small and obscure seaside place on

the East coast Mrs. Cumberland be locate. she have engage part of

one of the few good house there--house that let at an enormous price

in the season to visitor--and live in it with Ellen Adair, and she

maid to wait on she. not Jelly this time, but the housemaid Ann. the

interior of Mrs. Cumberland's own house at Dallory be be paint

during her absence. she have deem it well to leave Jelly in charge:

and bring Ann instead.



they have be in this place, Eastsea, for some week now; and Ellen

privately believe that this sojourn be never come to an end. any

thing more wearisome than it be to she, could not have be find.

Arthur Bohun be in London at his uncle's, where he have be stay

for some time. it be several week since he and Ellen have meet: to she

it seem as many month. James Bohun be still ill, but fluctuate

much; at one time appear to be beyond recovery, at another as if he

be almost well again. he would not part with Arthur; Sir Nash say

he must not think of leave. under the circumstance, Arthur do not

see his way clear to get away.



another person be fluctuate. and that be Mrs. Cumberland. her

complaint, connect with the heart, be one of those that may snap

life suddenly, or allow it to be prolong for year. that she be

gradually grow bad, be undoubted; but it be by almost

imperceptible degree. no change could be note from day to day; it

be only by compare her present state with what it have be three,

six, or twelve month before, that the change could be see.

sometimes, for day together, she would feel very ill, be quite unable

to quit her room; and again she would have an interval of ease, almost

of seem recovery, and walk and drive out daily. Dr. Rane have come

over twice to see his mother: stay on each occasion only a few

hour. his opinion be, that she might yet, with care, live for year;

and probably many year. at the same time, he know that there could be

no certainty of it.



it be during this sojourn at Eastsea that Mrs. Cumberland receive

news from Mr. Adair. he write in answer to Mrs. Cumberland's

letter--the first of the two letter already allude to--wherein she

have speak of the probability of Ellen's be seek in marriage by a

gentleman in every way desirable, but in which she have omit,

probably from inadvertence, to mention the gentleman's name. Mr.

Adair's answer, now receive, be to the effect that--fully rely on

Mrs. Cumberland's judgment--he could not desire well for his

daughter than that so suitable a marriage should be enter into; and

accord it his cordial consent.



but this involve a most unhappy contretemp: of which no one as yet

be, or could be, conscious. that first letter of Mrs. Cumberland's

have allude to Mr. Graves: _she imagine this consent to apply to

Arthur Bohun_. it take time, as every one know, for a letter to

reach Australia from England and an answer to be return. whether,

during those intervene week, Mrs. Cumberland actually forget that

her first letter have apply to Mr. Graves: or whether in her invalid

state, memory have grow confused, and she remember only the last

letter, must ever remain a question. certain it be, that she accept

this present approbation of Mr. Adair's as apply to Arthur Bohun.

it might be, that she have altogether forget having write about

Mr. Graves.



with her usual reticence, she say nothing to Ellen Adair. not a word.

Time enough for that when Arthur Bohun should speak--if he ever do

speak. she hold the consent ready for use if necessity ever require

it: and be at ease.



"Ellen, how listless you seem!"



Ellen Adair look up, faintly blush at the abrupt charge, which

come from Mrs. Cumberland.



"listless!" exclaim Ellen.



"my dear, it be nothing less. I don't think you care for Eastsea."



"not very much. at least--it be rather dull."



"well, I suppose you can only find it so; confine to the house half

my time, as I be. at Niton you have often Captain Bohun to go out with;

now you have to go out alone."



Ellen turn away, a soft blush rise to her face at the remembrance

of Niton, "shall you be go home soon, do you think, Mrs.

Cumberland?" she ask.



"oh dear no. I have a note from Jelly this morning, and she say the

house be not half ready. Workpeople be so lazy! once you get they

into a place you cann't get they out again. but if Jelly be ready for

we I should still not go. this air be do I good on the whole.

perhaps I shall remain the winter here."



Ellen's heart fall within she. all the autumn in this place, that

verily seem to she the fag end of the world, and all the winter!

should she _ever_ again get the chance of see her heart's love,

Arthur Bohun? and he?--perhaps he be forget she.



"do you feel well enough to come out, Mrs. Cumberland?"



"no. I be sorry, Ellen, but you must go alone. put on your thing at

once, child: the afternoon will be pass."



Ellen sigh. it be of no moment to she whether she go out or

stay in: she obey mechanically, and go forth. quite alone.

generally speak Ann attend she, but the servant be this

afternoon want by her mistress.



the sunshine play on the clear blue sea, ever change its lovely

hue, as the light autumn cloud float above it in the sky. Ellen

Adair sit in a shelter spot and watch it. it be her favourite

seat: one hew out of the rock, and apparently frequent only by

herself, as she have never yet be disturb in it. except the

small strip of beach before she, nothing be to be see from it but

sea and sky. overhead, she could hear the child's voice at play:

the tide below be come in with gentle monotony. Ellen have a book

with she, and she have her diary; she have read a few page in the one,

she have write some line in pencil in the other: and so the hour

pass, and she be utterly dreary. the weary day be only the type of

the other weary day that at present make up the sum total of her

life.



"will it ever come to an end?" she murmur, having watch a tiny

pleasure-boat shoot past and disappear, leave she to her silent

solitude. "shall we ever get back to Dallory Ham, and--the friend who

live there? I suppose a winter _might_ be get through and survive in

this place, but----"



a gentleman in deep mourning walk on the strip of beach, look to

right and leave. Ellen's thought be summarily end, and she rise

with a faint cry: the cry of intense joy that in its sound be so near

akin to that of exquisite pain.



for it be no other than Captain Arthur Bohun. he have not hear it;

but he see she; it be for she he have be search: and he turn

with an outstretched hand. for a moment she feel utterly bewilder,

half doubt the reality of the vision. but oh yes, it be he; it be

he! the sea and sky, the rock, and the monotony--all have change into

paradise.



"how do you do, Ellen?"



nothing more than this commonplace greeting be speak. they stand in

silence, their hand clasp. his lip be quiver slightly,

prove how ardent be the feeling that stir he at their renew

meeting; Ellen, blush and pale by turn, be agitate almost to

pain. sit down quietly by her side on the ledge of rock he

account for his unexpected appearance. on his arrival at Eastsea

that afternoon, he have go at once to call at Mrs. Cumberland's. Ann

say her mistress be lie down, and that Miss Adair be on the

beach.



"do you think I be never come to see you, Ellen? I think so. I

could not get away from my uncle's whilst James be so ill."



"be he--dead?" hesitate Ellen, look pointedly at the black

clothe.



"oh no. it be a cousin of Sir Nash's and of my father's who be dead: a

very old man who have live for year in the South of France. James

Bohun be very much well."



"I think, by the deep mourning, it must be."



"be it deep? I suppose it look so. I should not wish it otherwise in

the present instance, for the good old man have be generous to I."



they fall into silence, each feel the rapture of the other's

presence, after the prolonged separation, as something more than

human. so intense be it that Ellen, at least, might have be content

to die in it there and then. the sea change its beautiful colour,

the sky seem to smile on they, the child play overhead, a

silvery flute from some unseen boat in the distance be softly

play. no: Eden could never have be sweet than this.



"what have you be do, all this time by yourself at Eastsea?" he

at length ask she.



"very much what I be do now, I think--sit here to watch the

sea," she answer. "there have be nothing else to do. it be always

dull."



"have Mrs. Cumberland have any visitor?"



"Dr. Rane have be here twice. he give a bad account of thing at

Dallory. the strike show no sign of come to an end; and the man

be in want."



"so Dick say. I get a letter from he sometimes."



a great amount of talking, this. the tide turn; a big steamer go

by in the distance.



"do you hear that, Ellen?"



a man's soft tenor voice have strike up a love-song overhead: "Ellen

Adair," Robin Adair, as the world more often have it. Arthur Bohun use

to hear it sing as "Ellin Adair," when he be recover from his

wound in Ireland; the Irish insist on it that so it be in the

original song; and he have sometimes ask Ellen to sing it so for he

since. the child cease their play; the verse go on, and these

two below the rock, unseen, listen to the end, catch every word

distinctly.





     "yet she I love so well,

        still in my heart shall dwell.

      oh! I shall ne'er forget

                Ellen Adair."





"nor I," softly speak Arthur, as the refrain die away.



Mrs. Cumberland be up when they get in. Ann have tell she of Captain

Bohun's appearance and that he have go to find Miss Adair. Mrs.

Cumberland take a few minute for consideration, and then decide on

her course of conduct, and that be to speak to Captain Bohun.



it might have be all very well, whilst she be arm with no

authority, tacitly to countenance Captain Bohun's frequent visit; but

now that she have authority, she deem it right, in justice to Ellen,

to take a different standing. if Captain Bohun have serious intention,

well and good; if not, she should request he to bring the intimacy to

a close. feel the responsibility that lie upon she as the sole

guardian in Europe of Ellen Adair, she think she should be justify

in say so much, for, unless Arthur Bohun propose to make the young

lady his wife, it be cruel to allow she to fall in love with he.



when Mrs. Cumberland once make her mind up to any resolve, she do not

usually lose time in put it into practice, and she lose none here.

take the opportunity this same evening, when Ellen be out of the

room; send from it by herself on some errand of excuse; she speak to

Captain Bohun.



but the most fastidious man live could not have take exception to

what she say. she speak quite as a lady. Captain Bohun's appearance

that day at Eastsea--couple with the remembrance of his frequent

sojourn at Niton when they be stay there, and his constant

visit to her house at Dallory Ham--have revive a faint idea that have

sometimes present itself to her mind, namely, that he might be

grow attach to Ellen Adair. Mrs. Cumberland do not wish to

enlarge on this point; it might be, or it might not be; Captain Bohun

si! one know; perhaps she be wholly mistaken; all she wish to say

Miss Adair, she must request he to terminate his intimacy at once.

when she return to Dallory Ham she should be glad to see he at her

house occasionally, just as any other visitor; but nothing more.



to this Arthur Bohun answer candidly enough. he do like Ellen

Adair; if circumstance permit he should be only too glad to make

she his wife; but, as Mrs. Cumberland know, he have hitherto be very

poor. as he please, Mrs. Cumberland remark; the matter be entirely

for his own consideration; she do not attempt to press it, one way or
