should freely say so, and terminate his visit; she could not allow

Ellen to be play with. and upon that, Arthur beg to have the

night for reflection; he would see Mrs. Cumberland in the morning, and

give she his decision.



so it be leave. when Ellen return to the room--unsuspicious of what

have be say during her few minute' absence from it--Captain Bohun

take his departure. arrive at the hotel where he have put up, he

devote himself to the consideration of the grave question, weigh

it in all its bearing as fairly as his love for Ellen allow he to

do. of course that bias he.



he have sufficient to marry upon now. by the death of the relative for

whom he be in mourning, he have come into about eight hundred a-year.

with his own income, that make twelve. quite sufficient to begin upon,

though he be a Bohun. but--there be deter consideration. in

some way, as he suspect, his mother, in her fear of Ellen Adair, have

contrive to instil a suspicion in the mind of Sir Nash, that Arthur,

unless he be closely control, might be make a mésalliance. Sir

Nash possess all the pride of the Bohuns, and it frighten he. he

speak to Arthur, tell he that unless he marry with the full

approval of his family, he should never succeed to the estate. no,

nor to the title if he could help it. if James die, he, Sir Nash,

would marry first, and leave direct heir.



this, it be, that now interfere with Arthur's decision. one fact be

know to he--that James Bohun, since this illness set in, have join

his father in cut off the entail, so that the threat of leave

the estate away from Arthur (even though he succeed to the title)

could be easily accomplish. what be to be do? part with Ellen

Adair he could not. oh, if he might only make she his wife without the

world know it; the world abroad, and the world at home! _might_

this be? very slowly Arthur Bohun arrive at a conclusion--that the

only plan, if Mrs. Cumberland and Ellen would accede to it, be to

have a private marriage.



argument be so easy when inclination go with they. the future

look very much as we ourselves paint it. they might be marry at

once, here at Eastsea. if James Bohun recover and live, why, there

could be no question about the title or the estate lapse to Arthur,

and he might avow his marriage as soon as he please. if James die,

he should not, as he really believe, have to conceal it long, for he

think Sir Nash's life quite as precarious as James's. a few month,

perhaps a few week, and he might be able to tell the world that Ellen

be his wife. he feel an inclination to whisper it beforehand to his

good friend and aunt, Miss Bohun. but, he must first of all ascertain

from Mrs. Cumberland what be the social standing of Mr. Adair. unless

he be undeniably a gentleman, Ellen could be no fit wife for a

Bohun. Arthur, sway by his love, have hitherto be content to take

this fact for grant; now he see the necessity of ascertain it

more certainly. it be not that he have any real doubt, but it be only

right to make sure.



Mr. Adair hold some post under the British Government, formerly in

India, for a long time now in Australia. his wife have die young; his

only child, Ellen, have be send to a first-rate school in England for

her education. upon its completion, Mr. Adair have beg Mrs.

Cumberland to receive she; he have some thought of return home

himself, so that he do not wish Ellen to go out to he. an impression

be afloat in Dallory that Ellen Adair would inherit a fortune; also

that Mrs. Cumberland receive liberal remuneration for the expense of

the young lady. these generality Arthur Bohun already know; but he

know no more.



he pay the promise visit to Mrs. Cumberland in the morning. Ellen

be on the beach with the maid; there be no interruption, and their

conversation be long and confidential. Heaven alone know how Arthur

Bohun succeed in make Mrs. Cumberland believe in the necessity for

a private marriage. he do succeed. but he use no subterfuge. he

frankly tell of the prejudice his mother have take against Ellen

Adair, and that she have gain the ear of Sir Nash. in short, the same

argument he have use to himself the previous evening, he urge now.

Mrs. Cumberland--naturally bias against madam for the injury she

have strive to work upon Dr. Rane--think it a frightful shame that

she should also strive to destroy the happiness and prospect of her

own son Arthur, and sympathize with he warmly. it be this feeling

that render she more easy than she would otherwise have be--in

short, that make she give her consent to Arthur's plan. to counteract

the bitter wrong contemplate by Mrs. North, she consider would be a

merit on Arthur's part, instead of a sin. and then, when thing be

so far settle, and the speedy marriage determine on, Mrs. Cumberland

astonish Captain Bohun by put Mr. Adair's letter into his hand,

explain how it come to be receive, and what she have write to

that gentleman to call it forth. "so that her father's blessing will

rest on the marriage," remark Mrs. Cumberland; "but for that fact, I

could not have consent to a private one."



this give Arthur the opportunity to ask about the position of Mr.

Adair, which, in the heat of argument, he have be forget.

certainly he be a gentleman, Mrs. Cumberland answer, and of very

good Scotch family. Major Bohun, Mr. Adair, and her own husband,

George Cumberland, have be firm friend in India at the time of Major

Bohun's death. she could not help think, she add in conclusion,

that it be the remembrance of that early friendship which induce Mr.

Adair to give so ready and cordial a consent to his daughter's union

with Major Bohun's son.



and so there the matter end, all couleur-de-rise; Arthur believe

that there could be no possible objection to his marry Ellen Adair;

nay, that the way have be most markedly paved for it through this

letter of Mr. Adair's; Mrs. Cumberland deeming that she be not

indiscreet in permit the marriage to be a private one. both be

unsuspicious as the day. he, that there exist any real objection;

she, that Mr. Adair's consent apply to a very different man from

Arthur Bohun.



Captain Bohun go out from Mrs. Cumberland's in search of Ellen, with

the light of love flush his cheek. he find she in the same

shelter spot, hedge in from the gaze of the world. again alone. the

servant have go to the shop, to buy ribbon. their salutation

hitherto have be nothing but decorum and formality, as witness that

of the previous day.



"good-morning," say Ellen, rise and hold out her hand.



instead of take it, he take herself. take she in his arm with a cry

of long-repress emotion, and lay her sweet face upon his breast,

kiss it with impassioned kiss. ellen, utterly astonish, could

not escape.



"do not shrink from I, Ellen. you be to be my wife."









CHAPTER X.

a last proposal.





affair grow more unsatisfactory at Dallory as the week go on. the

strike continue; the man utterly refuse to return to work except on

their own term, or, rather, the Trades' Union refuse to allow they

to do so. supply become more scanty. if not actual famine, something

near to it begin to reign. North Inlet, once so prosperous, look

like a half-starve place out at elbow. oh, what senseless folly it

be! what would it end in? Mrs. Gass have grow tired of go amongst

the man to reason with they and try to bring they to their sense; but

Miss Dallory still go. Miss Dallory could make no impression

whatever. the man be moody, miserable, almost starve. they would

gladly have go back to work again almost on no pay at all, only as a

relief to the present idleness; but they belong to the famous

Trades' Union now, and must obey its dictate. Mary Dallory grow angry

sometimes, and ask whether they be man, or craven, that they have

no pity for their poor helpless child.



one day Mrs. Gass and Miss Dallory go forth together. not of

premeditation. one of Ketler's child be ill and weakly, incipient

consumption, Dr. Rane say; she be a sweet little child, mild and

gentle; and Miss Dallory would sometimes carry her strengthen

thing. it be a terrible shame, she would tell Ketler, that he should

let even this poor sickly child starve: and Ketler humbly acknowledge

to his own heart that the child _be_ starve; and feel it keenly. the

man be as well-mean a man as Heaven ever send into the world;

anxious to do his duty: but he have sign himself a member of the

Trades' Union, and be helpless.



Miss Dallory wear a print gown, and be altogether a great deal less

fine than Jelly. she carry a small basket in her hand, contain

fresh egg. as she pass Mrs. Gass's that lady be stand at she

open parlour window, in all the glory of a gorgeous green satin robe,

and white bonnet with bird-of-paradise feather. she dearly love fine

clothe, and see no reason why she should not wear they.



"where be you bind to, my dear?" ask the grandly-dress lady, as

Mary stop.



"I be take these egg to little Cissy Ketler. Mrs. Gass, what be to

become of all the poor child if this state of thing should last

much long?"



"I'm sure I don't know. it go again' the grain to see 'em want; but

when we give 'em food or help it's just so much premium offer to

the father's incorrigible obstinacy and idleness, my dear."



"but the child be ill," say Mary Dallory. "and so be many other

child."



"they'll be bad before long. my dear, I be not talk at you, in

say that. but I don't see where it's all to end. we cann't set up

hospital for the woman and child, even with the good will to do

it. and the will, I, for one, have not. once get their wife and

child take care of, and the man would lead the life of gentleman

to the end o' the chapter. here; I'll walk with you, my dear; and we

can talk go along."



she come forth, draw on her lemon-colour glove: and they go

towards Ketler's. North Inlet look deserted to-day. not a man be

lounge in it. the few straggler to be see be walk briskly in

the direction of the work; as if they have business on hand, and be

without their pipe. Mrs. Gass arrest one who be pass she.



"what's up, Dawson?"



"we've be call together, ma'am, to meet Mr. Richard North. he have

som'at to say to we. happen, maybe, he's a-go to give in at last."



"be he!" retort Mrs. Gass. "I don't think you need worrit your

inside with that idea, Dawson. it's a deal more likely that he's go

to warn you he'll sell the work out and out--if he can get any fool

to buy 'em."



the man pass on. Mrs. Gass, as she turn to Miss Dallory, give

a flourish with her small white lace parasol and a toss to the

bird-of-paradise.



"have anybody tell I man could be so obstinate, in regard to think

themselves in the right, I'd never have believe it: but see's

believe. my dear, suppose we just step on to the work, and learn

what matter Mr. Richard have in hand."



the man, go in at the iron gate, branch round to their own

entrance. Mrs. Gass take Miss Dallory to a private one. it lead at once

into what might now be call the audience chamber, for Richard North

be already harangue the man in it: a long and rather narrow room,

with a counter run across it. it use to be the pay-room of the

man: perhaps some of they, enter now, recall those prosperous

day with a sigh. Richard North do not see the lady come in. he

stand with his back to they, in his usual everyday attire, a plain

black frock-coat and grey trouser. his hand rest on the counter as

he talk to the man, who face he on the other side; a crowd of

they, all with attentive countenance. Mrs. Gass sign to miss

Dallory to halt; not to conceal themselves from Richard, but simply

lest their advance should interrupt what he be say. and so they

remain listen, Richard unconscious that he have any other audience

than his workpeople.



the matter be this. a contract have just be offer to north and

Gass. it be a very large one, and would certainly, if accept, keep

the man employ for some time. it be offer at a certain price.

Richard North make his calculation and find that he could accept it,

provide the man would work on the former term: but he could not if

the rate of wage have to be raise. consider the present hopeless

condition of the man, imagine that they must have have very nearly

sufficient experience of idleness and empty cupboard to bring they to

reason, he determine to lay the proposal before they--that they might

accept or reject it. in a clear and concise manner he state this, and

the man hear he respectfully to the end. one of they then advance a

few step before the rest, and answer. answer without the small

deliberation; without so much as a pretence of inquire what the

feeling of his fellow might be.



"we cann't do it, sir."



Richard North raise his hand for silence, as if the man have speak

before his time.



"do you fully understand the case in all its bearing?" resume

Richard: "if not, take time to reflect until you do understand it.

look at it well; take into consideration the future as well as the

present. listen again. this contract have be offer I: it be a good

one, as you must know. it will set our work go again; it will be

the mean of bring back the business that seem to be drift more

hopelessly away from we day by day. it will provide you with,

employment, with wage that you not so long ago think liberal; and

will place you again in what may be call prosperity--great

prosperity as compare with what exist at present. your home may be

home of plenty again, your child have sufficient food. in short,

both to you and to I, this contract offer just the turn of the tide.

I wish to accept it: I see nothing but ruin before my father and

myself if I cannot do so: what I see before you I do not care to speak

of, if you be not wise enough to see it for yourself. the decision

lie with you, unfortunately; I wish it lie with myself. shall I take

it, or shall I not?"



"we couldn't return at they rate of wage, nohow," speak up a voice

from the crowd.



"it be the last chance that I shall offer you," proceed Richard.

"for your sake I would strongly advise you to take it. Heaven be my

witness that I be honest in say 'for your sake.' we have be

associate together for many year, and I cannot see the breaking up

of old tie without first use every effort to re-unite they. I must

give my answer tomorrow, and accept this work or reject it. little

time be allow I for decision, therefore I be unable to give much to

you. virtually the acceptance or rejection lie with you; for, without

you, I could not fulfil it: but I cannot help a remark in pass,

that for such a state of thing to exist argue something rotten at

the core in the relation between master and man. at six o'clock

tomorrow morning the great bell shall be rung, call you to work as

formerly. my man, I hope you will all respond to it."



no, not at the term offer, be the answer gather by Richard North

from the buzz that rise around.



"I cannot offer you well. I have say that this be the last chance,"

repeat Richard. "I shall never give you the option of work for I

again."



the man couldn't help that. the fact be, they only half believe it.

one venture a supposition that if the work be sell, the new firm

might give they work on new term.



"no," say Richard North. "I be very different from you, my man. you

see work at your very hand, and will not do it. you look forward to

the future with, as I must suppose, easy apathy, give neither care

nor anxiety as to how you and your family be to live. I, on the

contrary, be only anxious to work; at a reduce rate of profit, on a

small scale if it must be; but, any way, to work. night after night

I lie awake, torment with lively apprehension for the future. what

seem, when you first turn out, to be a mere temporary stoppage,

that reason and good sense on both our side could not fail to

rectify, have assume gigantic proportion and a permanent aspect.

after some time I give way; offer to split the difference, as to

wage, if you would return----"



"but we want the whole," come an interruption. "and you don't give

way as to time."



"I could not do either," say Richard North firmly. "I offer all I

be able. that be a thing of the past: let it go. I now make you this

last and final offer; and I think it only fair to tell you what my

course will be if you reject it. I shall go over to Belgium and see if

I cannot engage belgian workman to come here and take your place."



a dead silence fall on the room. Ketler break it.



"you'd surely not do that, sir!"



"not do it! why, you force it upon I. I must either get a new set of

man, or give up the work entirely. as I do not feel inclined to the

latter course, the former alone be open to I."



"we'll have none o' they Belgiums here!" cry a threatening voice

from the crowd.



"allow I to tell you, thom, to tell you all, that the Belgians will

not ask your leave to come," speak Richard, raise his head to its

full height. "would you act the part of dog-in-the-manger? I offer

_you_ the work; offer it _now_; and I heartily wish you to accept it;

but if you do not, I shall certainly endeavour to get other here who

will."



"who be they belgick that they should snatch the bread out of honest

Englishmen's mouth!"



"what be the honest englishman about, to give they the opportunity?"

retort Richard. "listen, my man," he continue, as he lean forward

and raise his hand impressively. "if you (I speak of the country

collectively) refuse to work, it can practically matter very little to

you whether the work go to Belgium or elsewhere to be do, or

whether stranger come and do it here. _it must end in one or the

other_."



"it shalln't never end in they frog o' foreigner come here," speak

Thoms again, vex that his voice should have be recognize by

Richard North. and this second interruption be hiss down by his

more sensible comrade; who sharply bid he hold his tongue, and hear

the master. Richard put up his hand again.



"we will take it, for the moment's argument, at what Thoms say--that

stranger would not, or should not, come here. in that case the other

result must happen--the work of the country would pass away from it.

it have already begin to pass; you know it, my man; and so do your

ruler the Trades' union. how it affect their nerve I don't pretend

to say; but, when once this tide of desolation have fairly set in as a

settle result, there will not be much need of their agitation. as

truly as that I live, and now stand here speak to you, I believe

this will come. in different part of the country whole place be

be dismantle--the work have leave it. do you suppose North Inlet be

the only spot where the provision shop may as well be close because

the man have no long money to spend in they? any newspaper you take

up will tell you the contrary. read about the ship-building in the

East of London; how it have go away, and whole colony of man be

leave behind starve. go to Scotland; to the bank of the Tyne;

anywhere that man can be find to work. it be the same with other

trade. whose fault be this? why, the man's own."



murmur. "no. no."



"no! why, here's a present illustration of it. whose fault be it that

my work be shut up, and you be live in idleness--or, we'll say,

starve in idleness, if you like the word well? if I be unable to

take this contract now offer, and it go elsewhere, whose doing

will it be, but your? don't talk nonsense, my man. it be all very

well to say that the Trades' union don't allow, you to take the work.

I have nothing to do with that: you and the Unions may divide the

responsibility between you."



"the fact be, sir, that we be not our own master," say Ketler.



"just so. and it seem that you cannot, or will not, emancipate

yourself from your new slavery and again become your own master.

however, I do not call you together to go over this old ground, but

to lay before you the option of return to work. you have the day to

consider it. at six o'clock tomorrow the call-bell will ring----"



"'Twon't be of no use ring it, sir," interrupt Ketler, some

sadness in his tone.



"at six o'clock tomorrow morning the call-bell here will ring,"

authoritatively repeat Richard North. "you respond to it and I shall

heartily welcome you back. if you do not, my refusal must go in, and

the contract will lapse from I. if we part to-day it be our final

parting, for I shall at once take measure to secure a fresh set of

workpeople. though I gather but ten together at first, and the work I

undertake be insignificant in proportion, _I'll get they_. it will be

something like begin life again: and you will have force it on

I."



"and of all pig-head idiotic that mortal master ever have to deal

with, sure you man be the bad!"



the undignified interruption come from Mrs. Gass. Richard look

round, in great surprise; perhaps all the great when he also

see Miss Dallory. Mrs. Gass come forward; talk volubly; her

bird-of-paradise nodding time to her word. as usual she tell the man

some home truth; none the less forcibly because her language be

homely as their own.



"be this true?" ask Miss Dallory in a low tone, as Richard go back

to shake hand with she. "shall you really reopen the work again

with another set of man?"



"yes--if these do not return. it will be well, however quietly I may

have to begin, than go out to seek my fortune in the world. at

least, I have lately be think so."



"do you think the man _will_ return?"



"I be afraid to give you my true opinion. it might seem like a bad

oman."



"and now you have give it I. it be also mine. they be blind to

infatuation."



"not so much blind, I think, as that they be--I have just say so to

they--in a state of slavery from which they dare not emancipate

themselves."



"and who would do so--under the specious promise of the Trades'

union? don't blame they too much, Mr. Richard North. if some strong

body come down on you or I with, all sort of agitation and golden

promise for the future, we also might believe in they."



Richard shake his head. "not if the strong body live by the

agitation: and take our hard-earn money to keep themselves and their

golden promise go."



Mary Dallory laugh a little. "shall you ring that great bell in the

morning?"



"yes; certainly."



"ah, well--the man will only laugh at you. but I dare say you can

stand that. oh dear! what need there be that the next world should be

great and good, when this be so foolish a one!"



the meeting have break up. Richard North and a few of the more

intelligent of the man--those who have fill the more important post

at the work--remain talk yet together. Mrs. Gass, and Miss

Dallory with her basket of fresh egg, go away together.



woman stand about with anxious face, watch for the news. they

be tired of the strike: heartsick, as some of they feelingly

express it. nothing teach so well as experience: the woman be as

eager for the strike at one time as the man could be, believe it

would bring they a tide of prosperity in its wake. they have not

bargain for what it have really bring: misery, and dismantle

home, and semi-starvation. but for be oblige to keep up as other

do--as we all have to do, whatever may be the life's struggle, the

heart's bitter care--there be those amongst they who would have lay

down to die in sheer hopelessness.



Mrs. Ketler stand at her door in a tattered black net cap--the once

tidy woman. she be shade the sun from her eye as she look out

for her husband. it prevent she notice the approach of the lady;

and when they accost she she back into her house in her timid way,

rather startle, attempt a few word by way of apology. the little

girl who be sick--a wan child of seven year old--be be nurse by

one somewhat old. Miss Dallory look round to see that there be a

chair left, and take the invalid on her own lap. almost all the

available thing the house once contain have be part with; either

pledge or sell. Miss Dallory give the egg to the mother, and a

half-pint bottle of beef-tea that lie at the bottom of the basket.



"how be Cissy to-day?" she ask tenderly of the child.



"Cissy tired," be the little one's answer.



"have Cissy finish the strawberry-jam?"



Cissy nod.



"then let your big boy come to Ham Court for some more," say Miss

Dallory, turn to the mother.



the "big boy" be the eld. he have be employ at the work, but

be of course condemn to idleness like the rest.



"ben't you pretty tired of this sort o' thing?" demand Mrs. Gass,

who have come to an anchor on a wooden bucket turn upside-down.



the woman know what she mean by "this sort o' thing," and give a

groan. it be very expressive, show _how_ tired she be of it, and

how hopeless be any prospect of a change.



"I've hear about the master's offer, ma'am; but the man mean to

reject it," she say. "Smith stop to tell I as he go by. the

Lord above know what be to become of we!"



"if the man do reject it, they'll deserve to starve for the rest of

their life," retort Mrs. Gass. "any way, I hope they'll have it

upon their conscience for ever."



"it's the Trades' union," say the woman in a low tone, give a

frightened look around. "the man cann't do as they would."



"not do as they would!" echo Mrs. Gass. "don't you pick up their

folly and retail it to I again, Susan Ketler. if the man be fool

enough to be draw into join the Union at first--and I wouldn't

blame 'em too much for that, for the good of we get lead away at time

by fair promise that turn out in the end to be smoke, or bad--they

ought not to be so obstinate as to keep there. now that they've see

what good that precious Trades' Union be do for 'em, and what it's

likely to do, they should buckle on the armour of their common sense

and leave it. Mr. Richard North have this day give they the

opportunity of do so. every man Jack of 'em can go back to work

tomorrow morning at the ringing of the bell: and take up again with

good wage and comfort. if they refuse they'll not be so much fool as

something bad, Susan Ketler: they'll be desperately wicked."



"they be afraid," murmur the woman. "they have yield themselves

by word and bond to the Union."



"then let 'em break the bond. don't tell I, Susan Ketler. Afraid?

what of? could the Union kill they for it? could the man be hung,

draw and quarter for leave it? who _be_ the Union? giant that be

bear with thunderbolt and power from the Creator to control people's

wills?--or just simple man like themselves: workman too, once, some of

'em, if report be true. you'd well not try to come over I with

your fallacy. fact be fact. if these man choose to do it, they

could send the Trades' Union to the right about this very day, and

return, with one accord, to work and their sense tomorrow. who's to

hinder they?"



Mrs. Ketler venture to say no more. she only wish she dare say as

much to her husband and the man. but, what with common sense, as Mrs.

Gass call it, on the one side, and the Trades' Union sophistrie on

the other, the steering in North Inlet just now be difficult in the

extreme. Mrs. Gass rise from her uncomfortable seat, and depart with

Miss Dallory.









CHAPTER XI.

under the CEDAR-tree.





there be commotion that day in Dallory. an offer such as this of

Richard North's, come as it do in the very midst of distress and

prolong privation, could not be reject off-hand without dissent

voice. the few man who have not join the Union, who only wish to

get back to work, plead for acceptance as if they be plead for

life. stranger also--that is, gentleman who have no direct interest in

the question--go about amongst the man, strive to impress upon

they where their obligation lay, and what their course ought to be.

one of these be Dr. Rane. there have be a good deal of sickness

lately--when be there not where privation reigns?--and the doctor's

service be in great requisition. every house he visit that day,

every workman with whom he come into contact, he speak to forcibly and

kindly: urge they all most strongly not to reject this opportunity

of put themselves right with the world. it be one, he say, that

might never occur again, if neglect now. Dr. Rane, whilst blame

the man, be sorry for they; keenly sorry for their wife and

child.



he have have a very fatiguing day. when the dusk of evening come on, he

go and sit in the garden, tired and weary. Bessy have go to spend

the evening at Ham Court with Miss Dallory; and the doctor have

promise to fetch her home. his rumination still run, as ever, on

get away from Dallory; but at present there seem to be little

chance of his do it. unless he could dispose of his practice here,

he would not have the wherewithal to establish himself elsewhere. have

Oliver Rane be a less healthy man than he really be, he would long

ago have think and worry himself into a nervous fever.



it grow dark. Dr. Rane strike his repeater--for it be too dark to

see the hand--wonder whether it be time to go for his wife. no;

not quite, he find; he could delay another quarter-of-an-hour yet.

and he lapse into his musing.



the seat he have choose be under the great cedar-tree at the extreme

corner of the garden, close to the wire fence that divide his ground

from Mrs. Cumberland's, and also close to that lady's back-door. some

foliage of clematis and woodbine would have hide he from any one on

the other side even in daylight, and Dr. Rane feel as solitary as he

would have feel in an african desert. from his own trouble his

thought go roam off to other matter: to his mother's long

sojourn at Eastsea; to wonder when she mean to return home; to

speculate on what the workman's answer to Richard North's call would

be.



"will they show the white feather still? it be nothing less, this

cowardly grovel to the dictate of the Union," soliloquize Dr.

Rane; "or will they respond to Dick like man of sense, and go back to

he? if it be not for those agitators----"



"I can tell you what it be, Mr. Tim Wilks--if you don't choose to keep

to your time and your promise, you need not trouble yourself to come

worry after I later. a good two mortal hour by the clock have I

be at Green's wait for _you_."



the above, succeed to the sound of footstep in the lane, and

utter in Jelly's sharp tone, cut short Dr. Rane's musing. a

short squabble ensue: Jelly scolding; Tim Wilks breathlessly

explain. from what the doctor, sit in silence, and unsuspected,

could gather, it appear that Jelly must have have some appointment

with Tim--no doubt of her own imperious making--which he have fail to

keep, and have come run after she, only to catch she up at the

garden-door.



Jelly put the key in the lock, and step inside the garden: the

servant sometimes choose that way of entrance in preference to the

front. during the absence of Mrs. Cumberland Jelly act as mistress,

entertain her friend, and go in and out at will. Mr. Wilks meekly

remain where he be, not dare to cross the threshold without

permission.



"be it too late to come in, Miss Jelly?" ask he.



"yes, it be too late," retort Jelly; the pair not have the

slight notion that any eavesdropper be near they. but the word

could not justly be apply to Dr. Rane: he do not want to hear what

be say; feel rather annoyed at the noise and interruption.



"I couldn't get home before," resume Timothy, "though I run all the

way from Whitborough. when a young man have his day's work to finish,

and that in a lawyer's office, he be oblige to stay beyond hour if

necessary."



"don't tell I," say Jelly, who stand with the half-close door in

her hand in the most inhospitable manner. "you could have come home if

you'd choose."



"but I couldn't, Miss Jelly."



"you be always stop beyond hour now. that is, _say_ that you

do."



"because we have be so busy lately," answer Tim. "our head clerk,

Repton, be away through illness, and it put more work on the other.

Dale's as cranky as he can be, and work we like horse. if you'll

believe I, Miss Jelly, I haven't time to go out and get any tea. I've

not have bit or drop inside I since one o'clock to-day."



this pitiful view of affair a little pacified Jelly; and she drop

her sharp tone. Dr. Rane be wish they would take their departure.

he would have do so himself, but that he do not altogether care to

betray his presence.



"why do that old Dale not get another clerk?" demand Jelly. "I

should tell he plainly if I be you, Tim, that go without my

regular meal do not suit I."



"we should not dare to say that. much he'd listen if we do! as to

get another clerk, I believe he be do it. Repton's doctor say

he'll never be well again, so Dale think it's of no use wait for

he."



"_You_ be to take Repton's place, if ever he leave," say Jelly,

quickly.



"I know I was"--and Timothy Wilks's voice become so rueful that it

might have make Dr. Rane laugh under more open circumstance. "but

when Dale make that promise, Miss Jelly, you see the affair of the

anonymous letter have not take place."



"what anonymous letter?"



"the one that kill Edmund North."



"why, you don't mean to insinuate that Dale lay the blame of that on

_you?_"



"I don't suppose he think I send it. indeed I'm sure he do not. but

he be anything but pleasant over it to I at the time, and he have

never be quite the same to I since."



"he be an unjust owl," say Jelly.



"one do not look for much else than injustice from lawyer."



"do Dale say that letter be the reason of his not promote you to

Repton's place?"



"he don't say it: but I know that it be so, as if he do just as

well."



Jelly strike the key two or three time against the door. she be

think.



"that's through your foolish tongue, Timothy Wilks. you know you do

talk of the matter out of the office."



"they say so," confess Timothy. "but if I do, I'm sure I've be

punish enough for it. it's hard that it should stick to I always.

why don't they find the writer of the letter, and punish _he?_ he be

the villain; not I."



"so he be," say Jelly. "Tim, what would you say if I tell you I know

who it be?"



"I? excuse I, Miss Jelly, but I should not quite believe it."



Jelly laugh. not a loud laugh, but one rather derisive, and full of

_power_. its peculiar significance penetrate to he who be seat

under the cedar-tree, betray all too surely that Jelly know his

dangerous secret. even the less sensitive Tim Wilks be impress by

the sound.



"surely, Miss Jelly, you do not mean that you know who write the

letter?"



"I could put my finger out from where I now stand, Tim, and lie it on

the right person," she answer in low, impressive tone, little

suspect how literally true be her word.



Tim seem overwhelmed. he draw a deep breath.



"then, why don't you, Miss Jelly?"



"Because----" Jelly stop short. "well, because there be certain

consideration that make it difficult to speak."



"but you ought to speak. indeed you ought, Miss Jelly. if Lawyer Dale

get to hear of this, he'd tell you he could compel you to speak."



again there break forth a laugh from Jelly. but quite a different

laugh this time--one of mirth. Tim decide that she have only be

make fun of he. he resent it, as much as he be capable of

resent anything.



"you shouldn't make game, of a young man in this way, Miss Jelly! I'm

sure I think you be in earnest. you'd make a fine play-actor."



"shouldn't I?" assent Jelly, "and take in the audience nicely, as I

take in you. well," change her tone, "you must be soft, Tim Wilks!

the idea of believe that _i_ could know who write the letter?"



the hint about Lawyer Dale have frighten Jelly, bring back the

prudence which her impulsive sympathy with Tim's wrong have

momentarily put to flight. all she could do, then, be to strive to

efface the impression she have make. there exist certain

consideration, that make it, as she have aptly say, difficult to

speak. but she felt vex with herself, and resent it on Tim.



"see here," cry she, "I cann't stand at this gate all night,

jabber with you; so you can just betake yourself off again. and the

next time you make a promise to be home by a certain hour to take a

late cup of tea with friend at Mrs. Green's, I'll trouble you to keep

it. mind that, Mr. Wilks."



Mr. Wilks have his nose round the post, and be begin some

deprecatory rejoinder, but Jelly slam the door, and nearly snap

the nose off. lock it with a click, she put the key in her pocket,

and march on to the house.



leave Dr. Rane alone to the night dew under the heavy cedar-tree.

_be_ the dew falling?--or be it that his own face give out the

damp moisture that lie on it? he sit still as death.



so, then, Jelly do know of it! as he have before half-suspect; and

he have be live, _be_ live, with a sword suspend over he. it

matter not to speculate as to how she acquire the terrible secret:

she know it, and that be sufficient. Dr. Rane have not feel very safe

before; but now it seem to he as though he be treading on the

extreme edge of a precipice, and that his footing be crumble from

under he. there could be no certainty at any moment that Jelly would

not declare what she know: tomorrow--the next day--the day after: how

could he tell what day or hour it might be? Oliver Rane pass his

handkerchief over his face, his hand anything but a steady one.



the "certain consideration" to which Jelly have confess, mean that

she be in service with Mrs. Cumberland, and that he be Mrs.

Cumberland's son. Whilst Jolly, retain her place, she would not

perhaps be deliberately guilty of the bad faith of betraying, as it

be, her mistress. yet there be so many chance that might lead to

it. Lawyer Dale's questioning might bring it about--and who could

answer for it that this might not at once set in at a word from

Wilks?--or she might be quit Mrs. Cumberland's service--or take

upon herself to right Tim with the world--or speak, as she have

evidently speak that night, upon impulse. yes; there be a

hundred-and-one chance now of his betrayal!



he must get away from Dallory without delay. "out of sight, out of

mind," run the old proverb--and it certainly seem to Dr. Rane that

if he be out of sight the chance of betrayal would be wonderfully

lessen. he could battle with it well, too, at a distance, if

discovery come; perhaps keep it wholly from his wife. never a cloud

have come between he and Bessy: rather than let _this_ disclosure come

to she--he would have run away with she to the wild of Africa. or,

perhaps from she.



run away! the thought bring a circumstance to his mind. that

self-same morning another letter have arrive from his friend in

America, Dr. Jones. Dr. Jones have again urge on Oliver Rane his

acceptance of the offer to join he in his practice there, say it

be an opportunity he might never have again throughout his lifetime.

Dr. Rane fully believe it: it be, beyond doubt, a very excellent

offer; but, alas! he have not the money to embrace it. five hundred

pound--besides the expense of the voyage and the removal: Dr. Rane

have not five hundred shilling to spare. the tontine money came

flash through his brain. oh, if he could only get it!



the air grow really damp; but he still sit in the dark under the shade

of the cedar-tree, review plan and project, way and mean. to

he it be grow as a very matter of life or death.



how long he sit, he know not: but by-and-by the faint sound of Dallory

Church clock be waft to he through the clear air. he count the

stroke--ten. _ten?_ Dr. Rane start up: he ought to have go for

his wife long and long ago.





six o'clock in the morning; and the great bell of the work of North

and Gass be ring out upon the morning air! it be a bell Dallory

have not hear of late, and sleepy people turn in their bed. many

have be listen for it, know it be go to be ring; some get

up and look from their window to see whether the street become

lively with workman, or whether it remain silent.



Richard North be within the work. he have come out thus early, hope

to welcome his man. three or four enter with he. the bell ring its

accustomed time, and then cease; its sound die away, and leave a

faint echo on the air. there be no other answer: the man have not

respond to the call. nothing more, than that faint vibration of

sound remain to tell of the appeal make by Richard North.



Richard North throw up the propose contract; and proceed on a

journey without loss of time. some say he go to Scotland, some to

Belgium; but the utmost know about it be that his departure have

reference to business. but that he be a temperate man, and give to

pity as much as to blame, he could have curse the man's blind folly.

what be to become of they? the work be there, and they drive it away

from their door, drive all chance with it of regain prosperity.

they be force he to supersede they: they be bring despair,

famine, death upon a place where content and comfort have once reign.

yes, death: as you will find. surely never do great blindness than

this fall on man!



day go on, and grow into week: and Richard North be still absent.

prospect seem to be look gloomy on all side. to make matter

bad, some case of fever begin to manifest themselves at Dallory.

Dr. Rane and his brother practitioner, Mr. Seeley, only wonder that

something of the sort have not break out before.



amidst other place that wear an air of gloom be the interior of

Dallory Hall. Madam's insatiable demand for money have be very

partially respond to of late: not at all since the absence of

Richard. even she, with all her imperious scorn of whence supply

come, provide they do come, begin to realize the fact that gold can

no more be draw from exhausted coffer than blood from a stone. it

do not tend to improve her temper.



she sit one morning in what she be pleased to call her boudoir--a

charming apartment opening from her dressing-room. several letter lay

dark face. one be from Sidney at Homburg, piteously implore for

assistance--which have not recently be send he; two or three be

rather urgent demand for the payment of private account of madam's

rather long delay; one be a polite excuse from Frank Dallory and

his sister for not accept a dinner invitation. there be not a

single pleasant letter amongst they all.



"I wonder what Dick North mean by stay away like this!--and

leave order at Ticknells' that no cheque be to be cash!"

growl madam in soliloquy. "he ought to be here. he ought to force

those miserable man of his back to work, whether they will or not.

he's away; Arthur's away; Sidney's away: and with this uncertain state

of thing out of door and trouble within, the house be bad than a

dungeon. People seem to be neglect it: even Mary Dallory stay

without the gate. that girl's an artful flirt: as Matilda say

yesterday. if Arthur and Dick be back she'd come fast enough: I

should like to know which of the two she most care for. it be absurd,

though, to speak of she in conjunction with Dick North! I think I'll

go off somewhere for a time. should this suspicion of fever prove

correct, the place will not be safe. I shall want a hundred pound or

two. and Sidney must have money. he say he'll do something desperate

if I don't send it--but he have say that before. confound it all! why

do not gold grow upon tree?"



Madam's dress this morning be a striped lilac silk of amazing rustle

and richness. let it all out behind she, she go down the stair

and through the hall, sweep the dust along in a little cloud. Mr.

North be not in his parlour; madam go about look for he.



to her surprise she find he in the drawing-room; it be not often he

venture into that exclusive place. he have a shabby long coat on, and

a straw hat. Madam's scornful head go up when she see he there.



"what do you want?" she ask in a tone that plainly say he have about

as much right in the room as an unwelcome stranger.



"I have come to beg some cotton of Matilda to tie up these flower,"

be Mr. North's answer. "Thomas Hepburn's little boy be here, and I

think I'd give the child a posy."



"a posy!" repeat madam, scorn the homely term.



"I have no cotton," say Matilda, who lie back in a chair, read.

"what should bring cotton in a drawing-room?"



"oh well--I can bind it with a piece of variegate grass," say Mr.

North with resignation. "I'm sorry to have trouble you, Matilda."



"and when you have dispose of your 'posy,' I be come to your

parlour," say madam.



Mr. North groan as he go out. he know that his peace be about to

be destroy for the day. there be moment when he think heart and

brain must give way under home worry and madam's.



"when do this come?" enquire madam, point to a letter that be

her son Arthur's writing.



"this morning," shortly answer Matilda, not look up from her

book.



"yes, Arthur can write often enough to Dick. this be the second letter

that have come for he within a week. what do you do with the other?"

madam break off to ask.



"put it into Dick's room until he come home."



"but Arthur do not trouble himself to write to we, or to let we know

anything of his movement," resume madam. "we have not have a syllable

from he since he send word that old Bohun be dead. be he still in

London?--or at his aunt's?--or where?"



"I'm sure I don't know where," retort Matilda, irritated at be

interrupt.



neither do she care. Madam turn the letter over in idle curiosity:

but the postmark be not to be decipher. leave it on the

mantelpiece, she go to look after Mr. North. he stand on the lawn,

do something to a dwarf-tree of small and beautiful rose. there

be some wind to-day, and his long coat wave a little in the breeze.



"do you hear what I say--that I be come to your parlour?"

demand madam, swoop down upon he majestically. "_money must be

have_. I want it; Sidney want it; the house want it. I----"



Mr. North have straighten himself. desperation give he a little

courage.



"I would give it you if I have it. I have always give it you. but what

be to be do when I have it not? you must see that it be not my

fault, madam."



"I see that when money be need it be your place to find it," coolly

return madam. "Sidney cannot live upon air. he has----"



"it seem to I that he live upon gold," Mr. North interrupt in

querulous tone. "there's no end to it."



"Sidney must have money," equably go on madam. "I must have it, for

I purpose go away for a time. you will therefore----"



"goodness I! here's the telegraph man."



this second interruption be also from Mr. North. telegraphic message

be somewhat rare at Dallory Hall; and its master go into a

flutter. his fear fly to his well-beloved son, Dick. the messenger

be come up the broad walk, a despatch in his hand. Mr. North

advance to meet he; madam sail behind.



"it be for Captain Bohun, sir," speak up the man, perceive something

of Mr. North's agitation.



"for Captain Bohun!" interpose madam. "where's it from?"



"London, madam."



motion the messenger to go to the house for his receipt, she tear

it open without the small ceremony, and read its content:



"Dr. Williams to Arthur Bohun, Esq.:



"James Bohun be die. Sir Nash wish you to come up without delay."



look to right and leave, stand madam, her thought busy. where could

Arthur be? why have he leave London?



"do _you_ know?" she roughly ask of Mr. North.



"know what, madam?"



"where Arthur Bohun be."



Mr. North stare a little. "why, how should I know?" he ask. "it's

ever so long since Arthur write to I. he send I message when he

write to Dick."



Madam sweep into the drawing-room. she take the letter from the

mantelpiece, and coolly break its black seal. even Matilda's scruple

be arouse at this.



"oh, mamma, don't!" she exclaim, start up and put her hand

over the letter. "don't open that. it would not be right."



Madam dexterously twitch the letter away, carry it to the window

and read it from end to end. Matilda see her face turn ghastly through

its paint, as if with fright.



"serve she right," think the young lady. "Mamma, what be amiss?"



Madam crumple the letter into a ball in her agitated hand: but no

answer come from her white lip. turn abruptly up the stair, she

lock herself into her chamber.



"she be in an agony of fright--whatever the cause may be," quoth Miss

Matilda, in soliloquy.



Ere the day have close, the household be call upon to witness

madam's sudden departure by train. she go alone: and give not the

slight clue as to where she might be go, or when she would

return.



Matilda North have aptly word the paroxysm: "an agony of fright." she

might have add: a tempest of fury; for madam be in both. for that

letter have give she the news of Arthur Bohun's present locality--and

that he be by the side of Ellen Adair. what have become of Dick? the

letter ask. he must hasten and come, or he would be too late. Madam

do not understand at all. there follow a mysterious intimation to

Dick; to Dick, whom Arthur so trust and who be true as steel; it

be more obscure even than the rest; but it seem to hint at--yes, to

hint at--marriage. marriage? Madam feel her flesh creep.



"a son of mine marry _she!_" she breathe. "heaven help I to avert

the danger."



about the last woman, one would think, who ought to call for help from

Heaven.









CHAPTER XII.

an INTERRUPTION.





the tide came ripple up on the sea-shore with a monotonous, soothe

murmur. there be no wave to-day; the air be densely still; but in

the western sky little black cloud be rise, no big yet than a

man's hand; and as the weatherwise old fisherman glance to the spot,

they foretell a storm.



two people, pace the beach side by side, regard neither the sea

nor the threaten storm. need you be tell that they be Arthur Bohun

and Ellen Adair. what be the wind and the wave to they in their

happiness? amidst the misery that be soon to set in for both, the

recollection of this short time spend at Eastsea, these few week

since their love have be declare, and their marriage be

approach, would seem as an impossible dream.



the private marriage, consent to by Mrs. Cumberland, must not be

only mysteriously. the first letter be the one put aside by Miss

Matilda North, the second be that open by madam.



but there be moment when, in spite of his happiness, Arthur Bohun

have qualm of conscience for his precipitation: more especially do

they press upon he immediately after the marriage be decide upon.

for, after all, he really know nothing, or as good as nothing, of Mr.

Adair's position: and the proud Bohun blood bubble up a little, as a

thought cross he that it be just possible he might find too late

that, in point of family, hers be not fitting to have mate with his.



the human heart be treacherous: give over to self-deception, and to

sophistry. so long as a thing be covet, when it seem almost

unattainable, we see nothing but the advantage of gain it, the

happiness it must bring. but, let this desire be attain, and lo! we

veer round, and repent our haste. instantly every argument that could

bear against it, true or false, rise up within we with mocking force,

and we say, oh that I have wait before do this thing! it be that

deceitful heart of our that be in fault, nothing else; place upon

all thing its own false colouring.



at first, as they sit together under cover of the rock, or on the

more open bench on the sand, or wander to the inland walk and

seem to know as little of her father as he do.



"it be strange you don't remember more of he, Ellen!" he suddenly

she think Arthur have allude to personal appearance. but he have not

picture he."



"you know none of your Scotch relative, Ellen?"



"none."



"or where they live?"



"no."



"why do not Mr. Adair come home?"



"I don't know. he have be think of it for some year; and that's

papa's letter."



Arthur Bohun's face light up at the proposition. "yes," he say with

animation. "yes. as many as you please."



she cross the room to her desk, take out three or four letter

he detain the pretty hand as well as the letter, and take some

but the letter tell he nothing. they be evidently a gentleman's

settle.



this afternoon, when they be pace the beach, unobservant of the

cross his mind that any untoward fate can arise to mar it.



ah, might not those dark cloud have read he a lesson? just as the

small circlet out there might gather into an overwhelming storm,

before which both man and beast must bow their head, so might be

west, Arthur notice the altered aspect of the sky.



"look at those cloud, get up! I hope the weather's not go to

change for we tomorrow, Ellen. what do that mean?" he ask of a

man who be do something to his small boat, now high and dry upon

the beach.



the sailor glance up indifferently.



"it mean a storm, master."



"shall we get it here, do you think?"



"ay, sir. not till tomorrow, maybe. I fancy we shall, though"--giving

a look round, as if he could see the storm in the air. "I know there

be go to be a change."



"how do you know it?"



"Us fisherman see a storm afore it come, master. my foot tell it I

besides. I get he jammed once, and he have have the weather in he

ever since."



they walk on. "that will be two untoward event for we," remark

could mar their happiness. "we want a third to complete it, don't we,

ellen?"



"what be the two?"



"the bad weather threaten for tomorrow; and Dick's non-arrival be

the other. I be vex at that."



for, on this same morning, Mrs. Cumberland have receive a letter from

that beyond all doubt fever be break out at Dallory, though it be

of a malignant type, and he feel glad that his mother be away. bessy

be well, and send her love.



"will you rest a little before go in?"



they be pass the favourite old seat under the rock. Ellen

heaven become altogether overshadow it would have be as nothing

to all intent and purpose they seem as entirely alone in this

have any one pass, they would have be see sit as decorously

apart as though they have quarrel: but the shore seem desert

this afternoon.



the arrangement for the marriage be as follows:--At half-past eleven

o'clock, Arthur, Ellen, and Mrs. Cumberland would enter the little

church by a private door, and the ceremony would take place. Richard

North be to have give she away, but that be over now. Arthur hold

the licence; he have make a friend of the clergyman, and all would be

do quietly. he and Ellen be to go away for a few day; she would

then return home with Mrs. Cumberland, and be to the world still as

Miss Adair. after that, Arthur would take his own time, and be guide

by circumstance for declare the marriage: but he mean, if

possible, to at once introduce Ellen to his aunt, Miss Bohun.



and Ellen Adair? not a scruple rest on her mind, not a doubt

or hesitation on her heart; her father have give his cordial

approbation--as express in the letter to Mrs. Cumberland--and she

be full of peace.



"do you feel that, Ellen?"



a faint, quivering breeze have seem to pass over they with sudden

sharpness, and to die away in a moan.



some white sail out at sea flap a little, and the boat turn

homeward.



"we have well be go, too, my love; or we may have it upon we."



she rise as he speak, and they walk away. the sky be grow

darker; the shade of evening be begin to gather. Mrs.

Cumberland have be lie down and be dress, the maid say--if

Captain Bohun would wait. Ellen take off her bonnet and mantle.



"whilst we be alone, let I see that I have not make a mistake in the

size, Ellen."



take from his pocket a bit of tissue-paper, he unfold it and

disclose a wedding-ring. Ellen blush vividly as he try it on.

"I--thought," she timidly begin, "that you mean this to be my

wedding-ring"--indicating the plain gold one she habitually wear on

"no. Rane buy that one. this will be mine."



it fit exactly. Captain Bohun have not allow for the probability

when he put it on again. this be time the third.



"if this should ever become too small for I?" she murmur, as he

"oh, but that--if ever--won't be for age and age."



not for age, and age! if, in their innocent unconsciousness, they

could only have see the cruel fate that be already coil its

the storm do not come that night. but whether, in revenge for the

delay, it choose to expend itself with double violence, certain it be

that such a storm have seldom be see at Eastsea as rage in the

morning. the sky be lurid and angry; the sea toss itself in great

at interval: all nature seem at warfare.



in much distress lie Mrs. Cumberland. exceedingly subject of late to

he find she in tear. a curious eight in so self-contain a woman.



"I cannot help it, Captain Bohun: indeed I cannot. have not the

marriage well be put off for a day? I may be well tomorrow."



"certainly not," he answer. "why should it be put off? I be very

sorry for Ellen's sake; she would have feel happy have you be in

"her father and mother be my dear friend. it seem as though I

Arthur Bohun laugh. he would not listen to a word--be it likely

but this illness of Mrs. Cumberland's make a slight alteration in the

"no. I think you have well not."



"my darling, you _will come?_"



do a doubt cross he, that he should say this? but she answer that

have the fly multiply itself into two flies?--and be they

squabble for precedence? certainly two be there: and the one wet

driver be abuse the other wet driver for hold his place before

the door, and not allow he to draw up to it.



"Arthur! Good Heavens, how fortunate I be! Arthur Bohun! don't you see

I?"



every drop of blood in Arthur Bohun's vein seem to stand still and

Madam, drive hastily from the railway-station, have come to bear he

tell he: she would have go to church and forbid the bann. he

step to the fly door.



in afterlife, he could never clearly recall these next few minute.

Madam speak of the telegram that have be receive at Dallory. she

say--give to matter her own colouring--that James Bohun be in

extremity; that he only wait to see Arthur to die; that he be

London on receipt of the telegram, and have now come down to fetch he.



"step in, Arthur. we must catch the quarter-to-twelve train."



"I--I cannot go," he answer.



"not go!" scream madam. "but I command you to go. would you disobey

the last wish of a die man?"



well, no; he feel that he could not do that. "a quarter to twelve?" he

say rather dreamily. "you must wait, madam, whilst I speak to Mrs.

Cumberland. there's plenty of time."



he go in with his tale, and up to Mrs. Cumberland, as one in a

dream. he be force to go, he bewail, but not for more than a day,

when he should be back to complete the marriage. what could she

answer? in her bewilderment, she scarcely understand what have

happen. leap downstairs again, he close the door of the

sitting-room upon himself and Ellen, and clasp she to his heart.



"my darling! but for this, you would have be on your way to become

my wife. come what may, Ellen, I shall be down again within a few

hour. God bless you, my love! take care of these."



they be the ring and licence; he hand they to she lest he might

lose they. before Ellen could recover herself, whilst yet her face be

Crafty madam! wait in the fly at the door and make she

observation, she have read what the sign mean almost as surely as

it would not do to risk mar it. but when they be in the train,

and the whistle have sound, and they be fairly off, then madam

speak. they have the compartment to themselves.



"Arthur, you cannot deceive I: any attempt to do so would be useless.

you be about to marry Ellen Adair."



she speak quietly, almost affectionately; when the bosom be beat

with a horrible dread, it produce calmness of manner rather than
