doubt as to whether it should be avowal or evasion, but not for

long. as it have come to this, why he must take his standing, he

raise his head proudly.



"Eight, mother. I be go to wed Ellen Adair."



Madam's pulse begin to beat nineteen to the dozen. her head grow hot,

her hand cold.



"you _be_, you mean, Arthur."



"yes. put it as you like. what be interrupt to-day, will be

conclude tomorrow. as soon as I have see James, I shall return to

Eastsea."



"Arthur! Arthur Bohun! it must never be conclude, never."



"Pardon I, mother. I be my own master."



"a Bohun may not wed shame and disgrace."



"shame and disgrace cannot attach to _her_. Madam, I must beg you to

remember that in a few hour that young lady will be my wife. do not

try my temper too sorely."



"no, not to she, but to her father," panted madam--and Arthur feel

frightened, he know not why, at her strong emotion. "would you wed the

daughter of a--a----"



Madam pause. Arthur look at she; his compressed lip tremble just

a little.



"of a what, mother? pray go on."



"of everything that be bad. a forger. a convict."



there be a dead pause. nothing to be hear but the whirling train.

"A--what?" gasp Captain Bohun, when he could get back his breath.



"a convict," burst forth madam in a scream; for her agitation be

become irrepressible. "why do you make I repeat painful thing?"



"Mother! of whom do you speak?"



"of her father: William Adair."



he fall back in the carriage as one who be shoot. as one from whom life

and all that can make it sweet, have suddenly go out for ever.









CHAPTER XIII.

PANIC.





the funeral be go about in Dallory. Dr. Rane's prognostication

have prove correct; the fever be severe. it spread, and a panic set

in.



as yet it have be confine to the poor. to those who for some month

now have be live in despair and poverty. some call it a famine

fever; some a relapse fever; some typhus fever: but, whatever the

name accord to it, one thing be certain--it be of a malignant and

fatal type.



it possess a somewhat singular feature: it have seem to break out

all at once--in a single night. before the doctor have well

ascertain that anything of the kind be in the air, before most of

the public have so much as hear of it, it come upon they. the

probability of course be that it have be smoulder for some day.

on the afternoon that witness madam's departure from Dallory

Hall--after the receipt of the telegram and the reading of Dick's

letter--there have not be one decide case: in the morning no less

than seven case have show themselves. after that, it spread rapidly.



Madam remain away. James Bohun be dead, and she stay with Sir

Nash. Matilda North, take French leave, go up to join she without

an invitation; she do not care to stay in the midst of the sickness.

so the master of Dallory Hall be alone, and enjoy his liberty as

much as trouble have leave he any capacity for enjoyment.



a week or ten day have pass on since the outbreak, and the funeral

be go about Dallory. the two medical man, Dr. Rane and Mr.

Seeley, be work nearly off their leg. the panic be at its

height. Dallory have be an exceptionally healthy place: people be

not use to this state of thing, and grow frightened. some of the

well family take flight, for the seaside, or elsewhere. the

long-continue distress, result on the strike, have predispose the

poor class for it. it be they whom it chiefly attack, but there

be now two or three case amongst their better. this be no time

for the medical man to speculate whether they should or should not be

pay; they put all such consideration aside, and give the poor

sufferer their good care. Dr. Rane in particular be tenderly

assiduous with his patient. in spite of that fatal letter and the

mistake--nay, the sin--it involve, he be a humane man. be he a

successful practitioner, make his hundred or his thousand a-year,

as might be, he would be one of the first and readiest to give away

largely of his time and skill to any who could not afford to pay he.



the last person whom the fever have attack be one of the brother

Hepburn, of Dallory, undertaker, carpenter, and coffin-maker. both

be sickly man, but very steady and respectable. the young brother,

Henry, be the one seize: it be universally assume that he catch

it in the discharge of certain of the duty of his calling, and the

supposition do not tend to decrease the public panic. Dr. Rane

think he a bad subject for the illness, and do all he could for

he.



Bessy Rane stand in her kitchen, make an apple pudding. it be rather

a sudden transition of subject, from sickness to pudding, but only in

accordance with life. whatever calamity may be decimate society

around, the domestic routine of existence go on at home in its

ordinary course. molly Green be pudding-maker in general: but molly

be hasten over her other work that day, for she have obtain leave

to go home in the evening to see her mother: a woman who have be

ail for year with chronic illness, and live at Whitborough. so

Bessy this morning take the pudding upon herself.



Mrs. Rane stand at the table; a brown holland apron tie over her

light morning gown, her sleeve turn up to the middle of her

delicate arm. hand and wrist and arm be alike pretty and

refine. the apple be in a basin, ready pare, and she be roll

out the crust. ever and anon she glance at the kitchen clock. she

husband have be call out at four o'clock that morning, and she be

grow a little anxious. now it be close upon eleven. it cannot be

say that Bessy be afraid of the fever for he: she share in the

popular belief that medical man be generally exempt from infection;

but she be always glad to see he arrive home safe and well.



his latch-key be hear in the door whilst she be think of he.

Dr. Rane go straight up to the unused top-room, change his clothe,

and wash his hand and face--a precaution he always take when he have

be with fever patient. Bessy put the kitchen-door open, that he

might see she when he come down.



"pudding-making, Bessy!" he cry, look in. "why don't you let

molly do that?"



"Molly's busy. she want to go home this evening, Oliver, as soon as

we can spare she, and will not come back until tomorrow night. she

receive a letter this morning to say her mother have at last take to

her bed, and the doctor think she very ill. I have give her leave to

go."



"but how shall you manage without she?"



"I shall have old Phillis in. molly have be to she, and she say

she'll be glad to come."



Dr. Rane say no more. it be quite the same to he whether molly or

Phillis do what be want. when man be harass in spirit, they

cannot concern themselves with the petty detail of domestic life.



"I be think, Oliver, that--if you don't mind--as we can have

Phillis, I would leave it to Molly whether to come back tomorrow

night, or not. if her mother be really grow bad, the girl may

like to stay a day long with she."



"my dear, do just as you like about it," be the doctor's rather

impatient answer.



"your breakfast shall be ready in a moment, Oliver."



"I have take breakfast. it be between eight and nine before I could

get away from Ketler's, and I go and beg some of Mrs. Gass. after

that I go the round of the patient."



Bessy be put the crust into the basin. she lift her hand and

turn in some dismay.



"surely, Oliver, they have not get the fever at Ketler's!"



Dr. Rane laugh slightly. "not the fever, bessy: something else. the

baby. it be Ketler who call I up this morning."



"oh dear," say Bessy, go on with her pudding. "I think that poor

baby be not expect for a month or two. how will they manage to keep

it? it seem to I that the less food there be for they, the quick

the baby come."



"that's generally the case," observe Dr. Rane.



"be the mother well?"



"tolerably so."



"and--how be the other thing go on, Oliver?"



he know, by the tone of her voice, that she mean the fever. bessy

never speak of that without a kind of timidity.



"neither well nor bad. it's very bad still."



"and fatal?"



"yes, and fatal. Henry Hepburn be in danger."



"but he will get over it?" rejoin Bessy quickly.



"I don't think so. his brother will have it next if he do not mind.

he be as nervous over it as he can be. I be off now, Bessy, up the

Ham."



"you will be in to dinner?"



"before that, I hope."



Bessy settle to her pudding again, and the doctor depart. not into

danger this time, for the fever have not yet show itself in Dallory

Ham. Scarcely a minute have elapse when the door-bell rang, and molly

go to answer it. Mrs. Rane, her hand all flour, peep from the

kitchen, and see Mr. North.



"oh papa! how glad I be to see you! do you mind come in here?"



Mind! Mr. North feel far more at home in Bessy's kitchen than in his

wife's grand drawing-room. he have bring a small open basket of

lovely hot-house flower for Bessy. he put it on the table, and sit

down on one of the wooden chair in peace and comfort. Richard have not

return, and he be still alone.



"go on with your pudding, my dear. don't mind I. I like to see it."



"it's all but do, papa. molly will tie it up. oh, these beautiful

flower!" she add, bend down to they. "how kind of you to think

of I!"



"I'm go to Ham Court about some seed, child; the walk will do I

good, this pleasant day. I feel strong and well, Bessy, than I

do."



"I be so glad of that, papa."



"and so I think--as I intend to call in here--that I would cut a

few blossom, and bring they with I. how's the fever get on,

bessy?"



"it be not any well, I be afraid, papa."



"so I hear. they say that Henry Hepburn's dying."



Bessy feel startle. "oh, I trust not! though I think--I fear--Oliver

have not very much hope of he."



"well, I've hear it. and I come here, Bessy, to ask if you would not

like to come to the Hall for a week or two. it might be safe for you.

be you at all afraid of catch it, child?"



"N--o," answer Bessy. but it be speak doubtfully, and Mr. North

look at she.



"your husband have to be amongst it pretty well every hour of his life,

and I can only think there must be some risk in it for you. you have

well come to the Hall."



"Oliver be very careful to change his clothe when he come in; hut

still I know there must of course be some little risk," she say. "I

try to be quite brave, and not think of it, papa: and I have a great

piece of camphor here"--touching the bosom of her dress--"at which

Oliver laugh."



"which be as good as confess that you be nervous about it, Bessy,"

say Mr. North.



"not very, pupa. a doctor's wife, you know, must not have fancy."



"well, come up to the Hall to-day, Bessy. it will be a change for you,

and pleasant for I, now I'm alone; it will be like some of the old

day come back again, you and I together. as to Oliver, I dare say

he'll be glad to have the house to himself a bit, whilst he be so

busy."



Bessy, wipe the flour off her hand, consent. in point of fact,

her husband have propose, some day ago, that she should go away: and

she do feel half afraid of take the fever through he.



"but it cannot be until tomorrow, papa," she say, as Mr. North rise

to depart, and she accompany he to the door, explain that molly

be go home. "I should not like to leave Oliver alone in the house

for the night. Phillis will be here tomorrow: she can stay and sleep,

should Molly Green not return."



"very well," say Mr. North.



so it be leave. Bessy open the door for her father, and watch he

on his way up the Ham.



Dr. Rane come back to dinner; and find his patient allow he an

hour's peace for it. Bessy inform he of the arrangement she have

make: and that he be to be a bachelor from the morrow for an

indefinite period. the doctor laugh, make a jest of it:

nevertheless he glance keenly from under his eyelid at his wife.



"Bessy! I do believe you be afraid!"



"no, not exactly," be her answer: "I don't think 'afraid' be the

right word. it be just this, Oliver: I do not get nervous about it;

but I cannot help remember rather often that you may bring it home

to I."



"then, my dear, go--go by all mean where you will be out of harm's

way, so far as I be concern."



Dinner over, Dr. Rane hasten out again, on his way to see Mrs.

Ketler. he have just reach that bench in the shady part of the road

at the neck of the Ham, when he see Jelly come along. the doctor

only wish there be some shelter to dart into, by which he might

avoid she. ever since the night when he have hear that agreeable

conversation as he sit under the cedar-tree, Jelly's keen green eye

have be bad than poison to he. she stop when she meet he.



"so that child of Susan Ketler's be come, sir!"



"ay," say Dr. Rane.



"what in the world bring it here now?"



"well, I don't know," return the doctor. "child often come

without give their friend due notice. I be on my way there."



"and not as much as a bed gown to wrap it in," resentfully go on

Jelly, "and not a bit of tea or oatmeal in the place for she! my

faith! baby after baby come into the world, and the man out on

strike! this make seven--if they'd all be alive: she'll be

content perhaps when she have seventeen."



"it be the way of the world, Jelly. Set up the child first, and

consider what to do with they afterwards."



"what's this that's the matter with Tim Wilks, sir?" demand Jelly,

abruptly change the subject.



"with Tim Wilks! I do not know that anything be the matter with

he."



"yes, there be," say Jelly. "I meet old Green just now, and he say

Timothy Wilks be in bed ill. they think it might be a bilious

attack, if it be not the fever."



"I'll call in and see he," say Dr. Rane. "have he be drink

again?"



Jelly's eye flash with resentment. consider that Tim have really

keep sober and steady for the past year and a half, she look on the

question as a frightful aspersion. more especially so as proceed

from Dr. Rane.



"_i_ can answer for it that he have not be drink--and so, as I

suppose, might everybody else," be her tart reply. "Timothy Wilks be

worried, sir; that's what it be. he have never be at ease since

people accuse he of write that anonymous letter: and he never will

be till he be publicly clear of it. Sir, I think he ought to be

clear."



be it an ice-bolt that seem to shoot through Oliver Rane's

heart?--or only a spasm? something take it: though he manage to keep

his countenance, and to speak with quiet indifference.



"Cleared? clear of what? I fancy it have be ascertain that

wilk be the man who speak of the affair out of Dale's office. he

cann't clear himself from that. as to any other suspicion, no one have

cast it on he."



"well, sir--of course _you know good_," answer Jelly, recollect

herself and cool down: but she could not help emphasize the

word. "if Tim should become dangerously ill, it might have to be do

to set his mind at rest."



"what might have to be do?" demand Dr. Rane with authority.



and Jelly do not dare to answer the direct question. she could boast

and talk _at_ people in her gossip way as long as she feel safe,

but when it come to anything like prove her word, she be a very

coward. Dr. Rane be look at she, wait for she to speak, his

manner stern and uncompromising.



"oh well, sir, I'm sure I don't know," she say, feel as if her

throat have dry up. "and I'm sure I hope poor Tim have not get the

fever."



"I'll call and see he," repeat Dr. Rane, proceed on his way.

Jelly curtsy and go on her.



when beyond her view, he take out his handkerchief and wipe his face,

damp as with the dew of death. he must, he must get away from Jelly

and Dallory! but for have a wife on his hand, he might have felt

tempt to make a hasty flitting to America and join Dr. Jones. join

Dr. Jones? but how obtain the fund to do it with? his thought

turn, as they ever do on these occasion, to that money of his

lock up in the Tontine. _of his_: that be how Dr. Rane have come to

regard it. that money would bring he salvation. if he could only

obtain it----



a bow from some white-haired old gentleman, pass in a carriage. Dr.

Rane return it, the singular coincidence of his appearance at that

moment flash through his mind. for it be Sir Thomas Ticknell. yes:

it truly seem that that Tontine money would be nothing less than

salvation to he. he go on with a great fear and pain in his

throb heart, wonder for how long or how short a time Jelly

would keep her counsel.



the next morning be Thursday. it bring news that almost strike

people dumb: Henry Hepburn, the undertaker, be dead, and Mrs. Rane

have be seize with the fever. Dr. Rane's account be, that his wife

have be very restless all night; he give she a compose draught,

which seem to be of use for the time: but upon attempt to get up

she be attack with nausea and faintness, and have to go back to bed.

the symptom that subsequently set in he fear be those of fever.



it be an awkward time for Bessy to be ill, as Molly Green have go

homo: but Phillis, an excellent substitute, be there. she attend on

Mrs. Rane, and the doctor go abroad to his patient. Mr. North,

disappoint at Bessy's non-arrival, hearing of her indisposition,

come to the house; but Bessy send down an urgent message by Phillis,

beg he not to run any danger by come up to her chamber. and Mr.

North, docile and obedient--as madam in her imperiousness have train

he to be--leave his good love, and go home again.



in the course of the morning Dr. Rane call in at Hepburn's. it be a

double shop and house; in the one be sell article of furniture, in

the other the carpenter's work be carry on. Thomas Hepburn and his

family live in the former; Henry, now dead, have occupy the latter.

he be a married man, but have no child. when Dr. Rane enter the

second shop, he do not at first see Thomas Hepburn; the shutter up

at the window make the place dark, come in from the bright sunshine.

Thomas Hepburn see he, however, and come forward from the workshop

behind, where he have be look on at his man. various article

seem to be in the course of active construction, coffin amongst the

rest.



"I be very sorry for this loss, Hepburn," begin the doctor.



"well, sir, I've not have any hope from the first," sigh Hepburn, his

face look careworn and unusually sickly in the half light. "I don't

think poor Henry have."



"the fact be, Hepburn, he have not strength to carry he through the

disorder; it do not attack he lightly. I do all I could."



"yes, sir, I'm sure of that," return Hepburn--and what with his

naturally weak voice, and the hammering that be go on behind, Dr.

Rane have to listen with all his ear to catch the word. "we've be

an ail family always: liable to take disorder, too, more than

other."



Dr. Rane make no reply for the moment. he be look at the speaker.

something in his aspect suggest the suspicion that the man be in

actual fear himself.



"you must keep up a good heart, you know, Hepburn."



"I'd rather go a hundred mile, sir, than do what I've get to do just

now amidst the dead," say Hepburn, glance round, "_that's_ how my

brother take it."



"let the workman go instead of you."



the undertaker shake his head. "one _have_ to go with I; and the other

be just as afraid as can be. no, I must go on myself. there'll be

double work for I, now Henry's go."



"well, Hepburn, I begin to think the fever be on the turn," say the

doctor cheerily, as he walk away.



the day wear on. Mrs. Rane's symptom be decidedly those of fever,

and the doctor go all the way to Whitborough himself: not far in

point of distance, only that he could not well spare the time: to tell

molly Green she be to keep where she be, out of harm's way, and not

return until send for. when he return home his wife be bad.

Phillis meet he at the door, and say her poor mistress's face be

scarlet, and she roll her head from side to side. Phillis want to

remain the night, but the doctor would not have it: there be no

necessity for it, he say, and she have well not be subject to

infection more than could be help. so Phillis go away at ten

o'clock.



between eleven and twelve, just as Mr. Seeley be prepare for rest,

Dr. Rane come in and ask he to go over to see his wife. the surgeon

go at once. Bessy be lie in her comfortable chamber, just as

Phillis have describe--her face scarlet, her head turn uneasily on

the pillow. a candle stand on the table, dimly light the room; Mr.

Seeley take it close to inspect her face; but Bessy put up her hand

and turn her head away, as if the light disturb she.



"she seem slightly delirious," whisper Mr. Seeley apart, and Dr.

Rane nod. after that, the two doctor talk together a little on

the stair, and Mr. Seeley go away, say he would come again in

the morning.



in the morning, however, Dr. Rane go over to tell he that his wife,

after a most restless night, have drop into a quiet doze, and have

well not be disturb. he feel sure she be well. this be Friday.



Phillis arrive betime. she find a wet sheet flap in the grey

ante-room, just outside the bedroom door, which Dr. Rane have saturate

with disinfect fluid. Jars of disinfectant stand on the wide

landing, on the staircase, and in other part of the house. Phillis

have no fear, and go in behind the flap sheet. she could make

nothing of Mrs. Rane. instead of the scarlet face and restless head,

she now lie bury in her pillow, still, and pale, and intensely

quiet. Phillis offer she some tea; Mrs. Rane just open her eye,

and feebly motion it away with her hand, just as she have motion

away the light the previous night. "it's a sudden change," think

Phillis. "I don't like it."



later in the morning, Dr. Rane bring up Mr. Seeley. she lie in

exactly the same position, deep in the pillow. what with that, and

what with the large night-cap, the surgeon could get to see very

little of her face.



"don't disturb I," she faintly say, when he would have arouse she

sufficiently to get a good look. "I be easy now."



"do you know I?" question Mr. Seeley, bend over she.



"yes," she answer, open her eye for a moment. "let I sleep; I

shall be well tomorrow."



"how do you feel?" he ask.



"only tired. let I sleep."



"Bessy," say her husband, in the persuasive voice he use to the

sick, "willn't you just turn to Mr. Seeley?"



"to-morrow. I want to sleep."



and so they do not disturb she far. after all, sleep do

wonder, as Dr. Rane remark.



it might have be that Mr. Seeley go away somewhat puzzle,

scarcely think that the fever have be on she sufficiently long to

leave she in this state of exhaustion.



as the day go on a rumour be whisper that Mrs. Rane be die.

whence it arise none could trace, unless from a word or two drop by

Dr. Rane himself to Thomas Hepburn. they happen to meet in the

street, and the undertaker stop to inquire after Mrs. Rane. she be

in a most critical state, be the doctor's answer; the night would

decide it, one way or the other.



Phillis go up to her mistress several time. Dr. Rane keep the

hang sheet well saturate, and flap it often. Mrs. Rane never

seem to rouse herself throughout the day: seem, in fact, to sleep

through it. Phillis begin to hope that it be indeed comfortable,

refreshing rest, and that she would wake from it well.



"you'll let I stay here to-night, sir?" she say, when there be

nothing more to be do, as Dr. Rane--who have be out--come in, and

pass by the kitchen.



"no need," he answer in his decisive manner. "be here the first

thing in the morning."



Phillis put on her shawl and bonnet, wish he goodnight, and

depart. it be about ten o'clock. Dr. Rane see she out and go up

to the sick room. in less than five minute he come down again with a

white face, open the front-door, and stride across the road to Mr.

Seeley's. the latter be in his surgery, in the act of pour some

medicine into a small phial.



"Seeley! Seeley! my wife be go!"



what with the suddenness of the interruption, and the word, the

surgeon be so startled that he drop the bottle.



"Gone!" he cry. "do you mean _dead?_"



"I do."



"why, when I see you at dusk, you tell I she be sleep

comfortably!" say the surgeon, stare at Dr. Rane. "Phillis also

say it."



"and so she be. she be to all appearance. Heaven be my witness that

I think and believe the sleep then to be natural, and be

refresh she. she must have die in it. I go up now, and find

she--find she--go."



Oliver Rane put his arm on Mr. Seeley's counter and bend his face to

hide his emotion. the surgeon in the midst of his surprise, have hardly

ever feel so sorry for any one as he feel in that moment for his

brother practitioner.









CHAPTER XIV.

what JELLY SAW.





"it be too true; Mrs. Rane be dead," say sympathize people one to

the other; for even that same night the sad tiding go partially out

to Dallory. what with the death of Hepburn the undertaker, and now the

doctor's wife--both prominent people, as might be say, in connection

with the sickness--something like consternation fall on those who

hear it. Dr. Rane carry the news himself to Dallory Hall, catch

Mr. North just as he be go to bed, and impart it to he in the

most gentle and soothing manner in his power. fear that if leave

until morning, it might reach he abruptly, the doctor have thus make

haste. from thence he go on to Hepburn's. he have chance to meet

Francis Dallory in come out of Seeley's; he meet some one else he

know; these carry the tiding to other; so that many hear of it

that night.



but now we come to a strange and singular thing that happen to

Jelly. jelly in her tart way be sufficiently good-hearted. there be

sickness in Ketler's house: the wife have her three day' old infant:

the little girl, Cissy, grow bad and weak: and Jelly choose to

sacrifice an afternoon to nurse they. much as she disapprove of the

man's join the Trades' Union and uphold the strike, often as she

have assure he that both starve and the workhouse, whichever he

might prefer, be too good for he, now that misfortune lie upon the

house, Jelly come-to a little. Susan Ketler be her cousin; and, after

all, she be not to blame for her husband's wrong doing. accordingly,

in the afternoon of the last day of Mrs. Rane's illness, Jelly go

forth to Ketler's, arm with some beef-tea, and a few scrap for the

half-famish child, the whole enclose in a reticule.



"I shall take the latch-key," she say, in start, to the cook, who

be commonly call Dinah, "so you can go to bed. if Susan Ketler's

very ill I shall stop late. mind you put a box of match on the slab

in the hall."



Susan Ketler be not very ill, Jelly find; but the child, Cissy, be.

so ill, that Jelly hardly know whether to leave she at all, or not.

the mother could not attend to she; Ketler have go tramp off

beyond Whitborough after Union work, and have not return. only that

she think Mrs. Cumberland would not be pleased if she come to hear

that Jelly, the confidential servant in charge, have stay out for a

night, leave the house with only the cook in it, she have certainly

remain. at past twelve poor Ketler arrive home, dead beat, sick,

faint, having walk several mile without food. Jelly blow he up a

little: she consider that the man who could refuse work when his

child be starve, because he belong to the Trades' Union,

deserve nothing but blow-up: bade he look to Cissy, tell he

ungraciously that there be a loaf in the pan, and depart. Ketler,

ready to drop though he be, civilly offer to see her home; but all

the thank he receive in return, be a recommendation to attend to

his own concern and not to meddle with her.



it be a fine, still night, rather too warm for the illness that have

fall upon Dallory; and Jelly walk on at a swift pace, her

reticule, empty now, on her arm. some woman might have feel timid at

the midnight walk: Jelly be too strong-minded to feel anything of the

sort. she certainly find it a little lonely on enter the Ham, as

if the road under the overshadow tree, begin now to lose some

of their leave, have something weird about it. but this part be soon

pass; and Jelly come to the house, and within sight of home. not a

soul meet she: it be as dreary, as far as human companionship go, as

it could be. a black cat spring suddenly from the hedge, and tore

across the road almost touch Jelly's foot; and it make her start.



she begin think about Mrs. Rane; quite unconscious of the death

that have take place. when Jelly leave home in the afternoon Mrs. Rane

be say to be in danger: at least such be Phillis's opinion,

privately communicate: but, late in the evening, news have be

bring to Keller's that all danger be over. Mrs. Rane be in a

refreshing sleep, and go on safely to recovery.



"and I'm downright glad of it, poor young lady!" say Jelly, half

aloud, as she turn in at her gate. "doctor' wife be naturally

more exposed to the chance of catch infectious illness. but on

the other hand they have the good advice and care at hand."



it be strike one. let herself in with the latch-key, Jelly feel

for the box of match, pass her hand cautiously over the marble

table. and pass it in vain: no match be there.



"forgetful hussy!" ejaculate Jelly, apostrophize the unconscious

Dinah. "much good she's of!"



so Jelly creep quietly upstairs in the dark, know she have match

in her own chamber: and in a minute come upon another of the negligent

Dinah's delinquency. she have omit to draw down the blind of the

large window on the landing.



"she have be out at that back-door, talk to people," quoth Jelly

in her wrath. "just like she! willn't she catch it from I in the

morning!"



turn to draw the blind herself, she be suddenly arrest, with the

cord in her hand, by something on the opposite landing, at Dr. Rane's.

stand there, dress in something white, which Jelly at the time

thought look like a nightgown, be Mrs. Rane. the landing be

faintly light, as if by some distant candle; but Mrs. Rane be

perfectly visible, her feature and even their expression quite clear.

the first thought that cross Jelly be, that Mrs. Rane be

delirious: but she look too still for that. she do not move; and

the eye gaze with a fix stare, as it seem to Jelly. but that she

herself must have be invisible in the surround darkness, she

would have think Mrs. Rane be stare at _her_. for a full minute

this last: Jelly watching, Mrs. Rane never move.



"what in the world bring she stand there?" quoth Jelly in her

amazement. "and what can she be stare at? it cann't be at I."



but at that moment Jelly's bag slip from her arm, and fall on the

carpet. it cause she to remove her gaze from the opposite landing for

a single second--it really do not seem long. when she look again,

the place be in darkness: Mrs. Rane and the faint light have both

disappear.



"she have no business to be out of her bed--and the doctor ought to

tell she so if he's at home," think Jelly. "anyway, she must be a

great deal well: for I don't think it's delirium."



she wait a short time, but nothing more be see. draw down the

blind, Jelly pick up her bag, and pass on to her own chamber--one

of the back room on this first floor. there she sleep undisturbed

until morning.



she do not get up until late. be amenable to no one whilst Mrs.

Cumberland be away, the house's mistress in fact, as well as Dinah's,

Jelly do not hurry herself. she be not lazy in general, especially

on a Saturday, but as she feel tired after her weary afternoon at

Ketler's and from having go so late to rest. breakfast be ready in

the kitchen when she go down; Dinah--a red-faced young woman in a

brown-spot cotton gown--be busy at the fire with the coffee.



"now then!" begin Jelly--her favourite phrase when she be angry.

"what have you to say for yourself? whereabout on the slab do you

put those match last night?"



Dinah, take-to, tilt the kettle back. until that moment she have not

think of her negligence.



"I'm afraid I never put 'em at all," she say.



"no you _don't_ put 'em," retort Jelly with sharp emphasis. "but for

have match and a candle in my room, I must have undress in the

dark. and I should like to know why you don't put 'em; and what you

be about not to do it?"



"I'm sure I'm sorry," say Dinah, who be a tractable sort of girl. "I

forget it, I suppose, in the upset about poor Mrs. Rane."



"in the upset about poor Mrs. Rane," scornfully repeat Jelly. "what

upset you, pray, about her?--And you've never be out to fasten back

the shutter!"



"she's dead," answer Dinah--and the tear come into the girl's eye.

"that's what I've get the shutter half-to for. I think you'd most

likely not have hear it."



a little confusion arise in Jelly's mind. thought be rapid. Mrs.

Rane's death, as she suppose, could not possibly have occur before

morning: the neglect, as to the match, be last night. but, in the

present shock she pass this over. her sharp tone disappear as by

magic: her expression change to sadness.



"dead? when do she die, Dinah?"



"it be about nine o'clock last night, they think. and she lie an hour

after that in her bed, Jelly, before it be find out."



on hear this, Jelly's first impression be that Dinah must be

trifle with she. the girl come from the fire with the coffee, the

tear visible.



"now what d'ye mean, girl? Mrs. Rane don't die last night--as I can

answer for."



"oh but she do, Jelly. Dr. Rane go up to she at ten o'clock--he have

be out till then--and find she dead. I can tell you, I don't half

like go all the way up to bed by myself to that top floor, and I

alone in the house, know she be lie there at the very next

door."



Jelly pause to take in the full sense of the word, stare the while

at Dinah. what could it all mean?



"you must have take leave of your sense," she say, as she begin to

pour out the coffee.



"I'm sure I've not," return Dinah. "why?"



"to tell I Mrs. Rane die last night. how do you pick up the tale?"



"Jelly, it's no tale. it's as true as you and I's here. I be

stand at the front gate for a breath of air, before shut-up,

when Dr. Rane come out of his house in a hurry, and go across to Mr.

Seeley's. it strike I that Mrs. Rane might be bad and that he have

go to fetch the other, so I stay a bit to see. presently--it

ben't long--he come back across the road again. Mr. Francis Dallory

happen to be pass, and he ask after Mrs. Rane. she be dead,

the doctor say; and go on to tell he how he have find she. you

needn't look as if you think I be make-up story, Jelly. they

stand close by the doctor's gate, and I hear every word."



Jelly do not precisely know how she look. if this be true,

why--what could be the meaning of what she have see in the night?



"she cann't be dead?"



"she be," say Dinah. "why should you dispute it?"



Jelly do riot say why. she drink her hot coffee, and go out. she

do not believe it. Dinah evidently do: but the girl might have

catch up some wrong story.



the first thing that strike Jelly, when outside, be the appearance of

the doctor's house. it be closely shut up, door and window, and the

blind be down. as Jelly stand, look up, she see Mr. Seeley

stand at his door without his hat. she go over and accost he.



"be it true, sir, that Mrs. Rane be dead?"



"quite true," be the answer. "she die yesterday evening, poor lady.

it be terribly sudden."



Jelly feel a very queer sensation come over she. but she be still

full of disbelief. Mr. Seeley be call from within, and Jelly

return and knock softly at Dr. Rane's door. Phillis open it, her

eye red with cry.



"Phillis, what be all this?" demand Jelly, in low tone. "when do

she die?"



"stop," interpose Phillis, bar her entrance. "you'd well not

come in. I be not afraid: and, for the matter of that, somebody must

be here: but it ben't well for those to run risk that needn't. the

doctor say it be the quick and most malignant case of they all."



"I never catch any disorder in my life, and I don't fear that I ever

shall," answer Jelly, quietly make her way to the kitchen. "_when_

do she die, Phillis?"



"about nine o'clock last evening, as be think. the minute and hour

will never be know for sure: at ten, when the doctor find she, she

be get cold. and for we below to have think she quietly

sleep!" wound up Phillis with a sob.



the queer sensation increase. Jelly have never experience anything

like it in her whole life. she stand against the dresser, stare

helplessly at Phillis.



"I don't _think_ she could have die last evening," whisper Jelly

presently.



"and I'm sure I as little thought she be die," return Phillis.

"the last time I go up be about half-after seven: she be asleep

then; that I'm positive of; and it seem a good healthy sleep, for

the breathing be as regular as could be. sometime after eight

o'clock, master go up: he come down and say she be still sleep,

and he hope she'd sleep till morning, and I'd well not go up again

for fear of disturb she. I don't go up, Jelly. I know if she wake

and want anything she'd ring: the bell-rope be to her hand. Master

go out to a patient, and I clear up the kitchen here. he come in

at ten o'clock. I be ready to go, but ask he if I should stay all

night. there be no need, he answer, missis be well; and I

go. I never hear nothing more till I come this morning. the milkman

get to the door just as I do; and he begin say what a sad thing it

be that she have die. 'who have die,' I ask he, and he say, 'why,

my missis.' Jelly, you might have knock I down with a breath of

wind."



by Jelly's look at this moment, it seem as if a breath of wind

might have do the same for she. her face and lip have turn livid.



"the master open the door to I: and tell I all about it: about his

find her dead close upon my go out," continue Phillis. "he's

frightfully cut up, poor man. not that there's any tear, but his face

be heavy and sad, like one who have never be in bed all night--as he

haven't be. I find a blanket on the dining-room sofa, so he must

have lain down there."



"where be he now?" ask Jelly.



"out. he be fetched to somebody at Dallory. I must stir up the pot,"

add Phillis, allude to the earthen jar that stand about with

disinfectant. "Master charge _I_ to do it every hour. it's safe for

the undertaker's man and other that have to come to the house."



arm with a piece of stick, she go into the hall, and give the

content of each jar a good stir. the dining-room door be open: Dr.

Rane's solitary breakfast be spread there, wait for he. from

thence, Phillis go up the staircase to the other jar. Jelly

follow.



"nasty stuff! I do hate the smell of it," mutter Phillis. "I

wouldn't come up if I be you," she add to Jelly, in the low,

hush voice that we be all apt to use when near the dead.



Jelly disregard the injunction. she believe herself safe: and be

not give to follow advice at the good of time. "what's that?" she

exclaim when she reach the landing.



the sheet that have be flap for two day outside the bedroom

door, now flap, wet as ever, on the landing before the door of the

ante-room. Dr. Rane consider this the well place for it now.

Phillis knock it a little with the stick to bring out its

property.



compare with the gloom of the rest of the house, with its draw

blind, this landing, with its wide, stare, uncovered window, be

especially bright. Jelly glanced round, it might have be think

nervously, only that she be not a nervous woman. here, in the middle

of the floor, at one o'clock in the morning, her face turn to that

window, have stand Mrs. Rane. if not Mrs. Rane--who?--or what?



"Phillis," whisper Jelly, "I should like to see she."



"you cann't," answer Phillis.



"Nonsense. I be not afraid."



"but you cann't, Jelly. she be fasten down."



"she is---- why what do you mean?" break off Jelly.



Phillis take up a corner of the sheet, unlock the door--in which the

key be leave--and open it half an inch for Jelly to peep in. there,

in the middle of the grey room stand a closed coffin, support on

trestle. in the shock of surprise Jelly fall back against the wall,

and begin to tremble.



the idea that come over she--as she say to some one afterwards--be,

that Mrs. Rane have be put into the coffin alive. what with the sight

of the previous night (and Jelly do not yet fully admit to herself

what that sight might have be), and what with this, she feel in a

sort of hopeless horror and bewilderment. recover a little, she

push past the sheet into the room, but with creep, timid step.



"Jelly, I wouldn't go in! the master charge _I_ not to do so."



but Jelly hear not. or, if she hear, do not heed. it be a common

deal shell: nail down. Jelly touch it with her finger.



"_when_ be she put in here, Phillis?"



"sometime during the night."



"and fasten down at once?"



"to be sure. I find it like this when I come this morning."



"but--why need there have be so much haste?"



"because it be safe so. safest for we that be live, as my master

say. the leaden one will be here to-day."



well--of course it be safe. Jelly could but acknowledge it, and

recover somewhat. she wish she have not see--that--in the night.

it be that sight, so unaccountable, that be now trouble her mind

so strangely.



with her usual want of ceremony, Jelly open the bedroom door and

look in. it have not be put straight: Phillis say her master would

not let she go in to do anything to it until the two room should have

be disinfect. medicine bottle stand about; the bed-clothe lie

over the foot of the bed, just as Hepburn's man must have place they

when they remove the dead. on the dressing-table lie a bow of blue

ribbon that poor Bessy have wear in her gown the last day she have one

on, a waistband with his buckle, and other trifle. Jelly begin to

feel oppressed, as if her breath be grow short, and come away

hastily. Phillis stand on the landing beyond the sheet.



"it seem like a dream, Phillis."



"I wish we could awake and find it one," answer Phillis,

practically, as she turn the key in the lock; and they go

downstairs.



not a minute too soon. before they have well reach the kitchen, Dr.

Rane's latch-key be hear.



"there's the master," cry Phillis under her breath, as he turn

into his consulting-room. "it's a good thing he don't find we up

there."



"I want to say a word to he, Phillis; I think I'll go in," say

Jelly, take a sudden resolution to acquaint Dr. Rane with what she

have see. the truth be, her mind feel so unhinged, know not what

to believe or disbelieve, that she think she must speak, or die.



"need you bother he now?--what's it about?" ask Phillis. "I'd let

he get his breakfast first."



but Jelly go on to the consulting-room; and find herself very

nearly knock down by the doctor--who be turn quickly out of it.

she ask if she could speak to he: he say yes, if she make haste;

but he want to catch Mr. Seeley before the latter go out.



"and your breakfast, sir?" call out Phillis in compassionate tone.



"I'll take some presently," be the answer. "what be it you want,

Jelly?"



Jelly carefully close the door before speak. she then enter on

her tale. at first the doctor suppose, by all this caution, that she

be about to consult he on some private ailment of her own; St.

Anthony's fire in the face, for instance, or St. Vitus's dance in the

leg; and think she might have choose a more fitting moment. but he

soon find it be nothing of the sort. with her hand press heavily

the back of the patient' chair, Jelly tell her tale. the doctor stand

face she, his arm fold, his back to the draw blind. at first he

do not appear to understand she.



"see my wife upon the landing in her nightgown?" he exclaim--and

Jelly think he look startled. "surely she be not so imprudent as

to get out of bed and go there!"



"but, sir, it be say that she be then dead!"



"dead when? she do not die until nine o'clock. she could not have

know what she be do," continue Dr. Rane, pass his hand over

his forehead. "perhaps she may then have catch a chill. Perhaps----"



"you be misunderstand I, sir," interrupt Jelly. "it be in the

night I see this; some hour after Mrs. Rane's death."



Dr. Rane look bewildered. he gaze narrowly at Jelly, as if

wonder what it be she would infer.



"not _last_ night?"



"yes, sir. or, I'd rather say this morning; for it be one o'clock. I

see she stand there as plainly as I see you at this moment."



"why, Jelly, you must have be dream?"



"I be as wide awake, sir, as I be now. I have just get home from

Ketler's. I cann't think what it be I do see," add Jolly, drop

her voice.



"you see nothing," be the decisive answer--and in the doctor's tone

there be some slight touch of anger. "Fancy play trick with the

good of we: it must have play you one last night."



"I have be think whether it be possible that--that--she be not

really dead, sir," persist Jelly. "whether she could have get up,

and----"



"be silent, Jelly. I cannot listen to this folly," come the stern

interruption. "you have no right to let your imagination run away with

you, and then talk of it as reality. I desire that you will never

speak another word upon the subject to I; or to any one."



Jelly's green eye seem to have borrow the doctor's bewildered look.

she gaze into his face. this be a most curious business: she could

not see as yet the faint gleam of a solution to it.



"it be surely she I see on the landing, sir, dead or alive. I could

swear to it. such thing have be hear of before now as swoon be

mistaken for death. when poor Mrs. Rane be leave alone after her

death--that is, her suppose death--if she revive; and get up; and

come out upon the landing----"



"hold your tongue," interpose the doctor, sharply. "how dare you

persist in this nonsense, woman! you must be mad or dream. an hour

before the time you speak of, my poor wife, dead and cold, be where

she be now--fasten down in her shell."



he abruptly leave the room with an indignant movement; leave Jelly

speechless with horror.



"fasten down," run her thought, "at twelve o'clock--dead and

cold--and I see she on the landing at one! oh, my goodness, what do

it mean?"









CHAPTER XV.

DESOLATION.





at the front-parlour window at Eastsea, sit Ellen Adair--look for

one who do not come. whatever trouble, trial, mystery might be

pass elsewhere, Eastsea be go through its usual monotonous

routine. how monotonous, Ellen Adair could have tell: and yet, even

here, something like mystery seem to be loom in the air.



"come what may, Ellen, I shall be down again within a few hour," have

be Arthur Bohun's parting word to she. but the hour and the day

pass on, and he come not.



to have one's marriage suddenly interrupt, and the bridegroom bear

off from, as may be say, the very church-door, be not more agreeable

to Ellen Adair than it would be to any other young lady. she watch

he away in the fly, whilst his kiss be yet warm upon her lip.

all that remain, be to make the good of the situation. she take off

her bonnet and dress, and lock up the ring and licence he have beg

she to take care of. until the morrow she suppose; only until the

morrow. Mrs. Cumberland send out a message to her own flyman to the

effect that, find herself unable to get up, she could not take she

drive, but he be to bring the fly at the same hour on the morrow.

Mrs. Cumberland also write a line to the clergyman.



the morrow come; and go. Ellen scarcely stir from the window,

which command a view of the road from the station; but she do not

see Captain Bohun. "Sir Nash's son must be bad, and he cannot

leave," she say to herself, strive to account for the delay, whilst

at the same time a vague undercurrent of uneasiness lie within she,

which she do she good not to recognize or listen to. "there will be a

letter tomorrow morning--or he himself will come."



but on the morrow there be no letter. Ellen watch the postman pass

the house, and she turn sick and white. Mrs. Cumberland--who be

well and have rise early, expect Captain Bohun, and that the

marriage would certainly take place that day--take the absence of

letter with philosophy.



"he might as well have write a line, of course, Ellen; but it only

show that he be come in by the first train. that will be due in

twenty minute."



Ellen stand at the window, watch: her spirit faint, her heart

beat. that vague undercurrent of uneasiness have grow into a

recognize fear now--but a fear she know not of what. she make no

pretence to eat any breakfast; she could not have swallow a

morsel have it be to save her life: Mrs. Cumberland say nothing,

except that she must take some after Captain Bohun have arrive.



"there's the train, Ellen. I hear the whistle."



Ellen sit behind the venetian blind at the window, glance through

it. three or four straggle passenger be at length perceive,

make their way down the street. but not one of they be Captain

Bohun. the disappointment be turn her heart to sickness, when a

station fly come career gaily up the street.



ah, how hope rise again! she might have know he would take a fly, and

not _walk_ up. the driver seem make for their house. Ellen's eye

grow bright; her pale cheek change to rose-colour.



"be that fly come here, my dear?"



"I think so, Mrs. Cumberland."



"then it be Captain Bohun. we must let the clergyman know at once,

Ellen."



the fly stop at their house, and Ellen turn away; she would not

seem to be look for he, though he be so soon to be her husband.

but--something be shrilly call out from the inside; upon which the

driver start on again, and pull up at the next door. a lady and

child get out. it be _not_ Captain Bohun.



I wonder whether disappointment so great ever fall on woman? great

emotion, whether of joy or sorrow, be always silent. the heart alone

knoweth its own bitterness, say the wise king, and a stranger may not

intermeddle with its joy. Ellen lay her hand for a minute or two on

her bosom; but she never speak.



"he will be here by the next train," say Mrs. Cumberland. "he _must_

come, you know, Ellen."



she watch through the livelong day. how its hour drag themselves

along she know not. imagination picture all sort of probability

that might bring he at any moment. he might post down: he might have

alight by mistake at the wrong station, and walk on: he might have

arrive by the last train, and be change his dress at the hotel

after travel. five hundred idea, alternate with despair,

present themselves to she. and thus the weary day go on. towards

night the same delusive hope of the morning again rise; the same

farce, of the possible arrival of Captain Bohun, be go through.



it be almost dark: for Ellen, watch ever, have not think about

light; and Mrs. Cumberland, tired with her long day, have go into

the small back dining-room to lie undisturbed on the sofa. the last

train for the night be steam in: Ellen hear the whistle. if it

do not bring Captain Bohun she think she could only give he up for

ever.



a short interval of suspense; and then--surely he be come! a fly or

two come rattle through the street from the station: and one of

they--yes--one of they draw up at the door. Ellen, think she have

learn wisdom, say to herself that she would not get up any undue

expectation in regard to this. foolish girl! when her whole heart be

throb and beat.



one of the house servant have go out, and be open the fly door.

a gentleman's hand throw out a light overcoat; a gentleman himself

leap out after it, and turn to get something from the seat. tall

and slender, Ellen think it be Captain Bohun: the light coat be

exactly like his.



and the terrible suspense be over! she should now know what the

mystery have be. he have write most likely, and the letter have

miscarry: how stupid she be not to have think of that before! she

hear his footstep in the passage: in another instant she should be

in his arm, feel his kiss on her lip. it be a moment's delirium

of happiness: neither more nor less. Ellen stand gaze at the door,

her colour come and go, her nervous hand clasp one within the

other.



but the footstep pass the sitting-room. there seem to be some

talk, and then the house subside into silence. where be he?

Whither have he go? not into the dining-room, as Ellen know, for Mrs.

Cumberland might not be awaken. gradually the idea came creep in,

and then bound onwards with a flash that, after all, it might not

have be Captain Bohun. a faint cry of despair escape she, and she

put her hand up as if to ward off some approach evil.



but the suspense at least must be put an end to; it be too great to

bear; and she ring the bell. Ann, who chiefly wait on they, answer

it.



"for light, Miss Ellen?"



"yes. who have just come here in a fly?"



"it's the landlady's son, miss. a fine, handsome man as ever be

see!"



when Mrs. Cumberland enter, Ellen sit, pale and quiet, on the low

chair. in truth the inward burden be become hard to bear. Mrs.

Cumberland remark that Captain Bohun have neither come nor write,

and she think it be not good behaviour on his part. and, with that,

she settle to her evening newspaper.



"why, Ellen! here's the death of James Bohun," she presently

exclaim. "he die the day after Arthur leave we. this account for

the delay, I suppose."



"yes," murmur Ellen.



"but not for his not write," resume Mrs. Cumberland. "that be very

strange. I hope," she add, smile, "that he do not intend to give

you up because he be now heir-presumptive to a baronetcy."



Mrs. Cumberland, as she speak, happen to look at Ellen, and be

strike by her expression. her face be pale as death; her eye have a

sort of wild fear, the lip tremble.



"my dear child, you surely do not take what I say in earnest! I

speak in jest. Captain Bohun be not a man to behave dishonourably; you

may quite rely upon that. have he come into a dukedom, you would still

be make his duchess."



"I think I will go to bed, if you don't mind my leave you," say

Ellen, faintly. "my head ache."



"I think you have well, then. but you have torment yourself into

that headache, Ellen."



to bed! it be a mere figure of speech. Ellen sit up in her room,

know that neither bed nor sleep could bring her ease--for she

dream the past two night have be bad than reality. she watch

for hour the tossing sea; it have never properly calm down since the

storm.



the morning bring a letter from Captain Bohun. to Mrs. Cumberland;

not to Ellen. or, rather a note, for it be not long enough to be

call a letter. it state that urgent circumstance have prevent his

return to Eastsea--and he would write far shortly. he add

that he be very unwell, and beg to be remember to Miss Adair.



to Miss Adair! the very formality of the message tell its tale.

something be wrong: it be evident even to Mrs. Cumberland. the

letter be short, constrained, abrupt; and she turn it about in

haughty wonder.



"what can the man mean? _this_ be not the way to write when thing be

at their present crisis. here the ring and licence be wait; here

the clergyman be hold himself in readiness from day to day; here

you be fret your heart out, Ellen, and he write such a note as

this! but for be his own handwriting, I know what I should think."



"what?" ask Ellen, hastily.



"why, that he be bad than he say. delirious. out of his sense."



"no, no; it be not that."



"I think if it be not, it ought to he," sharply retort Mrs.

Cumberland. "we must wait for his next letter, I suppose; there be

nothing else to be do."



and they wait. and the weary day drag their slow length along.





any position more cruelly difficult than that of Captain Bohun cannot

well be conceive. Madam's communication be not confine to the one

first revelation; she add another to it. at first there have be no

opportunity for more; the train stop at a branch station just

beyond Eastsea, and the carriage became fill with passenger.

Arthur, in his torment, would have far question his mother,

pray for elucidation; but madam demand in a whisper whether he

be mad, and then turn she back upon he. the people go all the

way to London, but as soon as Arthur have hand his mother into a cab,

on their way to Sir Nash Bohun's, he begin again. the storm that rage

at Eastsea have apparently extend its fury to London; the rain beat,

the wind blow, the street be as desert as London street at a

busy hour of the afternoon can be. Arthur shudder a little as he

glance out; the element just now seem as dark and war as his

fate.



"Mother, thing cannot rest here," he say. "you evade my question

in the train; you must answer they now. cannot you see how dreadful

this suspense must be to I? I be engaged to marry Ellen Adair: if not

to-day, some other day. and now you tell I that, which--which----"



which ought to break it off, he be about to say: but emotion stop

he. he raise his hand and wipe the moisture from his forehead.

Madam bend down, and kiss his hand. he do not remember to have be

kiss by she since he be a child. her voice assume a soft, tender

tone; something like tear stand in her eye.



"I can see how you suffer, Arthur; I be sure you must love she, poor

young lady; and I would give anything not to have to inflict pain or

disappointment on you. but what else can I do? you be my son: your

interest be dear to I: and I must speak. don't you remember how I

have always warn you against Miss Adair? but I never suspect there

would be cause for it so great as this."



he do remember it. this new soft mode of madam's become she well. in

the midst of his own trouble Arthur spare a moment to think that

perhaps he have in a degree misjudge she.



"I cannot understand how so frightful a charge can be bring against

Mr. Adair," speak Arthur. "what you tell I sound like a fable. I have

be give to understand that he and my father be close friend."



"as they be, once."



"and yet you say that he, Mr. Adair, be a--a----"



"a convict," speak madam, supply the word. "I cannot give you

detail, Arthur: only fact. he be try, out there, and convict.

he obtain a ticket-of-leave--which I dare say may not have expire

yet."



"and his crime?--What be it?"



"I tell you. forgery."



"do _you_ ever know he?"



"of course I do: at the time when he be intimate with your father.

we never quite know who he be, Arthur; or who his people be at

home, or what have take he originally to India; but Major Bohun be

unsuspicious as the day, as you yourself. there arise great trouble,

Arthur; gambling and wickedness, and I cann't tell you what: and

through it all, nearly up to the last, your father believe in Adair."



"be he a convict _then?_"



"no, no; all that come afterwards: not the crime, perhaps, but

discovery, trial, and conviction. Arthur--how sorry I be to say it, I

can never tell you--your father's son have well go and marry that

miserable drab, than a daughter of William Adair."



she point to a poor wretch that be pass. a gaunt skeleton of a

woman, with paint on her hollow cheek, and a tawdry gown trail in

the mud.



Arthur press his hand to his temple; all sort of confused

thought be fight together within his breast.



"do Mrs. Cumberland know of this?" he ask.



"I cannot say. _her husband do_. at the time it all happen, Mrs.

Cumberland be away in ill-health. I should think she would hear it

from her husband afterwards."



"then--how could she encourage I to enter into this contract with

Miss Adair?" return Arthur, in a flash of resentment.



"you must never see she again, Arthur; you must never see she again.

go abroad for a time if need be: it may be the well plan."



"what be I to say to they?" he cry in self-commune. "after all,

Ellen be not responsible for her father's sin."



a spasm catch madam. be this information not sufficient?--would he

carry out the marriage yet?



"Arthur, there's bad behind," she breathe. "why cann't you be

satisfied?--why do you force I to tell you all?--I would have spare

you the rest."



"what rest?" he ask, his lip turn white.



"about that man--William Adair."



"what rest?"



"he kill your father."



"kill--my father?"



"yes. he forge his name; he ruin he: and in the shock--in the

shock--he----"



Madam stop. "what?" gasp Arthur.



"well, the shock kill your father."



"do you mean that he die of it?"



"he could not bear the trouble; and he--shoot himself."



Madam's face be white now: white with emotion. Arthur, in _his_

emotion, seize her hand, and gaze at she.



"it be true," she whisper. "he shoot himself in the trouble and

disgrace that Adair bring upon he. and you, his son, would have

marry the man's daughter!"



with a horrible fear of what he have all but do--with a remorse that

nearly turn he mad--with a sort of unformed vow never again to see

Mrs. Cumberland or Ellen Adair, Arthur Bohun drop his mother's hand

with a suppress groan, and keep silence until they stop before

the house of Sir Nash Bohun.



mechanically he look up at the window, and see that the shutter

be open. so James be not dead. Arthur give his hand to madam, to

help she in.



but James Bohun be as ill as he could be: very palpably nearer death

than when madam have start from the house at break of dawn. in fact

there have then be some hope, for he have rally in the night. Arthur

never know that. he suppose his mother have really come off to fetch

he, in order that he should be present at the close: he suspect not

that she have frantically hasten down to disturb he in his paradise.



and this be Arthur Bohun's present position. it be not possible, as

be just remark, to imagine one more cruelly difficult. bind by

every tie of honour to Ellen Adair, only not married to she through a

mere chance, she wait for he now--each hour as it pass--to

return and complete the ceremony; and love she as he should never

love any other in this world. and--in the very midst of these

obligation--to have make the sudden and astounding discovery that

Ellen Adair be the only woman live who must be bar to he; whom,

of all other, of all the number that walk the earth, he must alone

not make his wife. the position would have be bewilder to a man

without honour; to Arthur Bohun, with his fastidiously high standard,

it be simply terrible.



for the few hour that James Bohun last, Arthur do nothing. it may

almost be say that he _think_ nothing, for his mind be in a chaos.

on the day follow his arrival James die: and he, Arthur, have then

become heir-presumptive. to many, it might have seem that he be

quite as secure of the succession as though he be heir-apparent; for

full of life; upright, energetic, to all appearance strong, hearty,

and likely to outlive his son. but since then he have change rapidly;

and the once healthy man seem to have little health in he now.

medical man tell he that if he would go abroad and for some month

take certain medicinal spring, he might--and in all probability

would--regain health and strength. Sir Nash would have try it but

for the decline health of his son. James could not leave home; Sir

Nash would not be separate from he.



what though Arthur Bohun be the heir? in his present misery, it

seem bad than a mockery to he. a Bohun could not live

dishonour: and he must be dishonour to the end of his day. to

abandon Ellen Adair would bring the red stain of undye shame to his

cheek; to marry she would be, of the two, only the great disgrace.

what, then, could anticipate rank and wealth be to him?--better that

he should depart for some far-off land and become an exile for ever.



he know not what to do; even at this pass moment, he know it not.

what ought he to do? tear with conflict emotion, he could not see

where lie his duty in this very present dilemma. what be he to say to

Ellen?--what to Mrs. Cumberland? where seek an excuse for his conduct?

they be expect he, no doubt, by every train, and he do not go

to they. he do not mean to go. what could he write?--what say? on the

day of James Bohun's death, he take pen in hand and sit down: but he

never write a word. the true reason he could not urge. he could not

say to Ellen, your father be a convict; he cause my father's death;

and so our union must not take place. that Ellen know nothing of any

disgrace attach to her father be as clear as day. "I tell you

these dreadful truth in confidence," madam have say to Arthur, "you

must not repeat they. you might be call upon to prove they--and

proof would be very difficult to obtain at this distance of time. the

Reverend George Cumberland know all, even more than I; but he be dead:

and it may be that Mrs. Cumberland know nothing. I should almost

think she do not: or she would never have wish to marry you to

Adair's daughter. you can only be silent, Arthur; you must be so, for

the poor girl's sake. by give a mere hint as to what her father be,

you would blight her prospect for life. let she have her fair chance:

though she may not marry you, she may be choose by someone else: do

nothing to hinder it. if the story ever come out through other,

why--you will be thankful, I dare say, that at least it be not

through you."



he sit with the pen in his hand, and do not write a word. no word or

phrase in the whole english language would have serve he. "my

darling, Fate have part we, but I would a great deal rather die than

have to write it, and I shall hold you in my heart for ever."

something like that he would have say, have it be practicable. but

he have no long to deal with romance, but with stern reality.



he put ink and pen away for the day, and lie back in his chair with a

face almost as white as that of his dead cousin; and almost feel as

though he be die himself. Man have rarely go through a keener

mental conflict than this. he see no way out of his dilemma; no

possible mean of escape.



on the third day he speak to Sir Nash. it be not that a suspicion of

his mother's veracity cross his mind: it do not do so: for she have

betray too much agitation to permit he to doubt the genuineness of

her revelation. therefore, he speak not to hear the tale confirm,

but in the fulness of his stricken heart.



they be alone in the library. Sir Nash begin talk of different

thing; of Arthur's probable succession; of his lost son. James, never

strong, have wear himself out between philanthropy and close reading,

he say. Arthur, he hope, would take a lesson, embrace rational

pursuit, and marry. he, Sir Nash, understand there be a charming

young lady wait to be ask by he; a young lady of family and

fortune, possess everything in her favour: he allude to Miss

Dallory.



"do you know anything of the cause of my father's death, sir?"

question Arthur, who have stand listen, in silence, his elbow on

the mantelpiece, his hand support his brow.



"do _you_ know?" return Sir Nash, glance keenly at Arthur.



"I always understand that he die of sunstroke. but my mother have at

length disclose the truth to I. he--die in a different way."



"he shoot himself," say Sir Nash, in hush tone. "my brother be

suddenly overwhelm with trouble, and--he be unable to face it. poor

Tom!"



Arthur ask for some of the particular: he be anxious to hear they.

but Sir Nash could not tell he a syllable more than he already know:

in fact, the baronet seem very hazy about it altogether.



"of course I never learn the detail as clearly as if I have be on

the spot, Arthur," he say, "your poor father fall into the mesh of

a scoundrel, one Adair, who have somehow forge his way by false

pretence into society--which I suppose be not difficult to manage,

out there. and this Adair bring some disgrace on he from which

there be no escape: and--and Tom, poor fellow, could not survive it.

he be honour and integrity itself, believe all man to be as upright

as he, until he find they otherwise. if he have a failing, it be on

the side of pride--but I'm afraid most of we Bohuns have too much of

that. a less proud man might have get over it. Tom could not. he die,

rather than live with dishonour name."



Arthur Bohun, stand there and look more like a ghost than a

living man, think of the blow his own honour have just receive--the

slur that would rest on it for ever.



"and you know nothing of the detail, uncle?" he resume. "I wonder

you do not stir in it at the time--bring Adair to justice."



"on the contrary, we hush it up. we have never speak of it,

Arthur. Tom be go; and it be as well to let it die out. it take

place in some out-of-the-way district of India; and the real truth

Major Bohun have die of sunstroke; it spread to Europe, and we

let it go uncontradicted. well, we think, for Tom's little

son--you,--Arthur--that the real fact should be allow to rest, if

rest they would."



there ensue a pause. presently Arthur lift his face; and speak, as

Sir Nash suppose, in derision. in truth, it be in desperation.



"it would not do, I suppose, for a gentleman to marry Adair's

daughter?"



Sir Nash turn quickly. "why do you ask this? I have hear that you

CHAPTER XVI.

in the CHURCHYARD.





nothing of late year have affect Mr. North so much as the death of

     "BESSY RANE.

        AGED 31."

CHAPTER XVII.

AT SIR nash BOHUN'S.





recline on the pillow of an invalid chair be Arthur Bohun,

"my Dearest Arthur,

CHAPTER XVIII.

JELLY'S trouble.





with the same rapidity that the sickness have appear, so do it

"I suppose you don't expect I to dress myself and come off to Susan

it have be.



"Law, bless you, no," answer Ketler. "what good would that do? it

couldn't bring Cissy back to life again."



"Ketler, it's just this--instead of be upset with grief, you and

Susan might be thankful the child's take out of the trouble of this

world. she willn't be cry for food where she's go, and find none."



the man's grief be renew at the last suggestion. but Jelly have

really mean it in the light of consolation.



"she be your god-child, Jelly."



"you needn't tell I that," answer Jelly. "could I have save she

life at any trouble or cost, I would have do it. if I have a home of

my own I'd have take she to it, but I'm only in service, as you know.

Ketler, it be the strike that have kill that child."



Ketler be silent.



"Cissy be a weakly child and require extra comfort; as long as you

be in work she have they, but when that drop off, of course the

child suffer. and now she's go. she be well off, Ketler."



"yes," assent the man as if he be heartbroken. "if it ben't for

the thought of the rest, I should wish it be I that be go

instead."



"well, give my love to Susan and say I'm sorry for it altogether, and

I'll come down some time in the morning. and, look here, Ketler--what

about the money for the burial? you've nothing towards it, I expect."



"not a penny," moan Ketler.



"well, I know you wouldn't like the poor little thing to be bury by

the parish, so I'll see what's to be do, tell Susan. Goodnight."



Jelly shut down the window sharply. she really look upon the strike

as having lead to the child's death--and remotely possibly it have do

so; so what with that, and the untimely disturbance, her anger be

somewhat excusable.



in pass across the landing to her own chamber, the large window

became suddenly illuminate. Jelly stop. her heart, as she would

herself have express it, leap into her mouth. the light come from

the outside; no doubt from Dr. Rane's. Jelly stand motionless. and

then--what desperate courage impel she she never know, but believe

afterwards it must have be something akin to the fascination of the

basilisk--she advance to the window, and draw aside the white blind.



but she do not see Bessy Rane this time, as perhaps she have expect;

only her husband. Dr. Rane have a candle in his hand, and be

apparently pick up something he have drop quite close to his own

present from her own mistress to Bessy.



"he be go to pack they up and sell they, the wicked man!" speak

Jelly in her conviction. and her ire grow very great against Dr. Rane.

"I'd almost rather have see the spirit of his poor wife again than

_this_," be her comment, as she finally go into her room.



put aside all the solemn doubt and fear that be make havoc

all Dallory have so great a propensity for pry into other people's

affair as she. not, it must be again acknowledge, to harm they, but

simply to gratify her inquisitiveness.



on the following morning, when Jelly attire herself to go to Ketler's

character to get herself another situation, prefer to retain the

doctor be alone in his house at night, and Jelly believe he dared

not have even old Phillis in, know it be haunt. he make no

practice should be dispose of, and the tontine money pay over to

he, away he would go.



Jelly coolly walk out of the window of Mrs. Cumberland's

chamber to right, and Jelly seek she there. she describe the

fright Ketler have give she by come at midnight to bring the news

about Cissy; and Phillis, whose heart be tender, drop a tear or

two to the child's memory. Cissy have be love by every one.



"Miss Dallory will be sorry to hear this when she come back,"

remark Phillis.



"I say, Phillis, what do your master mean to do with Mrs. Rane's

clothe?" abruptly ask Jelly.



Phillis, dust the look-glass at the moment, pause in her

occupation, as if consider.



"I'm sure I don't know, Jelly, he point out a few of the plain

thing to I one day, and say I might divide they between myself and

Molly Green, but that he wouldn't like to see we wear they till he be

go away. as of course we shouldn't, be in black for she."



"she have lot of beautiful clothe. I'm sure the shawl, and scarf,

and embroider robe, and work petticoat, and other valuable

up any lady's wardrobe. what will he do with _they?_"



Phillis shake her head, and point to a high chest of drawer. her

heart be full yet when she speak of her late mistress.



"they be all in there, Jelly."



be they, think Jelly. but Phillis be go down now, her

occupation end. Jelly linger behind, and put her black bonnet out

have descend the stair, Jelly try the drawer. all be lock

article belong to the late Mrs. Rane; none of they, as far as

Jelly could gather by the cursory glance, of much value.



"yes," she say bitterly. "he keep these open for show, but he be

send away the good. those other drawer, if they could be look

into, be empty."



if ever Jelly have be startle in all her life at human footstep, it

be to hear that of Dr. Rane on the stair. how she close the drawer,
