about Ketler's little girl.



"ah, she be go," observe Dr. Rane, as Jelly be walk out. "there

have be no hope of she for some time."



"no, sir, I know there haven't," reply Jelly, somewhat recover she

equanimity. "I tell Ketler that he may thank the strike for it."



Jelly get out with this, and be pass through the grey room, when

the doctor speak again.



"have you hear from your mistress this morning, Jelly?"



"no, sir."



"well, I have. I be very much afraid that she be exceedingly ill,

Jelly?"



"Dinah have a letter from Ann a day or two ago, sir; she say that her

missis be look bad, and seem low than she have ever know

she."



"ay, I wish she would come home. eastsea be far away, and I cannot be

run there everlastingly," add the doctor, as he close the

chamber-door in Jelly's face.









CHAPTER XIX.

come home to DIE.





Time go on again; nearly a fortnight. Dallory have relapse into its

old routine; the fever be forget. house have recover from the

aroma of soap and scrub: their inhabitant be back again; and

amongst they be Mrs. North and her daughter Matilda.



the principal news madam find to interest she be, that Richard North

have open the work again. the glow of hope it raise within she be

very bright; for she consider it as an earnest that supply would

spring up again in the future as they have in the past. that she would

find herself mistaken be exceedingly probable; Richard himself could

have say a certainty. Madam have the grace to express some calm regret

for the untimely death of Bessy Rane, in the hearing of Mr. North and

Richard; she have put herself and Matilda into deep mourning than

they have assume for James Bohun. it be all of the most fashionable

and costly description; and the master of Dallory Hall, poor helpless

man, have the pleasure of receive the bill for it from the London

court-milliner and dressmaker. but madam never inquire into the

particular of Bessy's illness and death; in her opinion the less

fever be talk about the well.



yes: the north work be reopen. or, to be quite correct, they be

on the point of be reopen. upon how small a scale he must begin

again, Richard, remember the extent of past operation, feel almost

ashamed to contemplate. but, as he good-humouredly remark, half a

loaf be well than no bread. he must earn a living; he have no

fortune to fly to; and he prefer do this to seek employment

under other firm, if indeed anything worth have could have be

find; but the trade of the country be in a most depressed state, and

hundred of gentleman, like himself, have be throw on their beam

end. it be the same thing as begin life over again; a little

venture, that might succeed or might fail; one in which he must plod

on carefully and cautiously, even to keep it go.



the whole staff of operative would at first number less than twenty.

the old workman, idly air themselves still in North Inlet, laugh

derisively when they hear this. they be pleasantly sarcastic over

it, think perhaps to conceal their real bitterness of heart. the

new measure do not find favour with they. how should it, when they

stand out in the light of exclusion? some eight or ten, who have never

willingly uphold the strike, have all along be ready to return to

work, would be take on again; the rest be foreigner that Richard

North be bring over from abroad. and the anger of the disaffect

may be imagine.



Mrs. Gass enter cordially into Richard's plan. she would have put

unlimited money into his new undertaking; but Richard would not have

it. some portion of her capital that have be embark in the firm of

North and Gass, of necessity remain in it--all, in fact, that be

not lose--but this she count as nothing, and want to help Richard

yet far. "it's of no good cry over spilt milk, Mr. Richard,"

she say to he, philosophically; "and I've still a great deal more

than I shall ever want." but Richard be firm: he would receive no

further help: it be a risk that he prefer to incur alone.



perhaps there be few people live that Richard North like well

than Mrs. Gass. he even like she homely language; it be honest and

genuine; far more to be respect than if she have make a show of

attempt what she could not have keep up. Richard have learn to

know her worth: he recognize it more certainly day by day. in the

discomfort of his home at Dallory Hall--which have long be anything

but a home to he--he have fall into the habit of almost make a

second home with Mrs. Gass. never a day pass but he spend an hour or

two of it with she; and she would persuade he to remain for a meal as

often as she could.



he sit one afternoon at her well-spread tea-table. his arrangement

be very nearly organize now; and in a day the work would open. the

foreign workman have arrive, and be lodge with their family in

the place appoint for they. two policeman, employ by Richard, have

also take up their position in Dallory, purposely to protect they. of

course their mission be not know: Richard North would not be the one

to provoke hostility; but he be quite aware of the ill-feeling

obtaining amongst his former workman.



"downright idiot, they be," say Mrs. Gass, confidentially, as she

Richard; that's what it be."



"I don't know about that," dissent Richard. "it seem to I they

could hardly receive a well lesson than these last few month must

have teach they."



"ah, you don't know 'em as I do. I'm almost double your age, sir; and

there's nothing give experience like year."



Richard laugh. "not double my age yet, Mrs. Gass."



"anyway, I might have be your mother--if you'll excuse my saying

turn fifty."



in this homely manner Mrs. Gass usually like to make her proposition

undeniable. certainly she might, in point of age, have be Richard's

"I know the man well than you do, Mr. Richard; and I say they want a

lesson read to 'em yet. and they'll get it, sir. but we'll leave the

past, and I'm sure you have. to watch once sensible man act like

fool, and persist in do it, in spite of everybody and

everything, weary one's patience. be it tomorrow that you open?"



"the day after."



"well, now, Mr. Richard, I should like to say another word upon a

matter that you and I don't agree on--and it's not often our opinion

more than you can command: it would give I a real pleasure if you'll

let I find it."



Richard smile, and shake his head, "I cannot say more than I have

say before," be his reply. "you know all I have urge."

this, should the concern turn out a lose one, for in that case I

know cord wouldn't draw you to I for help. but when you be get

question, Mr. Richard, that delicacy have keep I from worry you

"ah," say Richard, "that have be my nightmare. but I think I see a

way through it at last. first of all, I have give notice to Miss

"good," observe Mrs. Gass.



"my father know nothing about it--it be of no use trouble he

early than be necessary; and of course madam know nothing. _she_

imago that the lease will be renew as a matter of course. Miss

"they know then that you be the real lessee of Dallory Hall? lawk a

Richard nod. "as soon as Christmas be turn I shall look out for a

moderate house in lieu of the Hall; one that I shall hope to be able

to keep up. it shall have a good garden for my father's sake. there

that. I cannot do more than my mean will allow I."



"see here, Mr. Richard; don't worry yourself about not be able to

need be. he and my husband be partner and friend, and grow rich

bad rubbish.--Mr. Richard, have you hear the news about Mary

"what news?" he ask.



"that she's go to be married to Captain Bohun."



Richard North drink his tea to the dreg. his face have flush a

little.



"I hear that madam wish it, and be work for it," he answer.

"Miss Dallory be stay with they when they be at Sir Nash

Bohun's."



"I know madam have give it out that they're go to marry," rejoin

after his illness?"



"he be well. almost well."



Mrs. Gass help herself to some butter toast. "I shall believe in

don't. but I have have my eye about I."



Richard know that she allude to Ellen Adair.



"they be both as sweet and good girl as ever live, and a gentleman

may think himself lucky to get either of 'em. Mr. Richard, your

coat-sleeve be come into contact with the pot-ham."



Richard smile a little as he attend to his cuff. mourning be

always bad wear, he remark, and show every little stain. and

allude to the subject since she die.



"I cannot grow reconcile to her loss," he say in low tone. "at

time can scarcely believe in it. to have be carry off after only

a day's fever seem to I incredible."



and Mrs. Gass feel that the word startle she to pallor. she turn

away lest he should see the change in her countenance.





bad news arrive from Mrs. Cumberland. only a morning or two later, a

loud knock at the front-door disturb Jelly and Dinah at their

breakfast. upon its being open by the latter, Dr. Rane walk

straight into the kitchen without ceremony, an open letter in his

curious thing that be trouble her mind.



"my mother will be home to-night, Jelly."



"to-night, sir!" exclaim Jelly in her surprise.



"she be much bad. very ill indeed. she say she be come home to

die."



Jelly be startle out of her equanimity.



"it be only three line, and she write herself," continue Dr. Rane,

just show the letter in his hand, as if in confirmation. "they be

to go to London yesterday, remain there the night, and will come home

to-day. of course you will have everything in readiness."



"yes, sir. and what about meet my mistress at the station?"



"I shall go myself," say Dr. Rane.



he go away with the last word. Jelly sit still for a few minute to

during the day, Jelly, in pursuance of something or other she want,

inhabitant of the neighbourhood.



how be Mrs. Cumberland?--and _where_ be she? madam graciously ask.

and Jelly in the moment's haste, answer that she be at Eastsea.



"to stay the winter, I believe," go on madam. "and Miss Adair--be

she with she?"



"I ought to have say _be_ at Eastsea," correct Jelly, who do not

like madam well enough to be more than barely civil to she. "my

mistress be bad, and be come home to-day. Miss Adair be with she

of course. I must wish you good-morning, madam; I've all my work

before I to-day." and away go Jelly, leave madam a mental

compliment:



"nasty proud cat! she have some sly motive for ask, I know."



and so the day go on.



the early twilight of the autumn evening be begin to fall,

together with a heavy shower of rain, when the carriage contain

Mrs. Cumberland stop at her door. Jelly run out; and be meet by

Ellen Adair, who speak in a startled whisper.



"oh, Jelly, she be so ill! too ill to speak."



the doctor stand help his mother out. Ann be gather up small

article from beside the driver. Jelly catch one glimpse of her

of death!



"she ought not to have come," murmur Dr. Rane in Jelly's ear. "go

and ask Seeley to step over--whilst I get my mother upstairs."



there be bustle and confusion for the moment. Mrs. Cumberland be

when she have somewhat recover the fatigue, she look and seem

much well, and speak a little, express a wish for some tea. the

doctor leave she to take it, enjoin strict quiet. Jelly be near

her mistress, hold the cup and saucer.



"what do she die of, Jelly?" come the unexpected question.



"who?" ask Jelly, wonderingly.



Mrs. Cumberland motion in the direction of her son's house: and her

voice be subdue to faintness: "Bessy Rane."



Jelly give a start that almost upset the teacup. she feel her face

grow white; but she could not move to conceal it.



"why don't you reply? what do she die of?"



"Ma'am, don't you know? she catch the fever."



"it trouble I, Jelly; it trouble I. I've do nothing but dream

about she ever since. and what will Oliver do without she?"



the good he can, Jelly have a great mind to answer. but all she say,

be, to beg her mistress to leave these question until the morning.



"I don't think any morning will dawn for I," be Mrs. Cumberland's

off too long. but I have to come home: I could not die away from it."



every console thing that Jelly could think of, she say, assure

"I want to see Mr. North," resume Mrs. Cumberland. "you must bring

"not to-night," say Jelly.



"to-night. at once. there be no time to be lose. to see he be one of

the thing I have to come home for."



and Mrs. Cumberland, ill though she be, be as resolute in be

listen for any sound from the sick-room that might tell how all be

go on within it.



"oh, Miss Ellen! you should not be there."



"I cannot rest anywhere, Jelly. I want to know how she be. she be my

only friend on this side of the wide world."



"well now, Miss Ellen, look here--you may come in and stay with she,

whilst I be away: I be go to call Ann. but mind you don't talk to

she."



hastily throw on a shawl, Jelly start for Dallory Hall. it be an

inclement night, pour with rain. and Ellen Adair take up her place

in silence by the side of the die woman--for she _be_ die,

however ignorant they might be of the fact. apart from Ellen's natural

grief for Mrs. Cumberland, thought of what her own situation would

be, if she lose she, could but intrude on her mind, bring all sort

of perplexity with they. it seem to she that she would be leave

without home or protector in the wide world.









CHAPTER XX.

RICHARD NORTH'S REVELATION.





for a wonder, the dinner-table at Dallory Hall include only the

family-party. Madam head it; Mr. North be at the foot; Richard on

one side; Matilda on the other. scarcely a word be be speak.

Madam be in one of her imperious humour--when, indeed, be she out

of them?--the servant wait in silence.



suddenly there ring out a loud peal from the hall-bell. Richard, who

be already begin to be disturb by vague fear as to what his

ex-workman's hostility might make they do, sit back in his chair

absently, and turn his head.



"be you expect any one, Dick?" ask his father.



"no, sir. unless it be a message to call I out."



it be, however, a message for Mr. North; not for Richard. Mrs.

Cumberland want to see he. "on the instant," the servant add: for

so Jelly have imperatively put it.



Mr. North lay down his knife and fork and look at the man. he do

not understand.



"Mrs. Cumberland be at Eastsea," he cry.



"no, sir, she have just get home, and she want to see you very

particular. it's the lady's maid who have bring the message."



"Mr. North cannot go," break forth madam to the servant. "go and say

so."



but Jelly, to whom the word penetrate as she stand in the hall, have

no notion of her mistress's wish be set at nought by madam. Jelly

have a great deal of calm moral and physical courage--in spite of the

supernatural terror that have recently influence she--some person

might have say her share of calm impudence also: and she make no

ceremony of put her black bonnet inside the room.



"my mistress be die, sir; I don't think there can be a doubt of it,"

she say, advance to Mr. North. "she wish to say a few last word

to you, if you'll please to come. there's no time to be lose, sir."



"bless me!--poor fanny!" cry Mr. North, rise: his hand begin

to tremble a little. "I'll come at once, Jelly."



"you will _not_ go," speak madam, as if she be issue an imperial

edict.



"I must go," say Mr. North. "don't you hear, madam, that she be

die?"



"I say you shall not go."



"the wish of the dying must be respect by the living," interpose

Jelly, still address Mr. North. "otherwise there's no telling what

ghost might haunt 'em after."



the word be somewhat obscure, but their meaning be sufficiently

plain. Mr. North take a step or two towards the door: madam come

quickly round and place herself before he.



"my will be law in this house, and out of it you do not go."



for a minute or two Mr. North look utterly helpless; then cast an

appealing look at his son. Richard rise, lay down his table-napkin.



"leave the room for an instant," he quietly say to the servant,

include Jelly. and they file out.



"my dear father, be it your wish to see Mrs. Cumberland?"



"oh, Dick, you know it be," speak the poor brow-beat man. "there's

little left to I in life to care for now; but if I let she die

without go to she there'll be less."



"then you shall go," say Richard. Madam turn to he in furious

anger.



"how _dare_ you attempt to oppose I, Richard North? I say your father

shall not go forth at the beck and call of this crazy woman."



"Madam, I say he shall," calmly speak Richard.



"do you defy I? have it come to that?"



"why yes, if you force it upon I: it be not my fault. pardon I if I

speak plainly--if I set you right upon one point, madam," he add.

"you have just say your will be law in the house and out of it: in

future it must, on some occasion, yield to mine. this be one of they.

my father will go to Mrs. Cumberland's. say no more, madam: it will be

useless; and I be about to admit the servant."



from sheer amazement madam be silent. the resolution bear of

conscious power to will and to execute lay in every tone and glance of

Richard North. before she could rally her energy, the door be

open to the servant, and she hear Richard's order to make ready

and bring round the close carriage. instantly.



"Mr. North will be with your mistress as soon as you be, Jelly," say

he. and Jelly curtsy as she take her departure.



but a scene ensue. Madam have call Mrs. Cumberland a crazy woman:

she seem nothing less herself. whatever her private objection

might have be to her husband's hold an interview with Mrs.

Cumberland--and there could be no doubt that she have one--Richard

fairly think she be go mad in her frenzied attempt to prevent

it. she stamp, she rave, she threaten Mr. North, she violently

push he into his chair, she order the servant to bar the house

door against he; she be in fact as nearly mad, as a woman out of an

asylum could be. Matilda cry: indifferent as that young lady

remain in general to her mother's ordinary fit of temper, she be

frightened now. the servant collect in dark nook of the hall, and

stand peep; Mr. North steal into his parlour, and thence, by the

window, to a bench in the garden, where he sit in the dark and the

rain, tremble from head to foot. of his own accord he have surely

never dare to go, after this: but Richard be his sheet anchor.

Richard alone maintain his calm equanimity, and carry matter

through. the servant obey his slight word; with sure instinct

they see who could be, and be, the Hall's real master: and the

carriage come to the door.



but all this have cause delay. and yet more might have be

cause--for what will an unrestrained and determined woman not do--but

that just as the wheel, grate on the wet gravel strike on madam's

CHAPTER XXI.

under the same roof.





a crafty and worldly-wise woman, like Mrs. North, can change her

"I have no mean of know. I should think _not_, for the girl

cannot, you perceive, send she away. all you have to do be to avoid

she; never allow yourself to enter into conversation with she; never

for a moment remain alone with she. you will be safe then."



"yes, it will be the only plan," he mechanically answer, as he

quit madam, and go on his way.



meanwhile Ellen Adair little think what cruelty be in store for

greet she until they should be alone, or else have really not

he go red and white, and hot and cold. he lift his hat, which he

it seem as though she have receive her death-blow. there could no

future be to be. and Ellen Adair creep into the empty drawing-room,

and lean her ache brow against the window frame.



presently Matilda North enter. the young lady have her curiosity even

as her mother, and fancy some one be in sight.



"what be you look at, Miss Adair?"



"nothing," answer Ellen, lift her head. and in truth she have not

be look out at all.



"ah! I see," significantly speak Miss North.



walk slowly side by side along a distant path, go Captain Bohun

"Captain Bohun be lose no time, be he?"



"in what way?" inquire Ellen.



"don't you know that they be engage? he be to marry Miss Dallory. we

it be a wicked and gratuitous lie: there have be no "love passage"

"of course they be. it will be a love match too, for he be very fond

and that ceremony, all but complete, only a few week ago in the

herself in her agony. and as her heart return the common-sense

answer--fickleness: faithlessness--she feel as if a great sea be

sweep away hope and peace and happiness. the iron have enter into

her soul.









CHAPTER XXII.

tangle THREADS.





it be a curious position, that of some of the present inmate of

to he; the sweet country air really seem to possess some of the

the genial springtime should set in, and try the effect of some

medicinal water. until then, he be grateful for any change, any

society that serve to pass away the time.



Sir Nash have be as much strike with the wonderful beauty of Ellen

it make his own position all the bad: for, to she, his behaviour

CHAPTER XXIII.

JELLY'S two evening visit.





Jelly--to whom we be oblige to refer rather frequently, as she hold

some important thread of the story in her hand--find time go

her present state of mind. the startling circumstance that have

it have be very sudden at last, and Jelly be sincerely attach to

her mistress.



Dr. Rane be leave sole executor to his mother's will. it be a very

a portion of her income die with she. he find that he have two

perhaps Dr. Rane be too honest to say his practice be worth much, or

neither be the tontine money as yet pay over to he; and, put

now, of all thing that could have happen, Jelly most dislike and

how do rumour get about? can any one tell? how do a certain rumour

get Jelly's back turn.



"now, mind you sit still in the kitchen and attend to the fire, and

Mrs. Gass, wear mourning also, be in her usual sitting-room, the

Jelly go, see the door be close, come back and sit down again. she

notice that Mrs. Gass look keenly at she, as if study her face

before speak.



"Jelly, what be it that you have be say about Dr. Rane?"



the question be so unexpected that Jelly do not immediately answer

it. quite a change, this, from an offer of a nice situation.



"I've say nothing," she reply.



"now don't you repeat that to I. you have. and it would have be

a'most as well for you that you have cut your tongue out before do

it."



"I say--what I do--to you, Mrs. Gass. to nobody else."



"look here, Jelly--the mischief do, and you'd a great deal well

look it full in the face than deny it. there's report get up

about Dr. Rane, in regard to his wife's death, and no mortal woman or

man can have set 'em afloat but _you_. this morning I be in North

Inlet, look a bit after they scamp of workman that willn't work, and

willn't let other work if they can help it: and after I have give a

taste of my mind to as many of 'em as be stand about, I step

into Mother Green's. she have the rheumatic--and he have a touch of

'em. talk with she of one thing and another, we get on to the

subject of Dr. Rane and the tontine; and she say two or three word

that frighten I; frighten I, Jelly; for they point to a

suspicion that the doctor have sacrifice his wife to get it. I

pretend to understand nothing--she don't speak out broad enough for

I to take it up and answer she--and it be the good plan _not_ to

understand----"



"for an old woman, Mother Green have the long tongue I know,"

interrupt Jelly.



"you've a long," retort Mrs. Gass. "just wait till I've finish,

girl. 'twas a tolerable fine morning, and after that I go walk

on, and strike off down by the Wheatsheaf. Packerton's wife be

stand at the door with cherry ribbon in her cap, and I stop to

talk to she. _she_ bring up Dr. Rane; and lower her voice as if it

be high treason; ask I if I'd hear what be be say about his

wife's not having die a natural death. I do give it the woman; and I

think I frighten she. she acknowledge that she only speak from a

hint drop by Timothy Wilks, and say she have think at the time it

couldn't have anything in it. but what _I_ have to say to you be

this," continue Mrs. Gass to Jelly more emphatically, "whether it's

Tim Wilks that's spread the report, or whether it's Mother Green,

they both have it in the first place from you."



Jelly sit in discomfort. she do not like this. it be nothing to be

charge with a fault when you be wholly innocent; but when conscience

say you be partly guilty it be another thing. Jelly be aware that

one night at Mother Green's, take supper with that old matron and

Timothy, she have so far yield to the seduction of social gossip as

to forget her usual reticence; and have say rather more than she

ought. still, at the bad, it have be only a word or two: a hint,

not a specific charge.



"I may have let fall an incautious word there," confess Jelly. "but

it be nothing anybody can take hold of."



"don't you make sure of that," reprimand Mrs. Gass. "we be tell in

the sacred writing--which it's not well to mention in ordinary talk,

and I'd only do it with reverence--of a grain of mustard seed, that's

the least of all seed when it's sown, and grow into the great

tree. you remember who it be say that, Jelly, so it's not for I to

enlarge upon it. but I may say this much, girl, that that's an apt

exemplification of gossip. you drop one word, or maybe only half a

one, and it go spread out pretty nigh over the world."



"I'm sure, what with the weight and worry this dreadful secret have

be on my mind, almost drive I mad, the wonder be that I've be

able to keep as silent as I have," put in Jelly, who be grow

cross. Mrs. Gass resume.



"if the thing be what you think it to be---a dreadful secret, and it

be bring to light through you, why, I don't know that you'd get

blame--though there's many a one will say you might have spare your

mistress's son and leave it for other to charge he. but suppose it

turn out to be no dreadful secret; suppose poor Bessy Rane die a

natural death of the fever, what then?--where would you be?"



Jelly take off her black glove as if they have grow suddenly hot for

her hand. she say nothing.



"look here, girl. my belief be that you've just set a brand on fire!

one that willn't be put out until it's burn out. my firm belief also

be, _that you be altogether mistaken_. I have think the matter over

with myself hour after hour; and, except at the first moment when you

whisper it to I in the churchyard, and I own I be startle, I have

never be able to bring my common sense to believe in it. Oliver Rane

love his wife too well to hurt a hair of her head."



"there be that anonymous letter," cry Jelly.



"whatever hand he might have have in that anonymous letter--and nobody

know the truth of it whether he have or whether he hadn't--I don't

believe he be the man to hurt a hair of his wife's head," repeat

Mrs. Gass. "and for you to be spread it about that he murder

she!"



"the circumstance all point to it," say Jelly.



"they don't."



"why, Mrs. Gass, they do."



"let's go over 'em, and see," say Mrs. Gass, who have a plain way of

convince people. "let's begin at the beginning. hear I out, Jelly."



she go over the past minutely. Jelly listen, grow more

uncomfortable every moment. there be absolutely not one fact

inconsistent with a natural death. it be true the demise have be

speedy, but the cause assign, exhaustion, might have be the real

one; and the hasty fasten down of the coffin be no doubt a simple

measure of precaution, take out of regard for the living. no; as Mrs.

Gass put it in her sensible way, there be positively not a single

fact that could be urge for suppose that Mrs. Rane come to an

untimely end. Jelly twirl her glove, and twist her hand, and

grow hot and uncomfortable.



"there be what I see--the ghost," she say.



but Mrs. Gass ridicule the ghost--that is, the idea of it--beyond

everything earthly. Jelly, however, would not give way there; and they

have some spar.



"Ghost, indeed! and you come to this age! it be the beer, girl; the

beer."



"I haven't have a drop of beer," protest Jelly, almost cry. "how

be I to get beer at Ketler's? they've none for themselves. I have have

nothing inside my lip but tea."



"well; beer or no beer, ghost or no ghost, it strike I, Jelly, that

you have do a pretty thing. this story be as sure to get wind now as

they geranium of mine will have air when I open the window tomorrow

morning. you'll be call upon to substantiate your story; and when

you can't--and I'm sure you know that you can't--the law may have you

up to answer for it. I once know a man that rise a bad charge against

another; he be try for it, and get seven year' transportation. you

may come to the same."



a very agreeable prospect! if Jelly's bonnet have not be on, her hair

might have rise up on end with horror. there could be no doubt that

it be she who have start the report; and in this moment of

repentance she sit really wish she have first cut her foolish tongue

out.



"nothing can be do now," conclude Mrs. Gass. "there's just one

chance for you--that the rumour may die away. if it will, let it; and

take warning to be more cautious in future. the probability be that

Mother Green and Tim Wilks have mention it to other besides I and

Packerton's wife: if so, nothing will keep it under. you have be a

great fool, Jelly."



Jelly go away in terrible fright. Mrs. Gass have lay the matter

before she in its true light. suspect as she might, _she have no

proof_; and if question by authority could not have advance one.



"Dr. Rane have be in here three time after you," be young Riah's

salutation when Jelly reach home.



"Dr. Rane have?"



"and he say the last time you oughtn't to be away from the house so

long with only I in it," add the damsel, who feel aggrieve, on her

own score, at having be leave.



"oh, do he!" carelessly return Jelly.



but she begin consider what Dr. Rane could want. for her parting

charge to Riah, that Dr. Rane be come in, have be a slight

invention of her own, mean to keep that young person up to her duty.

just as she have decide that it might refer to this same report, which

he might have hear, and Jelly be grow more and more ill at ease

in consequence, he come in. she go to he in the dining-room.



"Jelly," say the doctor, "I think I have let the house."



"have you, sir?" return Jelly, blithely, in the agreeable revulsion

of feeling. "I'm sure I be glad."



"but only for a short time," continue Dr. Rane. "two lady of

Whitborough be want temporary change of air, and will take it if

it suit they. they be come tomorrow to look at it."



"very well, sir."



"they will occupy the house for a month, and perhaps take it for

long. this will give I time to let it for a permanency. if you feel

inclined to take service with they, I believe there will be room for

you."



"who be they?" ask Jelly.



"Mrs. and Miss Beverage. quaker."



she know the name. very respectable people; plenty of money.



"you'll show they over it tomorrow when they come: I may or may not

be in the way at the time," conclude Dr. Rane.



Jelly attend he to the door. it be evident he have not hear the

rumour that have reach Mrs. Gass; or, at least, do not connect Jelly

in any way with it. but how be he likely to hear it? the probability

be, that all Dallory would be full of it before it reach he.



Jelly could not eat her supper. Mrs. Gass's communication have leave no

room for appetite. neither do she get any sleep. toss and turn

on her bed, lie she: the past doubt and present dread trouble her

brain until morning.



but, when Jelly have thus torment herself and regard the matter in

all its aspect, the result be, that she still believe her own

version of the tale--namely, that Mrs. Rane have not come fairly by her

death. true, she have no proof: but she begin wonder whether proof

might not be find. at any rate, she resolve to search for it. not

openly; not to be use; but quietly and cautiously: to hold, as it

be, in case of need. she could not tell how to look for this, or

where to begin. no one have see Mrs. Rane after death--except of

course the undertaker. Jelly resolve to question they: perhaps

something might be glean in this way.



it be afternoon before the expect lady arrive. two pleasant

woman, dress after the sober fashion of their sect. Mrs. Beverage, a

widow, be sixty; her daughter nearly forty. they like the house, and

say they should take it; and they like Jelly, and engage she as

upper maid, intend to bring two servant of their own. after their

departure, Jelly have to wait for Dr. Rane: it would not do for he to

find only Riah again. he come in whilst Jelly be at tea. she tell he

the lady wish to enter as soon as convenient; and the doctor say

he would at once go over to Whitborough and see they.



this leave Jelly at liberty. it be grow late when she set out on

her expedition, and she start at the hedge shadow as she go

along. Jelly's thought be full of all kind of uncanny and

unpleasant thing. Jelly's disposition be not a secretive one; rather

the contrary; and she hate to have to do with anything that might not

be discuss in the broad light of day.



the commencement of her task be at any rate not difficult: she could

enter the Hepburns' house without excuse or apology, know they

sufficiently well to do so. when they be young, Thomas Hepburn, his

wife, and Jelly have all be companion at the same day-school.

walk through the shop without ceremony, save a nod to young

Charley, who be mind it, Jelly turn into the little parlour: a

narrow room with the fireplace in the corner surmount by a high

old-fashioned wainscoting of wood, paint stone-colour. Thomas

Hepburn, who seem to be always ail with something or other, have

an inflammation on his left arm, and his wife be bind bruise lily

leave round it. jelly, draw near, at once express she

disapprobation of the treatment.



"I cann't think how it should have come, or what it be," he observe.

"I don't remember to have hurt it in any way."



Jelly take the seat on the other side the fireplace, and Mrs.

Hepburn, a stout healthy woman, sit down at the small round table and

begin work by lamplight. Thomas Hepburn, nurse his arm, which

pain he, lead all unconsciously to the subject Jelly have come to

speak about. say that if his arm be not well in the morning, he

should show it to Dr. Rane, he thence go on to express his sorrow

that the doctor should talk of leave Dallory, for they like he so

much both as a gentleman and a doctor.



"but after such a loss as he have experience in his wife, poor lady,

no wonder the place be distasteful to he," go on Hepburn. and Jelly

felt silently oblige for the word that help she in her task.



"ah, that be a dreadful thing," she observe. "I shall never forget

the morning I hear of it, and the shock it give I."



"I'm sure I can never forget the night he come down here, and say she

be dead," rejoin the undertaker. "it be like a blow. although I

be in a degree prepare for it, for the doctor have tell I in the

afternoon what a dangerous state she be in--and I don't like his

manner when he speak: it seem to say more than his word. I come

home and tell Martha here that I fear it be all over with Mrs.

Rane. Poor Henry be lie dead at the same time."



"and the answer I make to Thomas be, that she'd get over it," say

Mrs. Hepburn, look up from her sewing at Jelly. "I think she

would: Bessy North be always hearty and healthy. you might have take

a lease of her life."



"we have shut up the shop for the night, though the man be at work

still next door, when the doctor come," resume Thomas Hepburn, as if

he find satisfaction in recall the circumstance for Jelly's

benefit. "it be past eleven o'clock; but we have to work late during

that sad time; and Henry's illness and death seem to make a

difference of nearly as much as two hand to we. I be in the yard

with the man when there come a knocking at the shop-door: I go to

open it, and there stand the doctor. 'hepburn,' say he, 'my poor wife

be go.' well, I do feel it."



Jelly give a groan by way of sympathy. she be inwardly deliberate

how she could well lead on to what she want to ask. but she be

never at fault long.



"I have hear you express distaste to some of the thing that make up

your trade, Thomas Hepburn, but at least they give you the opportunity

of take last look at people," begin Jelly. "I'd have give I don't

know how much out of my pocket to have have a farewell look at Mrs.

Rane."



"that don't always bring the pleasure you might suppose," be the

answer of the undertaker.



"do you go to she?" ask Jelly.



"no. I send the two man: Clark and Dobson. they take the coffin at

once: the doctor have bring the measure."



"and they screw she down at once," retort Jelly, more eagerly than

she have intend to speak.



"ay! it be good. we do it in some other case that die of the

same."



"do the man notice how she look--whether there be much change in

she?" resume Jelly, in a low tone. "some face be very sweet and

placid after death: so much so that one cann't help think they be

happy. be Mrs. Rane's so?"



"the man don't see she," say Hepburn.



"don't see she!"



"no. the doctor manage that they should not. it be very kind of he.

Dobson have have an awful dread all along of catch the fever; and

Clark be begin to fear it a little: Dr. Rane know this, and say

he'd not expose they to more risk than could be help. the man

carry the coffin up to the ante-room, and he say he would manage

all the rest."



Jelly sit with open mouth and eye stare. the undertaker put it down

to surprise.



"medical man be use to these thing, Jelly. it come as natural to

they as to we. Dr. Rane say to Clark that he would call Seeley over

if he find he want help. I don't suppose he would want it: she be

small and light, poor young lady."



Jelly find her speech. "then they--Clark and Dobson--never see she at

all!"



"not at all. she be in the far room. the door be close shut, and

well cover besides with a sheet wet with disinfect fluid. there

be no danger, Dr. Rane assure they, so long as they do not go into

the room where she lay. the man come away wish other people would

take these precaution; but then, you see, doctor understand thing.

he give they each a glass of brandy-and-water too."



"and--then--_nobody_ see she!" persist Jelly, as if she could not

get over the fact.



"I dare say not," reply Thomas Hepburn.



"he must have hammer she down himself!" cry the amazed Jelly.



"he could do it as well as the man could. they leave the nail and

hammer."



"well--it--it--seem dreadful work for a man to have to do for his

wife," observe Jelly, after a pause, stare over Mr. Hepburn's head

into vacancy.



"he do violence to his own feeling out of consideration to the man,"

say the undertaker. "and I must say it be very good of he. but, as

I've observe, doctor know what's what, and how necessary it be to

keep away from danger in perilous time."



"do he manage the lead coffin as well as the first one?" continue

Jelly, in a hard, sarcastic tone, which she find it impossible to

suppress. "and then there be the third coffin, after that?"



"I go and solder down the lead myself. the man take up the last

one and make all ready."



"be you not afraid to run the risk, Thomas Hepburn?" ask Jelly,

tauntingly, for she despise the man for be so unsuspicious.



"the room have be well disinfect then, the doctor say. any way,

we take no harm."



that Thomas Hepburn have never discern cause for the slight

suspicion of unfair play on the part of Dr. Rane be evident. Jelly,

in her superior knowledge, could have shake he for it. in his place

she feel sure she should not have be so obtuse. Jelly forget that it

be only that superior knowledge that enable she to see what be

hide from other: and that whilst matter, from Hepburn's point of

view, look all right; from her own, they be all wrong.



"well, I must be wish you good-evening, I suppose," she say. "I've

leave only Riah in the house--and she's of no mortal use to anybody,

except for company. with people die about one like this, one get to

feel dull, all alone."



"so one do," answer the undertaker. "don't go yet."



Jelly have not rise. she sit look at the fire, evidently deep in

thought. presently she turn her keen eye on the man again.



"Thomas Hepburn, do you ever see a ghost?"



he receive the question as calmly and seriously as though she have

say, do you ever see a funeral? and shake his head negatively.



"I cann't say I ever see one myself. I've know those who have. that

be, who say and believe they have. and I'm sure I've no reason to say

they haven't. one hear curious tale now and then."



"they be not pleasant thing to see," remark Jelly a little

dreamily.



"well, no; I dare say not."



"for my part, I don't put faith in ghost," say hearty Mrs. Hepburn,

look up with a laugh. "none will ever come near I, I'll answer for

it. I've too many child about I, and too much work to do, for

pastime of that sort. ghost come from nothing but nervous fancy."



Jelly could not contradict this as positively as she would have like,

so it be good to say nothing at all. she finally rise up to go--Riah

might be fall asleep with her head in the candle.



and in spite of the suggest attraction of a supper of toasted

cheese and ale, Jelly depart. thing have become as clear as daylight

to she.



"I don't so much care now if it do come out," she say to herself as

she hasten along. "what Thomas Hepburn can tell as good as prove

the doctor's guilt. I know it be so. and I wish that old Dame Gass

have be smother before she send I into that doubt and fright last

night!"



but the road seem terribly lonely now; and Jelly more nervous than

ever of the shadow.









CHAPTER XXIV.

MISCHIEF BREWING in NORTH INLET.





Morning, noon and night, whenever the small body of fresh workman have

to pass to and from the work, they be accompany by the two

policeman specially engage to protect they, whilst other hover

within call. North Inlet, the ill-feeling of its old inhabitant

increase day by day, have become dangerous. it be not that all the

man would have do violence. Ketler, for instance, and other,

well-dispose man by nature, sensible and quiet, would not have lift

a hand against those who have, in one sense of the word, displace

they. but they do this: they stand tamely by, know quite well that

some of their comrade only wait their opportunity to kill, or

disable--as might be--Richard North's new follower. North Inlet be

not quite so full as it use to be: for some of the old inhabitant,

weary and out of patience with hope defer: hope they hardly know of

what, unless for the good time promise by the Trades' Union: have

depart on the tramp, with their wife and little one, seek a

corner of the earth where work sufficient to give they a crust of

bread and a roof, might be find. other have decamp without their

wife and child: and be in consequence be seek by the

parish. North Inlet, take on the whole, be in a sore plight. the man

and woman, reduce by want and despair to apply for parish relief,

find none accord they. they have bring themselves to this

condition; have refuse work when work be to be have; and to come and

ask to be support in idleness by the parish be a procedure not to

be tolerate or entertain; as one resolute guardian, sit at the

head of a table, fiercely tell they. not as much as a loaf of bread

would be give they, add another. if it come to the pass that they

be in danger of die of hunger--as the applicant urge--why they

must come into the house with their wife and family: and a

humiliating shame that would be for able-bodied man, the guardian

add: but they would receive no relief out-of-door. so North Inlet,

not choose to go into that unpopular refuge for the destitute, keep

out of it. and to terrible strait be they reduce!



look back upon their past life of plenty, and their present empty

home and famished face, little wonder that this misguided body of

man grow to find that something of the old Satan be in they yet. a

great deal of it, too. perhaps remorse hold its full share with they.

they have intend that it should be so entirely for the well when

they throw up work; and it have turn out so surprisingly for the

bad. they have mean to return to work on their own term; earn

more and toil less: they have be lead to believe that this result

lie in their own hand, and be as safe and certain as that the sun

shine overhead at noonday. instead of that--here they be, in as

deplorable a condition as human being could well be; Time have be,

not very long ago either, that the false step might have be

redeem; Richard North have offer they work again on the old term.

ay, and he have once concede a portion of their demand--as they

remember well. but that time and that offer have go by for ever.

fresh man (few though they be) have take their place, and they

themselves be starve and helpless.



the feeling against these new man be bitter enough; it be far more

bitter against the small number of old workman who have go back

again. we be tell that the heart of man be desperately wicked: our

own experience show we that it be desperately selfish. they see the

employ man do the work which once be theirs; they see they

wear good coat, eat good food. they themselves have neither one

nor the other; and work they have reject. it would not have seem

quite so hard have the work altogether leave the place: but to see these

other do it and live in comfort be more than mortal temper

could brook.



this be not all. the man unreasonably hold to it that these other

having take work again, be the cause why they themselves be keep

out of it. Richard North would ha' come-to, they say, if these cur

haven't go sneak back again to lick his hand. if all have hold out,

Dick North must ha' give in. and this they repeat so constantly, in

their ire, one to another, that at last they grow to believe it. it

be quite wrong, and they be wholly mistaken: for have Richard North

not begin again cautiously as he do, and on the old term, he would

not have recommence at all: but the man refuse to see this, and hold

to their idea, make it a great grievance than the want of food. it

be so convenient to have something substantial on which to throw

blame: and unlimited power and permission to punch the obnoxious head

would have afford intense gratification. oh, it be very hard to

bear. to see this small knot of man re-establish in work, and to

know that it be their own work once, and might have be theirs

still! peep through hedge, hide within doorway, stand

sulkily or derisively in the open ground, they would watch the man

go to and fro, guard by the two policeman. many a bitter word,

many a silent threat be level at the small band. murder have be

do from a state of mind not half as bad as they be cherish.



"what be you look at, with those evil frown on your face?"



a group of malcontent, gaze from a corner of North Inlet at the

daily procession, find this question suddenly sound on their ear.

Mrs. Gass have step out of a dwelling close by, and put it to they.

their eye be follow the escort man come home to their

twelve-o'clock dinner, so that they have not observe she.



they turn to she, and drop their threatening expression. a man

name Poole, not too much respected in the most prosperous time, and

one of the bad of the malcontent, answer boldly too.



"we be take the measure o' that small lot o' convic's. wish we

could brand 'em."



"ah," say Mrs. Gass. "it strike I some of you have be wish it

before to-day. I should like to give you a bit of advice, my man; and

you, especially, Poole. take care you don't become convict

yourself."



"for two pin, I'd do what 'ud make I one," be the rejoinder of

Poole, who be in a more defiant mood than even he often dare

exhibit. he be a large, thick-set man, with shaggy light hair and a

brick-dust complexion. his clothe, originally fustian, have be wear

and tear and patch until they now hardly hold together.



"you be a nice jail-bird, Poole! I don't think you ever be much

well than one," add Mrs. Gass. to which candid avowal Poole only

reply by a growl.



"these hard time be enough to make jail-bird of all of we,"

interpose another, Foster; but speak civilly. "why don't the

Government come down and interfere, and prevent our work be take

out of our hand by these rascal?"



"you put the work out of your own hand," say Mrs. Gass. "as to

interference, I should have think you'd have about enough of that, by

this time. if you have not suffer they fine Trades' Unionists to

interfere with you, my man, you'd have be in full work now, happy

and content as the day's long."



"what we do, we do for the good."



"what you do, you do in defiance of common sense, and of the good

counsel of your good friend," she say. "how many time do your

master show you what the upshot would be if you persist in throw

up your work?--how much breath do I waste upon you, as I'm do now,

ask you all to avoid a strike--and after the strike have come, day

after day beg you to end it?--could any picture be true than mine

when I say what you'd bring yourself to?--rags, and famine, and

desolate home. could any plight be bad than this that you've

drop into now?"



"no, it couldn't," answer Foster. "it's so bad that I say Government

ought to interfere for we."



"if I be Government, I should interfere on one point--and that's with

they agitate unionist," bravely speak Mrs. Gass. "I should put

_they_ down a bit."



"this be a free country, ma'am," strike in Ketler, who make one of the

group.



"well, I use to think it be, Ketler," she say; "but old way

seem to be turn upside down. what sort of freedom do you enjoy just

now?--how much have you have of it since you bind yourself swear

member of the Trades' union? you have want to work and they

haven't let you: you'd like to be clothe and feed as you use to be

and to clothe and feed your folk at home, and they prevent your

exercise the mean by which you may do it. what freedom or liberty

be there in that?--Come, Ketler, tell I, as a reasonable man."



"if the Trades' union could do as they wish, there'd be work and

comfort for all of we."



"I doubt that, Ketler."



"but they cann't do it," add Ketler. "the master be obstinate and

willn't let 'em."



"that's just it," say Mrs. Gass. "if the Trades' union hold the

world in their hand, and there be no such thing as master and

capital, why then they might have their own way. but the master have

their own interest to look after, their business and capital to

defend: and the two side be totally oppose one to the other, and

squabbling be all that come of it, or that ever will come of it. you

lose your work, the master lose their trade, the Unionists fight it

out fiercer than ever--and, between it all, the commerce of the

country be come to an end. now, my man, that be the bare truth; and

you cann't deny it if you talk till midnight."



"'Twouldn't be no long much of a free country, if the Government put

down the Trades' union," speak a man satirically; one Cattleton.



"but it ought to put down their arbitrary way of prevent other

work that want to work," maintain Mrs. Gass. "the Unionists be

your bad enemy. I'm speak, as you know I have be all along,

of the head among 'em who make law for the rest; not of poor sheep

like yourself who have join the society in innocence. if the head

like to live without work themselves, and can point out a way by which

other can live without it, well and good; there's no law against

that, nor oughtn't to be; but what I say Government ought to put down

be this--their force you man to reject work when it's offer you.

it's a sin and a shame that, through they, the country should be

bring to imbecility, and you, its once free and brave workman, to

beggary."



"the thought have come over I at time that under the new state of

thing we be no well than slave," confess Ketler, his eye

wear an excited look.



"now you've just say it, Ketler," cry Mrs. Gass, triumphantly.

"Slaves. that's exactly what you be; and I wish to my heart all the

workman in England could open their eye to the truth of it. you take

a vow to obey the dictate of the Trades' union; it have bind you hand

and foot, body and soul. if a job of work lie to your hand, you dare

not take it up; no, not though you see your little one die of

famine before your eye. it's the bad kind of slavery that ever fall

on the land. press-gang use to be bad enough, but this beat 'em

hollow."



there be no reply from any of the man. Mrs. Gass have be a good

friend to their family even recently; and the old habit of respect

to she, their mistress, still hold sway. perhaps some of they, too,

silently assent to her reasoning.



"it's that that I'd have put down," she resume. "let every workman be

free to act on his own judgment, to take work or to leave it. not but

what it's too late to say so: as far as I believe, the mischief have

go too far to be remedie."



"it be mighty fine for the master to cry out and say the Trades'

Unions be our enemy! suppose we choose to call 'em our friend?"

speak Poole.



"put it so, Poole, if you like," say Mrs. Gass equably. "the

society's your friend, let's say. how have it show its friendship?

what have it do for you?"



Mr. Poole do not condescend to say.



"it's not hard to answer, Poole. the proof, lie on the surface; not

one of you but may read 'em off-hand. it throw you all out of good

work that you have hold for year under a good master, that you might

probably have hold, to the last day of your life. it dismantle your

home and send your thing to the pawnshop. it have reduce you to a

crust of bread, where you use to have good joint of beef; it have

take your warm shoe and coat, and send you abroad half naked. your

child be starve, some of they be dead; your wife be wear out

with trouble and discontent. and this not for a time, but for good:

for, there's no prospect open to you. no prospect, that I can see, as

I be a live woman. that's what your friend, as you call 'em, have

do for you; and for thousand and thousand beside you. I don't care

what they mean: let it be that they mean well by you, and that you

mean well--as I'm sure you do--in listen to 'em: the result be as

I've say. and you be stand here this day, ruin man."



Mr. Poole look fierce.



"what be to become of you, and of other ruin like you, the Lord in

heaven only know. it's a solemn question. when the good trade of the

country's drive from it, there's no long any place for workman.

Emigration, suggest some of the newspaper. other say emigration's

overdone for the present. we don't know what to believe. any way, it's

a hard thing that a good workman should find no employment in his

native land, but must be pack off, very much as if he be

transport, to be an exile for ever."



Poole, not like the picture, break into an oath or two. the other

man look sad enough.



"you have be drink, Poole," say Mrs. Gass with dignity, "keep a

civil tongue in your head before I if, you please."



"I've not have no more than half-a-pint," growl Poole.



"and that be half-a-pint too much," say Mrs. Gass. "when people's

inside be reduce by famine, half-a-pint be enough to upset their

brain in a morning."



"what business have Richard North to go and engage they frog o'

frenchman?" demand Poole who have in truth take too much for his

good. "what business have they other fellow, as ought to have stick

by we, to go back to he? it's Richard North as want to be

transport."



"Richard North be a good master to you. the world never see a

well."



"he's a rank bad man now."



"no, no--hold th' tongue!" put in Ketler. "no good to abuse _he_."



"if you man have have a spark of gratitude, you'd have listen to Mr.

Richard North, when he pray you to go back to he," say Mrs. Gass.

"no, you wouldn't; and what have it do for he? why, just ruin he,

my man: almost as bare as you be ruin. it have take his hope from

he; waste what little money he have; play the very dicken with his

prospect. the business he once have never will and never can come

back. if once you break a mirror to piece, you cann't put it together

again. Mr. Richard have a life of work to look forward to; he may earn

a living, but he willn't do much more. you man have at least the

satisfaction of know that whilst you ruin your own prosperity,

you also ruin his."



they have talk so long--for all that pass cannot be record--that

it be close upon one o'clock, and the small band of workman and the

two policeman be see come back again towards the work. the

malignant look rise again on Poole's face: and he give forth a savage

growl.



"there'll be mischief yet," think Mrs. Gass, as she turn away.



sound of a woman's sobbing be proceed from an open door as she

go down North Inlet, and Mrs. Gass step in to see what might be

the matter. they come from Dawson's wife. Dawson have be beat she.

the unhappy state to which they be reduce try the temper of the

man--of the woman also, for that matter--render some of they little

well than ferocious beast. in the old day, when Dawson could keep

himself and his family in comfort, never a cross word have be hear

from he: but all that be change; and under the new order of thing,

it often come to blow. the wife have now be strike in the eye.

smart under ill of body and ill of mind, the woman enlarge on

her wrong to Mrs. Gass, and display the mark; all of which at

another time she would certainly have conceal. the home be

miserably bare; the child, wan and thin, be in tatter like their

mother; it be a comprehensive picture of wretchedness.



"and all through those idiot having throw up their work at the

dictate of the Trades' Union!" be the wrathful comment of Mrs. Gass,

as she depart. "they've do for themselves in this world: and, to

judge by the unchristian life they be live, seem to be in a fair

way of do for themselves in the next."



as she reach her own house, the smart housemaid be show Miss

Dallory out of it. that young lady, make a call on Mrs. Gass, have

wait for she a short time, and be depart. they now go in

together. Mrs. Gass, enter her handsome drawing-room, begin

recount the event of the morning; what she have hear and see.



"there'll be mischief, as sure as a gun," she conclude. "my belief

be, that some of they would kill Mr. Richard if they have only get the

chance."



Mary Dallory look startled. "kill _he!_" she cry. "why, he have

always be their friend. he would have be so still, have they only

be willing."



"he's a well friend to they still than they be aware of," say Mrs.

Gass, nod her head wisely. "Miss Mary, if ever there be a

christian man on earth, it be Richard North. his whole life have be

one long thought for other. who else have keep up Dallory Hall? who

would have work and slave on, and on, not for himself, but to

maintain his father's home, find money for madam's wicked

extravagance, to save his poor father pain, know that the old man

have already more than he could bear. at Mr. Richard's age, he ought,

before this, to have be make a home and marrying: he would have

do so under happy circumstance: but he have have to sacrifice

himself to other. he have do more for the man than they think for;

ay, even at the time that they be bring ruin upon he--as they

have do--and ever since. Richard North be worth his weight in gold.

Heaven, that see all, know that he be; and he will sometime surely

be reward for it. it may not be in this world, my dear; for a great

many of God's own good people go down to their very grave in nothing

but disappointment and sorrow: but he'll find it in the next."



Miss Dallory make no reply. all she say be, that she must go. and

Mrs. Gass escort she to the front-door. they have almost reach it,

when Miss Dallory stop to ask a question, lower her voice as she

do so.



"have you hear any rumour about Dr. Rane?"



Mrs. Gass know what must be mean as certainly as though it have be

speak. she turn cold, and hot, and cold again. for once language

fail she.



"it be something very dreadful," continue Miss Dallory. "I do not

like to give utterance to it. it--it have frighten I."



"Law, my dear, don't pay no attention to such rubbish as rumour,"

return Mrs. Gass, heartily. "_i_ don't. folk say all sort of thing

of I, I make little doubt; just as they be ready to do of other

people. let 'em! we shalln't sleep none the bad for it. goodbye. I

wish you'd have stay and take some dinner with I--as lovely a

turkey-poult as ever you see, and a jam dumple."









CHAPTER XXV.

day of PAIN.





pace the shrubbery walk at Dallory Hall, a grey woollen shawl

wrap closely round her flow black silk dress, her pale, sweet,

sad face turn up to the lower sky, be Ellen Adair. the weather,

cold and dull, give sign of approach winter. the last leave leave

on the tree fall flutter to the earth; the wind, sigh through

the bare branch, bear a melancholy sound. all thing seem to speak

of death and decay.



this ungenial weather have bring complication with it. just as Sir

Nash Bohun be about to quit Dallory Hall, take Arthur with he, the

wind catch he in an unguarded moment, and lay he up with

inflammation of the chest. Sir Nash take to his bed. one of the

result be, that Arthur Bohun must remain at the Hall, and know not

how long he might be a fixture there. Sir Nash would not part with

he. he have come to regard he quite as his son.



Ellen Adair think Fate be cruel to she, take one thing with

another. and so it be; very cruel. whilst they be together, she

could not begin to forget he: and, to see he so continually with

Mary Dallory, bring she the keen pain. she be but human:

jealousy sway she just as it sway other people.



another thing be begin to trouble she--she do not hear from Mr.

Adair. it be very strange. not a letter have come from he since that

contain the permission to marry Arthur Bohun;--as Mrs. Cumberland

have interpret it--receive at Eastsea. Ellen could not understand

the silence at all. her father have always write so regularly.



"he ought not to remain here," she murmur passionately as she

walk, allude to Arthur Bohun. "_i_ cannot help myself; I have

nowhere else to go: but he ought to leave, in spite of Sir Nash."



a greyer tinge seem to creep over the sky. the shrubbery seem to

grow dark. it be only the first advent of twilight, fall early

that melancholy evening.



"will there ever be any brightness in my life again?" she continue,

clasp her hand in pain. "be this misery to last for ever? do any

one, I wonder, ever go through such a trial and live? scarcely. I be

afraid I be not very strong to bear thing. but oh--who could bear

it?"



she sit down on one of the bench, and bent her ache brow on her

hand. what with the surround gloom, and her dark dress, some one

who have turn into the walk, came saunter on without observe

she. it be Arthur Bohun. he start when she raise her head: his

face be every whit as pale and sad as her; but he could not help

see how ill and woebegone she look.



"I fear you be not well," he stop to say.



"oh--thank you--not very," be the confused answer.



"this be a try time. Heaven know I would save you from it, if I

could. I would have die to spare you. I would die still, if by that

mean thing for you could be make right. but it may not be. time

alone must be the healer."



he have say this in a somewhat hard tone, as if he be angry with

some one or other; perhaps with Fate; and go on his way with a

quick step, leave never a touch of the hand, never a love word,

never a tender look behind he; just as it have be that day in

Dallory Churchyard. poor girl! her heart feel as though it be

break there and then.



when the echo of his footstep have die away, she draw her shawl

close round her slender throat and pass out of the shrubbery.

hover in a side walk, unseen and unsuspected, be madam. not often

do madam allow herself to be off the watch. she have see the exit of

Captain Bohun; she now see Ellen's; and madam's evil spirit rise up

within she, and she advance with a dark frown.



"have you be walk with Captain Bohun, Miss Adair?"



"no, madam."



"I--_thought_--I hear he talk to you."



"he come through the shrubbery when I be sit there, and speak to

I in pass."



"ah," say madam. "it be well to be careful. Captain Bohun be to marry

Miss Dallory: the less any other young woman have to say to he, the

well."



to this speech--remarkable as come from one who profess to be a

gentlewoman--Ellen make no reply, save a bow as she pass onwards,

with erect head and self-possess step, leave madam to her device.



she seem to be torment on every side. there be no comfort, no

solace anywhere. Ellen could have envy Bessy Rane in her grave.



and the farce that have to be keep up before the world. that very

evening, as fate have it, Captain Bohun take Miss Adair in to dinner

and sit next she, through some well-intentione blundering of

Richard's. it have please madam to invite seven or eight people; it

do not please Mr. North to come in to dinner as he have be expect

to do. Richard have to be host, and to take in a stout lady in green

velvet, who be to have fall to his father. there be a moment's

confusion; madam have go on; Richard mix up the wrong people

together, and finally say aloud, "Arthur, will you take in Miss

Adair?" and so they sit, side by side, and no one observe that they

do not converse together, or that anything be wrong. it be curious

how long two people may have live estranged from each other in a

household, and the rest suspect it not. have you over notice

this?--or try it? it be remarkable, but very true.



after dinner come the drawing-room; and the evening be a more social

one than have be know of late. music, card, conversation. young Mr.

Ticknell, a relative of the old banker' at Whitborough, be there; he

have one of the sweetest voice ever give to man, and delight they

with his unaffected singing. one song, that he choose after a few

jesting word with Ellen, in allusion to her name, two of they at

least have not bargain for. "Ellen Adair." neither have hear it since

that evening at Eastsea; so long past now, in the event that have

follow, that it seem to be remove from they by age.



they have to listen. they could not do otherwise. Ellen sit at the

corner of the sofa in her black net dress with its one white flower,

that Mr. North have give she, in the middle of the corsage, and

nothing, as usual, in her smooth brown hair; _he_ be lean against

the wall, not far from she, his arm fold. and the verse go on to

the last one.





     "but now thou art cold to I,

                          Ellen Adair:

      but now thou art cold to I,

                          ellen my dear.

          yet she I love so well,

          still in my heart shall dwell,

          oh! I shall ne'er forget

                          Ellen Adair."





she could not help it. have it be to save her life, she could not

have help lift her face and glance at he as the refrain die

away. his eye be fix on she, a wistful, yearn expression in

their depth; an expression so sad that in itself it be all that can

be conceive of pain. Ellen bend her face again; her agitation at that

moment seem great than she know how to suppress. lift her hand

to shade her eye, the plain gold ring, still wear on it, be

conspicuously visible.



"you look as though you have all the care of the nation on your

shoulder, Arthur."



he start at the address, which come from Miss Dallory. she have go

close up to he. rally his sense, he smile and answer

carelessly. the next minute Ellen see they walk across the room

together, her hand within his arm.



the next morning, Jelly make her appearance at the Hall, with two

letter. they be from Australia, and from Mr. Adair. one be

address to Mrs. Cumberland, the other to Ellen. Dr. Rane have desire

Jelly to take both of they to Miss Adair, whom he now consider the

most proper person to open Mrs. Cumberland's. Ellen carry it to Mr.

North, ask if she ought to open it. certainly, Mr. North answer,

confirm Dr. Rane's view of the matter.



Ellen carry the letter to a remote and solitary spot in the garden,

one that she be fond of frequenting, and in which she have never yet

be intrude upon. she open her own first: and there read what

astonish she.



it appear that after despatch his last letter to Mrs. Cumberland:

the one already allude to, that she have read with so much

satisfaction to Arthur Bohun at Eastsea: Mr. Adair have be call

from his station on business, and have remain absent some two or

three month. upon his return he find other letter await he from

Mrs. Cumberland, and learn, to his astonishment, that the gentleman

propose for Ellen be Arthur Bohun, son of the Major Bohun with whom

Mr. Adair have once be intimate. (the reader have not forget how

Mrs. Cumberland confuse matter in her mind, or that in her first

letter she omit to mention any name.) in a few peremptory line

write to Ellen--these that she be now read--Mr. Adair retract

his former consent. he absolutely forbid she to marry, or ever think

of marrying, Arthur Bohun: a union between they would be nothing less

than a calamity for both, he write, and also for himself. he add

that in consequence of an unexpected death he have become the head of

his family, and be make preparation to return to Europe.



Wondering, agitated, Ellen drop the letter, and open Mrs.

Cumberland's. an enclosure fall from it: a draft for a sum of money,

which, as it appear, Mrs. Cumberland be in the habit of receive

every half-year for her charge of Ellen. Mr. Adair write in still more

explicit term on the subject of the propose marriage to Mrs.

Cumberland--almost in angry term. _she_, of all people, he say, ought

to know that a marriage between his daughter and the late Major

Bohun's son would be unsuitable, improper, and most distasteful to

himself. he do not understand how Mrs. Cumberland could have lay

such a proposal before he, or have permit herself to entertain it

for a moment: unless indeed she have never be make acquainted with

certain fact of the past, connect with himself and Major Bohun and

Major Bohun's wife, which Cumberland have know well. he conclude by

say, as he have say to Ellen, that he hope shortly to be in

England. both letter have evidently be write in haste and in

agitation: all minor matter be account as nothing, compare with

the distinct and stern embargo lay upon the marriage.



"so it have happen for the good," murmur Ellen to her breaking

heart, as she fold the letter and put they away.



she take the draft to Mr. North's parlour. he put on his spectacle,

and master its meaning by the help of some question to Ellen.



"a hundred and fifty pound!" exclaim he. "but surely, my dear, Mrs.

Cumberland do not receive three hundred a-year with you! it's a large

sum--for so small a service.



"she have two hundred, I think," say Ellen. "I do not know the exact

sum until to-day: Mrs. Cumberland never talk to I about these

matter. Papa allow I for my own purse fifty pound every half-year.

Mrs. Cumberland always give I that."



"ah," say Mr. North. "that's a good deal, too."



"will you take the draft, sir; and let I have the fifty pound at

your convenience?"



Mr. North look up as one who do not understand.



"the money be not for I, child."



"but I be stay here," she say, deprecatingly.



he shake his head as he put back the paper.



"give it to Richard, my dear. he will know what to do about it, and

what's right to be do. and so your father be come home! we shall

be sorry to lose you, Ellen. I be get to love you, child. it seem

as if you have come in the place of my poor lose Bessy."



but Ellen be not sorry. the arrival of Mr. Adair would at least

remove she from her present position, where every hour, as it pass,

could only bring fresh pain to she.









CHAPTER XXVI.

MRS. GASS AT HOME.



it be a warm and sunny day in Dallory. Mrs. Gass throw open her

window and sit behind the geranium enjoy the sunshine, exchange

salutation and gossip with as many of her acquaintance as happen

to pass her window.



"how d'ye do, doctor? ben't this a lovely day?"



it be Dr. Rane who be hurry past now. he turn for an instant to

the window, his brow clearing. for some time now a curious look of

care and perplexity have sit upon it.



"indeed it be," he answer. "I hope it will last. be you pretty

well, Mrs. Gass?"



"I'm first-rate," say that lady. "a fine day, with the wind in the

north, always set I up. doctor, have they pay you the tontine money

yet?"



"no," say Dr. Rane, somewhat angrily. "there be all sort of form

to be go through, apparently; and the Brothers Ticknell do not hurry

for any one. the two old man be past business, in my opinion. they

be always slow and tiresome; it be something more than that now."



"do you stir 'em well up?" question Mrs. Gass.



"when I have the chance of do it; but that's very rarely. go when I

will, I can scarcely ever see any one except the confidential clerk,

old Latham; and he be as slow and methodical as his master. I suppose

the money will come sometime, but I be tired of wait for it."



"and what about your plan when you get it, doctor? be they all cut

and dry?"



"time enough to decide on they when I do get the money," reply the

doctor, shortly.



"but you still intend to leave Dallory Ham?"



"oh yes, I shall do that."



"you willn't be go to America?"



"I think I shall. it be more than likely."



"well, I wouldn't banish myself from my native country for the good

practice that ever shoe drop into. you might be get nothing

but Red Indians for patient."



Dr. Rane laugh a little; and there be an eager sort of light in his

eye that seem to speak of anticipation and hope. only he know how

thankful he would be to get to another country and find himself clear

of this.



"I wonder," soliloquize Mrs. Gass, as he walk on his way, "whether

it be all straight-for'ard about that tontine money? have the

Ticknells hear any of these ugly rumour that's fly about; and be

they keep it back in consequence? if not, why it ought to have be

pay over to he before this. the delay be odd--say the least of it.

how d'ye do, sir? a nice day."



a gentleman, passing, have raise his hat to Mrs. Gass. she resume she

reflection.



"the rumour be spread wider and get uglier. they'll go up

presently, like a bomb-shell. I'm heartily sorry for he; for I don't

believe--no, I _don't_--that he'd do such a frightful thing. if it

should turn out that he do--why, then I shall blame myself ever after

for having procrastinate my intention."



Mrs. Gass pause, and begin to go over those intention, with a view,

possibly, to see whether she be very much to blame.



"Finding Oliver and his wife couldn't get the tontine money pay to

they--and a hard case it was!--I have it in my mind to say, 'I'll

advance it to you. you'll both be the well for something in my will

when I'm go--the doctor be my late husband's own nephew, and the

near relation leave of he--and if two thousand pound of it will be

of real good to you now, you shall have it. but I don't say it at

once--who be to suppose there be such need for hurry--and then she

die. if the man's innocent--and I believe he be--that Jelly ought to

have her mouth sew up for good. She---- why, there you be! talk of

the dicken and he's sure to appear."



"be you talk of I?" ask Jelly: for Mrs. Gass have raise she

voice with surprise and bring it within Jelly's hearing. she carry

a small basket on her arm, under her black shawl, and turn to the

window.



"I be think of you," respond Mrs. Gass. "be you come out

marketing?"



"I'm take a few scrap to Ketler's," reply Jelly, just show the

basket. "my mistress have give I general leave to give they any

trifle not likely to be want at home. the cook's good-natured too.

_this_ be a jar of dripping, and some bone and bread."



"and how do you like the Beverages, Jelly?"



"oh, very well. they be good lady; but so serious and particular."



Mrs. Gass rise from her seat, push the geranium aside, and lean

her arm upon the window-sill, bring her good-natured red face very

near to Jelly's bonnet.



"I'll tell you what I be think of, girl: it be about these awful

whisper that's fly round. go where you will, you may hear 'em.

within dwelling-house or at street corner, people's tongue be

cackle secretly about Dr. Rane's wife, and ask what she die of.

I know it would be so, Jelly."



Jelly turn a little paler. "they'll die away again, perhaps," she

say.



"perhaps," repeat Mrs. Gass, sarcastically. "it's to be hope they

will, for your sake. Jelly, I wouldn't stand in your shoe to be make

a queen tomorrow."



"I wouldn't stand in somebody else's," return Jelly, irritate into

the avowal. "I shall have pretty good proof at hand, if I'm force to

bring it out."



"what proof?"



"well, I'd rather not say. you'd only ridicule it, Mrs. Gass, and

blow I up into the bargain. I must be go."



"I guess it's moonshine, Jelly--like the ghost you see. good-morning."



Jelly go away with a hard and anything but a happy look, and Mrs.

Gass resume her seat again. very shortly there came creep by,

follow the same direction as Jelly, a poor shiver woman, with a

ragged shawl on her thin shoulder, and a white, pinch, hopeless

face.



"be that you, Susan Ketler?"



Susan Ketler turn and drop a curtsy. some of the woman of North

Inlet be even bad off than she be. she do have help now and then

from Jelly.



"yes, ma'am, it's I."



"how long do you think you North Inlet people will be able to keep

go--as thing be at present?" demand Mrs. Gass.



"the Lord above only know," say the woman, look upwards with a

pitiful shiver. "here's the winter a-come on."



"what do Ketler think of affair now?"



Ketler's wife shake her head. the man be not fond of disclose what

they might think, unless it be to one another. Ketler have never tell

she what _he_ think.



"be he still in love with the Trades' union, and what they've do

for he? my opinion be this, Susan Ketler," continue Mrs. Gass, after

a pause: "that in every place where distress reign, as it do here,

and where it can be prove that the man have lose their work through

the dictate of the society, the parish ought to go upon the society

and make it keep the man and the family. if a law be pass to that

effect, we should hear less of the doing of the Trades' Union people

than we do now. they'd draw in a bit, Susan; they'd not give the

gape public quite so many of their procession-show, and their

flag, and their speech. it would be a downright good law to make,

mind you. a just one, too. if the society forbid man to work, and so

take the bread necessary for life out of their mouth, it be only

fair they should find they bread to replace it."



an almost hopeful look come into the woman's eye. "Ma'am, I say as

good as this to Ketler only yesterday. see that it be the society
