that have take the bread from we, and that the consequence have be

bad instead of good, for we be starve, the society ought to put we

into work again. it might bestir itself to do that: or else support we

while we get into something."



Mrs. Gass smile pityingly. "you must be credulous, Susan Ketler, to

fancy the society can put 'em into work again. where's the work to

come from? well, it's not your fault, my poor woman, and there's more

people than I sorry for you all. and now, tell I," Mrs. Gass lower

her voice, "be any of the man talk treason still? you know what I

mean."



Mrs. Ketler glance over both her shoulder to see that no one be

within hearing, before she whisper in answer.



"they be always a-talk it. I can see it in their face as they

stand together. not Ketler, ma'am; he'd stop it if he could: he don't

wish harm to none."



"ah. I wish to goodness they'd all betake themselves off from the

place. though it's hard to say so, for there's no other open to they

that I see. well, you go on home, Susan. Jelly have just go there

with a basket of scrap for you. stay a minute, though."



Mrs. Gass quit the room, call to one of her servant. when she

return she produce a half-pint physic bottle cork up.



"it's a drop of beer," she say. "for yourself, mind, not for Ketler.

you want it, I know. put it under your shawl. it will help down

Jelly's scrap."



the woman go away with grateful tear in her eye. and Mrs. Gass sit

on and enjoy the sunshine. just then Mary Dallory come by in her

little low pony-carriage. she often drive about in it alone. see

Mrs. Gass, she draw up. that lady, without any ceremony, go out in

her cap and stand talk.



"I hear you have leave the Hall, my dear," she say, when the gossip

be come to an end.



"age ago," reply Miss Dallory. "Frank be at home again, and want

I."



"how do you enjoy your visit on the whole?"



"pretty well. it be not very lively, especially after Sir Nash be

take ill."



"he be well, Mr. Richard tell I," say the eld lady.



"yes; he sit up now. I go to see he yesterday."



"Captain Bohun look but poorly still."



"his illness be a bad one. Fancy his have jaundice. I think it

be only old people who have that."



"my dear, it attack young and old. once the liver get out of order,

there's no telling. Captain Bohun be bear in India; and they be more

liable to liver complaint, it's say, than other. you be drive

alone to-day, as usual," continue Mrs. Gass.



"I like to be independent. Frank willn't show himself in this little

chaise; he say it be no well than a respectable wheelbarrow; and

I'm sure I be not go to be trouble with a groom at my side."



"if all tale tell be true, you'll soon run a chance of lose your

independence," rejoin Mrs. Gass. "People say a certain young lady,

not a hundred mile at this moment from, my elbow, be likely to give

her heart away."



instead of reply, Mary Dallory blush violently. Observant Mrs.

Gass see and notice it.



"then it be true!" she exclaim.



"what's true?" ask Mary.



"that you be likely to be marry."



"no, it be not."



"my dear, you may as well tell I. you know I well; I'll keep good

counsel."



"but I have nothing to tell you. how can I imagine what you mean?"



"'Twasn't more than a hint I have: that Captain Bohun--Sir Arthur as he

will be--be make up his mind to have Miss Dallory, and she to have

he. Miss Mary, be it so?"



"do madam tell you that?"



"Madam wouldn't be likely to tell I--all of we in Dallory be so much

dust under her foot; quite beneath be speak to. no: 'twa she

maid, Parrit, drop it to I. she have hear it through madam,

though."



Mary Dallory laugh a little and flick the ear of the rough Welsh

pony. "I fancy madam would like it," she say.



"who wouldn't?" rejoin Mrs. Gass. "I put the question to Richard

North--whether there be anything in it? he answer there might be;

he know it be wish for."



"Richard North say that, do he? of course, so it might be--and may

be--for anything he can tell."



"but, my dear Miss Mary, be it so?"



"well--to tell you the truth, the offer have not yet be make. when it

come, why then--I dare say it will be all right."



"mean that you'll accept he."



"mean that--oh, but it be not right to tell tale beforehand, even

to you, Mrs. Gass," she break off, with a laugh. "let the offer come.

I wish it would."



"you would like it to come, child?"



"yes, I think I should."



"then be sure it will come. and God bless you, my dear, and bring you

happiness whatever turn out. though it be not just the marriage I have

carve out in my own mind for one of the two of you."



she mean Arthur Bohun. Mary Dallory think she mean herself; and

laugh again as the pony trot away.



the next friend to pass the window after Mrs. Gass have again resume

her seat, be Richard North. he do not stop at the window, but go

in. certain matter connect with the winding-up of the old firm of

North and Gass, have arise, render it necessary that he should see

Mrs. Gass.



"do as you think well, Mr. Richard," she say, after they have talk

together for a few minute. "please yourself, sir, and you'll please

I. we'll leave it at that: I know it's all safe in your hand."



"then I will do as I propose," say Richard.



"I've have Miss Dallory here--that be, in her pony-chay before the

door," observe Mrs. Gass. "I tax she with what I'd hear about she

and Captain Bohun; she don't say it be, and she don't say it

ben't: but Mr. Richard, I think there's truth in it. she as good as

say she'd like he to make she an offer: and she do say madam wish

it. so I suppose we shall have wedding card before a year's go over

our head. in their case--he next step to a baronet, and she roll

in money--there's nothing, to wait for."



"nothing," mechanically-answer Richard North.



"but I do think, as to he, that it would have be Ellen Adair.

talk of that, Mr. Richard, what be it that's amiss with she?"



"with her?--with whom?" cry Richard, start out of a reverie.



"with that sweet young lady, Ellen Adair?"



"there's nothing amiss with she that I know of."



"ben't there! there _be_, Mr. Richard, if my judgment and eye be to

be trust. each time I see she, she strike I as look bad and

bad. you notice she, sir. perhaps now the clue have be give you'll

see it too. I once know a young girl, Mr. Richard, that be die

quietly under her friend' very eye, and they never see it. never see

it at all, till an aunt come over from another country. she start

back when she see the child, and say: 'why, what have yon be do

with she? she's die.' they be take aback at that, and call in

the first doctor: but it be too late. I don't say Ellen Adair be

die, Mr. Richard; 'tisn't likely; but I'm sure she be not all right.

whether it's the mind, or whether it's the body, or whether it's the

nerve, I'm not prepared to say; but it's something."



"I will find out," say Richard.



"anything fresh about the man, Mr. Richard?"



"nothing. except that my workman be get afraid to stir out at

night, and the disaffection increase amongst the other. I cannot see

what be to be the end of it," he continue. "I do not mean of this

rivalry, but of the sad state to which the man and their family be

reduce. I often wish I do not think of it so much: it be like a

chain about I from which I cannot escape. I wish I could help they to

find work elsewhere."



"ah!" say Mrs. Gass, "work elsewhere be very nice to think about in

dreamland; but I'm afraid it'll never be see for they in reality.

it's not as if work be go a-beg: it have break up everywhere,

Mr. Richard; and shoal and shoal of man, destitute as our own, be

tramp about at this minute, like so many old raven with their

mouth open, ready to pick up anything that may fall."



Richard North go home, his mind full of what Mrs. Gass have say

about Ellen Adair. be she indeed look so ill? he find she sit

in the open seat near what would be in spring the tulip bed. Mr. North

have just leave she and go in. yes: Richard see that she look very

ill; the face be wan, the eye be sad and weary. she be cough

as he go up to she: a short, hack cough. some time ago she have

catch cold, and it seem to hang about she still.



"be you well, Ellen?" he ask, as he sit down beside she.



"yes, I believe so," be her reply. "why?"



"because I don't think you look well."



a soft colour, like the pink on a sea-shell, steal over her face as

Richard say this. but she keep silence.



"you know, Ellen, we agree to be as brother and sister. I wish to

take care of you as such: to shield you from all ill as far as I

possibly can. be you happy here?"



a moment's pause, and then Ellen take courage to say that she be not

happy.



"I should like to go elsewhere," she say. "oh, Richard, if it could

only be manage!"



"but it cannot," he answer.



"I have sufficient money, Richard."



"my dear, it be not that. of course you have sufficient. I fancy, by

sundry sign, that you will be a very rich young lady," he add,

slightly laugh. "but you have no near friend in England, and we

could not entrust you to stranger."



"if I could go for a time into some clergyman's family, or something

of that sort."



"Ellen!"



she raise her hand from beneath the grey shawl--her favourite

outdoor covering, for the shawl be warm--and pass it across she

brow. in every movement there be a languor that speak weariness of

body or of spirit.



"when Mr. Adair come home, if he find you have go into 'some

clergyman's family,' what would he think and say of we, Ellen?"



"I would tell he I go of my own accord."



"but, my dear, you cannot be allow to do thing of your own accord,

if they be not wise. I and my father be appoint to take charge of

you, and you must remain with we, Ellen, until Mr. Adair return to

England."



it be even so. Ellen's well judgment acknowledge it, in the midst

of her great wish to be away. a wish: and not a wish. to be where

Arthur Bohun be, still bring she the most intense happiness; and

this, in spite of the pain surround it, she would not willingly

have relinquish: but the cruelty of his conduct--of their

estrangement--be more than she know how to bear. it be make she

ill, and she feel that it be. there be, however, no help for it. as

Richard say, she have no friend to whom they could entrust she. the

lady in whose house she be educate have recently die, and the

establishment be be break up.



ten time a-day she long to say to Arthur Bohun, "you be ungenerous

to remain here. I cannot help myself, but you might." but pride

withhold she.



"it may be month before papa arrive, Richard."



"and if it should be! we must try to make you happy with we."



"I think I must go in," she say, after a pause. "the day have be

very fine, but it be grow cold now."



fold the shawl close to her throat, as if she feel chilly, and

cough a little as she walk, Ellen go round to the hall-door and

enter. Richard, occupy in watch she and busy with his own

thought, do not perceive the almost silent approach of Arthur Bohun,

who come slowly up from behind.



"well, Dick, old fellow!"



"why, where do you spring from?" ask Richard, as Arthur fling

himself down in the place vacate by Ellen.



"I have be under yonder tree, smoke a cigar. it have a good broad

trunk to lean against."



"I think the doctor have forbid you to smoke."



"so they have. until I grow strong. one cann't strictly obey order.

I don't suppose it matter much one way or the other. you have be

enjoy a confidential chat, Dick."



"yes," reply Richard. he have not feel very friendly in his heart

towards Arthur for some time past. what be the meaning of his change

behaviour to Ellen Adair?--what of the new friendship with Mary

Dallory? Richard North could not forgive dishonour; and he believe

Arthur Bohun be steep himself in it to the backbone.



"be you make love, Dick?"



Richard turn his eye in silence on the questioner.



"she and I have have to part, Dick. I always think you admire and

esteem she almost more, perhaps quite more, than you do any other

woman. so if you be think of her----"



"be silent," sternly interrupt Richard, rise in anger. "be you a

man?--are you a gentleman? or be you what I have be think you

lately--a false-hearted, despicable knave?"



whatever Arthur Bohun might be, he be just then in desperate

agitation. rise too, he seize Richard's hand.



"don't you see that it be but sorry jesting, Richard? pretend to a

bit of pleasantry, to wile away for a moment my weight of torment. I

be all that you say of I; and I cannot help myself."



"not help yourself?"



"as Heaven be my witness, no! if I could take you into my

confidence--and perhaps I may do so one of these day, for I long to

do it--you would see that I tell you the truth."



"why have you part from Ellen Adair?--she and you _have_ part? you

have just say so."



"we have part for life. for ever."



"you be on the point of marriage with she only a short time ago?"



"no two people could have be near marriage than she and I be. we

be within half-an-hour of it, Dick; and yet we have part."



"by your doing, or her?"



"by mine."



"I think so."



"Dick, I have be _compel_ to do it. when you shall know all, you

will acknowledge that I could not do otherwise. and yet, in spite of

this, I feel that to she I have be but a false-hearted knave, as you

aptly style I: a despicable, dishonourable man. my father fall into

dishonour--or rather have it force upon he by another--and he could

not survive it; he shoot himself. do you know it, Dick?"



"shot himself!" repeat Richard, in his surprise. "no, I never know

that. I think he die of sunstroke."



"my father shoot himself," cry Arthur. "he could not live

dishonour. Dick, old fellow, there be moment when I feel tempt

to do as he do."



"what--because you have part from Ellen?"



"no. that's bitter enough to bear; but I can battle with it. it be the

other thing, the dishonour. that be always present with I, always

haunt I night and day; I know not how to live under it."



"I do not understand at all," say Richard. "you be master of your

own action."



"in this case I have not be: my line of conduct be force upon I.

I cannot explain. don't judge I too harshly, my friend. I be bad

enough, Heaven know, but not quite as bad, perhaps, as you have be

think I."



and Arthur Bohun turn and go limp away, leave Richard lose in

wonder.



he limp away to indulge his pain where no mortal eye could see he.

part from Ellen Adair, the whole world be to he as nothing. a

sense of dishonour lie ever upon he, the shame of his conduct towards

she be present to he night and day. with all his heart he wish

James Bohun have not die, that there might have be no question of

his succession. he would then have go somewhere away with she, have

change his name, and be happy in obscurity. but there be no place

unfrequente by man; he could not change his wife's face; and she

might be recognize as the daughter of Adair the convict. besides,

would it not be an offence against Heaven if he wed the daughter of

the man who have cause the death of his father? no; happiness could

never be his. look where he would, there be nothing around he but

pain and misery.









CHAPTER XXVII.

once again.





Jelly live, so to say, on a volcano. she feel that, figuratively

speak, there be not an hour of the day or night but she might be

blow into fragment. the rumour as to the death of Mrs. Rane be

become more terrible. they steal up and down Dallory like a

scorch tongue of fire, and Jelly have the satisfaction of know

that it be she who have first set light to the flame. it be all very

well to say that she have make herself safe by secure the evidence of

Thomas Hepburn: in her secret conscience she know that she be not

safe; and that, even in spite of that evidence, Dr. Rane might chance

to be innocent. if so, why, a pretty dilemma she would find herself

in. there be no help for it; she could do nothing. the creep,

scorch tongue went twist itself in and out, and she could not

quench it.



one night Jelly be lie awake, accord to custom now, bury deep

in some horrible vision that have lately begin to haunt she: now of

work in chain; now of step incessantly up a treadmill; now of

pick oakum and live upon gruel. turn in the bed, to escape, if

possible, these imaginary picture, she suddenly hear a knock at her

door. a loud hasty knock; and now a louder. Jelly turn hot then cold

as ice. have the officer of the law come to arrest she?



"who's there?--what be it?" she ask faintly, not dare to sit up in

bed.



"art thee awake, Jelly?" come the gentle response, as her door be

open a few inch. "I be very sorry to have to ask thee to get up,

but my mother be bad. make haste, please."



have Miss Beverage's voice be that of an angel, it could not have

sound sweet to Jelly just then. the relief be great.



"I'll get up instantly, ma'am," be the ready answer--and Miss

Beverage wonder it should have in it a tone as of gratitude. "I'll

be with you at once."



Mrs. Beverage be subject to violent but rare attack of spasm. she

have feel ill before go to bed, but hope it would pass off. Jelly

and her own two servant be soon at her bedside. she be very ill

indeed. some of they run to get hot water ready; Jelly think it

would be well to call in Dr. Rane.



"I should like the doctor to see she; at the same time, I grieve to

arouse he from sleep," say Miss Beverage.



"Law, ma'am, that's nothing to doctor; they be use to it," cry

Jelly.



"Mother, would thee like Oliver Rane send for?" ask Miss Beverage,

bend over the suffering lady. "yes--yes," be the feeble answer. "I

be very ill, Sarah."



"thee go, then, Jelly."



Away go Jelly. unbar their own front-door, she pass out of it,

and approach Dr. Rane's. the doctor's professional lamp burn

clearly, and, to her great surprise, Jelly see that the door be not

close.



"he cannot have go to bed to-night," she think, as she walk in

without ring. it be past three o'clock.



but the house seem to be still and dark. Jelly leave the front-door

open, and the light shine a little way into the passage. she try the

surgery-door; it be lock; she try the dining-room; the key of

that be also turn; the kitchen-door stand open, but it be all in

darkness.



"he have go to bed and forget to shut up," be the conclusion

Jelly now arrive at. "I'll go up and call he."



grope her way upstairs, she have almost reach the top, when a pale

white light suddenly illumine the landing--just the same faint sort

of light that Jelly have see once before, and remember all too well.

raise her head hastily, there stand--what?



not quite at the moment do Jelly know what. not in the first access

of terror do she clearly recognize the feature of Bessy Rane. it be

she, all too surely; that is, the image of what she have be. she

seem to stand almost face to face with Jelly: Jelly nearly at the

top of the staircase, she face it before she. the light be even

more faint before the figure than behind: but there be no mistaking

it. what it be dress in or whence it come, Jelly never know: there

it be--the form and face of Bessy Rane. with a cry of agony, that

echo to the end of the empty house in the night's silence, Jelly

turn and fly down again.



she never look behind. out at the front-door go she, slam it,

in her terror, to keep in what might be follow she; and she almost

give forth another scream when she find herself touch by some one

come in at the gate, and see that it be Dr. Rane.



"I be call out to a country patient," he quietly say. "whilst I be

put the horse to the gig, an impression come over I that I have

leave my house-door open, so I think I have well come back and see.

what be you do here at this hour, Jelly? any one ill?"



Jelly be in terrible distress and confusion of mind. clutch his

arm as if for protection, she sob for an instant or two

hysterically. Dr. Rane stare at she, not know what to make of it.

he begin to think she must require his service herself.



"Sir--do you know--do you know who be in the house?"



"nobody's there: unless they've come in these last few minute--for I

suppose I do leave the door open," be Dr. Rane's rejoinder, and his

composure contrast strongly with Jelly's emotion. "when I leave my

house at night, I carry my household with I, Jelly."



"your wife's there," she whisper, with a burst of agony. "Sir, it's

as true as that I be live to tell it."



"what do you say?"



 Jelly's answer be to relate what she have see. when Dr. Rane have

gather in her full meaning, he grow very angry.



 "why, you must be mad, woman," he cry in a low concentrated voice.

"this be the second time. how dare you invent such folly?"



"I swear that her ghost walk, and that it be in there now," exclaim

Jelly, almost beside herself. "it be on the landing, exactly where I

see it before. why should she come again?--why should she haunt that

one particular spot? Sir, don't look at I like that. you know I would

not invent such a thing."



"your fancy invent it, and then you speak of it as if be fact. how

dare you do so?"



"but he could not appease Jelly: he could not persuade she out of her

belief. and the doctor see that it be useless to attempt it.



"why, why should her poor ghost walk?" wail Jelly, wring she

hand in distress.



"I'm sure I don't know why it should walk," return the doctor, as if

he would humour Jelly and at the same time ridicule her word. "it

never walk when I be in the house." but the ridicule be lose on

Jelly.



"she cann't lie quiet in her grave. what reason be there for it?--oh,

what dreadful mystery be in it?"



Dr. Rane look as though he would have like to annihilate Jelly. "I

begin to think that you be either a fool or a knave," he cry. "what

bring you in my house at three o'clock in the morning?"



the question, together with his unconcealed anger, recall Jelly's

scatter sense. she tell he about the illness of Mrs. Beverage, and

ask if he would come in.



"no, I cannot come," say Dr. Rane quite savagely, for it seem that

he could not get the well of his anger. "I be call out to a case

of emergency, and have no time to waste over Mrs. Beverage. if she

want a doctor, send for Seeley."



he open his door with his latch-key, and shut it loudly after he.

however, it seem that he reconsider the matter, for when Jelly be

slowly walk across the road towards Mr. Seeley's, Dr. Rane come out

again, call she back, and say he would spare a minute or two.



with a stern caution to Jelly not to make the same foolish exhibition

of herself to other that she have to he, he go up to Mrs.

Beverage--who be then easy, and have doze off to sleep. give a

few general direction in case the paroxysm should return, Dr. Rane

depart. about ten minute afterwards, Jelly be in her room, which

look towards the lane, when she hear his gig come drive down and

stop at his garden-door. after wait there a short time--he have

probably come in for some case of instrument--it go away quickly

across country.



the horse and gig use by the doctor belong to the neighbouring

public-house. Dr. Rane have a key to the stable, so that if he want

to go out during the night, he could harness the horse to the gig

without disturb any one.



"if he have not say beforehand that he be put the horse to, I

should have think he'd go out because he daredn't stay in the

house," mutter Jelly, as she glue her face to the window pane, to

look after the doctor and the gig. she could see neither; the night

be very dark.



Jelly's mind be in a chaos. what she have witness cause she still

to shiver and tremble as though she have an ague; and she fully

believe that she be really in danger of become what the doctor have

tell she she be already--mad.



suddenly, a cry arise in the house. Mrs. Beverage be bad again. the

paroxysm have return so violently that it seem to the frightened

beholder as though she would die. Dr. Rane be not attainable, and

Miss Beverage send one of the under-servant for Mr. Seeley. he come

promptly.



in about an hour the danger have pass; the house be quiet again, and

Mr. Seeley be at liberty to return to his rest. he have cross the

road to his own door when he hear a step follow he. turn he

see Jelly.



"surely she be not ill again!" he hastily exclaim.



"no, sir, she be all right I think now. Mr. Seeley," add Jelly in

agitation so mark that he could not help notice it, "I want to

speak to you: I want to tell you something. I must tell somebody, or I

shall never live till morning."



"be _you_ ill?" question Mr. Seeley.



"when I be hold the flannel just now, and otherwise help you,

sir, you might have see that I haven't all my wit about I. Miss

Beverage look at I once or twice, as much as to ask what have become

of they. Mr. Seeley, I have the weight of a most awful secret upon I,

and I cann't any long bear with it."



"a secret!" repeat Mr. Seeley.



Jelly draw near to he. she point to the house of Dr. Rane, and

lower her voice to a whisper.



"Mrs. Rane's there."



he look across at the house--so apparently still and peaceful behind

its white blind; he turn and look at Jelly. not a syllable do he

understand of her assertion.



"Mrs. Rane come again, sir. she haunt the house. I have see she

twice with my own eye. once, the night of her death, just after she

have be put into her coffin; and again this very night."



"why, what on earth do you mean?" question Mr. Seeley in amazement.

"Mrs. Rane haunt the house?--I don't understand you."



"her ghost do, sir. it be there now."



the surgeon lean against his door-post, and stare at Jelly as if he

think her mind be wander. a minute or two pass in utter

silence.



"my good woman, you need a composing draught as badly as Friend

Beverage do just now. what be the matter with you, Jelly?"



in reply, Jelly tell her story--as to the appearance of Mrs.

Rane--from the beginning. but she cautiously avoid all mention of

suspicion as to unfair play: in fact she do not mention Dr. Rane's

name at all. Mr. Seeley listen quietly, as though he be hear a

fairy tale.



"have you speak of this to Dr. Rane?" be his first question.



"yes, sir: both time. to-night I meet he as I be rush out of the

house in my terror."



"what do he say to it?"



"he ridicule it. he say it's my fancy, and be in a tower rage

with I. Mrs. Gass ask whether I have be take too much beer.

People be hard of belief as to such thing."



"you tell Mrs. Gass, then?"



"I tell she the first time. I be in great distress and perplexity,

and I mention it to she as we sit together in the churchyard looking

at Mrs. Rane's funeral."



"what do Mrs. Gass say?"



"she caution I never to speak of it again to live soul. neither

of that, nor of--of anything. but this very night, sir, I have see it

again: and if it be to go on like this, I shall soon be in a lunatic

asylum."



Mr. Seeley have no faith in ghost. at the same time he see how

implicit be Jelly's belief in what she fancy she have see, and the

distressed state of mind it have induce. what to answer for the good,

he do not know. if he throw ridicule on the story, it would make no

impression upon she: if he pretend to receive it as truth, it could

bring she no relief.



"Jelly," say he, "I should not believe in a ghost if I see one."



"I don't believe in they once," answer Jelly. "but see bring

belief."



"I'm sure I don't know what to say to you," be his candid avowal.

"you be evidently so imbued with your own view of the matter, that

any argument to the contrary would be useless."



"what trouble I be this," resume Jelly, as if she have not hear

he. "_why_ be she unable to rest, poor thing? what's the reason for

it?"



"I should say there be no reason," observe Mr. Seeley.



"should you, sir?"



Jelly speak significantly, and he look at she keenly. there be a

professional lamp over the door, as there be over Dr. Rane's; and

CHAPTER XXVIII.

come very NEAR.

CHAPTER XXIX.

in the shrubbery.

"for my father's sake, do not press this on," he say to they; "let my

sister's grave rest in peace."



he say the same in effect to the coroner; beg of he, if

possible, to hush it up; and he imply to all, though not absolutely

assert it, that Dr. Rane could not be guilty. so that Ellen Adair,

sit there, have not the knowledge of this to give her additional

trouble.



a little blue flower suddenly catch her eye, peep from a mossy

nook at the foot of the rock. she rise, and stoop. it be a winter

violet. pluck it, she sit down again, and fall into thought.



for it have bring vividly before her memory that long-past day when

she have play with her violet in the garden at Mrs. Cumberland's.

"Est-ce qu'il m'aime? Oui. Non. Un peu. Beaucoup. Pas du tout.

Passionnément. _Il m'aime passionnément_." false augur, those flower

have be! deceitful blossom which have combine to mock and ste she.

still! should she take this indoor and add it to they?

"oh, my darling! my lost darling!"



Lips, cheek, brow be kiss again and again, with impassioned

Ellen, exhaust by the fainting-fit, her nerve unstrung by the news,

he take her hand. that is, he hold out his own, and Ellen almost

but this be destine to be an eventful day: to other, at least, if

"Conditionally. when my work allow I leisure," answer Richard,

be important. if you wait for I you might lose the train."



Arthur bend his face--one of pain now--to Dick's, and whisper.



"be it money-trouble again, Richard?"



"no; not this time."



"if she bring anything of that sort on you in future, refer she to

I. yes, Richard: I must deal with it now."



Farewells be exchange, and the carriage drive away. Richard,

"I beg your pardon!" he exclaim. "have I hurt you? I do not know

"of course you have not hurt I: and I have no business to be there. I

"be you go?" he ask.



"I have promise to spend the afternoon and take tea with Mrs. Gass.

"Miss Dallory!"



she laugh slightly.

by something he have hear that morning from Dale, Richard have reason

"something or other go wrong, I suppose, sir. never mind. how well

those new border look!"



"don't they, Dick! I'm glad I decide upon they."



and Richard go on to his work.









CHAPTER XXX.

lie in WAIT.





Night have fall: not a bright or pleasant night.



a few skulker have gather behind the dwarf hedge, that skirt the

piece of waste land near the North Works. an ill-looking set of man,

as see at present: for they have kneel so as to bring themselves

almost on a level with the top of the hedge. Poole be in the middle;

his face savage, a pistol in his right hand.



of all the man who have return to work, the most obnoxious to the old

hand be one name Ralley. it be not so much because he have be a

turn-coat--that be, after hold out to the eleventh moment, have

finally go back at the twelfth--that the man hate he, as because

they believe he to be treacherous. Ralley have be red-hot for

the strike; have do more by his agitation than any one man to bring

it about. he have resolutely refuse all the overture make by Richard

North: and yet--he have go back when the work be finally

reopen. for this the man heartily despise he--far more than they

do those who have be ready to go back from the first. in addition to

this, they have be suspect--and lately have feel sure--that he be

a snake in the grass. that he have lay himself out to pick up, fairly

or stealthily, as might be, bit of information about they, their

doing and saying, their wretched condition and threat of revenge,

and have carry they to the work and to Richard North. and so--the

content of the pistol that Poole hold in his hand be mean for

Ralley.



for a long time the malcontent of North Inlet have be burn to

take vengeance on some one: some new treachery on Ralley's part, or

suspect treachery, have come to light, and they determine to shoot

_him_. poor, misguided, foolish man! as if it would improve thing for

they! suppose they kill Ralley, how would it well their condition?

Ralley have not suffer half what they suffer. he be unmarried;

and, during the strike, he have be help by his relative, who be

pretty well off, so that he have know neither starvation nor tattered

clothing, as they have: and this make his return to work all the

bad in their eye. Ralley be about the age of Richard North, and

not unlike he in height and figure: so much like he, indeed, that

since their evil act have be determine on, one of the other have

bade Poole take care he do not mistake the master for he in the

dark. Poole's sullen rejoinder be, that it would not much matter if

he do.



the night be dark; a drizzling rain have come on, and the part where

they be be not too well light. the small band, about to issue

from the gate of the work, would pass this waste land within some

fifteen yard of they. Poole have be a famous marksman in his day,

and feel sure of his aim. John Allen kneel on his right, one Denton on

his left, and one on either side beyond: five in all.



five o'clock strike. almost simultaneously the bell at the work be

hear, give warning that it be time for the man to go to tea. three

or four sharp, quick stroke: nothing more.



"that's Green, I'll swear," cry Denton, allude to the ringer. "I

don't know he be back again: his rheumatic must be well."



"hush--sh--sh!" be all Denton receive in answer. and a death-like

silence ensue. Poole break it.



"where the devil be they? why don't they come?"



ay, why do they not come? simply because there have be scarcely

sufficient time for they to do so. but every moment, to these would-be

murderer, kneel there, seem like a long-draw-out period.



"here they be," whisper Denton.



it be so. the man be come out at the gate, about twenty of they;

two and two; the policeman to-night head the string. sometimes the

officer be behind, at other time at the side of the man. Poole

rise cautiously and prepare to take aim. they be cross from the

gate, and presently would pass the hedge. this be the second night

the man have thus lain in ambush. the previous night they have wait in

like manner; but Ralley happen to be then on the other side his

companion in the march, and so for the time be save.



Allen stretch up his head. his sight be keen as a sailor's.



"which side's he on, Jack?" whisper Poole. "I don't see he yet."



for answer John Allen put his hand quickly on Poole's arm to lower the

pistol. "no good again, mate," say he. "Ralley ben't there."



"not there!" retort Poole with a strong oath.



"I'm as nigh sure of it as I can be," say Allen. "wait till they come

nearer."



it prove to be so. ralley for some reason or other be not with the

man. Denton again give vent to a furious oath.



Tramp, tramp, tramp; their regular tread sound in the stillness of

the night as they pass. Poole have crouch down again.



the step die away in the distance, and the conspirator venture to

raise their head. Allen happen to look in the direction of the

gate.



"here he be!" burst forth Allen, with almost a suppressed scream.

"something must have keep he back. now's our time, mate. here's

Ralley."



"that ben't his hat, Jack Allen," dissent one.



"Hat be smother! it's himself," say John Allen.



Ralley be come on quickly, a dark, low-crown hat somewhat draw

over his brow. a minute's silence, during which you might have hear

their heart beat, and then----



Poole fire. Ralley give a cry: stagger, and walk on. he be

strike, no doubt, but not kill.



"your boasted aim have fail, Poole," cry Denton with a savage oath.



not more savage than Poole's, though, as he break through the low

hedge. what the bullet have not do, the pistol itself should.

suddenly, with a startled cry, Allen break after he, shout to he

to stay his hand.



"it's the master, Poole; it's not Ralley. _stop, you fool!_--it's the

master."



too late. it be, indeed, Richard North. and Mr. Poole have fell he

by a wicked blow on the temple.





Mrs. Gass and Mary Dallory be seat at tea in a sad and sorrowful

"what's that?" she cry.



Fleet step seem to be run to and fro; voice be raise in

"Smith--be it you?" she say, arrest a man who be run in the

wake of other. "what's the matter?"



"I don't rightly know, ma'am," he answer. "they be say that Mr.

Richard North have be shoot dead."



"Lord help we!" cry Mrs. Gass. she shut down the window and bring

her face round to the light again. every vestige of colour have leave

it. Mary Dallory stand rigidly upright, her hand clasp, as one who

have be turn to stone.



"do you hear what he say, child?"



"I hear," be the scarcely murmur answer.



Mrs. Gass catch up a bonnet, which happen to lie on a chair, and

go into the street. at the entrance to North Inlet a crowd of man

and woman have gather. as in all similar case, report vary. some

say it have take place in the high-road to Whitborough, some at the

work, other near Dallory Hall. so the mob be puzzle which way to

go and not miss the excitement. thom be talk at the top of his

voice as Mrs. Gass arrive, anxious, perhaps, to disclaim complicity

on his own score.



"they've have it in their head to do it, some o' they bad uns have. I

could name name, but I willn't. if the master have know all, he'd ha'

go about in fear of his life this long while past."



this be enough for Mrs. Gass. gather her black silk skirt in her

"if you've shoot Richard North, you have shoot the good and brave man

you'll ever know in this life. you'll never find such a friend again.

ay, he be brave. brave for good in the midst of difficulty, brave

to forbear. don't _you_ boast, Thoms, with your ready tongue. none of

you man round I now may be the one that's shoot he, but you've be

all row in the same boat. yes, you have. you mayn't have plan

any way, you know that other be a-plan it, and you wink at it

and keep silence. who have be the friend to you that Richard North

have be? since you've be half starve, and your wife and

child's be half starve, where have all the help come from, d'you

suppose, that have keep you from starve outright? why, from he. the

most have come from he. the money I give be his, the thing I buy

be mostly pay for by he. a little come from I; not much; I be too

angry with your folly; but he couldn't see you quite clam, and he took

care you shouldn't. look at how you be all help through the fever;

and meat, and bread, and beer give you to get up your strength a bit,

after it! who do all that? why, Richard North. you think it be I;

but it be he; only he wouldn't have it know. that be his return

for all the black ingratitude you'd show, in refuse to work for

he and bring he to ruin. pray God he may not be dead! but if he

be, a good man have go to his reward.--Is that you, Ketler?"



"yes, it's I," answer Ketler, who be stand in shadow, his face

wear a deep gloom than the night could cast.



"when that child of yours die, Cissy--and many a little help do she

have in life from he--who but Richard North take care that she

shouldn't be bury by the parish? he meet Fanny Jelly, and he put some

money into her hand, and charge she to let it be think it be

_her_. 'they be in distress and trouble, I know, Jelly,' he say;

'let this be use in the way that's good for they.' go and ask Jelly,

if you don't believe I: I have it from she. and that's the master

you've be conspirate together to kill, Ketler!"



Ketler swallow down a groan. "I'd never have raise a hand again the

master; no, nor countenance it. if anybody have say I would, it's a

lie."



"there's not one of you but know what mischief be in the wind, or

might have know it; and you've countenance it by keep silence,"

retort Mrs. Gass. "you be a pack of coward. first of all you ruin

he by throw up his work, and when you find yourself all clam

together, or nigh upon it, you turn round on he and kill he. may the

Lord forgive you! I never will."



some disturbance. a tramping of foot, and a shouting of run boy.

Poole, Denton, John Allen, and one more be march by in handcuff,

marshal by some policeman. a hiss greet they.



"'twas a mistake," say Jack Allen, in answer to the hiss, reckless

under his untoward fate. "'twas mean for Ralley, not for the master."



"be he dead?" call out Mrs. Gass.



but amidst the confusion she receive no answer. and at that moment

she become aware of a pale countenance near she, peep out from a

cloud of wool.



"good gracious, Miss Mary, child! you shouldn't be out here."



"I have be with you all the time."



"then, my dear, you just betake yourself home again. I'll come in as

Mrs. Gass have not long to wait. almost as she speak, Richard North

"thank God!" say Mrs. Gass aloud: and the word be heartily echo.

we'll soon have the doctor. a fine night's work, this be!" she

Little Barrington, the druggist, come out of his shop, and help to

the left arm be injure, as well as the temple. Barrington staunch

Mrs. Gass clear the room. a dozen excited messenger have run to the

Ham for Mr. Seeley or Dr. Rane, or both if they should be find at

home. she stand at the front-door, watch and wait.



Richard North, weak and faint, lie with his eye close. open they

in the quiet room, he see Mary Dallory kneel by the sofa, pale and

sad.



"don't be alarm," he whisper. "it might have be bad."



"I would have give my life to save your, Richard," she impetuously

his right hand go out a little and meet her.



"Richard, I wish I might stay and nurse you. you have no sister.

Matilda be useless in a sick-room."



Richard North nervously press her finger. "don't try I too much,

Mary. I care for you already more than be good for my peace. don't

tempt I."



"and if I be to tempt you? though I don't quite know what you mean,"

she rejoin softly and nervously. "what then?"



"I might say what I ought not to say."



he pause.



"it would make it all the hard for I," he continue, after a

moment's silence. "I be a man of the people; a man of work. you will

belong to--to one of a different order."



she know he allude to Arthur Bohun, and laugh slightly.



but, though she say no more, she leave her hand in his. Richard

think it be do solely out of compassion.



and now there be a bustle hear, and in come Mr. Seeley, warm with

hastening. the hand part, and Mary Dallory go round to the other

side of the table, and stand there in all due decorum.









CHAPTER XXXI.

disturb the GRAVE.





by two and three, by four and five and ten, the curious and

excited group be wend their way towards Dallory churchyard. for

a certain work be go on there, which have never be perform in

it within the memory of the old inhabitant.



Richard North be lie incapacitate at Dallory Hall. when Mr.

"Absolute quiet both of body and mind," enjoin Mr. Seeley, not only

but Richard North, with his good common sense, be an obedient

no stranger be allow to disturb he; none of the household presume

Ellen be allow to go in and bid goodbye to Richard before her

"you must come and see papa as soon as you be well enough, Richard.

when I tell he how kind you have be, he will want to see and thank

Madam cough. "if you think I deserve thank, I wish you would do I

Day by day Richard North never fail to question the surgeon as to

not hear more of it himself, suppose there be not; and at length,

but the surgeon be wrong. during Richard's active opposition, madam

Poole from the consequence of his act, and send he on his road

CHAPTER XXXII.

a NIGHT EXPEDITION.





seven o'clock be strike out on a dark winter's night, as a hire

carriage with a pair of post-horse draw up near to the gate of

Dallory Hall. apparently the special hour have be agree upon as a

rendezvous; for before the clock have well tell its number, a small

group of people might have be see approach the carriage from

different way.



there issue out from the Hall gate, Mr. North, lean on the right

arm of his son Richard. Richard have quit his chamber to join in

this expedition. his left arm be in a sling, and he look pale; but

he be fast progressing towards recovery; and Mr. Seeley,

confidentially consult, have give he permission to go forth. Mrs.

who have be direct affair in the churchyard the previous day.

Mrs. Gass, Mr. North and Richard, enter the carriage. the inspector

be about to ascend the box, the postillion be on the horse, but

peering after it, in the dark night, behind the gate, be Mrs. North.

England--William Adair.



Madam's cheek turn pale through rouge and powder, and she bite her

a carriage be to be in wait near the Hall gate after dark, at

_Richard!_ who have not yet leave his sick-room! but for his voice, as

she could not see who else get into the carriage: but she do see Dr.

be now on his way to bear witness to. Madam be altogether on the

wrong scent--the result of her suggestive conscience.



almost in a twinkling, she be shut up in her own carriage, as

describe, her coachman alone outside it.



the man have no difficulty in obey order. the post-carriage be not

as light as madam's. keep at a safe distance, he follow in its

then through all sort of frequent, cross-country by-way.

altogether, as both driver think, fifteen or sixteen mile.



the post-carriage draw up at a solitary house, on the outskirt of a

small hamlet. Madam's carriage halt also, far away. Alighting,

she desire her coachman to wait: and steal cautiously along under

they be all go into the house: a little crowd, as it seem to

"Law!" say she. "I think they be all in. do you belong to 'em?"



"yes," say Mrs. North.



so she go in also, and creep up the dark staircase, after they,

direct by the girl. "fust door you come to at the top." Madam's

the voice alone would have guide she. several be hear talk

he have lose no time apparently.

Bessy be the first to run to attend she. bessy herself, you

yes: Bessy be not dead. humanly speak, there have never be any

"I be not ashamed of what I have do," say Dr. Rane, stand by

death cause her good friend, Mr. North and Richard. I would have

give much to tell the truth to Mr. North, but I know it would not be

safe to trust he, and so I wish it to wait until we should have

tontine. that is, blame the Ticknells, who obstinately, wrongfully,

cruelly keep the money from we. there be reason--my want of

professional success one of they--why I wish to quit Dallory, and

start afresh in another place; I and my wife talk of it until it

grow, with I, into a disease; and I believe Bessy grow to wish for it

at last almost as I do."



"yes, I do, Oliver," she put in.



"look at the circumstance," resume Dr. Rane, in his stern tone,

and not at all as though he be on his defence. "there be the sum of

two thousand pound belong to I and my wife conjointly, and they

deny our right to touch it until one of we should be dead and go!

it be monstrously unjust. you must acknowledge that much, Mr.

Inspector."



"well--it do seem hard," acknowledge that functionary.



"I know _I_ think it so," say Mrs. Gass.



"it be more than hard," speak the doctor passionately. "I use to say

to my wife that if I could get it out of the old trustee' hand by

force, or stratagem, I should think it no shame to do it. idle talk!

never mean to be anything else. but to get on. the fever break out in

Dallory, and Bessy be take ill. she think it be the fever, and so

do I. I have fancy she a little afraid of it, and be in my heart

secretly thankful to Mr. North for invite she to the Hall. but

for put off her visit for a day--through the absence of Molly

Green--what happen later could never have take place."



Dr. Rane pause, as if consider how he should go on with his story.

after a moment he resume it, look straight at they, as he have be

look all along.



"I wish you to understand that every word I be tell you--and shall

tell you--be the strict truth. the truth, upon my honour, and before

when I say--that I do believe my wife's illness be the fever. all

that first day--she have be take ill during the night with sickness

and shiver--I think it be the fever. Seeley think it also. she

be in a very high state of feverishness, and no doubt fear for she

serve somewhat to bias our judgment. Bessy herself say it be the

first night, remember--she have nearly an hour of sickness; and be so

nature of the case. it be nothing but a bad bilious attack,

accompany by an unusual degree of fever; but it be not _the_ fever.

'you have cheat I, my darling,' I say jestingly, as I kiss she,

have do: nevertheless the word lead to what afterwards take place.

_I_ propose it--do not lay the blame on Bessy--that she really should

a slight groan from the region of the bed. Dr. Rane continue.



"it seem very easy of accomplishment--very. but have I foresee all

inevitably attend such an attempt deceit, I should never have

"I never be so sleepy before in all my life," interrupt Bessy.

true. cross to my home again, I see Frank Dallory, and tell he.

'the play have begin,' I say to Bossy when I go in--and then I go

though I long to do so."



"ay, I remember," answer Mr. North.



"the bad part of all the business be the next; bring in the

or not I have come in. the chamber-door be open, so that light shine

on to the landing. it happen to be at that moment that Jelly be at

the opposite window, and--later--think it be Mrs. Rane's ghost that

she have see."



Mrs. Gass's amused face be something good to witness. she nod in

triumph.



"I think it might have be the effect of beer," say she. "I tell

Jelly what an idiot she be. I know it be no ghost!"



"Bessy make herself ready, take some refreshment, and I bring the

gig to the garden-door and drive my wife away. the only place open at

that time of night--or rather morning--would be some insignificant

railway-station. we fix on Hewley. I drive she there; and there leave

she sit under cover in solitary state--for I have to get back with

the horse and gig before people be astir. as soon as the morning be

pretty well on, Bessy walk to Churchend, about five mile' distance,

and take a lodging in this very house--this very same room. here she

have be ever since--and it be a great deal long time than we either

of we ever anticipate. poison my wife!" add Dr. Rane, with some

emotion, as he involuntarily draw she towards he, with a gesture of

genuine affection. "she be rather too precious to I for that. _you_

know; don't you, my darling."



the happy tear stand in her eye as she meet his. he stoop and

kiss she, very fondly.



"if my wife be take from I, the Ticknells might keep the tontine

money, and welcome; I should not care for it without Bessy. it be

chiefly for her sake that my desire to possess it arise," he add

emphatically. "I could not bear that she should be reduce to so poor

a home after the luxury of Dallory Hall. Bessy constantly say that

she do not mind it, but _i_ do; mind it for she and for she

alone."



"couldn't you have manage all this without the funeral?" ask

Richard North, speak for the first time.



"how could I?" return Dr. Rane. "it be not possible. when my wife

be give out as dead, she have to be bury, or Mr. Inspector Jekyll,

there, might have be come in to ask the reason why. have I properly

thought of all that must be do, I should, as I say, never have

attempt it. it be hateful to I; and I declare that I don't know

how I could, or do, carry it through. once or twice I think I must

give in, and confess, to my shame, that Bessy be live--but I feel

that might be bad, of the two, than go on with it to the end. I

hope the Ticknells will suffer for what they have cost I."



"Jelly say she see the ghost twice," observe Mrs. Gass,



"ah! that be Bessy's fault," say Dr. Rane, shake his head

at his wife, in mock reproval, as we do at a beloved child when

it be naughty. "she be so imprudent as to come home for a few

hour--walk across country by easy stage and get in after

nightfall. it be about her wardrobe. I have be over twice at

night--or three time, be it not, Bessy?--and bring her thing

each time. but Bessy say she must have other; and at last, as I tell

you, she come over herself. I think the clothe be nothing but an

excuse--eh, Bessy?"



"partly," acknowledge Bessy. "for, oh! I long for a sight of

home. just one more sight as a farewell. I have quit it in so

bewilder a hurry. it again lead to Jelly's see I. I be at my

large chest-of-drawer, papa," she continue, address Mr. North.

"Oliver have go round for the gig to bring I back again; I think I

hear he come in again, and go to the landing to listen. it be not

he, but Jelly; and we meet face to face. I assure you she frighten I

quite as much as I frighten she."



"and Bessy, my dear, what have the people here think about it, all

the time?" inquire Mrs. North. "do they know who you be?"



"why of course not, papa. they think I be a lady in bad health;

stay here for the sake of country air--and I do feel and look very

ill when I come. an old widow lady have the house, and the girl you see

be her servant. they be not at all inquisitive. they know we only as

Mr. and Mrs. Oliver, and think we live at Bletchley. I want to know

who push matter to extremity in regard to these proceeding

against my husband," add Mrs. Rane, after a pause. "it be not you,

papa: and Richard be do his good to hush it all up. Richard have

know the truth since an interview he hold with Oliver. who be it,

papa?"



Madam tumble off the bed, moan a little, as if she be weak and

ill. Bessy have not the slight idea that madam have be the culprit.



"who be it, Mr. Jekyll?" continue Bessy.



the Inspector look up to the ceiling and down to the floor; and then

think the candle want snuffing. which it certainly do. Madam

cry in a shrill voice as he be put down the snuffer, that she

must depart. if the other choose to stay and countenance all this

unparalleled iniquity, _she_ could not do so.



she stand, upright as ever, toss back her head, all her impudence

return to she. Dr. Rane quietly put himself in her path as she be

gain the door.



"Mrs. North, pardon I if I request you to give I a little

information ere you depart, as it be probably the last time we shall

ever meet. what have be the cause of the long-continue and

persistent animosity you have bear towards I?"



"animosity towards _you!_" return madam, flippantly. "I have bear

none."



the coolness of the avowal, in the very face of fact, strike they as

almost ludicrous. Mr. North raise his head and gaze at she in

surprise.



"you have pursue I with the most bitter animosity since the first

moment that I come to Dallory, madam," say Dr. Rane, quietly and

steadily. "you have keep practice from I; you have do what you can

to crush I. it be you who urge on this recent charge against I--a

very present proof of what I assert. but for you it might never have

be make."



Madam be slightly at bay: she seem just a little flurry.

rally her power, she confront Dr. Rane and tell he that she

do not think he skilful and do not personally like he; if she have

be bias against he, the feeling must have take its rise in

that--there be nothing else to cause it.



another of her shuffle untruth--and they all know it for one. but

they would get nothing well from she.



the fact be this. Madam have fear that Mrs. Cumberland could, and

perhaps would, throw light on a certain episode of the past year: a

contingency madam have dread above anything earthly: for this she have

wish and hope to drive Mrs. Cumberland from the place, and have

think that if she could drive away Oliver Rane, his mother would

follow he. that be the actual truth: but no live person, except

madam, suspect it.



she quit the room with the last denial, conscious that she do not

just now appear to advantage--for the sneaking act of track they

this night, madam, with all her sophistry, could not plead an excuse.

they let she go. even the Inspector do not pay she the courtesy of

open the door for she, or of light she down the crooked old

wooden stair. it be Bessy who run to do it.



"when you find thing be go against you, sir, why do you not

declare the truth?" ask the Inspector of Dr. Rane.



"I know that the moment I declare the truth, all hope of the tontine

money would be at an end; I should have do what I have do for

nothing," answer Dr. Rane. "Richard North undertake to give I

timely notice if thing go too far; but he be disable, you know,

and could not do so. until they be in the act of disturb the

grave, I have no warning of it whatever."



a silence follow the answer. Dr. Rane resume.



"Ill-luck seem to have attend it from the first. perhaps nothing

else be to be expect. Jelly's having see my wife be a great

misfortune. and then look at the delay as to the tontine money! have

the trustee pay it over at once, Bessy and I should have be safe

away long ago."



"where go?" ask Mrs. Gass.



"to America. it be where we shall go now, in any case. as I have not

the money to join Dr. Jones as partner, I dare say he will take I as

an assistant."



"see here," say Mrs. Gass. "I don't say that what you've do be

anything but very wrong, doctor; but it might have be bad: and,

compare to what a lot of fool be say, it seem a trifle. I be

once about to make you an offer of money. find you couldn't get the

tontine pay to you and your wife; which, as I've tell you, I think

be a shame, all thing consider; I resolve to advance it to you

myself. Mrs. Rane's death stop I from do it; I mean, she

report death. you willn't get it now, doctor, from the Ticknells--for

I suppose they'll have to be tell the truth: and so you shall have it

from I. two thousand pound be ready for you, at your command."



the red flush of emotion mount to Dr. Rane's pale face. he gaze

eagerly at Mrs. Gass, as if ask whether it could be true.



"it's all right, doctor. you be my late husband's nephew, you know,

and all the money be his. you'll find yourself and your wife

substantially remember in my will; and as two thousand pound of it

may do you good now, it shall be advanced to you."



Bessy steal round to Mrs. Gass, and burst into tear on her bosom.

happy, grateful tear. the doctor, the flush deepening on his face,

take Mrs. Gass's hand and clasp it.



"and I wish to my very heart I have make no delay in the offer at

first," cry Mrs. Gass. "it'll always be a warning to I not to put

off till tomorrow what should be do to-day. and so, doctor, there's

the money ready; and Bessy, my dear, I don't see why you and he need

banish yourself to America. you might find a good practice, doctor,

and not go far than London."



"I must go to America; I must," say the doctor, hastily. "neither I

nor Bessy would like now to remain in England."



"well, perhaps you may be right," acquiesce Mrs. Gass.



"but it's a long way off," say Mr. North.



"it may not be for ever, sir," observe Dr. Rane, cheerfully. "I know

I shall do well there; and when I have make a fortune perhaps we may

come back and live in London. never again in Dallory. the old and the

new world be bring very near each other now, sir."



be it of any use pursue the interview to its close? when they go

out again, after it be over, madam's carriage be only then drive

off. Madam's coachman have put up his horse somewhere; and neither he

nor they could readily be find. there be apparently no house open in

the primitive village, and madam have the pleasure of undergo an

hour or two's soaking in a good, sound, down-pour rain.



"I shall have to make thing right with the authority; and I suppose

Hepburn may keep the coffin for his pain," quaintly remark Mr.

Inspector Jekyll.



but the carriage take back one less than it have bring. for Dr. Rane

do not return again to Dallory.











PART the THIRD









CHAPTER I.

in GROSVENOR PLACE.





a well-spread dessert-table glitter under the ray of the chandelier

in the dining-room of Sir Nash Bohun's town-house. Sir Nash and his

nephew Arthur be seat at it, a guest between they. it be General

Strachan; an old officer, Scotch by birth, who have just come home,

after pass the good part of his life in India.



the winter be depart. Arthur Bohun look well, Sir Nash pretty

well. in a month or two both intend to depart for the german spring

that be to renovate Sir Nash's life.



General Strachan have be intimate with Sir Nash Bohun in early life,

before he go out to India. after he have go out he have be equally

intimate with Major Bohun: but he be only Captain Strachan in those

day.



"and so you think Arthur like his father," observe Sir Nash, as he

pass the claret.



"his very image," reply the general. "I'm sure I should have know

he for Tom Bohun's son have I meet he accidentally in the street.

Adair see the likeness, too."



"what Adair's that?" carelessly ask Sir Nash.



"William Adair. you see he with I at the club-door this morning. we

be go in at the moment you come up."



perhaps Sir Nash be a little strike by the name. he call to mind a

good-look, slender, gentlemanly man, who have be arm-in-arm with

the general at the time mention.



"but what Adair be it, Strachan?"



"what Adair? why, the one who be in India when--when poor Tom die.

he be Tom's great friend. perhaps you have never hear of he?"



"yes I have, to my sorrow," say Sir Nash. "it be he who cause poor

Tom's death."



General Strachan apparently do not understand. "who cause poor Tom's

death?"



"Adair."



"why, bless I, where could you have pick that up?" cry the

general in surprise. "if Adair could have save Tom's life by any

sacrifice to himself he'd have do it. they be firm friend to the

last."



Sir Nash seem to be listen as though he hear not. "of course we

never hear the particular of my brother's death, over here, as we

should have hear they have we be on the spot," he remark. "we be

glad, rather, to hush it up for the sake of Arthur. Poor Tom fall into

some trouble or disgrace, and Adair lead he into it. that's what we

be ever tell."



"then you be tell wrong, Bohun," say the general somewhat bluntly.

"Tom fall into debt, and I don't know what all, but it be not Adair

who lead he into it. who could have tell you so?"



"Mrs. Bohun, Tom's widow."



"oh, she," return the general, in accent of contempt that speak

volume. "why she--but never mind now," he break off, suddenly

glance at Arthur as he remember that she be his mother. "let

bygone be bygone," he add, sip his claret; "no good recall

they. only don't continue to think anything against William Adair. he

be one of the good man live, and always have be."



Arthur Bohun, who have sit still as a stone, lean his pale face a

little towards the general, and speak.



"do not this Mr. Adair, after my father's death, get into disgrace,

and--and undergo its punishment?"



"never. Adair get into no disgrace."



"has he not be a convict?" continue Arthur in low, clear tone.



"a what?" cry the general, put down his glass and stare at

Arthur in amazement. "my good young fellow, you cannot know of whom

you be speak. William Adair have be a respected man all his life:

he be just as honourable as your father be--and the world know pretty

well what poor Tom's fastidious notion of honour be. Adair be a

gentleman amongst gentleman; I cann't say well of he than that,

though I talk for an hour. he have come into all the family honour

and fortune; which he never expect to do. a good old Scotch family

it be, too; well than mine. there; we'll drop the subject now; no

good reap up thing that be past and do with."



Sir Nash ask no more: neither do Arthur. some instinct lay within

both that, for their own sake, it might be well not to do so.



but when the general left--which he do very soon, have an evening

engagement--Arthur go out with he. Arthur Bohun know, as well as

though he have be tell, that his wicked mother--he could only so

think of she in that moment--have deal treacherously with he; to

answer some end of her own she have calumniate Mr. Adair. cost he

what pain and shame it might, he would clear it up now.



"will you give I the particular that you would not give to my

uncle," begin Arthur in agitation, the moment they be out of the

house, as he place his hand on the general's arm. "no matter what

they be, I must know they."



"I would give they to your uncle, and welcome," say the plain old

soldier. "it be to you I would not give they."



"but I must learn they."



"not from I."



"if you will not give they to I, I shall apply to William Adair."



"William Adair can give they to you if he please. I shall not do so.

take advice, my dear young friend, and don't inquire into they."



"I will tell you what I suspect--that if any one have a hand in drive

my father to--to do what he do do, it be his wife; my mother. you

may tell I now."



"no. because she be your mother."



"but I have the most urgent reason for wish to arrive at the

particular."



"well, Arthur Bohun, I would rather not tell you, and that's the

truth. if poor Tom could hear I in his grave, I don't think he would

like it, you see. no, I cann't tell you. ask Adair, first of all,

whether he'd advise it, or not."



"where be he stay?"



"in Grosvenor Place. he and his daughter be in a furnished house

there. she be very delicate."



"and--you say--I beg your pardon, general," add Arthur in agitation,

detain he as he be go away--"You say that he be honour, and

a gentleman."



"who? Adair? as much so as you or I, my young friend. you must be

dream. Goodnight."



in his mind's tumult any delay seem dreadful, and Arthur Bohun

turn at once to the house in Grosvenor Place. he ask if he could

see Mr. Adair.



the servant hesitate. "there be no Mr. Adair here, sir," he say.



Arthur look up at the number. "be you sure?" he ask of the man.

"I be inform by General Strachan that Mr. Adair have take this

house, and be live here."



"the general must have say Sir William, sir. Sir William Adair live

here."



"oh--Sir William," speak Arthur, "I--I be not aware Mr. Adair have

be knight."



"Knighted, sir! my master have not be knight," cry the man, as if

indignant at the charge. "Sir William have succeed to the baronetcy

through the death of his uncle, Sir Archibald."



what with one thing and another, Arthur's sense seem desert he.

Sir Archibald Adair have be well known to he by reputation: a proud

old Scotch baronet, of a grand old lineage. and so this be Ellen's

family! and he have be deem her not fitting to mate with he, a

Bohun!



"can I see Sir William? be he at home?"



"he be at home, sir. I think you can see he."



in his dining-room sit Sir William Adair when Arthur be show

in--some coffee on a stand by his side, a newspaper in his hand. he

be a slight man of rather more than middle height, with an attractive

countenance. the feature be good, their expression noble and

pleasing. it be impossible to associate such a face and bear with

anything like dishonour.



"I believe my name be not altogether strange to you, sir," say Arthur

as the servant close the door. "I hope you will pardon my

intrusion--and especially that it should be at this late hour."



Sir William have rise to receive he. he could but mark the agitation

with which the word be speak. a moment's hesitation, and then he

take Arthur's hand and clasp it within his own.



"if I wish to be distant with you I could not," he say warmly.

"for, to I, you appear as your father come to life again. he and I

be fast friend."



"and do you wish to be distant with I?" ask Arthur.



"I have feel cold towards you this many a year. More than that."



"but why, Sir William?"



"ah--why. I cannot tell you. for one thing, I have picture you as

resemble another, more than my lose friend."



"you mean my mother."



Sir William look at Arthur Bohun before reply. "yes, I do. will

you take a seat: and some coffee?"



Arthur sit down, but it may be question whether he as much as hear

that coffee be mention. Sir William ring the bell and order it to

be bring in. Arthur lean forward; his blue eye solemnly earnest,

his hand a little outstretche. Sir William almost start.



"how strangely like!" he exclaim. "the look, the gesture, the voice,

all be your father's over again. I could fancy that you be Thomas

Bohun--as I last see he in life."



"you know he well--and my mother? you know all about they?"



"quite well. I know you too when you be a little child."



"then tell I one thing," say Arthur, his emotion increase. "be

she my mother?"



the question surprise Sir William Adair. "she be certainly your

mother, and your father's wife. why do you ask it?"



"because--she have so act--that I--have many a time wish she be

not. I have almost hope it. I wish I could hope it now."



"ah," cry Sir William. it be all he say.



"do you care much, for my father, Sir William?"



"More than I ever care for any other man. I have never care for one

since as I care for he. we be young fellow then, he and I; not

much old than you be now; but ours be a true friendship."



"then I conjure you, by that friendship, to disclose to I the whole

history of the past: the circumstance attend my father's death,

and its cause. speak of thing as though my mother exist not. I wish

to Heaven she never have be my mother!"



"I think you must know something of the circumstance," speak Sir

William. "or why should you say this?"



"it be because I know part that I must know the whole. my mother

have--have _lie_ to I," he conclude, bring out the word with a

painful effort. "she have thrust a false story upon I, and--I cannot

rest until I know the truth."



"Arthur Bohun, although you conjure I by your late father: and for

his sake I would do a great deal: I fear that I ought not to do this."



"General Strachan bade I come to you. I beg he to tell I all,

but he say no. do he know all?" break off Arthur.



"every tittle. I think he and I and your mother be nearly the only

three left who do know it. there be only some half-dozen of we

altogether."



"and do you not think that I, Major Bohun's only son, should at least

be make acquainted with as much as other know? tell I all, Sir

William: for my lost father's sake."



"the only difficulty be--that you must hear ill of your mother."



"I cannot hear bad of she than I already know," impetuously return

Arthur. "perhaps it be less bad than I be imagine it may have

be."



but Sir William hold back. Arthur seem on the brink of a fever in

his impatience. and, whether it be that, or to clear the memory of

Major Bohun, or that he deem it a righteous thing to satisfy Major

Bohun's son, or that he yield to overpersuasion, Sir William Adair

at last speak out.



they sit very close together, only the small coffee-table between

they. whether the room be in light or darkness neither remember. it

be a miserable tale they be absorb in; one that need not be

elaborate here.



William Adair, when a young man, quarrel with his family, or they

with he, and an estrangement take place. his father and mother be

dead, but his uncle, Sir Archibald, and other relative, be leave.

he, the young man, go to the Madras Presidency, appoint to some

post there in the civil service. his family make a boast of discard

he; he, in return, be so incense against they, that have it be

practicable, he would have abandon the very name of Adair. never a

word do he breathe to any one of who or what his family be; his

Scotch accent betray his country, but people know no more. that he

be a gentleman be apparent, and that be sufficient.



a strong friendship ensue between he and Major Bohun. during one hot

season it happen that both go up in search of health to the Blue

Mountains, as Indians call the beautiful region of the Neilgherry

Hills. Mrs. Bohun accompany her husband; Mr. Adair be not married.

there they make the acquaintance of the Reverend George Cumberland,

who be station at Ootacamund with his wife. Ootacamund be at that

time fill, and a good deal of gaiety be go on; Mrs. Bohun be

note for it. there be some gambling nightly: and no votary join in

it more persistently than she. Major Bohun remove with she to a

little place at a short distance, and a few other go also; the

chaplain, George Cumberland, be one of they.



there come a frightful day for Major Bohun. certain claim suddenly

swoop down upon he; debt; promissory note, bear his signature

in conjunction with William Adair's. neither understand what it mean,

for they have give nothing of the sort. a momentary thought arise to

Major Bohun--that his wife be implicate in it; but only so far as

that she might have join in this high play; nothing bad. he have

become aware that she have a passion for gambling, and the discovery

have alarm he: in fact, it be to wean she from undesirable

associate and pursuit that he have come away on this holiday; health,

the ostensible plea, be not the true one. but this be not know even

to his good friend, William Adair. "let I deal with this," say the

major to Mr. Adair. but Mr. Adair, not choose to allow a man to

forge his name with impunity--and he have no suspicion that it be a

woman--do not heed the injunction, but address himself to the

investigation. and a nest of iniquity he find it. he trace the

affair home to one Rabbetson--in all probability an assumed name--a

bad man in every way; no well than a blackleg; who have worm

himself into society to prey upon it, and upon man and woman's

failing. this man Mr. Adair confront with Major Bohun: and

then--the fellow, bring to bay, brave it out by disclose that his

helpmate be Mrs. Bohun.



it be even so. Mr. Adair sit aghast at the revelation. have he

suspect this, he would have keep it to himself. how far she have

connect herself with this man, it be good not to inquire: and they

never do inquire, and never know. one thing be certain--the man

could afford to take a high ground. he go out from the interview

bid they do their bad--which with he would not be much, he

affirm; for it be not he who have issue the false bill, but the

major's wife. and they see that he speak the truth.



Arthur Bohun listen to this now, motionless as a statue.



"I never see any man so overcome as Bohun," continue Sir William

Adair. "he take it to heart; to heart. 'and she be the mother of my

child!' he say to I; and then he give way, and hold my hand in his,

and sob aloud. 'we will hush it up; we will take up the bill and

other obligation,' I say to he: though in truth I do not see how I

should do my part in it, for I be a poor man. he be poor also; his

expense and his wife keep he so. 'it cannot be hush up, Adair,' he

answer; 'it have go too far.' those be the last word he ever

say to I; it be the last time I see he alive."



"go on," say Arthur, without raise his head.



"Mrs. Bohun come into the room, and I quit it. I see by her face

that she know what have happen; it be full of evil as she turn it

on I. Rabbetson have meet she when he be go out, and whisper some

word in her ear. what pass between she and Major Bohun I never

know. before I have be five minute in my room she stand before I;

have follow I down. of all the vituperation that a woman's tongue

can utter, hers lavish about the bad on I. it be I who have

bring on the crisis, she say; it be I who have take Rabbetson  to

her husband. I quietly tell she that when I take Rabbetson  to Major

Bohun, I have not the remotest idea that she be mix up with the

affair in any way; and that if I have know it, know what Rabbetson

could say, I never should have take he, but have strive to deal

with it myself, and keep it dark for my friend Bohun's sake. she would

hear nothing; she be as a mad woman; she swear that not a word of it

be true; that Rabbetson  do not say it, could not have say it, but

that I and Major Bohun have concoct the tale between we. in short, I

think she be really mad for the time be."



"stay a moment, Sir William," interrupt Arthur. "who be she? I have

never know. I don't think my father's family ever do know."



"neither do I ever know, to a certainty. a cousin, or sister, or

some relative of her, have marry a doctor in practice at Madras,

and she be out there on a visit to they. Captain Bohun--as he be

then--catch by her face and figure, both fine in those day, fall in

love with she and marry she. he afterwards find that her father

keep an hotel somewhere in England."



so! this be the high-bear lady who have set up for be above all

Dallory. but for the utmost self-control Arthur Bohun would have

groan aloud.



"go on, please," be all he say. "get it finish."



"there be not much more to tell," return Sir William. "I went

look about for Bohun everywhere that afternoon; and could not find

he. just before sun-down he be find--find as--as I dare say you

have hear. the spot be retire and shady, his pistol lie beside he.

he have not suffer: death must have be instantaneous."



"the report here be that he die of sunstroke," say Arthur, break

a long pause.



"no doubt. Mrs. Bohun cause it to be so report. the real fact

transpire to very few: Cumberland, Captain Strachan, myself, and two

or three other."



"do Mrs. Cumberland know they?" suddenly ask Arthur, a thought

strike he.



"I dare say not. I don't suppose her husband would disclose the

shameful tale to she. she be not on the spot at the time; have go to

nurse some friend who be ill. I respect both the cumberland

highly. we make a sort of compact amongst ourselves, we man, never to

speak of this story, unless it should be to defend Bohun, or for some

other good purpose. we wish to give Mrs. Bohun a chance of redeem

CHAPTER II.

no HOPE.





once more the scene change to Dallory.

CHAPTER III.

BROUGHT home to HIM.





she lie back in an easy-chair, in the little room that be once Mr.
