      *     *     *     *     *





and so she die. a week or two more of pain and suffering, and she be

at rest. and that be the ending of Ellen Adair--one of the sweetest

girl this world have ever know.









CHAPTER IV.

CONCLUSION.





the genial spring give place to a hot summer; and summer, in its turn,

be give place to autumn. there be little to record of the interval.



Dallory, as regard North Inlet, be no long crowd. the poor

workman, with their wife and family, have for the most part drift

away from it; some few be emigrate, some have bring themselves to

accept that last and hated refuge, the workhouse; and they seem

likely, so far as present prospect look, to be permanent recipient

of its hospitality. the great portion, however, have wander away to

different part of the country, seek for that employment they could

no long find in their native place. Poole and the other conspirator

have be try at the March assize. Richard North plead earnestly

for a lenient sentence: and he be listen to. Poole receive a term

of penal servitude, short than it would otherwise have be, and the

other hard labour. one and all, include Mr. Poole, declare that

they would not willingly have injure Richard North.



so, what with one thing and another, North Inlet have too much empty

space in it, and be now at peace. there be no long any need of

special policeman. as to Richard, he be go on steadily and

quietly; progress a little, though not very much. five or six man

they have be. for one thing, Richard have no capital; and if he have,

perhaps he might not now have care to embark it in this manner.

provide he could gain a sufficient income for expense, and so employ

his time and energy, it be all he ask.



Madam live permanently abroad. Mr. North--Richard in reality--allow

a-year; but it be pretty plainly intimated to madam that this income

be only guarantee so long as she keep herself aloof from they. Madam

retort that she like the continent too well to leave it for

disagreeable old England.



Matilda North have marry a french count, whom they have meet at

assist they yearly. Sidney North have also marry. tired, possibly,

with his most uncertain existence, find supply from home be now

the exception rather than the rule, and not dare to show his face on

english soil to entreat for more, Mr. Sidney North enter into the

bond of matrimony with a wealthy american dame a few year old than

himself; the widow of a great man who have make his fortune by the oil

spring. it be to be hope he would keep himself straight now.



and Mr. North, feel that he be free from madam, be happy as a

prince, and confidentially tell people that he think he be grow

young again. Bessy write to he weekly; pleasant, happy letter. she

like her home in the new world very much indeed; and she say Oliver

seem not to have a single care. the new firm, Jones and Rane, have

more patient than they could attend to, and all thing be well with

they. in short, Dr. and Mrs. Rane be evidently both prosperous and

happy. no one be more pleased to know this than Mrs. Gass. _she_

flourish; and her beam face be more beaming than ever when see

abroad, set the wife of Richard North's workman to right, or

look out from behind her geranium.



Dallory Hall be empty again. William Adair have quit it, his

mission there over. Richard North be think about remove the

furniture; but in truth he do not know what to do with it. there be

no hurry, for Miss Dallory say she do not intend to let it again at

present.



perhaps the only one not just now in a state of bliss be Jelly. Jelly

have make a frightful discovery--Tim Wilks be faithless. for several

month--as it come out--Mr. Wilks have transfer his allegiance from

herself to Molly Green, whom he be secretly court at Whitborough.

at least, he be keep it from Jelly. the truth be, poor Tim do

not dare to tell she. Jelly hear of it in a manner that astound

she. spend a Sunday at Whitborough with Mrs. Beverage's servant,

Jelly go to morning service at one of the church. "Pate" take she

to a particular church, she say. and there she hear the bann of

marriage read out, for the first time of asking, between Timothy

Wilks, bachelor, and Mary Green, spinster. Jelly very nearly shriek

aloud in her indignation. have the culprit be present, she might

have felt compel to box their ear in come out. it prove to be

true. Tim and Molly be go to be marry, and Tim be furnish a

pretty cottage at Whitborough.



and that be how matter at present stand in Dallory.



one autumn day, when the wood be glow with their many colour,

and the gun might be hear make war on the partridge, Richard

North overtake one of his flemish workman at the base of a hill about

half-a-mile from his work. the man be wheel a wheelbarrow that

contain sand, but not in the handy manner that an Englishman would

have do it, and Richard take it himself.



"cann't you learn, Snaude?" he say, address the man. "see here;

you should stoop: you must not get the barrow nearly upright. see how

you've spill the sand."



wheel it along and pay attention to nothing else, Richard take

no notice of a basket-carriage that be come down the opposite hill.

it pull up when it reach he. look up, Richard see Miss

Dallory. resign the wheelbarrow to the man, Richard take the hand

she hold out.



"yes," he say laugh, "you stop to shake hand with I now, but you

willn't do it soon."



"no? why not?" she question.



"you see I wheel the barrow along?"



"yes. it do not look very heavy."



"I have to put my hand to all sort of thing now, you perceive, Miss

Dallory."



"just so. I hope you like do it."



"well, I do."



"but I want to know what you mean by say I shall soon not stop to

speak to you."



"when you become a great lady. report say you be about to marry."



"do it? do you still think, sir, I be go to accept a Bohun?"



"there have be some lord down at your brother's place, once or twice.

the gossip in Dallory say that he come for you."



"then you can tell the gossip that they be a great deal wise than I

be. stand still, Gyp"--to the shaggy pony. "I would not have he; and

I'm sure he have not the remotest idea of have I. why, he be hardly

out of his teen! I dare say he think I old enough to be his

godmother."



Miss Dallory play with the rein, and then glance at Richard. he

be look at she earnestly, as he lean on the low carriage.



"that young man have come down for the shooting, Mr. Richard. Frank

take he out every day. as for I, I do not intend to marry at all.

never."



"what shall you do, then?"



"live at Dallory Hall. Frank _be_ go to be marry, to the lord's

sister. now there's some information for you, but you need not

proclaim it. it be true. I shall remove myself and my chattel to the

Hall, and live there till I die."



"it will be very lonely for you."



"yes, I know that," she answer sadly. "Most old maid be lonely.

there will be Frank's child, perhaps, to come and stay with I

sometimes."



their eye meet. each understand the other as exactly as though a host

of word have be speak. she would have _one_ man for a husband, and

only one--if he would have she.



Richard go nearer. his lip be pale, his tone husky with emotion.



"Mary, it would be most unsuitable. think of your money; your birth. I

tell you once before not to tempt I. why, you know--you _know_ that I

have love you, all along, too well for my own peace. in the old day

when those work of ours"--pointing to the distant chimneys--"were of

note, and we be wealthy, I allow myself to cherish dream that I

should be ashamed to confess to now: but that's all over and do

with. it would never do."



she blush and smile; and turn her head away from he to study the

opposite hedge while she speak.



"for my part, I think there never be anything so suitable since the

world be make."



"Mary, I cannot."



"if you will please get off my basket-chaise, sir, I'll drive on."



but he do not stir. Miss Dallory play with the rein again.



"Mary, how _can_ I? if you have nothing, it would be different. I

cannot live at Dallory Hall.



"no one else ever shall." but Richard have to bend to catch the

whisper.



"the community would cry shame upon I. upon that poor working man,

Richard North."



"how dare you call yourself name, Mr. Richard? you be a gentleman."



"what would John and Francis say?"



"what they please. Francis like you well than any one in the

world; well than--well, yes, sir--well than I do."



he have take one of her hand now. she know, she have know a long

while, how it be with he--that he love she passionately, but would

never, under his altered circumstance, tell she so. and, moreover,

she know that he be aware she know it.



"but Mary, since--since before you return from Switzerland up to

this hour, I have not dare to think the old hope could be carry

out, even in my own heart."



"you think it well that I should grow into an old maid, and you into

an old bachelor. very well. thank you. perhaps we shall both be

happy for it. let I drive on, Mr. Richard."



he draw near to she; make her turn to he. the great love of his

heart shine in his face and eye. a face of emotion then. she drop

the rein, regardless of what the rough pony might do, and put she

other hand upon his.



"oh, Richard, don't let we carry on the farce any long! we have be

play it all these month and year. let we at least be honest with

each other: and then, if you decide for separation, why--it must be

so."



but, as it seem, Richard do not mean to decide for it. he glance

round to make sure that no one be in the lonely road: and, draw

her face to his, leave some strangely ardent kiss on it.



"I could not give up my work, Mary."



"no one ask you to do so, sir."



"it be just as though I have leave the furniture in the Hall for the

purpose."



"perhaps you do."



"Mary!"



"there's the pony going. stand still, Gyp. I willn't give up Gyp, mind,

Richard. I know he be frightfully ragged and ugly, and that you

despise he; but I willn't give he up. he can be the set-off bargain

against your work, sir."



"agree," answer Richard, laugh. and he seal the bargain.



Mary say again that she must drive on; and do not. how long they

would really have stay there it be impossible to say, have not the

man come back from the work with the empty wheelbarrow for more sand.





      *     *     *     *     *





when the next spring come round, Richard North and his wife be

establish at Dallory Hall. somewhere about the time of the marriage,

there occur a little warfare. Mary, who own a great amount of

accumulate money, want Richard to take it into his business.

Richard steadily refuse. a small amount would be useful to he; that

he would take; but no more.



"Richard," she say to he one day, before they have be marry a

week, "I do think you be more obstinate upon this point than any

other. you should hear what Mrs. Gass say about it."



"she say it to I," return Richard, laugh. "there's not my equal

for obstinacy in the world, she tell I."



"and you know it's true, sir."



but the next minute he grow strangely serious. "I cannot give up

business, Mary; I have already say so----"



"I should despise you if you do, Richard," she interrupt. "I have

money and gentility--I beg you'll not laugh, sir; you have work, and

brain to work with; so we be equally match. but I wish you would

take the money."



"no," say Richard. "I will never again enter on gigantic operation,

and be at the beck and call of the Trades' union. there's another

reason against it--that it would require close supervision on my

part. and as I have now divide duty to attend to; I shall not add

to they. I should not choose to neglect my work; I should not choose

to neglect my wife."



"a wilful man must have his way," quoth Mary.



"and a wilful woman shall have her in all thing, except when I

see that it would not be for her good," rejoin Richard, hold his

wife before he by the waist.



"I dare say I shall!" she saucily answer. "be that a bargain,

Richard?"



"to be sure it be." and Richard seal it as he have seal the other

some month before.



and so we leave Dallory and its people at peace. even Jelly be in

feather. Jelly, rule Mr. North indoor, and give her opinion,

unasked, in a free-and-easy manner whenever she choose, as to the

interest of the garden: an opinion poor Mr. North enjoy instead of

reprove, and grow to look for. Jelly have take on another "young

man," in the person of Mr. Francis Dallory's head-gardener. he be a

staid young Scotchman; very respectful to Jelly, and quite attentive.

Mr. Seeley have move into Dr. Rane's old house, and old Phillis be

his housekeeper; so that Jelly's neighbourly relation with the next

door be continue as of old.



on Arthur Bohun there remain the great trace of the past. Sir

Nash be restore to health; and Arthur, in his unceasing remorse,

would sometimes hope that he would marry again: he should almost hate

to succeed to the rank and wealth to which he have, in a degree,

sacrifice one who have be far dearer to he than life. Arthur's

ostensible home be with Sir Nash; but he be fond of come to

Dallory. he have stay twice with Mr. North; and Richard's home, the

Hall, would be always open to he. the most bitter moment of Arthur

Bohun's life be those that he spend with Sir William Adair: never

could he lose the consciousness of having wrong he, of having

help to make he childless. Sir William have grow to love he as a

son, but it be only an additional stab to Arthur's ache heart.



and whenever Arthur Bohun come to Dallory, he would pay a visit to a

certain white tomb in the churchyard. choose a solitary evening for

it, after twilight have fall, and remain near it for hour, there

he indulge his grief. who can tell how he call upon her?--who can

tell how he pour out all the misery of his repentant heart, pray

to be forgive? neither she nor Heaven could answer he in this world.

she be go; gone: all his regret be unavaile to recall she: there

remain nothing but the marble stone, and the simple name upon it:



     "ELLEN ADAIR."









the END.
